Iberian Legislatures in Comparative Perspective

This comprehensive book analyses the development of the legislatures in the Iberian countries over the past 40 years, since democracy was introduced, to comparatively understand their role in these political systems and in sustaining their democratic systems. Sharing many historical developments and institutional characteristics, Spain and Portugal also present crucial differences, notably Spain’s pure parliamentary system, a King as Head of State and a quasi-federal structure, and Portugal’s semi-presidential democracy. Starting with a historical and institutional contextualization of these two legislatures, the book examines the most important organizational and behavioural features of legislative life in Iberian legislatures in a comparative perspective. It also shows how new legislatures develop resilience capacity to support lasting democratic systems as full-fledged institutionalized bodies. This book will be of key interest to scholars and students of Spain and Portugal, legislative politics and parliamentarianism, and more broadly to European politics and comparative politics, journalists and practitioners.

Jorge M. Fernandes is a Post-Doctoral Fellow at the Institute of Social Sciences, University of Lisbon, Portugal. His work has appeared, among others, in Comparative Political Studies, European Journal of Political ­Research, Legislative Studies Quarterly, and Party Politics. His research interests are representation, legislatures, political parties, electoral parties, and Portuguese politics. He has been a Visiting Scholar at the Center for European Studies, Harvard University and at UC San Diego.

Cristina Leston-Bandeira is a Professor of Politics at the University of Leeds, UK. She has worked on parliaments for over 20 years and is a key scholar on the Portuguese Parliament. She is Co-Director of the Centre for ­Democratic Engagement and the Chair of the UK Study of Parliament Group. She is also co-editor of the journal Parliamentary Affairs and a Constitution Unit Fellow.

Acknowledgements

This volume deals with legislatures in Iberian countries in a comparative perspective. Spain and Portugal share more than their geographical location as Iberian countries. Indeed, the political and social development of both countries has gone hand-in-hand over the past decades. In the 1970s, after some of the longest rightist dictatorships in Western Europe, Iberian countries transition to democracy heralded the beginning of the third wave of democratisation. After a long and convoluted process of democratic consolidation, in 1986, both countries accessed the European Union. On the same day, a joint ceremony was held in Lisbon and Madrid, where the heads of government sealed the commitment of democratic elites to a path of multiparty liberal democracy. Spain and Portugal underwent tremendous economic, social and political changes in the European Union, benefiting from generous cohesion funds from their European partners. In 2008, both countries fell victim of the worldwide economic meltdown, whose political and economic consequences loom large to this day. For all their similarities, Spanish and Portuguese political systems have crucial differences that matter for the functioning of legislatures. First, Spain has a quasi-federal structure while Portugal has a strongly unitary character. Second, Spain is a pure parliamentary system, whose head of state is the King. Conversely, Portugal is a semi-presidential democracy, where the directly elected president holds critical political powers. Third, reflecting its quasi-federal structure, Spain has a bicameral legislature, as opposed to the Portuguese unicameral chamber. This volume has two main goals: First, to take stock of the most important organisational and behavioural features of legislative life in Iberian legislatures in comparative perspective. Our approach is topic-oriented – that is, we have selected some of the most critical aspects of legislatures and examined them in both countries under a similar theoretical framework. Our understanding of legislative organisation is one where representatives ­(micro-level) are part of internal structures (meso-level), such as parliamentary party groups and committees, operating in an environment where formal and informal rules are overarching constraints (macro-level).

1 Introduction

Jorge M. Fernandes

2  Jorge M. Fernandes

Second, we aim to offer the first-ever comprehensive comparative study of Spanish and Portuguese legislatures to a wide English language audience. Importantly, since the early 2000s, there has been an increase in ­international literature’s attention to legislative studies in Iberian countries. In Spain, Field (2016) contributes to the study of the legislature in making minority governments work. Chaqués and colleagues (2015) examine agenda-setting in the Spanish parliament. Recently, Coller and colleagues (2018) edited a volume on how representatives establish, and perceive, their dyadic relationship with their constituents. Finally, Verge (2010) has published extensively on gender and representation in Spain. Unlike Spain, the Portuguese Assembleia da Republica has yet to receive attention on several topics. Since the 1990s, Leston-Bandeira (2004, 2009) produced seminal works on Portuguese legislative politics. Recent work by Espírito-Santo and colleagues (2018) examined the role of gender in the legislature. Furthermore, Fernandes (2016) and Fernandes and colleagues (2018) examine the internal organisation of committees and the use of parliamentary questions, respectively. Legislative studies in both countries have therefore produced recently important studies; still much remains to be done. This volume hopes to present some of that recent research, whilst moving forward its research agenda.

Plan of the volume

Our volume is divided into five sections. The first section is devoted to the Historical Background of the Spanish and the Portuguese legislatures. In Chapter 2, Goes and Leston-Bandeira make an overview of the development of Portugal’s parliament over the past 40 years. The authors analyse the role of the parliament in the Portuguese political system, as well as the development of political parties and the party system. In Chapter 3, Field and Gray make an appraisal of the Spanish legislature since the 1970s, focusing on asymmetric bicameralism, party dominance and executive dominance. Furthermore, the authors analyse the frequent minority government and recent party system changes in Spain. The second section turns to Legislative Structures. In Chapter 4, Jalali and Teruel examine the internal organisation and functioning of parliamentary party groups. The authors examine how party groups relate with their MPs concerning their financial resources, also analysing how parliamentary party groups relate with extra-parliamentary party organisations and the executive. In Chapter 5, Fernandes and Riera inspect committee systems in Spain and Portugal. The authors explore these legislatures’ committee structures, size, and powers, and how they have changed over the past decades. The third section examines Actors and Representation. In Chapter 6, Freire and Coller use mass and elite surveys to appraise the level of ideological congruence between parliamentarians and their constituents before and

Introduction  3

after the Great Recession. In Chapter 7, Espírito-Santo, Verge and Morales make an intersectional analysis of descriptive representation in Spain and Portugal. The authors look at how gender interacts with age, sexuality, ethnicity and ableness to assess the extent to which legislatures are representative of the population at large. In Chapter 8, Lisi and Muñoz analyse how interest groups use parliamentary channels to influence policy-making, whilst also exploring informal channels of influence and their increasing use. In Chapter 9, Santana-Pereira and Martín use mass surveys to produce an account of citizens’ attitudes towards parliamentary institutions in Spain and Portugal. Specifically, the authors analyse at trust and performance measures to understand diffuse and specific support. Furthermore, they examine citizens’ opinions towards electoral reforms. The fourth section turns to Legislative Processes. In Chapter 10, Ribeiro, Araújo and Rodrigues probe the legislative process, arguably one of the most relevant functions of legislatures. The authors spell out the functioning of the legislative process, highlighting the dominance of the executive in law-making. The chapter offers extensive descriptive statistics on legislative initiatives in Iberian legislatures. In Chapter 11, Sánchez de Dios inspects accountability mechanisms. Specifically, the author looks at how legislative actors use tools of their repertoire to monitor the executive. The author offers an empirical account of the use of parliamentary written questions, question time, indirizzo, debates and so forth. The fifth section deals with Relationships with Other Bodies. In ­Chapter 12, Aranda and Sande analyse the relationships with the European Union. Specifically, the authors consider the role of committees and the Chamber as arenas for EU scrutiny. Moreover, Aranda and Sande examine inter-­parliamentary cooperation and the use of the early warning mechanism processes. In ­Chapter 13, Oñate and Belchior examine the relationship between national and regional parliaments. They show that parliamentary institutions are not efficient vertical integration mechanisms. Instead, Oñate and Belchior suggest that political parties are the best political integration mechanism. In ­Chapter 14, Garoupa, Solanes Mullor and Violante turn to the relationship between the parliament and the Constitutional Court. The authors offer a descriptive account of the role of the legislatures in the process of selection of members of the judicial branch. Furthermore, they examine the structure for litigation and make an empirical assessment of litigation over the past few years.

Section I Historical background

Introduction

Like most political institutions, the Assembleia da República (AR) is the result of both the agency of the political actors who designed it and a by-product of the political, economic, social and cultural contexts in which it emerged and evolved (Pierson 2004). Over its 40 years of existence, the AR has simultaneously responded to the circumstances of the times that surrounded its birth and growth as a mature representative institution and has set the template for Portugal’s democratic culture and political system. To consider these factors, the overview of the AR proposed in this chapter is divided into two main parts. The first part offers a landscape portrait of the political and economic context which informed the set-up and development of the Portuguese parliament. It therefore first charts the historical context leading to the introduction of the AR and then considers how European integration shaped Portuguese party politics and parliamentary practices. The chapter’s second part zooms in and explains in detail the role of parliament within the Portuguese political system. This section starts by offering an overview of Portugal’s main political institutions and an explanation of how they relate to each other. Given the predominant role of political parties in the AR, this section explains the development of the party system and the relationship between parliament and the executive. The final section of the chapter examines the key changes the AR underwent since 1976, demonstrating that its role has shifted from a predominantly legislative function to a scrutiny role.

Historical context

Before we focus on the development of the AR in the post-1974 democratic regime, it is useful to consider its historical context. Portugal had two experiments with democratic representative institutions before the Carnations’ Revolution of 1974 (during Salazar’s dictatorship there was a National Assembly but this was not a democratic institution). Both experiments, first the constitutional monarchy of 1822–1910 and second the First Republic of

2 The Portuguese Assembleia da República in context

Eunice Goes and Cristina Leston-Bandeira

8  Eunice Goes and Cristina Leston-Bandeira

1910–1926, were characterized by instability, oligarchic political parties and a weak democratic culture. Portugal’s constitutional monarchy of 1822–1910 followed the template of similar regimes of that period: legislative power was exercised by a directly elected Chamber of Deputies; executive power was vested in the figure of the Sovereign and his ministers, with judicial power in the hands of the courts. The Chamber of Deputies was dominated by two parties – the Progressive and the Regenerator – which took turns to lead, being however ideologically indistinct (Sardica 1997: 747; Jalali 2015: 42), as well as oligarchic and clientelistic in their modus operandi (Sardica 1997: 750; Leston-Bandeira 2004; Pinto and Rezola 2007: 353). In effect, parties were non-existent and politicians were driven by their personal agendas. This regime was dominated by what is commonly known as caciquismo, which refers to corrupt locally based networks developed around individual politicians with considerable local power. The reputation of the executive was equally tainted. The state of almost bankruptcy of the Portuguese economy, social unrest and crucially the unpopularity of King Carlos I’s response to the British ultimatum of 18901 ultimately led to the collapse of the Constitutional Monarchy on 5 October 1910. With the establishment of a democratic republic, the third one in Europe, the republican leaders aimed to transform Portugal into a modern Western European democracy. However, the First Republic was affected by the same political and social unrest that had doomed the constitutional monarchy. As Wheeler succinctly put it, ‘with a total of 45 governments, eight general elections and eight presidents in 15 years and eight months, the Portuguese Republic was the most unstable parliamentary regime in Western Europe’ (1978: 865)2. This chronic instability paved the way to the military coup of 1926. Invoking the need for a strong state to ‘regenerate’ the country and save the economy from bankruptcy (Telo, 1994 786), the military took over and ruled until 1933. Then an austere professor of economics named António de ­Oliveira Salazar, who had been Finance Minister since 1929, sidestepped the generals and established the New State (Estado-Novo). The new regime, entrenched in the Constitution of 1933, was led by a President of the Council (head of government),3 included a Corporatist (upper) Chamber which retained a consultative status in a weak, and mainly consultative, National Assembly. In turn, the National Assembly was dominated by the single party, National Union. Finally, there was a President of the Republic who had mostly a ceremonial role. Though not a fascist regime, Salazar’s conservative dictatorship had adopted a ‘certain fascist choreography’ (Pinto and Rezola 2007: 360). ­Italian fascism, in particular its combination of corporatism with ­Catholicism, as well as its sophisticated propaganda and censorship apparatus inspired the New State (Pinto 2008: 31).

Portuguese Assembleia da República  9

Portugal’s New State lasted for a surprisingly long period, but it was not immune to crisis. There were several attempted coups and active, though ultimately weak, opposition groups which plotted to overthrow the regime. But it would be the wars for independence in Portugal’s African colonies, started in 1961, that would pose the greatest threat to the regime’s survival. These wars overstretched the financial and human resources of the state (Hersvik and Larsen 2004: 179) and nurtured discontent in the armed forces (Maxwell 1999: 54–55). After a few unsuccessful attempts, the Movement of the Armed Forces (MFA), which gathered a small group of junior and middle-rank officers, put an end to 48 years of dictatorial rule in Portugal on 25 April 1974. The notoriously peaceful coup was enthusiastically supported by the population, who welcomed the young officers in the streets of Lisbon with red carnations. But if the Carnations’ Revolution marked the beginning of the third wave of democratization worldwide (Huntington 1991: 3), it was not clear then how it would end. Immediately after the coup, the Junta of National Salvation assumed sovereign power, elected a new president from its members and appointed a government. Another revolutionary body, the Council of State, exercised legislative power until the elections of the Constituent Assembly, called for a year after the revolution, the 25 April of 1975. At the time of the coup, political parties were largely absent. Apart from the Portuguese Communist Party (PCP), which operated as an organized albeit clandestine political force during the dictatorial period (Lisi 2008), most parties were created immediately before (the Socialist Party in 1973) or just after the coup of 1974 (the case of the centre-right Social-Democratic Party – PSD – and the conservative CDS). The weak anchorage of political parties in Portuguese society at the time meant that MFA took a leading role in Portuguese politics immediately after the coup (Pinto 2008: 269; Rezola 2008: 12). However, the MFA was weakened by its internal divisions. Apart from nebulous references to the three ‘Ds’ in its manifesto – democratize, decolonize and develop – the MFA could not agree about the aims of the coup (Rezola 2008: 6). In the meantime, the 1975 elections to the Constituent Assembly, which was tasked with drafting the new constitution, went ahead as scheduled. In these elections, the pro-European Socialist Party (PS) was the most voted party, attracting 37.9 per cent of the vote (Leston-Bandeira and Freire 2003: 57). The approval of the new Constitution in 1976, which established a parliamentary democracy within a semi-presidential political system, started the process of democratic consolidation. Two separate elections elected the first President of the Republic, general Ramalho Eanes, and led to the formation of a socialist minority government. However, these elections did not conclude the process of democratic transition. As the 1976 Constitution attributed powers to a military body, the Council of the Revolution, Portuguese

10  Eunice Goes and Cristina Leston-Bandeira

democracy was compromised (Rezola 2008: 14). It was only when the 1982 constitutional reform abolished this body4 that Portuguese democracy was consolidated (Linz and Stepan 1996: 116).

The Europeanization of Portugal: politics and economics

The path of democratic consolidation and the development of parliament’s role are very closely linked to Portugal’s process of Europeanization. Indeed, Portugal’s two main parties viewed membership of the European Economic Community (EEC) as a condition to the development of a solid representative democracy. The Socialist Party, which had strong links with sister parties in France, Germany and Britain, made membership of the then EEC a key national priority. In the electoral campaign to the AR’s first elections in 1976, the PS chose A Europa Connosco (Europe With Us) as its slogan (Sassoon 1997: 596; Lobo and Magalhães 2001: 27), and in February 1977 the socialist minority government presented its formal application to join the EEC. A month later, Brussels formally started the process of accession which lasted almost a decade (Soares 2007: 462).5 By agreeing to start accession talks with Portugal, the EEC showed that its initial reservations about Portugal’s democratic credentials (Teixeira and Pinto 2017: 21) had disappeared. Portugal’s accession to the EU in 1986 marked the beginning of a new era in foreign policy, which now prioritized Europe over other international commitments. This new understanding, shared by the two parties of government, the PS and the PSD, posited that Portugal could only avoid international and European marginalization by participating fully in the project of European integration. This bipartisan consensus about Europe implied that the two main parties relied on each other’s support in parliament to approve legislation pertaining to the EU. In practice, the new foreign policy consensus implied that both parties agreed that, first, Portugal had to demonstrate competence (which was translated in compliance with EU requirements) and, that, second, Portugal should fully participate in all dimensions of European integration (Gaspar 2000: 361–363; Teixeira and Pinto 2017: 27–28). To that end, Portugal signed the Schengen agreement, was an enthusiastic supporter of EU enlargement, was a founding member of the European Monetary Union (EMU) and took a leading role in the intergovernmental conference which culminated with the signing of the Lisbon Treaty of 2007. But these commitments, especially the decision to be a founding member of EMU, posed considerable challenges to the Portuguese economy. Doubtless, EU membership positively transformed the Portuguese economy. In the period 1994–2000, real GDP growth averaged more than 4 per cent annually, but structural problems, such as low productivity and a low-skilled workforce, meant it was still vulnerable to external shocks (Royo 2010).6

Furthermore, the goal to become a founding member of EMU presented Portugal with difficult challenges. Despite the efforts of António

Portuguese Assembleia da República  11

Guterres’ socialist government, in 1999 Portugal had only nominally met the Maastricht convergence criteria to join EMU. In truth, there was no real ­economic convergence with the rest of Europe (Royo 2010). In this context, it is not surprising that Portugal was amongst the first member states to breach the euro’s convergence criteria. As the public deficit rose to more than 4 per cent of the GDP and inflation reached 3.6 per cent in 2001, ­Portugal was the first Eurozone country to be subject to the EU’s Excessive Deficit Procedure (EDP). In the period 2002–2008, successive governments succeeded in temporarily bringing down the deficit to below the required 3 per cent of the GDP, but these periods of fiscal probity tended to be short-lived. In typical fashion, when the socialist government had finally managed to bring the deficit down to 2.2 per cent in 2008, those efforts were quickly eroded by the effects of the global financial crisis. Like other European countries, the government responded to the crisis with anti-cyclical policies which included major investments in public ­infrastructure, research and development, as well as tax rises and ­public-sector reforms. But these measures did not produce the desired effects. At the end of 2009, the public deficit rose to 9.3 per cent of the GDP, and the economy was in recession. At the request of the EU, the Socialist minority government implemented three packages of austerity measures which had a negligible effect over the public deficit. To make matters worse, unemployment rose from below 8 per cent in 2009 to 12 per cent in 2011, the economy shrunk by 9 per cent in the period 2008–2013, and borrowing costs were spiralling out of control. As the opposition parties voted down its fourth package of austerity in the spring of 2011, the government resigned whilst at the same time seeking a loan from the European Commission, the European Central Bank and the International Monetary Fund (IMF). In exchange for the 78 billion euros loan, Portugal agreed to implement a Memorandum of Understanding (MoU) which was implemented with considerable ideological zeal by the ­subsequently appointed centre-right coalition government. As a reward for its exemplary implementation of the MoU, the EU ­announced Portugal’s exit from the bailout programme in 2014, though the country’s fiscal position was far from stabilized. Public deficit was still above 7 per cent, unemployment affected 13.9 per cent of the working ­population (down from the peak of 16.2 per cent reached in 2013) and the severe public spending cuts and tax rises had impacted considerably the purchasing power of the population. By then the long-standing bipartisan consensus7 about Europe had visible cracks. Whilst the PSD saw the bailout as an opportunity to reform the Portuguese economy along monetarist lines and to align Portugal with Germany, the PS, historically the most pro-European party became increasingly aware that the policies associated with the euro were an obstacle to its programmatic goals. Since 2014, the Socialists started to challenge

12  Eunice Goes and Cristina Leston-Bandeira

Brussels’s pro-austerity line and have demanded reforms to the governance of the EMU with a view to promote greater social cohesion and economic convergence. In these circumstances, it is not surprising that following the inconclusive results of the 2015 parliamentary elections, the minority socialist government, which relied on the support of the radical left parties Left Bloc and PCP-Greens in parliament, changed the direction of macro-economic policy. Instead of pursuing further public spending cuts and privatizations, the government reversed some of the former and froze the latter. As Portugal’s public deficit fell to 2 per cent of the GDP in 2016, Portugal exited the EDP in the spring of 2017 and since then has been hailed a story of great economic success by the EU. Yet, the turning of this economic corner has not rendered Portugal’s immune to crisis. Public debt has remained high and economic convergence with the Eurozone remains a pipedream. Throughout this journey, parliament played a crucial role either by being the site where key decisions pertaining to the EU were taken or where the two main parties orchestrated Portugal’s full participation in European integration.

The role of parliament in Portugal’s political system

The core political institutions

As the previous sections showed, the full democratization of Portugal has been the main goal actively pursued since 1974 through diplomacy, ­economic policy and the development of political institutions. Of all the political institutions established in the 1976 Constitution, the AR was the one where democratic representative principles were most clearly asserted. This would be an institution providing key legitimacy to the new democratic regime, but also one which would learn from the legacy of its past. As such, establishing the new parliament was foremost about ensuring a democratic and representative legitimacy, but also about ensuring a platform for an enduring regime; crucially, one that would not fall foul of its past failures. The key consequences of this would be the placement of parliament at the heart of the new regime, whilst providing for a referee like figure in the President of the Republic, as well as giving considerable power to political parties to ensure the stability of democracy. The constitutional framework established by the 1976 Constitution provides therefore for a triangular organization of political power and legitimacy (Canotilho and Moreira 1991: 213), which is divided between parliament, the president and government. Despite some important amendments, namely in 1982, 1989 and 2004, in its essence the 1976 Constitution still today frames the essentials of the Portuguese political system. As in other European semi-presidential systems, the Portuguese president is directly elected by the people, has veto powers over legislation (it can ask parliament to revise

Portuguese Assembleia da República  13

draft bills considered unconstitutional) and, under certain conditions, can dissolve parliament and dismiss the government. These powers were more loosely used in the first years of democratic rule, particularly in 1978/1979, being restricted in the 1982 revision of the constitution, but have played an important part in Portuguese democracy. It is thanks to these powers, for instance, that recurrent Presidents have asked parliament to consider again key pieces of legislation, such as abortion laws or indeed austerity packages. It was also in use of these powers that in 2004 President Sampaio decided to dissolve parliament and call for new elections, considering that the government was not fulfilling its democratic functions. But if the President acts as a guarantor of the democratic functioning of political institutions, the main accountability relationship takes place between parliament and government, with the latter being politically ­responsible to the AR, enshrining core democratic accountability principles. Due to a separation of powers, which makes members of government separate to members of parliament, the executive does not need to derive directly from parliament’s political forces. But it needs the support from parliament and, in practice, it cannot govern without parliament’s support. This is best expressed through the process of government investiture ­(Leston-Bandeira and Fernandes 2015). New governments are formally invested by the president, but also need parliament’s tacit approval. Although parliament does not have to formally approve the new government, it does have the power to move a motion to reject its programme, which is presented at the investiture moment through a debate of several days in the AR. The AR can also move motions of censure to the government, which, if approved by an absolute majority, lead to the government’s dismissal. This tool was used recurrently in the first decade of democratic rule, leading namely to the dismissal of Cavaco Silva’s first government in 1987. More recently, this has been used mainly as a tool to set the political agenda (Leston-Bandeira and Fernandes 2015). Likewise, governments can ask for parliament’s confidence through an equivalent motion, which reaffirms, or otherwise, their legitimacy to govern. Besides these three core institutions, we should also note the role of the Constitutional Court, as the ultimate guarantor of Portuguese Constitution. Composed of 13 judges, 10 of which elected by parliament (with the remaining co-opted), the Constitutional Court plays an important role as a check on legislation which may be considered unconstitutional. It is independent from the other core political institutions, playing therefore a key role in safeguarding the Constitution. Finally, the last core element in Portugal’s constitutional design is the electoral system used for general elections. Despite constitutional amendments enabling a possible change to the electoral law, the Portuguese parliament has been elected through the same proportional representation d’Hondt system since 1976. The enshrining of the proportionality rule was a fundamental step in ensuring the democratic legitimacy of the new regime.

14  Eunice Goes and Cristina Leston-Bandeira

By choosing this electoral system, the drafters of the Constitution aimed to prevent the reemergence of the caciquismo phenomenon and disincentivize the cultivation of personal vote (Ware 1995), which had undermined the Constitutional Monarchy and the First Republic, as seen above; it also offset the weak institutionalization of political parties (Jalali 2015: 308). However, a consequence of this choice was, as the literature on electoral systems establishes (Gallagher and Marsh 1988; Carey and Shugart 1995), the centralization of power in the hands of party leaders who are in charge of selecting the individual parliamentary candidates. For those reasons, the Constitution established that the representative mandate is a national one, as set in Art 152b: “Deputies represent the whole of the country and not the districts through which they were elected”. As such, the AR’s 2308 Deputies are elected through closed lists across 22 separate districts – 18 across the continental Portugal, plus two for the autonomous regions of the Azores and Madeira islands, and two for emigrants, outside and within Europe; these districts are mere administrative divisions, they are not equivalent to the Anglo-Saxon conception of a constituency. The electoral system has led to a whole range of different types of electoral results, from minority governments, to absolutely majority ones, to different types of coalitions. This in turn has led to very different types of relationship between parliament and government as the following section shows.

The development of the party system

Since the parliamentary elections of 1976, the Portuguese multiparty system has been stable and predictable (Jalali 2014: 236), with contours largely unchanged. The party system has been defined by the electoral competition between five relevant parties (Sartori 1976: 123) – the PS, the PSD, the CDS-PP, and the anti-system parties PCP and Left Bloc (Freire 2005: 81; Jalali 2014: 237; Lisi 2015). Of these five, the PS and the PSD, typical catch-all parties (Jalali 2015), have been the parties of government since 1976; the CDS has been mostly a coalition partner to the centre-right PSD (though it briefly coalesced with the Socialists in 1978); the anti-system parties PCP and Left Bloc (this party only emerged in 1999) had always refused to join coalitions with the Socialists. But the impact of the 2011 deficit crisis and subsequent bailout agreement with the troika institutions, and perhaps the expectation of demonstrating to the electorate that they were not merely protest parties ­(Dunphy and Bale 2011: 500–501) led to a radical change in the political and electoral strategies of these parties. Following the 2015 parliamentary elections, both the PCP-Greens and the Left Bloc joined a quasi-coalitional arrangement with the PS (Fernandes 2016: 898). One of the remarkable features of the Portuguese party system has been its responsiveness to changing economic circumstances (Lobo 1996). Indeed, a pattern can be observed since the 1976 elections. In periods of economic downturn (1976–87; 2001–2005; 2009–2015), the party system tends towards

Portuguese Assembleia da República  15

greater fragmentation, whilst in periods of economic growth (1987–2001; 2005–2009) the system shows majoritarian tendencies. As explained below, this pattern is shown by the Effective Number of Parliamentary Parties (ENPP) index.9

In the first decade of Portuguese democracy, marked by economic crisis and two IMF interventions as seen above, the party system was fragmented and showed symptoms of ‘polarized pluralism’ (Opello and Claggett 1984: 167). In that period, the ENPP ranged from 3.47 in 1976 reaching the high point of 4.23 at the 1985 elections (at this election the new party PRD became the third largest party in parliament; this party disappeared soon after the 1985 elections). Unsurprisingly, this high level of party fragmentation resulted in either minority governments or coalition governments that never completed their terms (Lobo 2001). In the period 1987–2002, which coincided with a period of unprecedented economic growth, the party system manifested majoritarian tendencies (Lobo 2001: 687; Freire 2005: 86). The ENPP in this period ranged between 2.36 in 1987 and 2.57 in 2002. At the 1987 elections, the PSD managed the rare occurrence in proportional representation electoral systems of winning an absolute majority; an achievement repeated in 1991. Four years later, the PS capitalized on voter fatigue following ten years of PSD governments and won the 1995 election with a comfortable plurality of 43.76 per cent of the vote (four seats short of a majority). As the economy registered the highest rate of growth since democratization, the PS increased slightly its majority to 44.06 per cent in the 1999 elections. However, this government was short-lived. Following the disappointing results obtained at the 2001 local elections, the then Prime Minister António Guterres resigned, and early elections were called for 2002. By then the economy was on the verge of recession and Portugal was found to be in breach of the convergence criteria governing the euro, as seen above. But despite the unpopularity of the socialist government, the PSD failed to win a majority and formed a coalition government with the CDS which did not last its full term. The deteriorating economic circumstances and the turmoil within the leadership of the PSD led President Jorge Sampaio to dissolve parliament in 2004 and call early elections (Neto and Lobo 2009). Voters’ desire for political stability led to the first overall majority of the PS in 2005, however this period of relative stability proved to be a brief interlude. The PS lost its majority in 2009. In truth, none of the two main parties has won an overall majority since then. These results confirm the thesis that voters often punish incumbent parties for their challenging economic circumstances (Bartels 2013: 49; Magalhães 2017: 756). The resulting pattern of party fragmentation at the time of crisis was shown by the ENPP index which rose to 3.13 in 2009, and the only reason why it declined to 2.71 in 2015 was because the PSD and the CDS-PP contested the election in a joint platform (Para a Frente Portugal (PAF) – Portugal Ahead).

16  Eunice Goes and Cristina Leston-Bandeira

In 2009, the PS led a short-lived minority government which collapsed in 2011 because of the fragile state of public finances. Following the 2011 parliamentary elections, the PSD formed a coalition government with the CDS, which despite the severity of the crisis, lasted its full term. When the 2015 parliamentary elections took place, the recession was officially over, however its effects were still felt by most voters. Consequently, the electorate reacted by denying a majority to the two parties that were blamed for the decline in their living standards (Magalhães 2017). The rightwing platform PAF won a plurality of votes – 36.33 per cent of the vote share – but fell short of a majority of seats in parliament. The Socialists, on the other hand, had very disappointing results, winning only 32.31 per cent. By contrast, the Left Bloc obtained its best result, winning 10.19 per cent of the vote and becoming the third largest party in parliament. The coalition ­PCP-Greens won 8.25 per cent of the vote. So far, so predictable. But, as Fernandes argued, what happened immediately after the 2015 elections can potentially change the pattern of relationships between the relevant parties and therefore bring significant changes to the party system (2016: 898). Instead of seeking a coalition agreement with the centre-right PSD, the PS asked the Left Bloc and the PCP-Greens to support the formation of a socialist minority government. After 35 days of negotiations, the PCP-Greens and the Left Bloc agreed to support the PS. This was a remarkable turn of events that put an end to what the socialist leader António Costa called the Berlin Wall of Portuguese politics and which had prevented any type of dialogue between the different left-wing parties since democratization. Challenging expectations, this arrangement proved to be stable and is highly likely to last its full term. However, it is too early to establish whether this arrangement will leave a permanent mark in the party system. If the pattern that has dominated Portuguese party politics since 1976 endures, the period of economic growth that started in 2016 may well herald the return of majoritarian tendencies to the party system.

The relationship between parliament and government

As the development of the party system makes clear, the role of the AR is fundamentally defined in accordance to its relationship with the government. This encompasses mainly two key roles, the legislative role and the scrutiny role, the salience of which varies according to the type of political support the government enjoys in parliament (Leston-Bandeira 2004). Indeed, the AR becomes the central political institution when the party system is fragmented and no party holds a majority, however the AR is somewhat sidelined when the party of government controls an absolute majority of seats. Comparatively to other legislatures, the AR has considerable legislative powers. It can initiate, amend and reject legislation, regardless of its origins. The Constitution establishes a number of policy areas that can only be legislated through parliament. However, the government also has independent

Portuguese Assembleia da República  17

powers of legislation, being able to issue decrees-law in a variety of policy areas. Although parliament can recall some of these for scrutiny, they represent a considerable portion of the legislation passed. Still, parliament’s reserved areas of policy touch on the fundamentals of policy-making, such as education or defence (CRP, Art. 164), or indeed health and taxation which are “relative reserved” policy areas (CRP, Art. 165), for example, the government may legislate in those areas, as long as it secures a delegation of power from parliament. The Portuguese parliament’s legislative power is illustrated by the fact that Deputies have legislative powers to initiate, amend and/or reject legislation, regardless of whether they are in opposition or in government, and regardless of the size of their parliamentary group. This means that even if the opposition does not have enough support in parliament to pass their own legislation or to amend the government’s proposals, they can nonetheless present their own proposals (projects of law), which are discussed together with government proposed legislation (proposals of law). This is an important agenda setting power, almost non-existent in legislatures such as the UK House of Commons. In practice, however, the AR’s legislative power over the past 40 years has fluctuated according to the size (and type) of government’s political support in parliament. When minority governments are in power, the AR tends to have considerable legislative powers, forcing the executive to negotiate with parliament on a law-by-law basis. This was the case of Cavaco Silva’s 1985 government, Guterres’s 1999 government and Costa’s 2015 government, which only exists thanks to the support of key parliamentary groups. However, in situations of absolute majority, the AR’s legislative role becomes minimal, such as during Cavaco Silva’s 1991 and 1995 governments or Sócrates’s 2005 government. The party composition of each legislature is therefore key to determine how much leeway governments have in shaping their legislation outside the AR. Likewise, its scrutiny role has also varied considerably depending on the size of the government’s majority in parliament. The scrutiny role becomes particularly pressing and active at times of absolute majority government. However, in the 1970s this role was not a priority for the constitutional drafters; at that time parliament’s key role was undoubtedly seen primarily as a legislative one, albeit of course with important accountability responsibilities which could be triggered through motions of no-confidence. As Leston-Bandeira has stated, scrutiny was initially “seen as a guarantee of democracy, rather than a practice of democracy” (2001: 150). It was only when democracy became more established and politics more stable, that scrutiny tools started to expand. Scrutiny as a routine day-to-day check on government would therefore only develop properly from the early 1990s onwards, namely in the form of further opportunities for questioning, more opportunities for debates and stronger roles for committees. The shaping of parliament’s role is also closely linked to its own internal development, as the next section shows.

18  Eunice Goes and Cristina Leston-Bandeira

The development of the institution

The AR’s modus operandi has changed considerably over its first 40 years of democratic rule. However, one element has remained at its core: the central role of political parties as an antidote to the corruption and instability stemming from the factionalized and individualized politics that characterized earlier parliamentary experiences. Thus, in the mid-1970s political parties emerged as the core unit of political representation tasked with the role of stabilizing the political system. As a result, a number of regulations ensure that still today political parties – in the form of parliamentary groups – are the core unit of representation, controlling much of what happens in parliament. This is ensured, in the first instance, by the Constitution, which establishes that political parties hold the monopoly in candidate selection (CRP, Art. 151), this in turn centralizes power in the hands of party leaders (Magalhães 2011). In addition, Art. 180 of the Constitution specifically ascribes to parliamentary groups a number of rights, such as interpellations (a form of debate). Within parliament, most of the key resources, such as quotas of parliamentary business or budget entitlement, are distributed by parliamentary groups (rather than individual Deputies). Although revisions to the Rules of Procedure (namely in 1993 and in 2007) introduced some rights to independent Deputies, such as time to speak in the Chamber and the identification of specific Deputies in the presentation of bills. Other than some exceptions specifically listed in the Rules of ­Procedure, parliamentary business is therefore organized according to parliamentary groups. This includes most of the parliamentary business, from the time allocated to groups to speak in the Chamber, to the composition of Committees or the entitlement to scrutiny tools. The predominance of parliamentary groups, and within this MPs’ dependence on their parties to be reelected, explains why party discipline is so strong in the Portuguese parliament (Leston-Bandeira 2009). Personifying the importance of the parliamentary groups to the organization of parliamentary business, the Conference of Leaders, which brings together the presidents of all parliamentary groups and the President of the AR, is a key decision-making body within parliament. The other key determinant of parliamentary business is no other than its own rulebook: the Regimento, for example, Rules of Procedure. The Rules of Procedure were absolutely key in 1976 to establish what sort of institution the AR would be and how it would organize its business. In the absence of a democratic parliamentary practice, and despite the existence of staff and representatives with parliamentary experience under dictatorial rule, this regulatory document would be key in shaping the new legislature. Forty years later, the Rules of Procedure remain as important. However, over time they have undergone considerable revisions, namely in 1988, 1993 and 2007. Overall, these revisions have shaped the legislature into a more professional

Portuguese Assembleia da República  19

and efficient institution, but also one more open to the external world; although in absolute terms, the legislature of the 1970s was in many ways far more democratic. This was partly achieved through a rationalization of procedures in 1988, seen at the time as very controversial and restrictive of the rights of the opposition (Leston-Bandeira 2004). After all, the Rules of Procedures set-up in 1976 made clear that their goal was to enshrine a strong democratic legitimacy into the new system. For that reason, the Rules of Procedure were incredibly democratic, providing for strong opposition rights. Indeed, in many ways the opposition had more rights than the government. This is because many of the rights were protected as opposition rights, regardless of the size of the group. This translated, amongst other things, into very long plenary debates. The 1988 revision of the Rules of Procedure put an end to this. From then on, rights would be ascribed according to the size of the parliamentary group – this meant that the larger the group, the more rights it would be entitled to, leading to an emboldening of those groups supporting the government. The 1988 revision rationalized procedures, leading to more streamlined parliamentary processes and activities. Likewise, and again personifying the pursuit of enshrining democratic ideals, the AR in the 1970s was the Chamber (Leston-Bandeira 2004). Most of the parliamentary business focused on the discussion of legislation (the legislative package that would shape the new democratic regime) and most of this happened in the Chamber. Committees were not allowed to meet at the same time, as the Chamber had hardly any resources available to support its work. With successive changes to the Rules of Procedure, taking place over the past 40 years, Committees have become a core part of parliamentary business in the AR, having now their own staffing and having become far more professionalized. What is more, Committees are now a core forum for the scrutiny of government legislation and policies. Connected to this, in terms of professionalization of the institution, it is also worth noting that the job of Deputy has also undergone considerable change over the past 40 years. For instance, in the 1970s, the term of a Deputy could last as little as a single day. The use of substitutions in the list system was not only perfectly normal and accepted, but also regular. For example, 576 substitutions took place within the single year of 1983–1984, from a pool of only 250 Deputies (Leston-Bandeira 2004: 93). Overtime this has been restricted, with now fewer substitutions taking place, leading to a higher professionalization of the job of Deputy. Likewise, if in the early years Deputies had no offices as such (let alone support staff), gradual revisions have introduced offices and strengthened the provision of support staff (though this is still mainly ascribed per parliamentary group, rather than Deputies individually considered). Successive revisions to the Rules of Procedure have also fine-tuned the type of scrutiny tools available to Deputies, with these becoming far more important as democracy became consolidated, as we saw above. If the AR was

20  Eunice Goes and Cristina Leston-Bandeira

established in the 1970s mainly as a forum to debate legislation, over its subsequent 40 years, other roles would emerge and become as important, such as scrutiny but also its relationship with citizens. The 1993 revision was an important step in this path, later on consolidated through the 2007 reform. In terms of openness to citizens, the AR still has some way to go, comparatively with other parliaments in Europe, but it is noteworthy that there is a far more explicit concern with nurturing the relationship with the public (Leston-Bandeira and Tibúrcio 2013). This is reflected in the development of petitions since 1991, reinforced since 2005 with its online provision and now again more recently, in 2018, with the Digital Parliament Group’s initiatives. It is also manifested in a commitment to open the institution to visitors more regularly and in the development of the Parliament of Young People programme, in place since 1995. In addition, the launch of the internet in 1990s introduced a direct communication channel between voters and Deputies. This is of particular significance in a system such as the Portuguese, where the unit of representation is embedded into the party rather than in the Deputies individually considered. In the months after the 1974 revolution, it was very common to witness long queues to enter the AR to attend the public galleries and follow the ongoing debates. This clear interest dwindled over the years though and both turnout and trust in parliament have declined over the past decades. To revert this tendency, the AR is making a concerted effort to seem more aware of public attitudes and to actively reach out to citizens.

Conclusion

This chapter has shown that, since its establishment in 1976, the AR has been a resilient and highly adaptable institution that has played a key role in Portugal’s democratic life. If in its early years, the architects of the AR wanted above all to avoid adopting the design flaws that had plagued earlier experiences with representative institutions, in the last decade the focus has been in improving the quality of Portugal’s democratic life. After more than 40 years of existence, the Portuguese parliament is now a very active law-maker which has become more professional, transparent and aware of citizens’ expectations as well as more robust in its scrutiny role. The development of the AR was largely decided by political parties. As we saw, in the period of transition to democracy, political parties used their democratic legitimacy in the Constituent Assembly to shape the democratic institutions (including the party system) which would be established in the 1976 Constitution. Henceforth, political parties have guided the evolution of the different political institutions and have shaped parliament’s relationship with the Executive, but they have also determined which policy issues should reach the political agenda. Over the past four decades, a pattern can be discerned in the role the AR performs in Portugal’s political system. The AR becomes the centre of

Portuguese Assembleia da República  21

political life when none of the two main parties controls the majority. In these periods, the party of government tends to consult parliament more often to get its agenda approved. In these periods, the AR has been able to set the rules of the game and to introduce innovations that have strengthened the role of parliament as a rule-setter and scrutinizer. This in turn has consolidated political pluralism as in these circumstances smaller parties and individual deputies acquire greater visibility to agenda-set their issues and to therefore influence the legislative agenda of the government of the day. By contrast, when one of the main parties holds an absolute majority, the AR becomes more peripheral. As party discipline is strong, the party of government tends to consult less with other parties because it is in a position to resist any attempt to constrain its room for manoeuvre. Because legislative approval is guaranteed, parliamentary debates become less important. Nonetheless, the AR has significant legislative powers in comparison with other European legislatures and plays a significant role in Portugal’s political life. In addition, the Portuguese parliament seems attuned to the pressures of public opinion and to the changing practices of other European legislatures and has gradually become a more open and responsive institution. For these reasons it can be argued that the AR has been, since its foundation, the barometer of Portugal’s democratic life.

Introduction

Spain’s transition to democracy followed Francisco Franco’s death in 1975. It has traditionally been hailed as a highly successful example of a top-down democratisation process that King Juan Carlos I, Franco’s chosen successor (Gunther 1992), spearheaded. In this elite-centred account, the monarch led the transition to democracy rather than continuing the dictatorship, working together with Prime Minister Adolfo Suárez and a select group of key reformist political actors. This top-down transition contrasts with the ­revolutionary nature of the Portuguese transition. Nonetheless, recent scholarship draws attention to the significant role of civil society in pushing reform forward (Radcliff 2011; Threlfall 2008). Re-democratisation led to the installation of a parliamentary regime, in which government is responsible to parliament, with positive parliamentarism (Bergman 1993). This means that parliament must affirmatively select the government in a formal vote, called an investiture vote. The regime also has a constitutional monarch as the head of state, initially King Juan Carlos I until he abdicated in 2014. Currently, his son, King Felipe VI, reigns. This chapter examines the context in which Spain’s parliament, the Cortes Generales, has developed since the transition to democracy. It is Spain’s second democratic regime (and therefore parliament) following its first experience with mass democracy during the Second Republic in the 1930s, and in many ways that experience shaped the current regime. The parliament also developed in the context of Spain’s ascension to the European Union, which the overwhelming majority of Spanish society embraced, and generally positive economic performance, until the more recent economic crisis that began in 2009. Considering its place in the political system, parliament, and particularly the lower house Congress of Deputies, is clearly the central representative institution. Nonetheless, an institutionally powerful executive constrains parliament’s power, and devolution and Europeanisation disperse policy-making authority. While parliament’s institutional features have ­remained fairly constant over the democratic period, changes in the party system and alternation between minority and majority cabinets affect parliament’s power in practice.

3 The Spanish parliament in context

Bonnie N. Field and Caroline Gray

26  Bonnie N. Field and Caroline Gray

The historical context and re-democratisation

In Spain, liberal parliamentarism dates, first, to the constitution of 1812 (Marcuello and Pérez Ledesma 1996). However, prior to the current regime, Spain’s only proper experience of democracy had been the short-lived Second Republic of 1931–1939, a coup against which led to a brutal civil war (1936–1939) and a longstanding fascist-tinged dictatorship (1939–1977). Spain’s Second Republic implemented negative parliamentarism, meaning that parliament did not formally vote to select the government (Bergman 1993). Instead, the president of the Republic selected the prime minister, and, on the latter’s instructions, the government ministers. Once in office, the government was responsible to parliament. The government was presumed to have the confidence of parliament unless or until it formally withdrew it (Vintró Castells 2007: 236). Unlike Spain’s current democracy, the Second Republic ceded significant powers to the head of state, including appointing the prime minister and making the government separately responsible to the president. It was also an unusual, hybrid regime which was neither fully parliamentary nor semi-presidential (Álvarez Tardío 2004). The constitution stipulated that parliament would elect the first president, but subsequently the president would be elected by parliament and by electors, equal in number to the members of parliament, who are popularly elected. In practice, this only occurred once before the regime was defeated in a civil war. The 1931 Constitution afforded legislative power to a single chamber, the Congress of Deputies. Historically, Spain had also had an upper house, the Senate, which dated back to 1834. Spain’s legislature first became bicameral under the royal statute of that year, following which the lower and upper houses were officially designated as the Congress of Deputies and the Senate in the 1837 constitution. The Senate was initially royally appointed and later remade as an elected chamber under the Restoration period (1875–1923), before its suppression, along with the Congress of Deputies, following the coup of Miguel Primo de Rivera in 1923. The Senate would not be restored until the creation of the bicameral legislature in 1977 during the constituent period following Franco’s death. During the Second Republic, the party system was highly fragmented, and political parties were weak and divided (Juliá 1995). Governments were unstable, multiparty coalitions, amounting to 17 between the approval of the constitution in December 1931 and 18 July 1936 (Vintró Castells 2007: 240). On 18 July, a coup against the young regime led to a brutal civil war, and, by 1939, the authoritarian regime of General Francisco Franco. During the Franco regime (1939–1977), Spain’s faux parliament, las Cortes Españolas, was an undemocratic, corporatist institution that the executive controlled, rather than the reverse. It was inspired in part by the shortlived assembly (Asamblea Nacional Consultiva) that the Primo de Rivera dictatorship had set up between 1927 and 1929 (Giménez Martínez 2012).

The Spanish parliament in context  27

The Francoist parliament was nonetheless significant in Spain’s transition to democracy. Under the leadership of its president—the reformist softliner Torcuato Fernández-Miranda—it approved the Law for Political Reform in November 1976 that was the blueprint for the transition, thereby committing [political] suicide, in the words of Francoist hardliner José Antonio Girón de Velasco.1

The challenge for the monarch and the politicians leading the transition to democracy was to strike a balance between reform and continuity so that the fundamental bases of democratic institutions, as laid out and approved in the 1978 constitution,2 could be accepted by a wide range of political forces from Franco’s heirs on the right through to the communists (Colomer 1998). The transition was negotiated with authoritarian elements still intact, and it was agreed to “let bygones be bygones”, meaning that crimes ­Francoists committed went unpunished and unrecognised (Aguilar 2002). This situation did not gradually start to be reversed until the Historical Memory Law approved decades later in 2007, under then Prime Minister Zapatero, though it has been criticised as not having gone far enough. Greatly influenced by their perceptions of Spain’s political history, transition political leaders also weighed issues of democratic representation along with political stability. Parliament was established as the institution that represented the Spanish people, and was and is the only national institution that is directly elected.3 However, transition leaders’ concern with political stability led them to adopt a variety of institutions that they anticipated would strengthen governments, as discussed below.

Europeanisation and economic transformation

One of the key priorities of Spain’s political elites at the time of the transition was to pave the way for Spain to enter the then European Economic Community (EEC). Following the failure of Franco’s economic model of autarchy in the earlier decades of his dictatorship, the regime had belatedly tried to join the EEC but was rebuffed. It only managed to achieve a preferential trade agreement in 1970, which was very limited (Martínez Chacón 2009). After winning Spain’s first democratic elections in 1977, Suárez, leader of the centrist Union of the Democratic Centre (UCD) that governed Spain from 1977 to 1982, relaunched the country’s accession bid. After eventually being admitted to the EEC in 1986 (alongside Portugal), Spain went on to become one of the founding members of the Eurozone in 1999. Until recent years, EEC/EU membership had not been a controversial aspect of parliamentary politics (Palau, Muñoz Márquez, and Chaqués-­ Bonafont 2014). For example, in 2008, the Congress of Deputies ratified the Lisbon Treaty with 322 votes in favour, six against and 2 abstentions. The Senate ratified it with 232 votes in favour, 6 against and 2 abstentions (Kölling and Molina 2015: 362).

28  Bonnie N. Field and Caroline Gray

The Partido Socialista Obrero Español (PSOE) and the Partido Popular (PP) alike have always been strongly in favour of EU membership and integration, sharing an ambition for Spain to play a leading role in the EU and supporting the project of an ever-closer Union. When first in power from 1982 to 1996 under Felipe González, the PSOE quickly shifted towards a classically liberal economic agenda in supporting privatisation to modernise the Spanish economy and allow for its successful European integration (Chari 1998). Indeed, with both the PP and the PSOE sharing quite a lot in common on the economic front (Royo 2009), studies have shown that the main drivers of left-right party competition and voting patterns in parliament have tended to be other more divisive issues. These include territorial decentralisation, immigration and the state-Church relationship ­(Sánchez-Cuenca and Dinas 2012). For a long time, the common economic agenda paid off. Spain benefited hugely from EU structural and cohesion funds to develop public infrastructure, while the euro brought immediate economic benefits by lowering interest rates. This helped fuel Spain’s construction boom that brought high growth for a decade until the bubble burst. This all contributed to Spain’s gradual economic convergence with other EU member states over the first 20 years of its membership: Spanish GDP per capita rose from 71.6 per cent of the EU-15 average in 1985 to 91.1 per cent in 2005 (100 per cent of the EU-28). Over that period, average GDP in the EU-15 only increased at a faster pace than Spain’s during the 1992–1994 recession, which hit Spain harder than others (Real Instituto Elcano 2006). For society, the overwhelming consensus was therefore not surprisingly in favour of the EU, which many associated with political progress and economic modernisation. Only since the global financial crisis of 2008 have some real signs of dissatisfaction with the EU project begun to emerge, albeit still to a lesser degree than in many other member states. For a long time, no far-right Eurosceptic party had any degree of success in Spain (Alonso and Rovira Kaltwasser 2015). Spain’s historically higher-than-average levels of support for European integration help explain this (González-Enríquez 2017). Moreover, Euroscepticism has so far not been a strong feature of Vox, the far-right challenger that did eventually secure a significant number of seats for the first time at the Andalusian regional elections in late 2018, thereby becoming crucial to government formation. It is more focused on domestic issues such as the Catalan independence crisis. Some dissatisfaction with the EU has come from representatives and supporters of the new left, encapsulated by Podemos and its political allies at municipal and regional levels. While not against the concept of the EU itself, they are critical of its current form, which they see as an elite-driven capitalist and neoliberal project, especially in light of the strict austerity agenda that core EU political elites imposed from 2009 on debt-stricken Southern Eurozone members.4 Moreover, they reject the fact the both the PSOE and the PP in practice supported this agenda by combining forces in 2011 to change the constitution to cap Spain’s structural deficit allowance.

The Spanish parliament in context  29

This was an attempt to reassure the international markets about Spain’s commitment to budgetary stability. But, the unpopularity of the measure, coming in the wake of the unprecedented mobilisation of the anti-austerity movement known as the 15-M, contributed to the rising dissatisfaction with Spain’s traditional parties. In Europe, Spain was among the hardest hit by the global financial crisis since the economic liberalisation process over previous decades had resulted in an economy that was excessively dependent on construction and property and on domestic household consumption (Salmon 2017). Spain’s problem started as one of high private debt held by companies and households, but once the property bubble collapsed and the economy began to struggle, the public debt rate also soared, eventually reaching the 100 per cent mark in 2014.5 Since then, Spain’s debt levels have stabilised at around 98–99 per cent of GDP—far above the European Commission’s 60 per cent recommendation. Despite the depth of the recession, Spain’s economy managed to return to growth in 2014. It then exceeded growth rates of 3 per cent of GDP in 2015, 2016 and 2017, far higher than the EU average. This expansion, however, has primarily been fuelled by low-productivity sectors such as tourism. Historic problems such as insufficient R&D spending persist, limiting the development of a more knowledge-based economy to fill the gap left by construction. Moreover, while unemployment has been reducing, many of the new contracts are poorly paid and precarious. The economy’s return to growth has not helped to reduce rates of inequality, poverty and social exclusion, which are among the highest in the EU (European Commission 2017; OECD 2016). Numerous legacies of the crisis thus persist, impacting parliamentary activity and dynamics.

Parliament in Spain’s political system

The main task of Spain’s first elected parliament in 1977 was to design the new constitution. The political system established was one in which parliament was central but a strong executive, an active constitutional court, political decentralisation and European integration circumscribed its power. In addition to its constituent nature, the 1977–1979 bicameral parliament was in many ways distinct from the ones that followed it. First, the monarch appointed 41 senators, alongside the 207 that voters directly elected and the fully elected Congress of Deputies. Second, the Senate was a more equal chamber to the lower house, Congress of Deputies, than it would be under the 1978 constitution. For example, both chambers had to approve the constitution. Third, in 1977, the king had the authority to appoint the prime minister, Adolfo Suárez; parliament had no role in selecting the government. This is significant because Suárez’s party did not have a majority of seats in parliament. Finally, a high degree of interparty consensus characterised decision-making in parliament (Capo Giol 1990; Field 2005), which, in part, is why Spain’s transition is characterised as a pacted or negotiated one (Gunther 1992).

30  Bonnie N. Field and Caroline Gray

Between 1979 and today, very similar rules have structured the parliament’s activities. The 1978 constitution established a bicameral parliament, with political power strongly tilted towards the Congress of Deputies. Most significantly, it is the Congress of Deputies (and not the Senate) that elects and can bring down governments. And it is the Congress of Deputies that is the central legislative actor. The Senate is a review chamber that can amend and veto legislation; however, the Congress of Deputies can, with a majority vote, reject them. The electoral system for the Congress of Deputies was designed to assure representation for the political right in the new political system and to limit party system fragmentation, which had existed during the Second Republic. Therefore, Spain uses a system of proportional representation (PR) that greatly compromises proportionality. This is because, on average, district magnitude (the number of seats allocated per district) is low (6.7),6 and each district is guaranteed a minimum of two seats, regardless of its population. In practice, this has favoured the two largest parties, the UCD and PSOE between 1977 and 1982, and the PSOE and AP/PP, subsequently. It has typically not punished (and at times benefited) regionally based parties that only present candidates in a limited territory and concentrate their votes geographically. The electoral system has also led to the underrepresentation of smaller parties whose support is spread across the Spanish territory, such as the United Left (previously the Spanish Communist Party). The closedlist PR system also encourages party-centred legislative behaviour, and discourages personalistic behaviour, discussed below. The Senate, in contrast, is primarily elected using a limited vote system in provincial districts (typically a smaller territorial unit than the autonomous community). The regional parliaments also indirectly elect about 1/5 of the senators. Because of the more majoritarian nature of the electoral system for the Senate, it has often reinforced the position of the larger parties. The electoral system also limits the Senate’s ability to act as a chamber of territorial representation, as does its comparatively weak powers. Unlike in Portugal, Spain’s 1978 constitution gives the head of state a ceremonial role. It is therefore the prime minister and the government that are the key executive actors in this parliamentary regime. The government is institutionally quite powerful vis-à-vis parliament. We can see this in the rules for the election and termination of governments. In fact, the Congress of Deputies does not elect the government as a whole. Rather, it only elects the prime minister, who then has full authority to appoint his or her cabinet, with the largely ceremonial intervention of the monarch. Moreover, while the prime minister needs absolute majority support to be elected in a first-round vote, he or she7 only needs more yes than no votes in a second-round vote. In effect, abstentions and absences do not count in the second-round outcome. Parliament can only bring down the government with a constructive vote of no confidence, which happened for the first time in 2018. This means that parliament must have the votes to simultaneously censure the government

The Spanish parliament in context  31

with an absolute majority and elect a new prime minister who will, in turn, form a new government. This insulates the government from censure when the opposition is united against it but not on an alternative. Moreover, the parliament cannot vote to remove individual ministers. The government also has a number of agenda setting and control prerogatives that enhances its power (Ajenjo and Molina 2011; Field 2016: 124–140). Government bills are favoured in the parliamentary agenda, and, in practice, dominate it. The government can veto parliamentary bills and amendments to any bill that alter the current budget, though a 2018 court ruling required the government to demonstrate a budgetary effect (García Majado 2018). The government can make the last amendment proposal in plenary debates, helping it get what it wants (Heller 2001). The government has exclusive legislative initiative related to the general budget, which is automatically carried over into the next year if the government does not get a new one through parliament (Guerrero Salom 2004: 171–174). It also has the authority to issue decree laws. While the Congress of Deputies must subsequently approve or reject them, they are law when issued. Additionally, the government can call a confidence vote and survive if it wins a simple majority of more yes than no votes, which has occurred twice since the transition and the government survived both votes.8 While the government is certainly powerful vis-à-vis parliament in cross-national perspective, its power also depends on whether or not it has a majority in parliament, discussed below. Political parties structure and dominate parliamentary activity, leaving little room for personalised political behaviour. During Spain’s transition to democracy, party leaders sought to foster the development of strong, disciplined political parties given the lessons they drew from the Second Republic, the extremely weak state of party organisations at the time and to assure the compliance of their parties’ rank and file with the deals they were making (Field 2013). A variety of institutions favour party dominance. As discussed previously, deputies are elected in a closed list, PR election system. Generally, the parties control candidate selection processes, though there is variation across parties. In the two main parties, PSOE and PP, central party leadership bodies maintain ultimate control of who appears on the ballot in national parliamentary elections, a reality MPs recognise in surveys (Cordero, Jaime-Castillo, and Coller 2016). The parliamentary and party group rules are highly party-centric, and parties impose sanctions for indiscipline (Oñate 2000; Sánchez Medero and Aldeguer Cerdá 2018). In practice, voting discipline is extremely high (Field 2013, 2016: 167–169). While the basic institutional arrangements set up during the early years of democracy have persisted, the Equality Law in 2007 mandated electoral gender quotas and advanced descriptive representation. According to the law, neither sex can occupy fewer than 40 per cent of the spots on electoral candidate lists. The rule applies to each section of five positions within a list. In districts with few seats, parties need to allocate positions as equitably as possible. Nonetheless, left parties had adopted voluntary party quotas a

32  Bonnie N. Field and Caroline Gray

decade earlier, which produced a contagion effect and advanced women’s representation in parliament prior to the law’s adoption (Verge 2012). In 2016, women held 39 per cent of the seats in the Congress of Deputies and 40 per cent of the seats in the Senate (Gobierno de España 2016), compared to only 6 per cent in the Congress of Deputies in 1977.

The evolution of the party system(s)

Spain’s party system has changed during its more than 40 years of democratic governance. It is useful to divide it into four periods: the transitional multiparty system (1977–1982), a predominant party system (1982–1993), a two-party dominant system (1993–2015) and today’s multiparty system with new party challengers (2015–present) (Table 3.1).

Transitional multiparty party system (1977–1982)

Between 1977 and 1982, parliament was writing the new constitution, developing the legal norms necessary to accompany it, and setting up the contours of the decentralised state. The new democracy also faced important challenges from sectors of the armed forces, culminating in an attempted coup on 23 February 1981, and from terrorist attacks, such as from the Basque separatist group ETA (Basque Homeland and Liberty). It was also a time of party formation, and, in some cases, of party disintegration. During this period, no party had a majority in parliament, and the centre-right UCD governed in minority. The party system comprised four main statewide parties—the UCD, the Socialist Party (PSOE), the Communist Party (PCE) and the rightist Popular Alliance (AP) (later Popular Party/PP)—as well as a variety of small regional parties from Catalonia, the Basque Country, and elsewhere. The UCD was the largest party during this period, winning about 48 per cent of the seats in the Congress of Deputies. Its principal competitor was the PSOE, with about 35 per cent of the seats. To the PSOE’s left, the PCE won an average of 6 per cent of the seats. To the UCD’s right, the AP won 4.5 per cent seats in 1977 and 2.6 per cent seats in 1979.9

Other parties were relevant during this period, including the regionally based parties—the Catalan Convergence and Union (CiU), the Basque ­Nationalist Party (PNV), the Basque Herri Batasuna (HB), and the Socialist Party of Andalusia (PSA)—along with the statewide Popular Socialist Party (PSP), which ultimately disbands and merges with the PSOE in 1978. While the leftist PSA disappears as a national parliamentary actor after 1982, the centre-right, moderate nationalist parties, CiU and PNV, remain significant, though small, parliamentary actors. The secessionist, far left HB was a radical Basque nationalist party, with ties to ETA. It refused to participate in parliamentary activity, deeming Spanish institutions illegitimate. Eventually, the Basque radical left, with ties to ETA, was banned.
