The Spanish parliament in context  35

With respect to statewide or national parties, the main left-right cleavage also overlapped with parties whose leaders had opposed the rightwing authoritarian regime of Francisco Franco (PCE and PSOE) and those that were linked to it (UCD and AP). In contrast, the centre-right regionally based nationalist parties did not have links to the prior regime, which had repressed and persecuted peripheral nationalism. A secondary cleavage, the centre-periphery one, also existed and persists. The leftist PCE and PSOE were less centralist and more tolerant of diverse national identities than the UCD and AP, indicating an overlap with the left-right division. However, the overlap is not as neat among regional parties. There are several regional parties on the left (e.g. ERC, HB) and centre-right (e.g. CiU, PNV) that advance pro-periphery positions. Regional parties tend to be more pro-periphery than the statewide parties. Yet, there is also great variation among them, ranging from parties that advocate secession to those that want greater regional autonomy within the existing state.

Predominant party system (1982–1993)

During the 1982–1993 period, the Socialist Party, under the leadership of Felipe González, dominated the national party system and governed in majority. The 1982 parliamentary elections produced a stunning defeat of the incumbent UCD, as a result of infighting and party splits. Winning only 3 per cent of seats in the Congress, it subsequently disbanded as a political party. In contrast, the PSOE won a comfortable absolute majority of the seats in 1982 (58 per cent) and 1986 (53 per cent), and exactly half of the seats in 1989. After the 1982 elections, the Popular Alliance became the largest party of the right. However, the electoral support for the two largest parties was more uneven than in the past. In the Congress of Deputies (with 350 MPs), the UCD had had 48 or 47 more MPs than the PSOE during the 1977–1979 period. In contrast, the PSOE had 97 (1982), 79 (1986) and 68 (1989) more seats than the AP/PP in the new party system. The ideological distance between the two main parties was also greater because the AP was anchored solidly on the political right. The centre-right Democratic and Social Centre (CDS)—a new party that split from the UCD in 1982—won seats through the 1989 elections. Among the smaller parties, the electoral fortunes of the PCE varied. In part due to internal divisions, the PCE saw its support drop dramatically in 1982 (from 23 to 4 seats). In 1986, it began to run candidates in an alliance of leftist and new left parties, United Left (IU). While it never returns to its transition era fortunes, its representation in Congress improved by 1989 (17 seats). The most important regional parties during this period were the centre-right Catalan CiU and the Basque PNV. However, their support was not necessary for the PSOE to govern.

36  Bonnie N. Field and Caroline Gray

Two-party dominant system (1993–2015)

Between 1993 and 2015, two parties dominate the party system, the Socialists and the Popular Party (PP), though there are smaller relevant parties whose support is critical when one of the main parties does not win a majority. Governments were either single-party majority or minority ones. In 1989, AP was re-founded as the Popular Party. In the process, it consolidated much of the political right into a unified party, except the regionally based nationalist parties. Under party leader José María Aznar, the party also moved towards the centre-right (García-Guereta Rodríguez 2001). These changes and the decline in support for the PSOE, due to corruption and the costs of governing, led to stiffer competition between the two main parties after 1993. The PSOE maintained its position as the largest party in parliament in the 1993 elections, without a majority (45 per cent of seats). Yet, the PP won the 1996 parliamentary elections for the first time since the transition, also without a majority (45 per cent of seats). It won a majority in 2000 (52 per cent of seats). The Socialists won the elections in 2004 and 2008, under the leadership of José Luis Rodríguez Zapatero, 12 and 7 seats short of an absolute majority, respectively. When they did not have a majority, each of the large parties relied on smaller political parties in order to govern and accomplish their legislative priorities. Before 2000, the moderate centre-right regional parties were key: the Catalan CiU, the Basque PNV and the Canary Islands Coalition (CC). After 2000, leftist regional parties, including the Republican Left of ­Catalonia (ERC) and the Galician Nationalist Bloc (BNG), along with IU, grow in importance for PSOE minority governments. Signs of party system change appear in the 2011 elections. The international economic crisis began to hit Spain hard in 2009 and eventually led to the Euro crisis and a deep political crisis in Spain. Voters punished the governing Socialists at the polls, where they won the fewest seats (31 per cent) since the transition to democracy. However, newcomers did not emerge to disrupt the party system as a whole. The elections instead delivered a major victory to the Popular Party, under the leadership of Mariano Rajoy. The PP won with an absolute majority of seats (53 per cent). The opposition in parliament was much more fragmented.

Multiparty system and new party challengers (2015–2019)

Spain’s current party system is one in which there are four significant statewide parties, along with a set of smaller regional parties. A fifth, Vox, is projected to enter parliament in 2019. The 2015 parliamentary elections transformed Spain’s national party system and thus far has made governing challenging. The PP and PSOE are still the largest parties. However, newcomers Podemos (We can) and Ciudadanos (Citizens/C’s) rose to challenge them

The Spanish parliament in context  37

(Rodríguez-Teruel and Barrio 2016; Rodríguez-Teruel, Barrio, and ­Barberà 2016). Studies show that the party system’s transformation is rooted in Spain’s economic crisis as well as in dissatisfaction with the political system, related to a crisis of representation and political corruption (e.g. Vidal 2018). Downplaying ideology, the newcomers clearly campaigned on their outsider status, and won support for it, bringing an element of an insider-­ outsider cleavage to Spanish politics. However, they have come to easily fit into the deep-seated left-right and centre-periphery cleavages. Podemos occupies a far-left ideological space and has been the national party that has been most supportive of diverse national identities, such as in Catalonia, going as far as backing a referendum on Catalan independence from Spain. Ciudadanos, on the other hand, occupies a centre-right ideological space. Yet it is hostile to multinational identities. In fact, it began as a regional party in Catalonia that opposed Catalan nationalism. The challenge Podemos posed to the Socialists was initially more acute, with polls at times having indicated it would surpass the PSOE. Ultimately, Podemos won about 20 per cent of the seats in the 2015 or 2016 elections, compared to about 25 per cent for the Socialists. On the right, the PP held on to its position as the largest party in parliament, with 35 per cent and 39 per cent of the seats in 2015 and 2016, respectively. Ciudadanos won 11 per cent (2015) and 9 per cent (2016) of the seats. Yet, in early 2019, it is the PP that faces a serious threat from Ciudadanos and the far right Vox. Changes within the Catalan parties also mark the new party system. The previously moderate Catalan CiU, which in fact was a federation of two parties, split before the 2015 general elections. One of the component parties embraced independence for Catalonia, Democratic Convergence of Catalonia (CDC), and the other, Democratic Union of Catalonia, disbanded in 2017. In 2016, CDC was refounded as the Catalan European Democratic Party (PDeCat) to accommodate its secessionist position and to attempt to escape the taint of corruption scandals. Jointly with the leftist ERC, it launched a push for independence that led to the most severe crisis Spain has faced since the early years of democracy.

Governance: single-party minority and majority cabinets

Spain has had 15 governments since its transition to democracy in 1977 until 2018. All have been single party governments. Leaving aside the (minority) caretaker government of PP Prime Minister Rajoy between 2015 and 2016, four were majority governments and ten were minority ones. Minority governments are those in which the governing party (or parties) does not simultaneously have an absolute majority of the seats in the parliament. Spain is among the European countries with the highest incidence of minority government (Field 2016) (Table 3.2). In the period before 2015, parliament normally formed governments quite easily, even when no party had a majority. PM Calvo-Sotelo in 1981

38  Bonnie N. Field and Caroline Gray

and PM Zapatero in 2008 were the only two prime ministers elected in a second-round vote, and Zapatero strategically preferred it. Spain’s governments also tended to last, with an average relative duration—the percentage of the maximum possible duration—of 81 per cent between 1977 and 2015, and 90 per cent in the post-transition years between 1982 and 2015. Governments, majority and minority, typically had high legislative productivity and passed their bills, with a success rate of 89 per cent between 1982 and 2011 (Field 2016: 79–80). They also tended to fulfil their election pledges, even in minority (Artés 2013). Because of their institutional and partisan political power, majority governments diminish parliament’s strength. PM González governed in majority twice (1982–1986; 1986–1989). González’ 1989 government was unique. The PSOE held exactly half of the seats in the Congress of Deputies (putting it in the minority government category). However, because the radical Basque HB did not take its seats, the PSOE governed with a de facto majority, except when the rules required an absolute majority vote of all of the members of the chamber, which is rare. The PP has also governed in majority, under PM Aznar (2000–2004) and PM Rajoy (2011–2015). During periods of minority government, parliament is more central and nongovernmental parties gain influence, as one would expect. However, between 1982 and 2011, there was little difference between the performance of minority and majority governments when it came to their ability to pass legislation, accomplish their political agendas and last (Field 2016). Field’s

The Spanish parliament in context  39

study identifies several reasons: Spain’s institutions strengthen governments generally. Spain’s decentralised state makes it possible for (governing) parties to trade political support across state levels (national, regional, local). The governments tended to have strong partisan bargaining positions, with a large plurality of the seats in parliament and potential allies that needed support at other state levels. And, importantly, governments relied on regional parties that had little interest in governing Spain (and therefore supplanting the government) and a strong interest in collaborating with a government that needed them, in exchange for concessions. Yet not all minority governments have been the same. During the early years of democracy, the UCD governed in minority under Prime Ministers Adolfo Suárez (1977–1981) and Leopoldo Calvo-Sotelo (1981–1982). These governments did not rely on the support of any particular party in parliament. Parties behaved quite consensually in law making, including the governing party and the main opposition PSOE, among others (Capo Giol 1990; Field 2005). The minority governments between 1993 and 2011 primarily relied on regional parties. In the 1990s, the minority governments of Socialist PM Felipe González (1993–1996) and conservative PM José María Aznar (1996– 2000) primarily relied on the parliamentary support of the centre-right regionally based nationalist parties—the Catalan CiU, the Basque PNV and the Canary Islands CC (Field 2016: 98–111; Heller 2002; Reniu i Vilamala 2002). In contrast, the first minority government of Socialist PM José Luis Rodríguez Zapatero (2004–2008) relied to a greater extent on the support of leftist regionally based parties, such as the Catalan ERC and IU (Field 2016: 111–115). This pattern flipped during Zapatero’s second government (2008–2011), when it allied more frequently with the centre-right regional parties (Field 2016: 115–118). Also compared to the Aznar and González minority governments, the Zapatero governments frequently shifted parliamentary allies in parliament (referred to as a strategy of variable geometry or geometría variable). Parliament-government relations have shifted in the aftermath of party system change in 2015, and produced minority governments with weaker bargaining positions. In 2015 and with no party winning more than about a third of the seats, parliament was unable to form a government. This left the Rajoy government in power with caretaker status and new elections had to be called in 2016. Yet, parliament again struggled to agree on a government. With elections again looming, parliament invested Rajoy as prime minister in a second-round vote. Ciudadanos and the Canary Islands Coalition, with which Rajoy had agreements, supported his investiture. Yet it was the abstention of most of the deputies of the Socialist Party that allowed the government to form. This occurred only after the party faced a severe internal crisis over the investiture vote, leading to the removal of the party’s leader Pedro Sánchez and to several deputies breaking party discipline—in this normally highly disciplined party—to vote against Rajoy’s investiture.

40  Bonnie N. Field and Caroline Gray

Rajoy then formed a minority government, with the governing party holding the fewest seats in the Congress of Deputies (38 per cent) since the transition. This government would prove to be short-lived. A vote of no confidence by the Socialist Party and with the support of Podemos and almost all of the regionally based parties ousted the Rajoy government in 2018 and brought to power a Socialist single party minority government under a resurrected Pedro Sánchez, with even fewer seats in parliament than the PP had under Rajoy. The Rajoy government between 2016 and 2018 broke the pattern of effective minority governments, with few legislative initiatives and a comparably lower approval rate for government bills, which its inability to pass a general budget in 2017 for 2018 most prominently exemplifies.10 The Sánchez minority government also faced challenging governing circumstances, and early elections were called in 2019.

Decentralisation: the dispersion of policy-making authority

Unlike in Portugal, Spain’s parliament shares law-making authority with 17 regional parliaments, as well as with European institutions. One of the biggest challenges of the transition to democracy was how to accommodate Spain’s different regional identities within the Spanish state following their repression under Franco. The constitution of 1978 ended up designing what Colomer (1998) describes as an “ambiguous constitutional compromise” akin to “non-institutional federalism”. This was an attempt to reach a minimum consensus that could satisfy not only the regionally based nationalists who wanted special recognition for their territories and ideally some form of confederal arrangement, but also the variety of Spanish political forces, from those on the right who wanted a unitary, centralised state to those on the left who preferred a form of symmetric decentralisation. Broadly speaking, the compromises involved maintaining the provinces first created in the 19th century and grouping them into 17 autonomous communities or regions, each with its own regional parliament and government, and its own regional autonomy statute, akin to a regional constitution. Three of the autonomous communities, the Basque Country, Catalonia and Galicia, were given a “fast-track” route to a certain degree of autonomy via article 151 in an attempt to satisfy the nationalists in those regions. All three had approved a regional autonomy statute for the first time in the 1930s, subsequently abolished under the dictatorship of Franco. The Spanish constitution did not recognise them as nations, conceiving of one Spanish nation only, but it did designate them as “historic nationalities”. The remaining 14 autonomous communities were given a slower route to autonomy via article 143, though Andalusia then won the right to be considered a historic region instead (Colomer 1998: 45). Overall, the decentralisation formula was left deliberately vague in terms of exactly what policy competences the regions would ultimately achieve. Eventually, many of the standard autonomous communities ended up seeking

The Spanish parliament in context  41

more or less the same level of competences as the historic ones. The system therefore became known as café para todos11 (“coffee for all”)—which the nationalist parties from the historic regions strongly criticised. This model inadvertently ended up encouraging competition between regional governments themselves—not only for competences, but also for the resources to finance them. There are a handful of fora that bring together the central government and the regional governments in Spain—primarily the fiscal and financial policy council (Consejo de Política Fiscal y Financiera, CPFF), meetings of the national and regional education ministers and the consultative conference on European affairs. However, conflictive rather than cooperative dynamics have beset them, since each regional government seeks the best outcome for its own territory (Colomer 1998: 40, Gray 2016: 65). This, in turn, has consequences for the behaviour of statewide parties in the Spanish parliament too. For example, regional politicians in longstanding PSOE strongholds in southern Spain—most notably Andalusia, but also traditionally Extremadura and Castilla-La Mancha—have often had a significant influence on the behaviour of the party in the Spanish parliament. The PP also has its regions where it has tended to win time and time again, such as Valencia, which it governed for two decades (1995–2015), until the corruption crisis engulfing the party eroded its support base. The dominance of each party in certain regions helps to explain, for example, why Spanish governments have repeatedly failed over the years to overhaul the common financing system to resolve the situation criticised by successive Catalan governments, whereby the region arguably loses more than a fair share of its wealth to other regions (Gray 2014: 24–27). Improving that situation for Catalonia would inevitably have a detrimental impact on some other regions in Spain which are PP- or PSOE-dominated. By the late 1990s, growing calls were emerging from Basque and Catalan nationalists in particular for a revision of their regional autonomy statutes (and also of the Spanish constitution), which also unleashed a wider wave of statute reform in other regions. Supporting minority central governments via “mutual back scratching” (Field 2014) arrangements had by then become the main bargaining tool the regionally based nationalists used to secure decentralisation gains. Yet this was inherently subject to the risk of instability. It gave the nationalists’ extensive leverage at times, but left them with little leverage at other times when a party won the Spanish general elections with an absolute majority, as the PP has done twice since 2000 (Gray 2016: 66). Moreover, the Spanish government can still introduce basic laws that override regional laws even in the case of decentralised competences. Notable sources of grievance in recent years in times of absolute majority PP government include the highly controversial 2013 education law, known as the “Wert Law” after its creator José Ignacio Wert, then PP education minister. This sought to increase Spanish control over regional education systems, the language of instruction, and course content.

42  Bonnie N. Field and Caroline Gray

The Catalan independence movement that rapidly grew from around 2010 onwards has undoubtedly posed the greatest challenge to Spain’s decentralisation model. In response, the Spanish authorities eventually had recourse to article 155 of Spain’s constitution for the first time. Known as Spain’s “nuclear option”, this allows the central government to intervene in the governance of any region that goes against the constitution or Spanish law. This is one of the few occasions on which the Senate has more power than the Congress of Deputies, for the measure requires absolute majority approval from the Senate only. The Senate overwhelmingly approved Rajoy’s request to implement article 155 shortly after the Catalan parliament had voted in favour of declaring independence from Spain on 27 October 2017, following an unconstitutional referendum the pro-independence Catalan government organised. This paved the way for Spain to dismiss the Catalan parliament and call new regional elections, held on 21 December 2017. Thus, while extensive powers have been devolved to Spain’s regional governments, the Spanish upper house retains the power to suspend those with one vote. Developments in Catalonia have also affected politics in the national parliament. The disappearance of moderate Catalan nationalists, who had been such important parliamentary allies of Spain’s minority governments, makes governing in minority more difficult.

Conclusions

Parliamentary institutions in Spain have remained remarkably stable over the democratic period. Nonetheless, the context in which parliament has operated and indeed the key actors in parliament have changed either progressively or intermittently. Spain has Europeanised since it first sought admission into the EEC and it remains one of Europe’s most pro-EU countries. Spain has also progressively devolved political authority to its autonomous communities, which now count among the regions with the highest level of political authority in Europe. Europeanisation and devolution dispersed policy making authority, circumscribing the role of the central parliament. While Spain has certainly experienced economic ups-and-downs, its economic progress since the transition until the worldwide economic crisis began in 2008 was astounding. After 2009, however, the economic crisis triggered serious challenges that tore at Spain’s social fabric, transformed its party system and complicated the formation and stability of governments. Whether this generates institutional change remains to be seen. Also, despite institutional continuities, parliament’s power and centrality in practice depend on the type of government in office, whether it is a majority or minority one, and indeed on the bargaining strength of the minority government in power. More often than not, Spain has had minority governments, necessitating frequent negotiations to achieve support from parties in parliament. For a long time, this afforded Spain government stability,

The Spanish parliament in context  43

but also helped, as a by-product, to paper over the cracks in Spain’s model of territorial decentralisation. When minority governments needed the support of regionally based nationalist parties in parliament, it gave the latter opportunities to secure concessions for their home region in return. Considering representation, women have progressively gained access to political office in parliament (and government), thereby significantly improving women’s descriptive representation since the early days of democracy. Nonetheless, the descriptive representation of Spain’s immigrant and multi-ethnic populations is wanting.12

The party system has changed periodically during past four decades, while the key political cleavages—the left-right and centre-periphery—that shape parliamentary politics remain. At times, parliament has been more fragmented (e.g. 1977–1982 and 2015–2019) and at other times the two main parties, PSOE and PP, dominated (1993–2011), though Spain has never had a perfect two-party system. Parties have also come and gone, and with them political positions in parliament. Among the most significant statewide parties, the centrist UCD disappeared in 1982. The rightwing Alianza Popular moderated and was refounded as the Popular Party 1989. Newcomers Podemos and Ciudadanos surged as sizeable challengers in 2015, and Vox in 2018. That said, they have slotted into the established left-right and centre-periphery axes of competition. There have also been important changes among the regionally based parties. The radical left Basque nationalists with ties to terrorism were outlawed from parliament, reappearing under a new formation (EH Bildu) only once they explicitly renounced violence, and moderate right Catalan nationalists embraced secessionism. The latter meant the disappearance of CiU and the refounding of CDC as PDeCat. It also went along with close collaboration between the centre-right PDeCat and the historically secessionist leftist ERC, and tense relations with Spain’s major parties. Whether or not the shift towards a multiparty system from 2015 ends up becoming a permanent feature of Spain’s political landscape or a passing phase, the very fact that two new statewide parties have broken through, where others had failed in the past, and a third is set to do so, is a significant feat that reveals the extent of the crisis in representation in Spain.

Section II Internal organisation structures

Introduction

Parliamentary party groups (PPGs) are

an organised group of members of a representative body who were elected either under the same party label or under the label of different parties that do not compete against each other in elections, and who do not explicitly create for technical reasons only. (Heidar and Koole 2000: 249)

Together with parliamentary committees, PPGs are the pivotal organisational units of parliaments (Saalfeld and Strøm 2014). To be sure, PPGs heighten, first, the organisation of “responsible party governments”, which are instrumental for accountability in liberal democracies (Aldrich 1995). Second, they help MPs in their efforts to attain their goals: being returned to parliament, influence policy-making and have prestige in the chamber (Mayhew 1974). Canonical accounts of party organisation place PPGs as part of a more comprehensive structure. Key (1955) makes a conceptual division between “party in the electorate”, “party as organisation” and “party in government”. Each of these party facets (Katz and Mair 1993) fulfils a role in the functioning of the party, creating, at times, competing incentives for MPs. In this chapter, we focus on the role of PPGs in the Iberian legislatures. For all its importance, this topic has been largely overlooked in the extant comparative literature (see, Heidar and Koole 2000). Indeed, in Portugal and Spain, PPGs are an important element in political parties due to the political environment in which they operate. To be sure, the closed-list electoral system creates few incentives to cultivate a personal vote. In return, the party leadership becomes the pivotal principal for MPs seeking re-election, through the obeisance of the latter to the PPG (and, in turn, of the PPG to the party leadership). In Spain, the parliamentary fractions first achieved legal status under the Second Republic (1931–1936) through the parliament’s statutes. These early

4 Parliamentary party groups in the Iberian democracies

Carlos Jalali and Juan Rodríguez Teruel

50  Carlos Jalali and Juan Rodríguez Teruel

PPGs were highly autonomous from the extraparliamentary parties (except for the Radical Party) and quickly became the main actors in the chamber at the expense of individual MPs. The Civil War (1936–1939) and the ensuing Francoist period (1939–1977) aborted this evolution, despite the authoritarian regime’s attempts to emulate the existence of parliamentary groups in the non-democratic chamber (the Cortes). With the return of democracy, the 1978 Constitution introduced a rationalised regime of parliamentarism, with a robust formal position for the PPGs, which were, however, the pure expression of political parties. Previous democratic experiments in Portugal – the Constitutional Monarchy (1820–1910) and First Republic (1910–1926) – were characterised by individual MPs that were largely unbeholden to a parliamentary group or party in their behaviour and coalesced more on personal or clientelistic lines than partisan ones. The first inklings of recognition of party groups in parliament occurred with the constitutional amendment of September 1919, which created a Parliamentary Council, to include “all currents of opinion” in Parliament. However, this was more an (unsuccessful) attempt to mitigate parliamentary instability than a recognition of strong and cohesive PPGs. Salazar’s authoritarian Estado Novo (New State) largely coincided with the Francoist regime but did not replicate its Spanish counterpart’s attempts at emulating PPGs in parliament. While the Estado Novo retained a parliament (the Assembleia Nacional), this was a rubber stamp – and an increasingly weakened one at that – entirely monopolised by the Estado Novo-sanctioned União Nacional (National Union, renamed Popular National Action in 1970) throughout the regime’s 41-year existence. The sole partial exception to this pattern was the reformist “liberal wing” (Ala Liberal) that Salazar’s successor, Marcello Caetano, allowed to run in the lists of the União Nacional in the 1969 elections, part of his attempts to liberalise the regime. This liberal wing – comprised of some 30 deputies – can, arguably, be seen as a brief proto-parliamentary group in the Estado Novo, pushing for some liberalising reforms before largely dropping out of parliament by the ensuing elections of 1973 (and then mostly re-emerging post-1974 in the centre-right PSD). This chapter examines the formal rules and practices that define the role of PPGs in contemporary Iberian legislative politics. In the Spanish case of weak bicameralism, we will focus on the lower chamber (Congreso de los Diputados) because of its predominance over the Senate. The chapter begins by assessing the formal status of PPGs in the two countries. It then examines the resources of PPGs, before dealing with their internal functioning; relationship with the extra-parliamentary party; and with the executive.

Institutional status of PPGs in parliaments

Portuguese and Spanish PPGs are highly regulated. Formal rules, particularly the statutes of the assemblies, extensively develop their functions, structures and powers. In this respect, they have followed the same pattern

Parliamentary party groups  51

developed by groups in other West European parliaments, adopting an internal organisation according to the model of a parliamentary party complex, with high external organisational resources and a high degree of discipline (Heidar and Koole 2000: 11). In Spain, PPGs have been constitutionally recognised since 1978 and extensively regulated by the chamber’s rules of procedure, besides the internal statutes of the groups themselves. The Constitution refers to them only when it sets that the Permanent Deputation of the parliament will be composed by representatives of the different groups (art 78.1). The regulation (or lack thereof) of PPGs in the Constitution reflects the constitutional drafters’ view that the parliamentary groups should remain an issue for the chambers’ statutes (Saiz Arnaiz 1989: 90). Overall, the constitutional regulation displays some tension between the relevant role given to the party (and indirectly to the PPG), and the individual autonomy of the representatives insofar as the principle of the compulsory mandate is rejected (art 67.2). Parliamentary rules are more detailed in defining the legal status and composition of the PPGs, as well as their prerogatives and functions in the legislative process. According to these rules, groups can be formed in the five days after the opening the chamber. Typically, rules require a minimum of 15 members of the parliament to form a group from the same or different parties. Alternatively, parties could also achieve a PPG with at least five MPs, and either 5 per cent of the total vote in Spain or 15 per cent of the vote in each of those districts where it was running. This constellation of criteria aims to reduce the number of statewide parties forming groups while allowing for non-statewide parties with a relevant role in some regions to have separate groups in the parliament. Consequently, Catalan (CiU) and Basque (PNV) nationalist parties have always had distinct groups representing regional interests, which gave them a prominent role in the legislative process at the national level. On the contrary, some minor national parties could not form their own PPG, despite garnering a similar or even more significant number of votes than these regionalist forces. In other situations, the major parties lend some of their MPs to small parties in order to achieve the group status. Five days after the constitution of the chamber, all the MPs not included in any PPG will automatically form part of the Mixed group. A more controverted issue has been related to the possibility that electoral alliances could form different PPGs. Indeed, in 1977 and 1979, the Socialist Catalan party (PSOE’s sister organisation in Catalonia) had its group (the PSOE’s Basque branch did the same in 1979). In doing so, the Socialists got more resources and floor time in the parliament. To avoid such fragmentation, in 1982, the new chamber rules introduced a new requirement that MPs from electoral coalitions of different parties that had not competed against each other could not form different groups. PPGs have several crucial functions in the internal organisation of the Spanish legislature (Pérez Serrano 1989: 205). First and foremost, PPGs

52  Carlos Jalali and Juan Rodríguez Teruel

elect representatives and participate in the leading bodies of the chamber: the parliamentary committees (Comisiones), the praesidium (Mesa) of the Congress and the board of the spokesmen (Junta de Portavoces). Second, PPGs are the only body entitled to present amendments, dissenting opinions and non-government bills to the parliament during the legislative process. Third, the groups also must allow the members to present several measures of political control to the government, like information requests, interpellations, or non-legislative motions. To be sure, some of these initiatives can be proposed by the MPs, but their PPG spokesmen must sign them. MPs are fully autonomous only when submitting oral or written questions. Finally, the PPGs share with the MPs the power to propose constitutional review or comprehensive constitutional reforms. Against this backdrop, it is possible to conclude that MPs’ autonomy is highly curtailed. There is one relevant exception, though: the PPG cannot force an MP to resign. Ultimately, most of the crucial decisions and activity in the chamber depend on a handful of MPs, usually leaders of the central office in the extra-parliamentary party (Oñate 2000: 109). In this respect, the standing orders aim to strengthen the PPG system in the ordinary functioning of the chamber, and more generally the role of the political parties in the legislative process. Table 4.1 shows the size of parliamentary party groups in Spain from 1977 through 2016. As in Spain, PPGs in Portugal have a central role in parliament and are very much the “primary core” of its functioning (Leston-Bandeira 2004: 43).

Parliamentary party groups  53

Individual MPs – whether inside or out of PPGs – have a reduced role. However, as in Spain, they are not subject to a compulsory mandate and cannot be forced to resign. MPs that relinquish their group are not allowed to change their parliamentary group. While the overall pattern is similar, the Portuguese case goes much further than its Spanish neighbour regarding constitutional recognition of PPGs. As in Spain, parties are mentioned concerning the Standing Committee (art 179). However, unlike Spain, the Portuguese Constitution specifically recognises parliamentary groups (art 180). The extent of recognition of PPGs in the Portuguese Constitution is exceptional. Overall, it makes 15 references to PPGs, a number that is only exceeded by the Greek Constitution (17 references) in the 32 post-war European democracies with codified constitutions analysed by van Biezen (2012: 202). This quantitative result largely echoes other more qualitative ones, with Jiménez (1979) assessing the Portuguese Constitution as the one that “most extensively recognises” parliamentary groups in Europe. On the other hand, Portugal is also considerably more permissive than its Iberian neighbour regarding forming PPGs. Thus, any party electing at least two MPs can constitute a parliamentary group. Moreover, again unlike Spain, the Portuguese Rules of Procedure allow parties that contest elections in electoral alliances to form separate PPGs, a possibility that the Portuguese Communist Party has consistently exploited in the democratic period. However, while it is considerably easier to create a parliamentary group in Portugal than in Spain, the number of groups has been lower in Portugal. As Table 4.2 shows, a total of 11 different PPGs have existed in Portugal since democratisation, while in Spain this total is 16 (including the Mixed group). The number of PPGs in each legislature has ranged from 4 to 8 in Portugal, with an average of 5.7. In Spain, the average is 7.2, with a range of 6 to 10. The countries’ party systems largely explain this difference. Unlike Spain, Portugal never had regional parties, thus reducing PPGs to typically four/ five state-wide parties; and it has mostly avoided the collapse or emergence of relevant parties as happened in Spain with the Union of the Democratic Centre (UCD) or, more recently, with Podemos and Ciudadanos. The Portuguese Constitution not only recognises PPGs, but it also grants them extensive prerogatives that are further fleshed out in the Rules of Procedure. Indeed, the primacy of PPGs vis-à-vis individual MPs is itself a constitutional provision in Portugal, with article 180 establishing that MPs “who do not belong to any parliamentary group shall be ensured certain minimum rights and guarantees” – thus making clear that it is PPGs that have maximal rights and guarantees. The powers of parliamentary groups can be divided in three types: collective powers, that PPGs exercise conjointly; concurrent powers, that both PPGs and individual MPs have; and exclusive powers, reserved solely for PPGs.

Note: This table does not reflect MPs that left their PPG during the legislature. Information on such cases available in the Parliament website.

56  Carlos Jalali and Juan Rodríguez Teruel

Regarding collective powers, the most important pertain to the Conference of Leaders, which is comprised of the leader of each parliamentary group. In this body, each party has a number of votes that is commensurate to its number of seats in the plenary.1 As Leston-Bandeira (2004: 44) notes, the Conference of Leaders is the primary internal decision-making body and sets most of parliament’s agenda. Likewise, each of the seven largest PPGs – which usually means every PPG – is represented in the Board of Administration of parliament. Concerning exclusive powers, we can identify three key areas: dismissal of governments; appointment to intra-parliamentary bodies; and agenda-­ setting. About the first, only PPGs can present a motion to reject a government’s programme. Moreover, while motions of no confidence can formally be presented either by a parliamentary group or by one-quarter of MPs, the higher hurdle of the latter option de facto limits such motions to PPGs. As for the second area, virtually all legislative bodies are appointed through PPGs. This includes parliamentary committees, both regarding their membership and chairs; parliamentary delegations; parliamentary friendship groups; and parliament’s Standing Committee (Fernandes 2016). It also embraces the Assembly’s Bureau, formally the highest body in Parliament, with only PPGs able to propose candidates. Finally, PPGs also have some exclusive powers regarding setting the parliamentary order of business and summoning ministers to parliament. As for concurrent powers, the most salient pertain legislative initiative. Unlike Spain, both individual MPs and PPGs can introduce bills and amendments. However, the rules for voting bills reflect the subordination of MPs to their PPGs. In the usual form of voting, each PPG’s official line is attributed a number of votes equal to its number of MPs (regardless of MPs’ presence in parliament), minus the votes of individual MPs that vote differently from the official line. This has yielded situations where a bill is rejected (or approved) even though the majority of MPs in the chamber voted in favour (or against) it (Marujo 2018).

Internal functioning of PPGs and relation with individual MPs

This section analyses the internal structure and organisation of PPGs and how they interact with individual MPs. We do so by examining the rules that govern their internal organisation, again looking into formal structures and non-codified institutionalised practices. In general terms, PPGs in both countries have adopted highly hierarchical structures and patterns of ­behaviour similar to the mainstream parties in other European democracies. Some peculiarities show the influence of party politics in their organisation. In Spain, beside the parliament’s standing orders, the internal functioning of PPGs is defined by the groups’ internal rules, although it is not compulsory to have them. Some parties – like PSOE, PP or Ciudadanos – have produced formal internal regulations, while other (like Podemos or the

Parliamentary party groups  57

regionalist groups in the lower chamber) rely on party statutes and informal procedures.2 Overall, the chamber’s standing orders set the primary scheme of organisation, which is afterwards developed by PPGs according to internal party rules and orientations. The basic common structure that all PPGs use distinguishes four actors: the plenary (composed by all the members of the group); the board, including the main group offices (president, secretary general, whose label and number depend on the party); the groups’ spokesman; and some members responsible for coordination and control of the various areas in which the group is organised. The party leader holds the presidency within the group, delegates the function of spokesperson in another member. This one is the main responsible for the organisation of the other internal bodies and members, and the implementation of the decisions made by the PPG’s board. This board reflects the translation of the party leadership into the PPG, an aspect further elaborated in Section ‘Relations established with the ­extra-parliamentary party organisation’ below. Indeed, despite the PPG’s plenary being usually considered as the formal selector of these leading internal positions, actually it is the party which designates who will be in the PPG’s board. Only in some rare occasions does the party leadership differ from the group. On the one hand, there have been situations where a change in the central office after an election promoted as a party leader someone without a seat in the chamber. This was the case of Antonio Hernández Mancha in AP (Alianza Popular, Popular Alliance) between 1987 and 1989, while he was a senator. Recently, Pedro Sánchez became the PSOE leader again after winning the party primary unexpectedly in May 2017, some months after resigning as a party leader and as an MP. In both cases, this situation has fostered some internal problems and paved the way to present a vote of no-confidence to the executive in order to give parliamentary visibility to the extra-parliamentarian leader in the opposition. On the other hand, the regionalist groups tend to be represented by delegates of the party board, while the main leaders stay in the regional parliament (or the regional executive). This has been the case, for instance, of the Catalan (CiU) and the Basque (PNV) groups, which behave as agents of the principal (usually the regional prime minister). In this case, the linkage between the PPG and the extra-parliamentary party becomes more intense (Oñate 2000). Finally, those PPGs composed of two or more parties or coalitions have a more complex configuration at the top of the group. Indeed, the ‘confederal’ PPG of Podemos, its allies and United Left does not have a formal internal regulation, and the main decisions are made among the central offices of the extra-parliamentary organisations. This informal decision-making can produce the exclusion of the minor components of the PPG in important parliamentary decisions, as has happened with Anova, the smallest member of the Podemos PPG.3

58  Carlos Jalali and Juan Rodríguez Teruel

The party constitutions state the principle of obeisance to PPGs’ members, whose actions are under the supervision of the group’s leaders. In this respect, the main disciplinary procedures to keep party cohesion under control include fines, a downgrade in parliamentary offices and threats of expulsion from the PPG. The amount of a fine for disobeying the PPG’s voting orders can fluctuate among different groups (100–600 euros in the PP, 200–600 euros in the PSOE, 600–900 euros in Ciudadanos, but no fee established in Podemos yet). The use of fines is not exceptional, although the lack of discipline is often related to individual votes for ethic motivations, rather than the political division of the group. The primary examples of deep indiscipline in the PPG happened with the UCD and AP in the first years of the democratic period. The more relevant one in recent times was the decision of 15 PSOE parliamentarians to oppose the party’s decision to vote in favour of Mariano Rajoy as a prime minister in October 2016. They were penalised with a fine, and some of them sacked from PPG responsibilities. The more effective procedure to ensure discipline is the risk of not being included in the electoral list in the following election, as the party board completely controls these lists. In the case of parliamentary groups in Portugal, the only formal organisational requirement set in the Rules of Procedure is that they have a president. Beyond this, each PPG can decide how it is structured. Examining the five main parties, there is significant variation in how their PPGs are formalised. In the case of the PS, PSD and CDS, the parliamentary groups are formally recognised in the party statutes and have their internal regulations (although these are not publicly available). The situation is different in the Communist Party and the Left Bloc. These two parties give significantly less formal importance to the parliamentary groups, and they are formally subordinated to the extra-parliamentary party. In both cases, the PPGs officeholders are appointed by the party rather than elected by MPs (Leston-Bandeira 2009: 699; Lopes 2015), making these PPGs considerably less autonomous in formal terms than those of PS, PSD and CDS. In practice, all PPGs have not only a president, but also vice-presidents, with the number varying according to the size of the parliamentary group and to the party.4 Party leaders who are MPs do not take up positions at the helm of the parliamentary group. However, as in Spain, the PPG leadership is closely aligned with the party leadership. Thus, while the PPGs of PS, PSD and CDS elect their presidents, it is rare for an election to be contested by more than one candidate; and the PPG board requires the de facto confidence of the party leadership to endure. The coexistence between party leaders and PPGs can be tense after a change in the party leadership, when a new party leader has to coexist with a parliamentary group he did not choose, and with MPs that often hail from a different internal faction. The PSD after the election of Rui Rio in 2018 is the most recent example of such tensions, though it is far from being an isolated case. However, these tensions rarely boil over into open rebellion, and the party leader usually prevails.

Parliamentary party groups  59

As in Spain, the most striking instances of internal dissension occurred in the early phase of democratisation. Since the mid-1980s, there have been only five MPs that left their PPG, becoming independent MPs. Of these, only one was a consequential departure: that of a CDS MP in 1999, casting the deciding vote in favour of the Socialist government’s budget, against his party’s orientation. Three factors disincentivise such departures. First, party statutes: parties establish MPs allegiance to the party’s orientation, although in Portugal sanctions are disciplinary rather than fines. Second, the closed-list proportional representation electoral system, allowing the party leadership to exclude recalcitrant MPs from subsequent elections. Third, the parliamentary Rules of Procedure, which severely limit what an independent MP can do. These three factors also strengthen voting discipline in parliament. The general perception is that voting discipline is almost absolute in the Portuguese parliament (Braga da Cruz 1988). However, voting dissent does exist in Portugal, with between 5 and 44 votes having internal dissent per legislature from 1976 through 1999 (Leston-Bandeira 2009). In total, the number of MPs dissenting from the party line ranged from 12 to 104 per legislature over this period, with dissent being more common in the PSD (54 votes with dissent) and PS (53 votes) than in the CDS (7 votes), while Portuguese Communist Party (PCP) MPs always toed the party line (Leston-Bandeira 2009: 706–707).5

Examining the available data from 2009 to 2018, we find that dissent continues to be present.6 Once again, dissent is more frequent in the PS, PSD, and CDS-PP than in the PCP, with the BE echoing the Communist Party’s pattern. In the case of the PCP, dissent continues to be entirely absent; while the BE has only two MPs that have voted against the party line (out of 56 seats since 1999), both in the current legislature. This contrasts with the case of the PS (225 MPs have dissented over these three legislatures); PSD (147 MPs); and CDS (59 MPs), and is consistent with the pattern of weaker autonomy of the parliamentary groups of PCP and BE noted in the previous section. The data also show dissent to be generally higher among MPs that are not party members. Finally, dissent appears to be on the rise. In the 2009–2011 legislature, 87 MPs voted against their party’s line, with a total of 243 votes against the party. In the 2011–2015 legislature, these numbers rise to 175 and 1955, respectively; and, in the 2015–2018 period, the numbers are 173 MPs and 2406 votes. In part, this increase in dissent may reflect a change in the Socialist Party, which relaxed voting discipline for its MPs from 2011 onwards (Lisi 2015: 58). The PS saw its share of dissenting MPs increase from 48 per cent in the 11th legislature (2009–2011) to 57 per cent in the current 13th legislature (2005–2018), even though both have a minority Socialist government in power. At the same time, it should be emphasised that such dissent is still largely inconsequential: majorities are almost invariably determined by how the PPGs define their vote, despite such instances of dissent.

60  Carlos Jalali and Juan Rodríguez Teruel

Resources

In this section, we present the resources provided to PPGs. The main focus is on finance, but we also consider other resources provided to the groups. These include traditional formal resources (e.g. personnel, space); and more modern resources (e.g. website space and other digital communication tools). Spanish PPGs have increasingly received financial assistance from the chamber’s budget since the beginning of the democratic period. The parliamentary group was the first source of party public funding established after the first democratic election when the provisional chamber’s rules set up internal subsidies to sustain the activities of the PPGs. Some years later the party finance law included parliamentary subsidies as one of the leading public sources for party funding, although in the last years, the economic crisis of the last decade reduced the total amount of the allocation. The impact of parliamentary subsidies on the main parties’ finance has remained stable, meaning the 8–6 per cent of the total incomes (as shown in ­Figure 4.1), even if the proportion of party revenues from national institutions have declined because of the increasing local and regional public allocations (Bértoa et al. 2014).

Figure 4.1  Party finance from national PPGs (both chambers) in Spain. Source: Own elaboration from official data (Court of Audit). Note: Columns shows the amount of parliamentary subsidies for all the PPGs in millions of euros. Lines show the proportion of parliamentary funding among total party income for both PP and PSOE.

Parliamentary party groups  61

The parliamentary allocation is distributed among the PPGs according to two criteria: a fixed amount for each group, and a variable allocation proportional to the seats decided by the Board of the chamber. The central party office manages these economic resources with very few restrictions. They sustain the PPG activities related to the legislative tasks, but can also be dedicated to the extra-parliamentary party activities. The Spanish Court of Audit generically scrutinises the supervision of these funds. An additional economic source comes from the contributions made by the MPs extracted from their parliamentary salaries, based on the proportion decided by the party board (Pauner Chulvi 2010). MPs’ salaries are usually received in the party bank account, and it is up to the party organisation to decide how to distribute them among the PPG members. Interestingly, Ciudadanos’ statutes say that the party will provide additional economic funds to the PPG if the subsidies received by the chamber are not enough to develop its activity. Staff working for the MPs are funded by the chambers’ budget separately from the party allocations. The amount of personal resources is also decided at the beginning of the term, and distributed proportionally among the different PPGs. In 2016, up to 250 staff members were assigned to the MPs, at the cost of 7.5 million euros.7 These employees are appointed by the PPG’s spokesperson and are usually focused on the legislative job, but they can also work on extra-parliamentary tasks. The infrastructures assigned to the PPG have been increasing over the years, with the construction of new buildings around the parliament. Digital resources are, however, still in the process of development. The PPGs’ web portals are still embedded in the general party websites and are mostly dedicated to providing information about the background of the representatives, and some general diffusion of the parliamentary initiatives.8 Similarly, the presence of PPGs in social networks is always subordinated to the party image, despite groups like the PSOE, Ciudadanos and the PP having their own Twitter account. In this respect, the interaction with citizens in social networks is instead developed by individual MPs, particularly Twitter and Facebook. As in Spain, public funding plays a central role in party finances in Portugal. Funding has always been primarily tied to parliamentary representation since the inception of public subventions in 1977. However, the usage of this funding by parties reflects the subordination of the PPG to the ­extra-parliamentary party, with parliamentary subventions often used for general party tasks. The first public funding to parties was included in the Organic Law of the Assembly of the Republic (Law 32/77), granting an annual subvention to each party represented in Parliament; and the relevant article also established that the funding was so parties could “carry out their objectives, namely those of a parliamentary nature”. However, this latter proviso was largely ignored by the parties, with this subvention becoming another element of their general budget.

62  Carlos Jalali and Juan Rodríguez Teruel

Funding underwent two significant changes since 1977. The first is that, from 1998 on, this subvention was extended to parties not represented in parliament that gained more than 50,000 votes (a condition that was met once, in the 2011 elections); and it also lost the formal requirement that the fund be aimed at parties’ parliamentary work. The second is that, with law 77/88 of 1988, another source of funding was added, specifically tied to parliamentary representation, with each PPG being entitled to an annual subvention. As per the first subvention, in this case also the monopoly of PPGs was subsequently relaxed: from 2010 onwards (Law 55/2010), this subvention was extended to MPs outside of a PPG. Formally, this funding is for “costs of support [encargos de assessoria] for MPs”. In practice, however, this funding has also become an additional source of party income rather than specifically aimed or delimited to providing support for MPs. In addition to these funds, PPGs have also been entitled to support staff. The number of staff to be hired is limited by an amount that depends on the number of MPs. This ends up being the largest source of funding for the PPGs: out of just over 9.5 million euros received by PPGs in 2016, 89 per cent were to pay for staff. As subventions are granted based against a set figure, the total received by PPGs is relatively stable over time. Parliamentary groups are free to set wages and use their amount for staff as they choose. As such, the number of staff varies across years and parties (see Figure 4.2). As can be seen, the number of staff is close to the number of MPs, and in recent years often exceeds this number. On average, from 2006 to 2016, the ratio of staff to MPs is 1:1, which would allow each MP to have his own staff. However, in practice, the majority of MPs do not have their own staff. Once again, the pattern is one of prevalence of the extra-parliamentary party over the PPG. The PPG staff funds are frequently used to pay general party staff rather than specific staff of the parliamentary group and MPs. As the former president of the legal body that oversees party accounts put it, this funding for parliamentary staff has become “a means for financing parties” (quoted in Davim 2018: 51). Concerning MPs salaries, unlike Spain, these are paid directly to the individual MPs. Only in the case of the PCP are MPs formally expected to give the party the difference between their salary as an MP and their previous salary. Regarding digital tools, all PPGs have the possibility of having a page on Parliament’s website. The actual usage of this possibility is correlated to the degree of formal independence of the PPGs. All three parties – PS, PSD and CDS – that have formally independent PPGs have this page within the parliament’s website. Inversely, PCP and BE do not have these pages, instead hosting the online presence of their parliamentary group solely on their party website or on a separate website, albeit linked to the party website, which is consistent with the pattern of formal subordination of their PPGs to the party.

Parliamentary party groups  63

Relations established with the extra-parliamentary party organisation

In this section, we examine how PPGs interact with the extra-parliamentary party organisation. We do so by analysing how formal party statutes establish relations between the PPGs and the party-as-a-whole and then examining the dynamics of these relations in the two countries. Spanish political parties have become highly parliamentarised organisations (Sánchez de Dios 1999) in that daily decision-making has been transferred to PPGs at both national and regional levels. However, it does not mean that the PPG have gained autonomy vis-à-vis the extra-­parliamentary party organisation because all parties’ constitutions impose a strong subordination of the MPs to the party decisions, as was shown previously. For the PP, the PPG is an instrument for the party’s political action in the institutions. Likewise, PSOE, Ciudadanos and Podemos have similar rules. Moreover, the existing overlap between the party and parliamentary leadership strengthens this dependence link in all political parties. Hence, the PPG boards are designed by the party headquarters, and the parliamentary

64  Carlos Jalali and Juan Rodríguez Teruel

spokesman is usually a member of the party board ex officio. When the party comes to the government, the PPG subordination increases, as part of the party elite shifts from the parliament to the executive. The personal overlap between both arenas makes the parliamentary leadership highly dependent on the internal evolution of the extra-parliamentary party: a change in the party leader leads ineluctably to a change in the PPG board. This has been the case in the recent political reshuffles occurred after the party conferences in the main forces. The resignation of Pedro Sánchez as PSOE’s secretary general produced a complete reshuffle of the PPG board in October 2016, which was changed again some months later when Sánchez won the primary election for the party leadership. The fall of Mariano Rajoy after being sacked from the government through a vote of no-confidence produced a party leadership replacement that extended later to the PPG. In Podemos, the realignment of the balance among factions in the 2017 conference (Vistalegre II) also brought significant changes to the board composition. Ultimately, party instability has been the primary source of conflict and lack of discipline within the PPGs. In this respect, the first years of the democratic period showed how the party internal opposition could challenge the national leaders through the PPG, as it happened with UCD and AP, whose MPs were divided according to party factions (Oñate 2000). Similar to Spain, the party in public office has a central role in Portugal (Jalali 2007). Indeed, van Biezen’s (2003: 218) description of party organisations “controlled from a small centre of power located at the interstices of the extra-parliamentary party and the party in public office” largely holds, with the notable exception of the Communist Party. However, while the party in public office is essential, the PPG is not the locus of internal power in Portuguese parties. For the national party organisation, the critical public offices are at government level; and for the sub-national party structures, these are the municipal executives. Rank-and-file MPs hold comparatively little sway within the party. Moreover, while a PPG president and its board will be relevant within the party organisation, this importance is mostly by virtue of having the party leader’s trust rather than from having the support of MPs. Overall, then, the pattern is one of subordination of the PPG to the party leadership – a pattern that is consistent with the previous analysis concerning how PPGs operate internally and use their funds. Formally, the PPG is recognised as a national body of the party in the statutes of the PS, PSD and CDS. In all three cases, the PPG president is also a member ex officio of the executive committees of the party. Again, the main line of difference is between these three parties and their counterparts to the left, PCP and BE. The latter do not recognise the PPG as a body of the party. The Left Bloc mentions its national and European parliamentary groups in the statutes, but only to clarify which national party body oversees them. As for the Communist Party, there is no mention of the PPG at all, with its statutes only specifying that elected officeholders of the party must act according to the party’s orientations.

Parliamentary party groups  65

As in Spain, the selection of the PPG leadership is carefully articulated with the party leadership, and a PPG leader needs to have the trust of the party leader. Thus, while the PSD, CDS and PS establish the PPG as an independent body, changes in the party leadership invariably entail a change in the PPG leadership if the latter is not aligned with the former. The most recent instance of this situation took place in the PSD. In the aftermath of the leadership election of Rui Rio to the party’s leadership in January 2018, the president of the PPG – who had been elected to the role in July 2017 – was forced to resign, after having backed Rio’s opponent in the internal leadership election. While the erstwhile leader of the PPG was willing to continue in the role under the new leadership, he is reported to have conceded that “although the PSD’s parliamentary group is an autonomous body, no president of the parliamentary group can endure against the party president’s wish” (Lusa 2018).

Relations between PPGs and the executive

In this section, we examine how PPGs are involved in the process of government both formally and informally. We do so across two dimensions: government formation and survival; and government operation. In Spain, the leaders of the parliamentary groups are intensively involved in the process of government formation, although they participate as party leaders rather than parliamentary actors. The central party office formally approves the PPG’s decision on presenting or supporting a candidate for the investitures as PM. Paradoxically, the PPGs are less visible in the process when the party is in the cabinet. For instance, the PSOE’s statute defines its PPG as the parliamentary support when the party rules the government and must report regularly to the party office about its activity. Traditionally, there are have been two additional constraints to the role of the PPGs in the cabinet building process. First, the parliamentary group does not operate as a springboard to the cabinet, since Spain is one of the parliamentary democracies in Western Europe with less amount of parliamentary cabinet ministers (Rodríguez Teruel 2011). Second, in situations of minority government, both PSOE and PP have preferred to negotiate the parliamentary support with regionalist parties, which are policy-seeking at the national level and office-seeking at the regional level (Field 2016). Thus, the statewide parties have built this kind of multilevel exchanges by making agreements at the party elite level and setting aside their PPGs. However, recent changes in the party system have altered this pattern, as the old big parties now need to build parliamentary majorities with the new PPGs, although these new parties have preferred so far to negotiate policy concessions instead of entering the cabinet. In this context, it is particularly important the role of those junior ministers or general directors assuming the function of liaison between the executive and the parliamentary committees. They become one of the main channels of influence and

66  Carlos Jalali and Juan Rodríguez Teruel

information for the PPGs – particularly those at the opposition – about the policy agenda of the government. The Spanish PPGs have two types of resources to develop policy analysis seeking influence on the policy-making at the parliamentary level: internal services provided to the standing committees of parliament, and parliamentary groups, and external services and expertise provided by political organisations and experts. It is the limits of such resources that constrains the extent of influence of PPGs, which have not achieved a stable pattern of participation in the process of policy-making (Maurer 2008). Consequently, as the influence of the individual MPs on the policy agenda is somewhat limited, the interest groups tend to focus on the party leaders and the PPG board in order to gain effectiveness in the process (Caballero Miguez 2007). In this respect, the internal hierarchy in the PPGs and the strong party discipline increases the cost of individual transactions among parliamentarians and hinders the chances to achieve policy agreements without the consent of the PPG leaders (Caballero Miguez 2007). The result is meagre effectiveness of the parliamentary opposition to pass private member’s bill, in opposition to the high amount of government bills approved, even in minority situations. In Portugal, the fact that PPG leaders are generally not in the party leadership constitutes a significant difference from Spain. As such, PPGs generally have a weak level of involvement in the process of government formation, taking a backseat role to the party leadership. This weakness of PPGs within parties is compounded by the rules of the parliamentary investiture vote. The Portuguese case is characterised by Cheibub, Martin and Rasch (2015) as a relatively weak investiture vote, with parliament’s influence reasonably limited. The investiture vote is ex post, with parliament solely able to vote on the President’s appointed government; and it requires a negative absolute majority to topple the government, leading to a new round again initiated by a presidential appointment (or dissolution). The significant differences with Spain are that the investiture by the Congreso is ex ante, a characteristic that is considered stronger by Cheibub, Martin and Rasch (2015); and it requires a simple majority rule if an absolute majority is not achieved in the first round. Typically, government formation is amply settled well in advance of any involvement of the PPGs, either because the elections produce a clear mandate for a single party; or the party leadership negotiates a post-electoral coalition government. Only twice has the parliamentary vote of investiture rejected a government: in 1978, when the parties blocked one of President Eanes’ non-partisan “governments of presidential inspiration”; and in November 2015, when PS, PCP, BE, PEV and PAN voted down the minority coalition government of PSD-CDS, paving the way for a PS minority government with the parliamentary support of PCP, BE and PEV. While this latter case marks the highpoint of parliamentary intervention in government formation, it also reveals the limitations of the PPGs. While some members of the PPG boards were generally in the negotiating teams between these parties, which ultimately resulted in formal agreements, their position was a reflection of the trust the party leader had in them rather than arising

Parliamentary party groups  67

from their role within the PPG. Moreover, the ultimate decisions regarding these deals were taken by the party leadership rather than the PPGs. Regarding government survival, the main difference from Spain is that Portugal does not require a constructive motion of no confidence, unlike its Iberian neighbour. On paper, this should strengthen the role of PPGs in terms of its ability to dismiss governments. In practice, however, the effect is minimal. Motions of no confidence are considerably more frequent in Portugal than in Spain. Every legislature in Portugal bar one (the 7th legislature, 1995–1999) has had at least one such motion; and there have been 30 such motions since the first legislature of 1976, 20 of which occurred from 2000 to 2017 (Leston-Bandeira and Fernandes 2015). This contrasts with the Spanish case, with only four such motions since democratisation. However, while the number of motions is much higher in Portugal, the ultimate impact on government survival is identical, with only one motion succeeding in Portugal (in 1987) and in Spain (2018). Concerning policy influence, the subordination of PPGs to the party leadership noted in previous sections is also evident here. In the specific case of the political forces in the executive, this means that the party in government prevails over the PPG, as the party leadership is concentrated in the former, with the member of government for parliamentary affairs playing a pivotal role in linking government and PPG. At the same time, it should be noted that the overall role of PPGs in policy is also dependent on the type of government. In single-party majority governments, the influence of the party in government is higher than in coalition governments or minority governments. In the latter, the opposition can also push through policy, and governments are obliged to negotiate across party aisles, making parliament a more important arena for policy formulation (Moury and Fernandes 2018; Leston-Bandeira 2004: 28, 86).

Conclusion

This chapter has compared the main features of the PPGs in the national lower chambers of Portugal and Spain. Both countries have typical patterns of historical evolution of parliamentary democracies, which conditioned the institutionalisation of PPGs negatively until democratisation. However, these groups have quickly developed during these four decades of democracy. The main feature in both countries is the strong link between political parties and PPGs that subordinates the groups to the extra-parliamentary organisation. Although this is a feature shared with the rest of parliaments in democratic Europe, these cases show a particularly high degree of dependence of the groups on the party decision-making, which deeply erodes the role of the individual MPs deeply. The analysis has found some factors that influence the extent of fluctuation of the powers of the PPGs in Spain. On the one hand, the multilevel dimension in Spanish politics has altered the traditional role of the national PPGs in the policy agenda. Subnational institutions have gained a greater autonomy to make policies, reducing the power of the national parliament. Moreover, the particular strategies adopted by regionalist parties (­policy-seeking at the national level, office-seeking at the regional level)

68  Carlos Jalali and Juan Rodríguez Teruel

allows them to keep a vital degree of capacity to negotiate with the national government, to the detriment of the parliamentary groups in the chamber. On the other hand, the rise of new parties has also altered the internal dynamic of national parliaments. The increasing fragmentation of the chamber and the high replacement of MPs in the last years could have renewed the powers of the chamber against the government, although it has simultaneously eroded the capacity to achieve agreements and thus precluded a greater role for PPGs in policy-making. While PPGs are considerably easier to form in Portugal than in Spain, the number of parliamentary groups has been lower in Portugal, a reflection of the comparative absence of a multilevel dimension and a generally more stable party system. Overall, PPGs in Portugal have a double life in political terms. On the one hand, parliamentary groups overwhelmingly prevail over individual MPs, with the formal rules – not least in the Constitution – making PPGs, rather than MPs, the critical political actor within Parliament. Indeed, this is reflected in how voting takes place: MPs that are absent do not reduce a parliamentary group’s numbers in a parliamentary vote. On the other hand, PPGs play a secondary role to the extra-parliamentary party and, in particular, to the party leadership, even if several parties grant their PPGs formal autonomy. At the same time, as this chapter has noted, this matryoshka-like ­pattern – of MPs subordinated to the PPGs, which in turn are subservient to their parties – is not identical across all parties. In particular, a distinction emerges between the PS, PSD and CDS, on the one hand; and PCP and BE, on the other hand. The latter have much more cohesive and disciplined PPGs than the former and grant their parliamentary groups considerably less formal autonomy. Interestingly, this distinction between PPGs mirrors a line of separation that structured the Portuguese party system until 2015, with PCP and BE excluded from national government. The post-2015 ‘contraption’ – a minority Socialist government that has the unprecedented support of PCP and BE to form a government and in key policy instruments, such as the state budget – has eroded some of this historical divide, at least temporarily. With the post-2015 legislature potentially marking an inflexion point in the Portuguese party system, it will be interesting to see how such a change percolates through to the PPGs in the future.
