Introduction

Legislative committees are the workhorses of efficient parliaments. LaPolambora highlights that “if the national legislature is to be a significant political actor, then it must have specialized committees of limited membership and considerable scope” (1974: 123). Together with political parties, committees offer the organizational arena to aggregate preferences, curb transaction costs and facilitate information acquisition and dissemination (Strøm 1998). According to Sartori, committees are optimal decision-­making bodies because they “shun majority rule, seek unanimous agreements via internal deferred payments, and adjust to the outer world, or incorporate its demands, via side payments” (1987: 236). The study of committees has a long tradition in American politics. The three theories of legislative organization – distributional (Shepsle 1978), informational (Krehbiel 1991) and partisan (Cox and McCubbins 1993) – constitute a sophisticated and fertile field. The last decade witnessed an explosion in the number of scholarly works devoted to committees beyond the US Congress.1 For example, work on the German Bundestag explores how electoral system incentives shape committee assignments (Gschwend and Zittel 2018). In Ireland and Denmark, Hansen (2010, 2011) finds that, except for expertise, there is not a clearly defined pattern in assignments. Yordanova (2011) shows that expertise plays a significant role in the ­European Parliament. With a few exceptions (Fernandes 2016; Fernandes et al 2018; Riera and Cantú 2018), committee systems in Iberian legislatures remain largely overlooked in the international literature. The primary goal of this chapter is to fill in this gap in committee literature. Specifically, we have two goals. First, we aim to map the critical indicators of committee organization and powers in Portugal and Spain. Second, we seek to examine the singularities in the evolution of the committee system in the two legislatures since democratization in the 1970s. The chapter proceeds as follows. First, we discuss some of the critical dimensions of committee organization, using stylized propositions derived from the US literature. This section aims to present several indicators employed in the previous literature, against which we later compare our two

5 Committee systems in Portugal and Spain

Jorge M. Fernandes and Pedro Riera

72  Jorge M. Fernandes and Pedro Riera

cases. Second, we adopt a historical approach to the evolution of committee systems in Portugal and Spain. Third, we display data on the dimensions identified in the first section. Finally, we conclude the chapter by highlighting the main similarities and differences of the committee systems of the two parliaments under study.

Committee systems in legislatures

Over the past decade, committee systems in European legislatures have benefited from a growing interest among comparative legislative scholars. Most of this literature, however, has focused almost exclusively on the politics of assignment (Hansen 2011; Fernandes 2016; Mickler 2018; Fernandes et  al. 2018). We draw on this body of scholarly work to establish several institutional and behavioural assumptions for this chapter. First, MPs are reelection-­seeking political actors, whose decisions and actions aim at maximizing their chances of regaining their seat (Mayhew 1974). Second, political parties have a pivotal role in the organization of the delegation of power from voters to elected officials. In Müller’s formulation, in parliamentary democracies, “no one would seriously consider any alternative to political parties as the most important political coordination mechanism” (2000: 316). Third, the inter-connected relationship between the legislature and the executive creates an environment in which “professional advancement and policy influence [are] a single indivisible good controlled by the party leadership” (Kam 2009: 26). Fourth, electoral rules have an impact on legislative organization. MPs operating in systems with low incentives to cultivate a personal vote will be spurred to become party-centred (Carey and Shugart 1995). The institutional environment in Iberian legislatures generates “centripetal” incentives for MPs to put parties first if they want to achieve reselection, reelection, meet their policy goals and, above all, maximize their political power in the chamber. Importantly, Portugal and Spain have a closed-list proportional electoral system whose rules make the party the central unit. MPs have few, if any, incentives to cultivate a personal vote.2 Instead, the legislature and the national party are the arenas in which they need to deliver to gain reelection. Against this institutional and behavioural background, we turn onto Strøm’s (1998) work on European committees, in which this author articulates an array of empirical indicators that help us map the structure and powers of committees in legislative decision-making across countries. This classification scheme has been extensively used in comparative literature (for a review see Martin and Mickler 2018), which helps us to situate Portugal and Spain in the general European context. In what follows, we discuss those indicators.

Committee structure

Committees are highly heterogeneous institutions. Their structure varies not only across, but also within, countries, as actors reshape committees’

Committee systems in Portugal and Spain  73

structure to maximize their benefits. Strøm (1998) focuses on six criteria to map the structure of committee systems in European parliamentary and semipresidential democracies. First, several types of committees exist, and they differ in tenure – that is, they have different functions and duration. Mostly, if we focus on tenure, committees can be divided between permanent and ad hoc. Typically, the latter have a circumscribed duration and object. Legislative actors decide to create a committee with a specific ­object – for example, to conduct an inquiry into a scandal – that will terminate at the end of its work. Permanent committees have a continuing existence over legislative terms. Concerning types, Strøm (1998) distinguishes between law-­making committees, specialized committees and non-law-making committees. Second, the number of committees is of great importance if one wants to understand the institutional capacity of the committee system. Indeed, a higher number of committees is associated with more institutional arenas for specialization and also heightens the capacity to deal with more workload simultaneously. Third, committee size matters for the efficient functioning of these structures. According to Verba (1961), committee size is what makes them an optimal decision-making arena for it enables expertise acquisition. There is a curvilinear effect in the relationship between size and efficiency. Indeed, if committees are too small, they will not be useful in that they will not allow for the inclusion of enough members to digest information and deliver benefits to the parties. Fourth, jurisdictional arrangements are essential to understanding property rights and whether the committee structure mimics executive organization. To be sure, the latter matters to heighten oversight capacities of the executive branch, which is particularly vital in coalition government contexts (Carroll and Cox 2012). Furthermore, well-defined and stable jurisdictions promote active specialization, mainly when MPs serve for several terms in the same committee. Fifth, multiple membership restrictions are essential to understanding whether rules promote specialization. MPs face time and capacity constraints. If they need to sit on multiple committees, their capacity to acquire and disseminate specialized knowledge is curbed. In principle, multiple membership restrictions will depend on legislative size. Finally, subcommittees play an important role in the functioning of committees because they permit for further specialization by asking a subset of committee members to deal with a specific topic. Crucially, in parliamentary democracies, subcommittees are the arena where membership hinges on self-selection. We note, however, that, although subcommittees promote further specialization, they also heighten transaction costs in decision-­ making because they add a further layer of complexity.

Committee procedures

Committee procedures are intimately linked to legislative organization. Herein, we focus on five dimensions identified by Strøm (1998). First, we

74  Jorge M. Fernandes and Pedro Riera

study committee assignments – that is, the process through which members gain access to committees. In parliamentary democracies, political parties are the gatekeepers to these positions, which are distributed based on the size of the party delegation on the floor (Rasch 2009). Committee assignments have been subject to intense examination in the literature if anything because delegating power to the best agent in each arena would minimize moral hazard and hidden information problems. Thus, political parties need to be cautious about whom they assign to committees. In most countries, seniority and party loyalty are essential factors in the screening of candidates. Furthermore, membership stability is relevant to appraise whether committees become specialization arenas. Second, chair allocation and selection are important to understanding whether occupying these positions yields policy influence to the MP and their party. Furthermore, it matters whether chair allocation follows a majoritarian rule whereby all payoffs accrue to the governing parties or, differently, it follows a consensual approach heightening the influence of opposition parties. Rules governing committee chairs selection are heterogeneous. In some countries, rules of procedure establish the necessary steps for chair appointment. On the contrary, other countries have only sparse regulations, which results in letting parties decide the allocation of chairs freely. Third, the powers endowed to committee chairs are essential to understand what MPs and parties can do once they control these positions. Recent literature establishes that committee chairs are used as coalition governance mechanisms to keep tabs on partners. This implies, of course, that chairs have agenda-setting powers and special voting rights or sit in partisan coordinating bodies. Surprisingly, in their comparative work on committee chairs, Sieberer and Hoffman find that “committee chairs are institutionally weak in most parliaments”. Indeed, “holding the chair does not provide actors with many additional rights they could use” (2017: 316). Fourth, committee openness influences the extent to which members perceive them as an arena for informational advantage. Committees can operate either behind closed doors or openly, with constant media scrutiny and diffusion of their work, much in line with the character of the plenary sessions. This has behavioural implications for the functioning of committees and, often, entails trade-offs. Committees that operate behind closed doors stimulate inter-party cooperation and information sharing. Within this framework, blurring party lines and engaging in some log-rolling within the committee are optimal strategies whose benefits accrue to all committee members in the long-run. Open committees tend to be more adversarial in that MPs do not want to be perceived as soft on their adversaries. Consequently, they mimic their floor style whereby they engage in advertising and position taking (Mayhew 1974). Fifth, the committee stage in the legislative process determines how influential committees are in the law-making process. Committees have the opportunity to weigh in either before a bill proposal reaches the floor or only

Committee systems in Portugal and Spain  75

after it reaches the floor. By and large, committees whose intervention in the legislative process happens before the floor stage have much more power, whereby committee members can shape the menu of choices and become informed about the potential consequences of the bill. Mainly, transmitting that information to the party leadership helps the latter to map the bill onto policy consequences before the floor has a say on the bill’s fate.

Committee powers

Our third block of indicators aims to measure the powers that committees possess to conduct their business efficiently in the legislature. Our three final indicators originate from Strøm’s (1998) previous work as well. They have one thing in common: all three capture from different angles the extent to which committees act independently from the floor. First, revision of bills matters to understand the extent to which committees can change bills without exogenous interference from the floor. The more capacity to act independently, the more influential committees are. Indeed, according to Strøm (1998), committees are well equipped to take over such function because of their informational advantages and small size. Second, control of the committees’ timetable is also linked to committee autonomy vis-à-vis the floor. In contexts in which committees can determine their agenda by freely choosing the witnesses they want to hear, and when they want to do so, they are more powerful. On the contrary, if the parties can meddle in committee business and shape its agenda, then committees are weaker, which ultimately conducts to a wearying of the legislative branch vis-à-vis the executive. Third, the power of requesting documents determines the extent to which committees act at their volition in their search for information. This is crucial to have highly knowledgeable committees. The more institutional prerogatives to request from the bureaucracy, interest groups and so forth, for information and documents, the more influential committees are.

Committee systems in the Iberian legislatures in historical perspective

This section makes an overview of the evolution of committee systems in the first decades of democracy in Portugal and Spain. As discussed in previous chapters, both countries had a similar temporal framework in the institutionalization of their legislature. For this reason, we can more easily appraise how committee systems evolved in both countries. The committee system is the most critical decision-making arena in today’s Portuguese Assembleia da República. However, it has not always been like that. In the early days of democracy, the floor remained the predominant arena in the legislature. According to Leston-Bandeira (2004), in the aftermath of a long dictatorship, political actors needed to conduct their

76  Jorge M. Fernandes and Pedro Riera

work in the Plenum to make it as visible and transparent as possible and heighten, as a result, the legitimacy of legislative procedures. Opello’s work (1986) offers a good insight into the functioning of the committee system in the first decade of democracy in Portugal. More specifically, this author makes the following four points. First, the fledgling committee structure was well developed, ranging from 12 to 20 committees. To be sure, this allowed the committee system to foster specialization and labour division from the early days of democracy. However, despite parties’ awareness of the benefits of allocating MPs to committees depending on their expertise, there was a high turnover rate from one term to the next. Second, political actors realized early that the committee system needed to shadow the jurisdiction of the cabinet´s portfolios. Thus, there was enough latitude to adapt the committee structure depending on the needs. Third, until the mid-1980s, committees only considered bills after the first reading plenary stage (votação na generalidade). In practice, according to Opello, committees were left to “work out the details of compromises made in plenary or to polish up the language” (1986: 302). Fourth, the first decade of the democratic legislature was plagued with what Opello dubs “confusion about the jurisdictional boundaries of some of the committees” (1986: 304). Frequently, bills would be considered by committees only to find out that their competences lied elsewhere and to remit the bill back to the Speaker to reconsider what the proper committee was. Ultimately, at the time of writing, Opello concluded that “Portugal’s Assembleia da República shows no sign of becoming institutionalized and contributes minimally if at all to the policy outputs” (1986: 313). The decade mediating from the mid-1980s through the mid-1990s marked a watershed in committee influence in Portugal. Indeed, committees were a pivotal part of a general movement towards a more rationalized chamber. MPs pushed for several significant reforms of the Rules of Procedure to maximize their influence in the decision-making process and to gain more power vis-à-vis the executive. Leston-Bandeira (2001) summarized the fundamental changes that took place in the committee system in this decade. First, committees were given privileges not only on the pre-floor stage but also in the after-floor stage. This constituted a significant change in their capacity to influence the legislative process. Second, only in the 1980s, were committees given the possibility to meet at the same time as the Chamber. Considering the constraints that time (or lack thereof) imposes on legislatures, this reform fundamentally changed the internal balance of power between committees and the Chamber. Third, the openness of committees also changed during this time, with publicity to media becoming the rule. As Leston-Bandeira (2001) argues, this fits the overall patterns towards a legislative life revolving around committees at the expense of the Plenum. Moving now to the other case study, committees are also nowadays one of the most relevant decision-making bodies in the Spanish Congreso de los Diputados, even though leaders of parliamentary party groups completely dominate their operation (Caballero Míguez 2007). Similar to what has

Committee systems in Portugal and Spain  77

happened in other democracies, committees within the Spanish Parliament have become more numerous and acquired increasing political relevance over time as a response to the growing need of division of labour (Oñate 2000). Moreover, committees seem to be a very convenient place to facilitate bargaining among political parties given their lower number of people, the comparatively lower publicity of their meetings and the theoretically higher technical preparation of their members. As Fernandes and colleagues (2018) claim, it is remarkable the almost complete lack of analytical studies on the Spanish Parliament and, above all, its committee system. Although committees play a crucial role within legislatures, and Mattson and Strøm (1995) deem the Spanish ones to be relatively powerful regarding drafting authority and agenda control, research on parliamentary committees of this country has primarily adopted a legal approach. Rubio Llorente (1994) offers a good insight into the operations of the committee system in the first 15 years of democracy in Spain. More specifically, this author contends that, despite the proliferation, diversity and increasing role played by the committees in Spain, parliamentary groups and parties’ central office are still the chief decision-makers. Besides, the fact that committees have become micro-parliaments prevents them from emerging as spaces for proper debate and productive work. In the same volume, Arce Janáriz (1994) criticizes the lack of specialization of the members of the committees, their excessive size and the presence of media in their sessions. Although, overall speaking, the Spanish Congreso has played a significant role only in those legislative terms in which there was a minority government (Martínez 2000), the number and relevance of parliamentary committees in this country have steadily increased over time (Oñate 2000). However, this increasing specialization and broadening of the competences of committees go hand in hand with the continuity of some of the main characteristics present in their first decades of operation such as the lack of real effects of inquiry committees (Arévalo Gutiérrez 1994) and the inconsequentiality of delegating legislative powers from the floor to the committees (Ruiz ­Robledo 1994).

Committee systems in Iberian legislatures in the 21st century

Committee structure

Our foray into committee systems in Iberian countries starts with the committee structure. Table 5.1 displays key elements of the committee structure. In Portugal, there are two types of committees: permanent (comissões permanentes) and ad hoc (comissões eventuais). The former has a permanent nature whereby they survive changes in legislative majorities. This type of committees includes, for example, Finance, Foreign Affairs, Defence and Education. The number of committees is not fixed and has ranged between 14 in 1999 and 12 in 2015.3 Ad hoc committees are also standard in the Portuguese legislature. They do not have a predefined role – that is, rules of

Committee systems in Portugal and Spain  79

procedure establish that each new ad hoc committee should take as its first task to define its object and competencies. This is an essential point because political parties need to bargain over the purpose of the committee, which in itself may shape the efficiency and outcome of the committee. Ad hoc committees have gained traction and visibility in legislative life over the past decade (Fernandes 2016). Indeed, parties have found them to be an essential oversight arena and also a good way to gain visibility in topics that matter for the party brand. For example, there have been several ad hoc committees on the banking system, resulting in heightened oversight of the bureaucracy, the executive and regulatory agencies. The size of legislative committees in Portugal varies within legislative terms and over time. From the late 1990s until the mid-2000s, permanent committees’ membership ranged from 21 to 51. Recently, the size of legislative committees decreased, ranging from 17 to 23 nowadays. Another critical dimension to understand committee structure consists in knowing whether jurisdictional arrangements follow the cabinet´s structure. Portuguese committees shadow executive portfolios. To be sure, according to several clerks, there is an effort to maximize the overlap between the committee system and executive portfolios by adapting the former accordingly at the beginning of each term (Fernandes 2013). Typically, this is done via merges or splits in previous committees. Rules of Procedure establish that MPs may be members of multiple committees. However, they can only be effective members of one committee and sit as a substitute in a second committee. Rules give small parties more latitude for they allow them to allocate their MPs to up to three committees. Finally, we turn to subcommittees and working groups. In Portugal, it is possible to create them to heighten efficiency and create a further level of specialization within the committee arena. Moving now to Spain, in this country, there are five types of parliamentary committees: permanent legislative, permanent non-legislative, permanent mixed, non-permanent and inquiry committees. The first three exist in almost every legislative term whereas the other two are created on an ad hoc basis. Permanent legislative committees are in charge of examining and amending if needed the proposed bills before sending them back to the Chamber. The number of permanent legislative committees is not fixed and has ranged between 13 in the term that started in 1996 and 19 in the current term. Hence, there is a clear upwards trend. The list of permanent legislative committees typically includes Constitutional Affairs, Foreign Affairs and Defence, among others. Permanent non-legislative committees are also standard in the Spanish legislature, and their tasks are unrelated to legislative production. Furthermore, permanent mixed committees are bodies that are composed of deputies and senators. They are usually in charge of the relationships between the Parliament and other institutions such as the Spanish Ombudsman or the European Union. Finally, we must refer to the two types of ad hoc committees that exist within the Congreso.

80  Jorge M. Fernandes and Pedro Riera

On the one hand, the assembly may decide to create general non-­ permanent committees to tackle specific issues. The life of these committees expires when their task is over or when the legislative term ends. In contrast to what has happened with the permanent legislative committees, the number of non-permanent committees has not increased in recent years. Finally, inquiry committees are also common, mainly over the last decade. This type of committee usually works as an additional arena of oversight of government performance. For example, in the 12th term (2016-present) there is an inquiry committee about the illegal financial dealings of the Popular Party. The size of parliamentary committees in Spain varies within legislative terms and over time, according to decisions taken by the Parliament’s Presidium at the beginning of each term. Since 1996, permanent legislative committees’ membership has ranged between 37 and 50. Despite the critical changes the party system has undergone over the past few years (see ­Chapter 3), there is no clear trend over time in this dimension. In contrast, another important indicator to understand committee structure, such as the degree of overlap between committees and cabinet portfolios, has changed over time. In the past, committees split or merged at the beginning of each term and every time the organization of the government changed in order to maximize the intersection between the competencies of committees and ministries. This way, the committee system shadowed perfectly the government structure. This pattern started to change slightly in 2008 when the Committee on International Development Cooperation was created. Since then, the number of permanent legislative committees has always been higher than the number of cabinet portfolios and, as a result, in some cases, two committees are in charge of the oversight of one single ministry, even though more often than not the committees’ names still mirror the ministries’ names.4

Concerning the possibility of multiple memberships, the Rules of Procedure of the Congreso allow its members to belong to as many committees as they want. The only limit that exists concerning this point is that the size of the party delegation in permanent legislative committees must be proportional to the total number of MPs of each group. All other MPs who are not part of the party delegation in the committee have the right to attend its meetings and express their opinion, but lack voting rights. On average, each deputy belongs to slightly more than two committees. However, some considerable variation exists not only between groups (membership rates of representatives of small parties are higher) but also within groups (in some parties, a group of MPs become committee specialists and are members of a comparatively higher number of these bodies). Finally, we turn to subcommittees and working groups. In Spain, it is possible to create these to heighten efficiency and add a further level of specialization within the committee arena. In the term that started in 2016, parliamentary groups have considerably resorted to this device, and there are currently seven subcommittees in the Congreso on topics such as electoral reform, party finance, or climate change, among others.

Committee systems in Portugal and Spain  81

Committee procedures

In the Portuguese legislature, political parties are the gatekeepers of committee assignments. There are inter- and intra-party dynamics in the assignment of committees. The latter are not observable in that we do not have access to the internal discussion that takes places within each party about who goes where. Our analysis, thus, needs to be restricted to the inter-party part. The Rules of Procedure establishes that parties have the right to assign MPs to committees – that is, committee members become delegates (agents) of the party leadership (the principal) in the committee arena. Furthermore, parties are also bestowed with the capacity to deselect committee members. Indeed, rules of procedure explicitly posit that parties can remove any MP from any committee should they wish to. Committee assignment in Portugal follows a proportionality rule. However, small parties are protected for and, regardless of their size on the floor, they have the right to have at least one seat in every committee. Chair allocation and selection, a coveted payoff, presents a much more complicated picture. In the Portuguese legislature, rules of procedure establish that each committee should have its presidium, with one chair and at least two vice-chairs. Committee chairs and vice-chairs are elected in the first meeting of the committee, which should be chaired by the Speaker. Formal rules establish that the selection and allocation of presidium positions in committees should follow a proportionality rule. However, the inherent small number of available positions creates a de facto majoritarian distribution of chairs. Chair selection and allocation happen as follows.5 The staff prepares a proposal of chairs allocation following a proportional distribution, using the D’Hondt method to rank parties for the institutional positions. Parties take turns in choosing committee chairs. The party with the most MPs gets to have the first iteration and to choose the most coveted chair. However, there is a tacit agreement between the two largest parties, the PS and the PSD, that the chair of the Finance committee should be reserved from the outset for the main opposition party. Typically, parties need to have, at least, 18 MPs on the floor to enter the race for a committee chair. In practice, this means that the two main parties tend to cartelize committee chairs. However, there is also some latitude for small parties to obtain a chair as payoff of a coalition agreement. For example, in the context of the 2002 coalition government, an MP reports that CDS did not have “the size to hold a committee chair. However, PSD offered us one of their committee chairs, as part of the coalition agreement. We were asked to choose between Agriculture and another committee, and we chose the former”.6 Interestingly, in the Portuguese context, committee chairs have not been used as shadowing mechanisms. Instead, as interviews with MPs from CDS – traditionally the junior party in right-wing ­coalitions – have shown, the party cares more about holding chairs in areas that are important for its brand and to keep an electorally attractive profile.

82  Jorge M. Fernandes and Pedro Riera

Rules of procedure are elusive about the powers of the committee presidium. Existing qualitative research offers a foray into how they operate in practice (Fernandes 2013). Most of their benefits are symbolic. First, committee chairs and vice-chairs operate in a non-partisan mode – that is, they do not work for the benefit of their party. Instead, they need to keep a neutral position in their role of checking times, summoning witnesses and conducting committee work. Second, committee chairs benefit from important media exposure in each jurisdiction. Commonly, they are the party’s face in this arena. Such privilege matters for ambitious MPs whose goal may be, for example, to obtain an executive post on that area in the future. Third, committee chairs sit in restricted parliamentary party group meetings where they can voice their opinion and influence the party’s position. Fourth, committee chairs have a monetary benefit, with a marginal increase of 300 euros in their monthly salary. The extent to which committee work has been open to the public has changed dramatically in Portugal over the past decades. Until the mid-1990s, committee work was virtually absent from public scrutiny. The 1993 reform made publicity the rule, opening the committee arena to media access. The 2000s witnessed a new development with the creation of a state-funded television channel solely devoted to parliamentary broadcast (AR-TV). The openness of committee work changed its nature. According to one of our interviewees,

today one can see that media coverage of committee work, which is an accountability advantage, brought less efficiency, because most of the times we are repeating plenary arguments, not for our committee-peers, but to make sure that the media transmit our party’s opinion.7

This evidence suggests that, while publicity may heighten oversight capacities, it is detrimental to efficiency. MPs are more willing to engage in inter-party consensus behind closed doors. Interestingly, this movement fostered the role of subcommittees and working groups. The focus of consensus has changed to the latter because “they offer purely informative, less institutionalized arenas, and have no records or the presence of journalists”.8

Finally, we turn to the committee stage in the legislative process. In Portugal, committees have the power to examine bill proposals before and after the plenary stage. Committees involvement works as follows. Parliamentary parties put forward their bill proposals to the presidium whose role is to select the committee with a best fitting jurisdiction. Subsequently, in the pre-floor stage, committees make a reading of the bill and issue a report. This offers MPs the opportunity to acquire information on the bill before it reaches the floor and to avoid the possibility of parties voting it without understanding potentially unintended consequences. If the floor passes the bill proposal, then it returns to the committee for further debate and vote. The after-floor stage offers the possibility to engage in more fine-tuned discussions and to exchange benefits. Finally, the bill proposal leaves the committee for a final vote on the floor. Committee assignment also follows a proportionality rule in the Spanish legislature. Each party receives a number of posts in the committees

Committee systems in Portugal and Spain  83

based on their total number of seats, and all parliamentary groups get at least one seat in each committee. Likewise, parties cannot trade positions in committees with other parties. Hence, the partisan composition of Spanish committees perfectly resembles that of the whole Chamber, and there is not a “separation of purpose” (Cox and McCubbins 2001) between them and the floor. For this reason, committees do not work as effective checks on government performance when the party in office has a parliamentary majority. Once the Presidium of the Chamber has calculated the total entitlement of seats per parliamentary group in each committee, the party can decide at will the distribution of these positions among its members. Rules of procedure also establish that parties have the right to remove committee members freely and to appoint new members without restriction in case of resignation, death or new elections. Hence, in contrast to what happens on the floor, the parliamentary group is the “owner” of the seat, and committee members become a mere delegate of their party leadership, always toeing the party line when they vote, in order to keep their post. Second, it is worth to pay attention to the allocation and selection of committee chairs. According to the Rules of Procedure, the presidium of most committees should have one chair, two vice-chairs and two secretaries. All these “mega-seats”, to use Carroll et al.’s (2006) terminology, are elected according to the rules applicable to the election of the Presidium of the Chamber. Hence, nothing is said about who should occupy each of these ­mega-seats that are, as a result, subject to negotiations between party groups. What is the outcome of this inter-party bargaining process? Several patterns emerge in this regard. First, except for the first term after the democratic transition, the largest party always enjoys a clear overrepresentation in the distribution of chairs. Second, if we exclude the first democratic term again, the aforementioned overrepresentation of the largest party tends to become smaller over time. Third, the number of chairs allocated to non-government parties depends on the type of cabinet (majority or minority) in each term. Opposition parties are more likely to obtain committee chairs when they support a minority government. Fourth, a non-written agreement between the groups establishes that the Chair of the Budget Committee should be from an opposition party. Finally, because of being mostly powerless, the partisan composition of committees’ presidia does not follow any clear pattern and can differ from the composition of the Presidium of the Chamber. To sum up, we cannot extract clear insights from the experience registered thus far about whether committee chairs could work as control devices in case of a future coalition government in Spain. Initial conclusions in this regard suggest that, when deciding what chair non-government parties would like to occupy, they care more about the personal skills and ambition of their leader or the political ideology of the party than about anything else. Rules of procedure are vague about the powers of the committees’ presidia. For example, committee chairs are formally in charge of calling its meetings, but two-party groups or one-fifth of its members can force them to do so. Moreover, the committee presidium sets the meetings’ agenda and

84  Jorge M. Fernandes and Pedro Riera

have the final word on the documentation to circulate among the members of the committee. If members of committees’ presidia want to participate in a debate, they must leave their seat to make their speech. Even though the Rules of Procedure confer to each presidium the same powers as the Chamber Presidium’s (e.g., the chair decides on the amount of time allocated to the discussion of each topic), their main powers are above all symbolic. As in the case of Portugal, the members of the committees’ presidia must behave neutrally and cannot operate in a partisan way. Chairs are usually party ­notables – often, former ministers – and the only ones receiving at least some media coverage together with parties’ spokespersons. Overall, the meetings of the committees within the Spanish Congreso are not public. However, media have access to the sessions unless the committee decides by a majority to keep them secret. The 1982 Rules of Procedure effectively confirmed the regulations about media presence introduced in 1977, and since 2000 a publicly funded television channel, Canal Parlamento, broadcasts all public parliamentary meetings. Although one could think that making committee work open to media contributes to increase transparency, it is also true that the continuous presence of journalists in their sessions complicates the possibility of reaching agreements between parties and, above all, leads to an absurd overlap between the Plenum and the committees. Within this framework, inter-party negotiations no longer take place at the committee level but at a lower level (the ponencia), where parties feel free to exchange legislative payoffs between them. Finally, we turn to the committee stage in the legislative process. In Spain, committees have the power to examine bill proposals after, before, or in-­ between the plenary stage. Moreover, in some circumstances, committees have the final word on the bill and can send it directly to the Senate without any intervention by the plenary. However, in most cases committees’ participation in the legislative process proceeds in any of the following three ways. First, when a party group puts forward a global amendment on a bill proposal submitted by the cabinet (enmienda a la totalidad), or the bill proposal is filed by either a party group or at least 15 deputies (toma en consideración), the floor needs to agree on the idea of taking further action on the bill. If it does so, or when no party group submits a global amendment to a bill proposal originating from the government, bill proposals pass to the Presidium of the Chamber, whose role is to select the committee with a best fitting jurisdiction. Once the Presidium has decided in this regard, the selected committee appoints a working group within it (the ponencia) that will draft a report on the bill. The lack of publicity of the meetings of these ponencias helps party groups to reach consensus more easily. The report of the ponencia will then be discussed and voted by the committee and the floor. The members of the ponencia will have a weighted vote in proportion to the seat share of their party groups. This fact together with the allocation of committee seats based on the total size of the party delegation within the chamber makes very likely that the committee and the floor end up agreeing on the text previously issued by the working group. Finally, the bill proposal leaves the Congreso for a final vote at the Senate.

Committee systems in Portugal and Spain  85

Committee powers

Committees in the Portuguese Assembleia da República have an essential role in legislative law-making (see Chapter 10). The said structures have the power to revise bills after their approval in the first reading in the Plenum. Changes introduced at committee stage are still subject to a final vote on the floor (votação final global). In practice, political parties reach consensus in committees about the final version of the bill to be voted upon. Turning to the power to control their timetable, committees in Portugal are entirely independent from the Chamber. We should note, however, that MPs liaise with party leaders to coordinate the balance of work between Chamber and committees. With few exceptions, for example, the fortnightly debate with the prime minister, committee work takes precedent over plenary work. That is, MPs tend to focus on committee work at the expense of plenary work. ­Finally, committees have the power to request documents for their work. Turning now to the powers of the committees in the Spanish Congreso de los Diputados, permanent legislative committees are in charge of assessing the proposed bills and taking the final decision on them in case of full legislative power (competencia legislativa plena). This latter power is automatically delegated from the floor to the committee when the Constitution allows doing so.9 According to Rubio Llorente (1997: 262), one of the most important traits of the Spanish legislative procedure is committees’ high relevance, because they do not only have a massive impact on the Chamber’s decisions, but also often have the last word on proposed bills. More generally, all committees can request the presence of public officials and debate and vote on political statements that push government’s action in one direction. They are also free to set their timetable, even though they are expected to finish their works with regard to each issue within two months. They also have the power to request any document they see as appropriate to conduct their work.

Conclusion

This chapter provides a comparative analysis of committee systems in the Iberian legislatures. Several lessons emerge from this exercise. Importantly, there has been a rationalization of committee work in both countries since the democratization through to the 21st century. Committees are now much more important than they once were compared to the Chamber. For example, both countries witnessed an increase in the number of committees accompanied with a decrease in committee size. Our analysis permits us to draw several conclusions about similarities and dissimilarities between Portugal and Spain. First, the Iberian legislatures differ in their level of effort to make committees shadow portfolios. Portugal has, by and large, a higher matching between committees and portfolios, which empowers the legislative branch. In contrast, in Spain, since 2008, there has been a decrease in the matching between committees and portfolios. Such difference is puzzling in that both

86  Jorge M. Fernandes and Pedro Riera

countries have had a long tradition with minority governments, which tend to be correspond to strong matching committees (Strøm 1990). Second, Portugal and Spain differ in MPs’ incentives to specialize. Whilst in Portugal there are restrictions to multiple membership, in Spain such restrictions do not exist. In the Congreso de los Diputados, MPs tend to sit in two committees on average while in the Assembleia da República MPs sit in only one committee. Moreover, Spanish MPs are unlikely to sit in the same committee in two consecutive terms. Third, over the past few years, committee systems in Portugal and Spain witnessed a similar experience in their relationship with media. There has been a movement towards less business conducted behind closed doors to heighten accountability and curb citizens’ lack of trust in institutions. Both committee systems adapted similarly. Our evidence suggests that subcommittees, which are less visible to the public eye, are now widely used to conduct business that used to be carried out in committees. This enables parties to obtain gains from negotiations and to work in a more consensual fashion. Fourth, both committee systems operate similarly in their allocation procedure. To be sure, political parties are the main gatekeepers, in line with most of their European parliamentary counterparts. Parties have the power to select and deselect members. The latter, in turn, are motivated to deliver benefits to the party as committees are useless for fiscal particularism in Portugal and Spain, and the electoral system provides MPs little incentives to cultivate a personal vote. Our comparative examination of Portuguese and Spanish legislatures suggests that both countries have rationalized and developed their committee systems. Our empirical indicators suggest a fairly balanced picture of both systems. Portuguese committees are more powerful in some areas while Spanish fare better in others. Overall, both committee systems are powerful and can influence policy-making in the legislature.

This research was supported by the Portuguese Science and Technology Foundation (FCT), as part of the “Into the ‘secret garden’ of Portuguese politics: parliamentary candidate selection in Portugal, 1976–2015” project (PTDC/CPO-CPO/30296/2017).

Section III Representation and actors

Introduction1

Political representation has four basic dimensions (Pitkin 1967): descriptive, substantive, formalistic and symbolic. This chapter focuses on the first two, in the context of Portugal and Spain, with a special emphasis on the period before and after the Great Recession. It analyses the levels of resemblance between these countries’ national legislatures and their respective societies in terms of socio-demographic characteristics (gender, age, education and profession), and it identifies levels of voters-elites’ congruence on the leftright ideological scale and how these have changed. But why does this matter? First, because the levels of congruence between MPs and voters, especially in relation to substantive representation, are a key element for the quality of the democratic functioning of any polity: the greater the levels of congruence between voters and policy-makers, the more likely public policies reflect the will of the people (Manin, Stokes, and Przeworski 1999; Wessels 2007). Second, because some level of (sociodemographic) resemblance between voters and their representatives is desirable, at least in certain circumstances and up to a certain point (see Phillips 1995; Mansbridge 1999). A closer resemblance leads to higher social diversity and a better incorporation of usually marginal groups, disparate sensibilities and points of view and different talents. Third, because the Great Recession has had a major impact upon the functioning of the Southern European democracies, namely in Portugal and Spain, and considerably impacted democracy in each polity (Freire and Lisi 2016; Morlino and Sotilotta 2019). The chapter proceeds by first outlining the theoretical framework, where the concepts of descriptive and substantive representation are discussed, to then outline the methodology and data used before analysing the results.

Descriptive and substantive representation

“Descriptive representation” refers to the sociodemographic characteristics of the political elite representing citizens in parliaments (MPs). The issue at

6 Social distance and ideological congruence between MPs and citizens

Iberian Legislatures before and after the Great Recession

André Freire and Xavier Coller

92  André Freire and Xavier Coller

stake here is the extent to which MPs are similar to the citizens who elect them, whether parliaments reflect the social structure of societies with its social cleavages, or, to put it differently, whether parliaments and societies are socially distant. Descriptive representation can be studied from two opposite models of elite configuration: independence and agglutination (Putnam 1976: 21–22). On the one hand, the independence model assumes that every person in a society has the same chances of becoming an MP irrespective of their position in the social structure. In other words, the chance of serving in parliaments should be similar independently of gender, wealth, education or job. Consequently, in this model parliaments would mirror the social cleavages structuring a particular society. On the other hand, the agglutination model assumes that some characteristics are more or less infrequent in society, but that people with political responsibilities tend to agglutinate (e.g. around education, wealth). Thus, a socioeconomically privileged group is usually found in positions of political power, like MPs (Putnam 1976: 22). These two models raise two specific considerations. First, these are theoretical extremes of an elite formation model; consequently, as Weberian ideal types, they do not exist in society in pure form. For instance, no society can be constructed upon the independence model since there are always social biases in conforming a political elite—not all citizens have equal chances of becoming part of the political elite.2 Second, however, these two models are heuristically helpful to approach the characteristics of political elites—irrespective of the country and the historical period analysed, all political elites tend to follow the agglutination rather than the independence model. There are always specific features found to be more frequent among MPs than in society—university education, for instance, or specific professions (e.g. lawyers, professors, liberal professionals). ­Although societies are socially diverse (gender, education, professions, etc.), the consequence of the agglutination model is a more or less internally homogeneous political elite distant from society, or what Wright Mills (1956: 294–295) called ‘an integrated elite’. Coller and ­Santana (2009), for instance, found a high level of homophily among Spanish MPs, especially in regional parties. However, from a historical point of view, the internal composition of representative political elites (MPs) has changed dramatically, reflecting deep social changes. The most historically comprehensive study of political elites in ­Europe, that of Best and Cotta (2000),3 shows that over the years, landed aristocracy and army officials, ‘the notables’, are progressively replaced by middle class (or upper middle class) liberal professionals, university trained politicians that, following Gaxie (2018: 491), tend to become professional politicians. Additionally, women are incorporated into parliaments, although at different intensities according to countries and periods of time (­Santana, ­Aguilar, and Coller 2016). Social changes combined with institutional incentives for

Social distance and ideological congruence  93

consociationism and positive discrimination, as indicated by Norris and Lovenduski (1995: 93–95), mediated by the cognitive framework and actions of selectorates (Coller and Cordero 2018), help to explain growing (although limited) social diversity inside political elites and the institutions they occupy. In this sense, the “rule of distance” between societies and political elites (MPs) formulated by Sartori et al. (1963: 317), is expected to be diminishing, meaning that the distance travelled (and consequently, the effort deployed) by a citizen from disadvantaged origins to become an MP shortens. Powell (2004) divided the research on political representation into two main groups (see also Wessels 200). The first group includes works analysing the relationship between the distribution of the electorate’s preferences for parties in (national) elections, and the distribution of parliamentary seats amongst these parties according to the electoral and party system. The second group follows the path laid out by Miller and Stokes (1963). These authors established a tradition of empirical research in which the underlying assumption is that the congruence between political preferences of elected representatives and those they represent is a key part of the quality of political representation, particularly when the preferences of the elected representatives correspond with the behaviour of MPs in parliament. That is to say, the greater the degree of congruence between the preferences of the electorate and MPs, the greater the probability the electorate will feel to be well represented and the greater the probability MPs (and the government) will act in accordance with the people’s preferences, which is a basic axiom of any representative democracy (Manin, Stokes, and Przeworski 1999). These types of studies are rarely found in Spain: the existing studies comparing MPs’ and voters’ attitudes and values do not usually tap into this issue in terms of ideological or policy preferences (see Méndez-Lago and Martínez 2002; Coller et al. 2018; Sánchez-Ferrer 2018). In the Portuguese case, these studies are more common but need to be updated in the post crisis period (Belchior and Freire 2013; Freire and Belchior 2013; Freire, Tsatsanis, and Lima 2016). Moreover, these Portuguese studies are mainly based on issue/policy representation and not on ideological representation. The congruence model can also be associated with a populist vision of democracy, considering its bottom-up perspective in relation to political representation, which places a great deal of emphasis on the electorate’s preferences and on the need for congruence between the actions of MPs and voters. However, some empirical studies have shown there is also a structuring of policy preferences made from the top (i.e. by the elected representatives and the parties but also high officials, as Genieys 2017 shows) which is a type of ‘representation from above’ (Esaiasson and Holmberg 1996), thus pointing to a more top-down vision of democracy. We consider these two ‘models of democracy’ to be ideal-types, thus we believe they are not wholly incompatible in practice. Considering what we

94  André Freire and Xavier Coller

know from previous studies, there are possibly four scenarios in terms of concurrent voter moves (Esaiasson and Holmberg 1996). First, if the position of MPs moves towards that of voters, and the positions of the latter are either stable or strengthened in the same direction, we can talk about bottom-up representation. Second, if the position of voters moves towards that of MPs, and the positions of the latter are either stable or strengthened in the same direction, we can talk about top-down representation. Third, ‘confluential dynamic representation’ is when both voters and deputies move towards each other’s opinions from different starting points. Finally, ‘disconnecting dynamic representation’, is when voters and deputies move in opposite directions from a similar starting point. Since we are using very limited data, especially in the Spanish case in terms of time span, and since we lack in many previous studies to be able to construct hypotheses (specially for the Spanish case), we consider this an exploratory, tentative and provisional effort to map changes in ideological representation before and after the Great Recession, in Portugal and Spain.

Methods and data

Measuring social distance

Based on previous work (Coller 2008; Coller, Navarro, and Portillo 2016; Coller et al. 2018) we measure how distant parliaments (and MPs) are from their societies and compare this distance on two dimensions—historical and territorial. To allow for a systematic comparison, we use the Index of Social Disproportion (ISD), or Index of Electoral Bias as Norris and Lovenduski (1995: 96) call it.4 The ISD relates to a particular group (women, university trained and so forth) in two populations, namely, MPs and citizens. Its formula is quite intuitive:

ISD = Proportion of a group in Parliament Proportion of the same group in the population

ISD adopts values around 1. We shall take the example of women, although other groups (religious, professional, ethnic, etc.) could be used as well. If the proportion of women in parliament is the same as the proportion of women in society, ISD will be 1 or close to this value. If ISD reaches a value over 1, then that means that there is an overrepresentation of women in Parliament. Conversely, if ISD gets a value below 1, it means there is an underrepresentation of women in Parliament. The level of under or over representation is given by the magnitude of the ISD. In order to allow for a more systematic comparison across time and territories, we use the Synthetic Index of Social Disproportion (SISD), which calculates the level of identity between a population of MPs (pi) and citizens (Pi) on different characteristics (I) weighted by the proportions of

Social distance and ideological congruence  95

those characteristics (gender, age, profession, educational level) in the population (Pi).

SISD can reach values between 0 and 1, with 0 indicating the unlikely case of total dissimilarity between MPs and society and 1 the also unlikely situation of parliament mirroring exactly society’s social structure. Taken together, by comparing periods and countries, we can ascertain how distant parliament is from the society electing its MPs, and how it has changed over time.

Measuring ideological congruence

The Great Recession may have led to changes in the social structure of parliaments, but what changes can we discern in ideological congruence between MPs and voters in Portugal and Spain? Several methodological issues need to be addressed before proceeding with the analysis (­Andeweg 2011; Belchior and Freire 2013). First: what data and measures to use? We use both mass surveys (to measure voters’ ideological orientations) and MP surveys (to measure elite positions; instead of party manifestos, expert surveys or voters’ perceptions) because they are the most appropriate to tackle the dispersion of preferences at each level. Additionally, the choice of left-right self-placement, instead of issue/policy preferences, to measure the levels of congruence between voters and MPs, is dependent on data availability, namely in terms of variables allowing comparison across levels (voters versus representatives), countries and time. Although in some countries left-right self-placement is weakly related with substantive issues (like in Portugal), in general it can be said to encapsulate the major conflicts in a polity, thus functioning as a type of super issue (Freire 2015). A second decision relates to the unit of analysis, the party (in the electorate and in parliament) or the voters in each district and their representative. Since we are dealing with closed list proportional representation systems, where the party is the fundamental agent in the political representation process, we decided to compare party voters (as measured by party vote) and MPs, segmented by parties. A final and third decision relates to the statistical measures of congruence to use. We know that relative measures (i.e. those that take into account the dispersion of preferences among the electorate) are better than absolute measures (means, percentages, etc.) (Andeweg 2011; Belchior and Freire 2013). Here we adopt relative measures that take into account the full distributions at both levels, which are proposed by Golder and Stramski (2010).

96  André Freire and Xavier Coller

Data

For descriptive representation, we use biographic data on MPs and census data on citizens, to compare the levels of resemblance between the social demographic profiles of MPs and the population, before and after the crisis. For comparative purposes we have selected MPs information that coincides with that of the census. The variables compared are age, gender, education and profession. For substantive representation, we rely mainly on elite and mass surveys. For Portugal, similar elite and mass surveys were conducted in 2008 and 2016, that is, before and after the crisis, as part of major international research projects, namely CCS (Comparative Candidates Survey—references included with tables and ). To study ideological congruence between MPs and voters, we use the left-right self-placement of each group, segmented by party; this methodology is also used for Spain. For Spain, we utilise the elite and citizen surveys conducted by the INTUNE project (Cotta, Isernia, and Bellucci 2007a and 2007b), and Coller (2009–2012) (see CIS 2009, 2012). These are complemented by mass surveys with comparable variables (in terms of left-right self-placement) in the relevant time points. More recent elite data is currently unavailable because the CCS—Spain, for the last elections, is still being fielded. Thus, the analysis of post crisis congruence must be undertaken with greater caution because of the timing of the Spanish surveys, which neither include the more recent changes in public and elite opinions, nor the new parties (above all Podemos and Ciudadanos) that entered the parliamentary arena after the crisis.

Social disproportion in Portugal and Spain

It is remarkable how MPs, taken together, have similar sociographic characteristics and how similar their evolution is, although they have different departing points. Table 6.1 illustrates this. Table 6.1 compares MPs and electors in Portugal and Spain at two different moments: around 2001 and around 2011. These two observation points were selected for two reasons. First these are the years in which census data are collected and consequently provide a more accurate picture of how society looks. For the MPs, we have selected the legislatures close to these years, giving priority to the more recent ones. Second, comparing these two moments allows us to analyse the effects of the crisis in the configuration of the political elite and its distance with society. Given the data available, we are limited to four sociodemographic characteristics (gender, age, education and profession) to compare MPs and citizens. In both countries, although the population is divided almost in two halves in terms of gender, the proportion of women among MPs is lower, although in Portugal (20 per cent in 2002, 33 per cent in 2015) they seem to have more problems in accessing parliament than in Spain (29 per cent and 39 per cent, respectively). The proportion of female MPs has increased with differing intensity in each country.

98  André Freire and Xavier Coller

In both countries, the average age of politicians is similar to that of the population, although in Portugal MPs are slightly older than the average citizen. The most common age group is that of MPs over 50 years old, although the presence of young politicians in Spain is noteworthy, with a higher proportion of MPs in the 36–50-year-old group. In both countries, contrary to the population, MPs are characterised by having the highest educational credentials with minor differences at the beginning of the century (83 per cent in Portugal vs 89 per cent in Spain). Ten years later, the proportion of university-trained politicians had increased to the 90 per cent level, as a reflection as well of a significant change in society, where the presence of citizens with university credential had also increased. It is not strange, then, that the most common professions are those associated with the institutionalised cultural capital provided by universities: lawyers, teachers, but also other liberal professions not shown here but grouped under “others” like architects, doctors, etc. By far, the most common profession among MPs is that related to the law (lawyers, judges, prosecutors) in both periods and in both countries, but particularly in Portugal. That of teachers is more common in Spain, but has diminished in recent years. Employees are more prone to enter parliament in Spain than in Portugal, whereas managers represent about a tenth of the MPs, although their presence in Portugal has diminished recently. These changes over the years hide something important: taken together, MPs have become more socially diverse showing that parliaments are more open to society. Whether this is an effect of the crisis or a trend that was intensified by the crisis needs to be explored further. In terms of causing more or less open parliaments, the crisis may have affected Spain more than Portugal since it was only there that new parties whose success is directly related to the effects of the crisis (Podemos and Ciudadanos) have emerged. According to recent studies, these two parties have added more diversity to parliaments (Kakepaki et al. 2018). However, contrary to Spain, it seems that traditional parties in Portugal have opened the electoral lists to citizens involved in protests against the effects of the crisis (Lisi 2018), generating as well renewal and social diversity. We turn now to the analysis of how distant MPs are from society and whether the expected gap closes or widens. As explained above, we employ the Index of Social Disproportion (ISD) to ascertain whether the distortion between parliament and society is large and how it has changed. Table 6.2 shows that men are overly overrepresented in both countries, although less so in Spain than in Portugal. Consequently, women are underrepresented making up half of society. This is so in both periods considered. However, ISD lowers for men and rises for women between 2001 and 2015, which means that, although women are still underrepresented, their presence in the political elite has grown. In comparative terms, Portuguese parties have made a greater effort to incorporate more women into the national parliament, although the Portuguese Parliament is still behind that of the Spanish Congress of Deputies. Therefore, in terms of gender, the gap between MPs and society has narrowed over the years to the point

Social distance and ideological congruence  99

that current MPs in both countries are more socially representative than in 2001. On average, MPs of both countries reflect well the average age of electors in their respective countries. However, when we analyse age cohorts, the picture is slightly different. Table 6.2 shows that while the over 50-year-old MPs are present in parliament in similar proportions as in society, the other two groups behave differently. The younger group (under 36 years old) is clearly underrepresented in both countries and in both periods. However, in Spain the gap marginally closes (the ISD goes towards the 1 pole) while in Portugal it widens (the ISD distances itself from the 1 pole). That is an effect of a lower proportion of MPs in this group age combined with a lower proportion of people in the same group age (perhaps due to migration and a lower birth rate). The intermediate age group (between 36 and 50) is overrepresented in both countries and in both periods. Although in Spain this overrepresentation diminishes over the years and in Portugal it grows slightly, the fact is that this age group is more present in the political elite

than in society. The behaviour is therefore slightly different between the two countries. While in Spain the age gap closes in every age group (except for the 50+ group, where the ISD does not change over the years), in Portugal, the disproportion grows slightly except in the 50+ cohort; a group that reduces the gap with the population. However, taken together with the average age, there is a good fit between MPs and society. One of the most persistent features of any political elite is that its members usually have a disproportionately high level of educational qualifications and, consequently, depart from society on this variable. Table 6.2 shows this is also the case for Portugal and Spain. Except for the 2002 legislature in Portugal, people with only secondary or primary education are underrepresented, contrary to those who have attended university. Overrepresentation of university educated MPs is the highest, although in comparative terms it has declined over the years in both countries. In this respect, parliaments tend to mirror society more in 2015 than in 2001—the educational gap may be closing. We have selected four professional posts for their relevance in political elites and in society. By far, the largest group of people in industrial or even postindustrial societies is that of employees; those employed by others and who cannot be considered professionals or technicians. Among MPs, the most frequent profession is that of lawyers and teachers, although civil servants have also become common lately. Managers, entrepreneurs, owners and associated jobs are also present in modern political elites. Table 6.2 shows the ISD for these four professional groups. Lawyers are clearly overrepresented in the political elite of both countries in both periods, although their weight has declined in Portugal, and is now similar to Spain. With a different magnitude, a similar situation is found among teaching professionals: their presence is greater among MPs (especially on the left, as demonstrated by Coller et al. 2018) vis-à-vis their presence in society, although their weight has also declined slightly. Managers have a more balanced presence, although they were overrepresented among the political elite at the beginning of the century. In Portugal, their relative weight among parliamentarians diminishes whilst it increases in Spain; indicating that the representation of managers, owners and associated jobs is on the rise in Spanish politics. Finally, workers are the professional group most underrepresented. Their presence in society is very large, but they get the lowest ISD of all, and this has not improved over the years. Using data from Table 6.1, we can corroborate the results obtained with the Index of Social Disproportion as shown in Table 6.3. Portugal reaches a value of 0.75 around 2001, while in 2015 (using the 2001 census data for the population) the ISDS rises to 0.76. This means that, although the change may seem small (ISDS varies between 0 and 1), it goes in the direction of resembling society more, rather than the opposite. This means the Portuguese Parliament is gaining some social diversity. A similar situation is found in Spain, albeit more intensely. Comparing both countries in both periods, Spain’s Congreso de los Diputados seems closer to society than Portugal’s Assembleia da República. ISDS in Spain is 0.82 and 0.83.5

Social distance and ideological congruence  101

And yet, it goes in the same direction: that of becoming closer to society over the years. In this case, the incorporation of new parties (Podemos and Ciudadanos) into national institutional politics has added more social diversity (Kakepaki et al. 2018).

the socialists (PS) and the conservatives (CDS-PP) show a decrease in ideological congruence MPs-citizens. However, the two main parties, PS and PSD, remain the more congruent in 2016, as was already the case in 2008 (then joined also by CDS-PP). Most previous research has focused on policy representation rather than less left-right ideological representation (Belchior 2010; Belchior and Freire 2013; Freire and Belchior 2013; Freire, Tsatsanis, and Lima 2016; Freire, Tsatsanis, and Correia 2018), so it is difficult to make comparisons. In terms of left-right ideological representation, previous studies have shown (at least with ‘absolute measures’ of congruence) that the PS and the PSD, often joined by the CDS-PP, are the more congruent parties, and the radical left parties (BE and CDU) the least congruent (See Belchior 2010; Belchior and Freire 2013; Freire and Belchior 2013). However, the picture is different when policy representation is at stake: the left-wing parties are more congruent with voters on socioeconomic issues than the right-wing parties; the opposite is true for libertarian-­authoritarian issues; and both of these patterns were reinforced with the Great Recession (Tsatsanis and Lima 2016; Freire, Tsatsanis, and Correia 2018). These differences might of course be due to the fact that

Figure 6.1  (Continued).

104  André Freire and Xavier Coller

the left-right ideological scale contains both identity and policy components (Freire 2015; Sánchez-Ferrer 2018), whereas in the case of policy representation we are dealing only with the latter. We not turn to the changes in ideological congruence, 2008–2016, in the Portuguese Parliament, taking into account the four scenarios presented above. Figure 6.1 presents the full distributions of left-right self-placement for both MPs (black lines) and voters (grey lines) for the five parties represented in the Portuguese Parliament,6 before and after the Great Recession. The increase in congruence for both the BE and the CDU can be best characterised as ‘confluential dynamic representation’: CDE and BE MPs are less extreme in 2016 than in 2008, while their voters’ preferences became more widespread (on the left). The case of the PSD can be best described as ‘top-down/from above representation’: both MPs and voters are more concentrated around the centre, in 2016 than in 2008, but the move is towards the previous MPs’ mode. In the case of the parties that witnessed a decrease in ideological congruence, the PS and the CDS-PP, are best described as ‘­disconnecting dynamic representation’: less centrist (i.e. more leftist) voters and stable MPs, in the case of the socialists; and more rightist MPs and more centrist voters, in the case of the CDS-PP. Let us move now to Spain, before and after the Great Recession, that is, 2007 and 2011. Table 6.5 provides a synthetic perspective of the levels of ideological congruence (MPs-citizens) for each party between 2007 and 2011. The figures for ‘congruence’ refer to the common areas (pictured in grey in Figure 6.2) in the distribution of left-right ideological preferences of MPs and citizens. In the Spanish Parliament, all four parties exhibited an increase in ideological congruence, between the period prior to the Great Recession (2007) and the middle of it (2011), with the highest among the socialists and the lowest among ERC MPs. The two largest national parties (PSOE and PP) are the more congruent ones, although in 2007 this was not the case for the PSOE (a parliamentary group much to the left of their voters), but Catalan
