nationalists were close behind. As said above, the studies about left-right ideological congruence on Spain are scarce, but the few existing studies reveal a Spanish Parliament that, overall, is biased towards the left (vis-à-vis a more centrist electorate), like in Portugal (Belchior 2010 and 2013). At the party level both the PSOE and IU (United Left, a radical left alliance with former communists within it) are the least congruent parties, and the PP and CiU the more congruent, especially the PP (Belchior 2010). Figure 6.2 shows the full distributions on the left-right self-placement for both MPs (black lines) and voters (grey lines), for the four parties for which we have survey data at both levels, PSOE (socialists), PP (conservatives), CIU (Catalan conservatives) and ERC (Catalan republican left), in the two periods (2007 and 2011), before and after the Great Recession. Taking into account these distributions of ideological preferences, we can conclude the following. The increase of congruence in both the PSOE and ERC can be best characterised as ‘top-down/from above representation’: socialist MPs more concentrated on the left, voters less centrist (overall) and moving towards the previous mode of the MPs; less dispersed ERC voters and moving towards the mode of the (previously) more homogenous and leftist MPs. In the cases of both the PP and the CiU the situation of increased left-right congruence can best be characterised as ‘confluential dynamic representation’: more rightist voters and less centrist MPs, in the case of the PP; more centrist MPs and more dispersed voters, in the case of CiU.

Conclusion

Summarising the findings in relation to the social distance between MPs and citizens, and comparing the two points in time before and after the Great Recession, in both countries, we conclude that in some respects (gender, age, education, profession), with different magnitudes, parliaments are ‘becoming closer’ to societies. The ISDS confirms this analysis. This is nothing but a trend that has been historically observed (Best and Cotta 2000), which is ratified in this analysis despite voices claiming that MPs do not socially represent societies. On the contrary, there is a tendency towards micro-­representation or demographic representation, that is, the gap between parliaments and societies is getting narrower rather than broader. This means that over the years, more social diversity has been added to parliaments, closing the gap with society and making parliament a closer representation of ­society—at least on the variables for which we have enough information to compare. Would it be different if we could compare income levels between MPs and citizens? Or additional factors, like religion or place of birth? These are questions to explore elsewhere. What also remains to be seen in future research, namely with comparisons with more historical depth, is the relative contribution of long-term trends vis-à-vis the Great Recession for the changes we mapped between 2001 and 2015. In terms of substantive representation, our research questions were: first, are there any effects of the Great Recession on the pattern of transformation

Social distance and ideological congruence  107

of votes into seats and, if so, in what direction? The answer is that there are significant effects for both Spain and Portugal. However, in the Spanish case we witnessed both a major change in the party system format (highly volatile elections and a huge increase in party system fragmentation, due to the entrance of two new medium-sized parties, Podemos and Ciudadanos) and a major change in the patterns of government alternation. In the case of Portugal, we witnessed no relevant change in the party system format, but a major change in the patterns of government alternation: for the first time in around 40 years of democracy the left-wing parties were able to cooperate to form a government. Second, in terms of left-right congruence between MPs and voters, are there signs of relevant changes after the Great Recession and, if so, in what direction? The Spanish data is limited because the latest time point is 2011 and the data neither includes Podemos nor Ciudadanos, let alone IU. These limitations notwithstanding, we detected more congruence between 2007 and 2011 for all the four parties analysed (PSOE, PP, ERC and CiU), although for different reasons; and we also found that the largest national parties are those that are more congruent (like in Portugal). The question that can be raised is whether this pattern would still hold in 2016–2018, namely due to the entrance of new parties and internal disarray in the PSOE and the PP. For the Portuguese case, we witnessed a more congruent radical left (BE and CDU) and a more congruent PSD, although for different reasons. In the cases of the PS and the CDS-PP, both less congruent in 2016 than in 2008, the reasons are also different: stable MPs and more leftist voters; more rightist MPs and more centrist voters, respectively. The questions for the future are different in the Portuguese case: will these trends consolidate in the 2019 general elections and what will the consequences be for the parties (considering that the findings for policy representation are significantly different)?

Introduction

The degree of diversity found in legislatures is a crucial dimension against which we can assess the quality of representation in liberal democracies. Existing work on political representation consistently suggests that white older heterosexual fully abled middle-class men still dominate parliaments. Not only are they more numerous, but white males are also the “ideal” or “prototypical” seat-holder; that is, the one that a majority of electors and party selectors have in mind when thinking about political representatives (Purdie-Vaughns and Eibach 2008; Kenny and Verge 2016). The “diversity gap”, – the skewed social composition of legislatures – not only structures how political institutions operate, but it also may also contribute to shaping hegemonic cultural perspectives of race, class, gender, ethnicity, age, ableness and sexuality (Norris and Lovenduski 1995). In this chapter, we deal with the following research question: to what extent do parliamentarians in Iberian legislatures reflect the social diversity in the population at large? Descriptive representation – herein defined as “who the representatives are” – matters for multiple reasons. First, the “diversity gap” in political office leads to a serious democratic deficit with relevant implications for democratic politics (Phillips 1998). While MPs are expected to represent the interests of the society as a whole, what a legislator “stands for” in terms of descriptive representation is connected to what (s)he will “act for” regarding substantive representation (Mansbridge 1999). Research on the political representation of gender and ethnicity has shown that the number of women and ethnic minorities in legislatures influences the likelihood of approval of equality and non-discrimination bills (SchwindtBayer and Mishler 2005; Wüst 2014). Likewise, openly LGBT (lesbian, gay, bisexual and transgender) MPs help to bolster rights for their community and, at the same time, transform the perceptions and voting behaviour of their non-LGBT counterparts (Haider-Markel 2010; Reynolds 2013). On the contrary, the literature suggests that the absence of disabled people from parliaments has created hurdles to shift “the guiding principle of ‘welfare’

7 The diversity gap in the Portuguese and Spanish parliaments

Descriptive representation through an intersectional perspective

Ana Espírito-Santo, Tània Verge and Laura Morales

112  Ana Espírito-Santo et al.

toward the principles of ‘citizenship’ and ‘rights’” (Drake 2001: 423). The chronic underrepresentation of young people also raises issues about their substantive representation in legislatures (IPU 2016). Second, the composition of parliaments evokes particular meanings to citizens (Pitkin 1967). MPs from minority and historically discriminated social groups may bring a symbolic value insofar as their bodily presence increases the legitimacy of, closeness to and satisfaction with political institutions. Furthermore, their presence may also heighten the levels of political efficacy of the citizens belonging to such groups, providing them with role models and signalling that politics is an arena open to their participation (Espírito-Santo and Verge 2017). Existing research on the compositional similarity between the representatives and the represented in Iberian parliaments has mostly focused on gender (Valiente et al. 2004; Meirinho Martins and Pequito Teixeira 2005; Baum and Espírito-Santo 2012; Monteiro 2011; Verge and Espírito-Santo 2016; Verge et al. 2017). The representation of immigrants has benefited from a few studies (Pérez-Nievas et al. 2014; Vintila and Morales 2018). This chapter constitutes the first foray at examining the descriptive representation of social groups that have been traditionally excluded from politics and whose underrepresentation strongly correlates with patterns of socioeconomic injustice and/or lack of recognition. More specifically, it pays attention to the various forms of discrimination that structure privilege and power dynamics in political institutions like gender, age, ethnicity, sexuality and ableness. In doing so, we adopt an intersectional approach – that is, we focus on how gender interacts with other identity markers. Our analysis uses an original biographical dataset of MPs. Our window of observation focuses on two legislative terms, namely the terms 2000–2004 and 2016–2016 for Spain, and the terms 2002–2005 and 2015–2018 for Portugal. Such empirical strategy permits us to investigate the evolution of the descriptive representation of the abovementioned social groups before and after the adoption of statutory gender quotas (2006 in Portugal and 2007 in Spain). Furthermore, it also enables us to assess how political parties – the primary gatekeepers to elected office in parliamentary democracies – have sought (or not) to promote the descriptive representation of each social group. The chapter is structured as follows. First, we review the body of scholarship within which we place our study. Second, we describe the data and methods used. Subsequently, we examine the diversity gap in political representation in the Portuguese and Spanish parliaments. Lastly, we evaluate the degree of inclusiveness of the Iberian legislatures and present our conclusions.

An intersectional approach to the study of the diversity gap in parliaments

The descriptive representation of women has benefited from a burgeoning body of scholarly (see, among many others, Dahlerup and Leyenaar 2013; Paxton

Diversity gap in Portuguese and Spanish parliaments  113

and Hughes 2014). To a less extent, the study of ethnic minorities and immigrants in legislatures has also gained traction (Ruedin 2009; Bird et al. 2011; Wüst 2014). This body of scholarly work points out to an increasing presence of women and a steady poor representation of ethnic minorities and immigrants. By contrast, very little is known about other underprivileged social groups (Lamprinakou et al. 2017). There is a dearth of scholarly work on the political representation of the LGBT community (but see Rayside 1998; ­Haider-Markel 2010; Everitt and Camp 2014). Between 1976 and 2011 only 151 LGBT MPs had been elected to the national assemblies of 27 countries ­(Reynolds 2013: 260). In the case of disabled people, empirical work is especially scarce, and it mostly comes from statutory non-departmental public bodies (FRA 2014), or anecdotal evidence reporting a minimal presence – for example, in the UK parliament, less than 1 per cent of MPs are disabled (Ryan 2017). Recently, a burgeoning strand of research has adopted an intersectional approach by examining the descriptive representation of women from ethnic and racial minorities (Ruedin 2010; Hughes 2013). Such an approach implies looking at the intersection of two markers of identity, which is not a mere addition of various sources of inequality (Crenshaw 1989). Instead, a compounded and reinforced type of inequality may emerge (Collins 2000). An intersectional approach changes the question from “To what degree are women politically empowered” to “Which women are politically empowered?”, that is, “To what degree are women of color, immigrant women, indigenous women, working-class women, and lesbian and bisexual women politically empowered?” (Hughes and Dubrow 2018: 81). Age has been found to intersect with gender in specific ways. The majority of women MPs are in their 40s and 50s, whereas younger and older women are less well represented (IPU 2016: 12). The sexual division of labour that assigns women the lion’s share of care work explains the higher absence of mothers as compared to fathers from the ranks of parliament, as has been found in the British and Spanish cases (Campbell and Childs 2014; Verge et al. 2017). Other studies point out that power dynamics underpin the intersection of age and gender. As noted by Burness (2000: 97) in her study of the Swedish parliament, the promotion of young women signals “something new and vital” but older men hold onto political power. While diversity is formally assured, the “prototypical” men remain in their positions. Regarding ethnicity and migrant origin, studies have shown that in some countries ethnic minority women MPs outnumber ethnic minority men MPs (e.g. Celis et al. 2014; Hughes and Dubrow 2018), but this is not a pattern found across all established democracies. Thus, a “competitive advantage” may develop in specific contexts whereby ethnic minority women are perceived as less threatening to the majority population than ethnic minority men (Celis et al. 2015). Concerning LGBT MPs, while their presence has increased over time, cross-national studies show that very few of them have been from a minority

114  Ana Espírito-Santo et al.

ethnic group. Moreover, openly gay men tend to outnumber lesbians – about three to one (Reynolds 2013). As Juvonen (2016) notes in the German case, openly lesbian politicians receive less media attention and lower approval rates from both the public and their parties than gay politicians, and the former also present higher turnover rates. As for ableness, despite the lack of official statistics on the political representation of women and men with disabilities, gender disparities in education, higher unemployment rates amongst women with disabilities, and the fact that they face disproportionately higher rates of ­gender-based violence and sexual abuse may explain women’s lower political engagement (Schur 2003). Although we know little about how gender and disability interact (Guldvik et al. 2013), female representatives with disabilities may be affected by stereotypes based on both their sex and ability (Connors 2015). Ultimately, the empirical findings on Western liberal democracies suggest that, albeit slow and imperfect, there has been a growth in the inclusion of minorities in legislatures. Our expectation for the Portuguese and Spanish parliaments is that they have witnessed a similarly limited increase in diversity over time. How the different markers of identity analysed here intersect with gender, as well as cross-party variance concerning group representation, is an open empirical question that our original database of MPs allows exploring for the first time in the Iberian legislatures.

Data and methods

Unlike other parliaments, such as the British House of Commons, the Portuguese and Spanish parliaments do not publish official reports on the diversity of MPs. To be sure, in the Spanish case, there is an official report on descriptive representation focusing exclusively on gender. The Spanish gender quota law includes a provision that establishes that the government must produce a report after each election surveying the implementation of the legislated electoral gender quota passed in 2007. The said law mandates that party lists must include a minimum of 40 per cent and a maximum of 60 per cent of either sex, a proportion to be met as well in each stretch of five candidates. Non-compliant lists shall be withdrawn by the electoral authorities (Verge 2012). In Portugal, the Parity Law (2006) established that party lists must include at least a third of candidates of either sex and neither sex shall occupy more than two consecutive positions, with financial penalties being applied to non-compliant parties (Baum and Espírito-Santo 2012). No official reports are published in Portugal as regards the effective implementation of quotas, in any case. To make an empirical appraisal of diversity in Iberian legislatures, we use an original biographical dataset of all MPs who held a seat at some point during two selected legislatures. Data for Spain is an extension of the Pathways project, whereas for Portugal the authors collected their data. We

Diversity gap in Portuguese and Spanish parliaments  115

concentrate on the 2000–2004 and the short 2016–2016 legislative term. Legislative terms for Spain, and the 2002–2005 and the 2015–2018 legislative terms for Portugal.1 Our sampling strategy covers one period before the implementation of the statutory gender quotas and one period after that. Our sample includes 630 biographical records for Portugal and 767 for Spain, some of which correspond to MPs that served in both terms. For most of the variables, we disaggregate the data by political party. For Portugal, we include the Social Democratic Party/Partido Social Demócrata (PSD), the Portuguese Socialist Party/Partido Socialista (PS), the Portuguese Communist Party/Partido Comunista Português (PCP), the Democratic Social Centre/Centro Democrático Social (CDS-PP), the Left Block/ Bloco de Esquerda (BE) and the People, Animals and Nature Party/Pessoas Animais e Natureza (PAN). For Spain, we focus on statewide parties: the Spanish Socialist Workers’ Party/Partido Socialista Obrero Español (PSOE), the Popular Party/Partido Popular (PP), Citizens/Ciudadanos (Cs), United Left/Izquierda Unida (IU) and We Can/Podemos (Ps). Our analysis comes with methodological caveats. First, there are legal constraints that prevent foreign nationals from being eligible candidates for national elections (Oliveira and Carvalhais 2017). Therefore, we follow the definition used by the Pathways project (Morales et al. 2017) whereby we code MPs as having an immigrant background when they have been born abroad with a foreign nationality or either of their parents were born abroad with a foreign nationality. For the Portuguese case, MPs official biographies are not standardised. Moreover, their nationality and that of their parents’ at birth (or even currently) is extremely hard to find.2 We were for the most part only able to collect systematic information about the country of birth, but not the MPs’ original nationality or that of their parents (with the exception of Table 7.1, where we present the first MPs with immigrant backgrounds).3

To measure ethnicity, we turn to two different indicators. First, we measure whether citizens may perceive MPs as belonging to a minority. Trained coders were asked to classify any MP as a ‘visible’ minority member when they could be perceived as such either by their physical appearance in pictures or by their names.4 Second, we classify MPs as being part of an ethnic minority if the MP self-identifies as a member of any ethnic minority. For our purposes, an ethnic group is defined as a group of people whose members identify with each other through a common heritage, often consisting of a common language, a common culture (which can include a religion) and/or a belief in common ancestry (Morales et al. 2017).5

Regarding our LGBT indicator, we only classify MPs if they publicly self-identified as such at the time of their election. Finally, in the case of ableness, only visible or publically self-declared disabilities are considered. In order to identify both LGBT or disabled MPs, trained coders checked for statements relating to sexual orientation and gender identity and watched videos to ascertain any apparent disability for the MPs studied.

116  Ana Espírito-Santo et al.

Diversity representation outcomes in the Portuguese and Spanish parliaments

By and large, the election of the first MP from each social group is a significant event, one that encroaches upon the symbolic domain of politics. That is mainly the case when the marker of identity is visible, when it is intentionally made public, and when it captures media attention. For instance,

Diversity gap in Portuguese and Spanish parliaments  117

the election of Jorge Falcato in 2015, the first disabled MP in Portugal, had a significant impact on the news.6 As did Rita Bosaho Gori’s election in Spain, in 2016, as the first black MP. Table 7.1 shows a relatively similar picture for both countries, with Spain having elected the first MPs of the various diversity groups slightly earlier. Women joined both parliaments for the first time in the 1930s. Both the Portuguese and Spanish lower houses have included ethnic minority MPs since the late 1970s. With regards to immigrant background, in Spain, the first elected MPs had one of the parents (typically, the mother) of foreign – predominantly Western – origin. In Portugal, the first MPs with an immigrant background were born abroad in countries under Portuguese colonial rule (in Goa and Guinea), but to parents with foreign nationality. The inclusion of citizens with a fully migrant origin did not occur until the mid-1990s in Spain, and it has yet to occur in Portugal. Spain also preceded Portugal as regards the election of the first outed gay and lesbian MPs, and only Spain has seen the election of a bisexual MP. No openly transgender or intersex MP has ever served in the Portuguese or Spanish lower houses, but one trans woman (Carla Antonelli, PSOE) was elected in the Madrid regional parliament in 2011, and one trans man (Guillem Montoro, Compromís) became a local councillor in Paiporta (Valencia) in 2018. In turn, the BE included (in a non-winnable position) the first trans candidate in Portugal (a trans woman, Júlia Mendes Pereira) for the 2015 legislative elections. Lastly, Spain elected the first visibly disabled MP in the early 2000s, whereas Portugal took an extra decade to do so. Next, we turn to the analysis of the “diversity gap” in the two legislative periods under examination. Concerning the representation of women, both countries witnessed a vital growth. In Portugal, the percentage of women increased from 19.6 per cent (2002) to 33 per cent (2015). In Spain, there has also been an increase from 28 per cent (2000) to 39 per cent (2016). How have the various political parties performed regarding gender outcomes? Whereas in the Portuguese case statutory quotas have substantially boosted women’s representation, in Spain the legislated quota has only had a substantial impact on those parties not using already voluntary party quotas (Verge and Espírito-Santo 2016). As Table 7.2 shows, women’s presence increased across almost all political parties between the two periods. In ­Portugal, the introduction of the gender quota law blurred the difference between the two largest parties (PS and PSD). By contrast, in Spain, the PSOE keeps surpassing the PP. In the most recent legislature under examination, Ps and the PCP (in Spain and Portugal, respectively) outperform all other parties. Similar to most countries, in Iberian legislatures, there is an overwhelming tendency for parliamentarians to be middle-aged. In Portugal, MPs’ average age was 46.1 years at the time of their election in the 2002–2005 term and 47.6 at the start of the 2015–2018 term, as can be seen in Table 7.3. Around 60 per cent of MPs are between 41 and 60 years old. The situation in Spain is relatively similar, with an average age of 46.7 years at the time of the election for the 2000–2004 term, and of 48.5 at the beginning of the 2016–2016 term. In order to examine the intersection of gender and age,

Diversity gap in Portuguese and Spanish parliaments  119

Table 7.3 also includes the percentage of women and men MPs by age cohort. Whereas the Portuguese case reveals the absence of any clear pattern, in Spain we can see that women are more likely to be younger than male MPs (especially from the 18–30 group) and less likely to be from the cohorts above 51 years. The emergence of new parties has failed yet to rejuvenate the Spanish Parliament, although MPs from Cs and, mainly, from Ps are relatively younger than MPs from the PSOE and the PP, as Table 7.4 shows. In Portugal, whereas in the 2002–2005 term two radical left-wing parties, namely the BE and the PEV, had significantly younger parliamentarians (average below 40 years), in the most recent legislature age differences across parties have vanished. Regarding immigrant background, in the two Portuguese legislative terms under examination over 20 MPs were not born in the country, representing 6 per cent in 2002–2005 and 8 per cent in 2015–2018, as reported in Table 7.5. Portuguese ex-colonies dominate the countries where those MPs were born: half of them were born in Angola and 23 per cent in Mozambique. However, most of these MPs cannot be considered of “immigrant” background insofar as they were probably born with Portuguese nationality, possibly with at least one of the parents born abroad with a foreign nationality. In Spain, four MPs of immigrant origin have been identified in both legislative terms, which represent approximately 1 per cent of the total pool of MPs. Most of them (three out of four of them for the 2000–2004 term, and two out of four for the 2016–2016 term) were born in Spain to one parent of foreign origin. Over the two legislative terms, only two MPs in total were first-generation migrants. For all the rapid growth of immigrant

population in Spain, which reaches around 20 per cent when the children of first-­generation immigrants are included, such low numbers have remained stable over time (Vintila and Morales 2018). The figures increase for MPs born abroad (ten in the 2000–2004 term and eight in the 2016–2016 term), mostly the children of Spanish emigrants or individuals who just happened to be born abroad to Spanish parents. Turning to ethnicity, in Portugal, of the 604 MPs of our sample, ten are likely to be perceived by citizens as belonging to an ethnic minority either by their physical appearance or by their names (Identifiable minority; six in the first legislative term analysed and four in the second one). However, only six self-identify as belonging to an ethnic minority (Ethnic ID – see Appendix for more details). The two groups do not entirely overlap. Only three of those ten who are perceived as belonging to an ethnic minority self-identify as such, whereas three MPs do not have visible traits revealing their belonging to an ethnic minority and yet they consider themselves to belong to one. Diversity gap in Portuguese and Spanish parliaments  121

In Spain, only two MPs (less than 1 per cent) of the 2016–2016 term are likely to be perceived by voters as belonging to an ethnic minority, but they do not self-identify as such in their public statements. With regards to intersectionality, while both men and women with immigrant or ethnic minority backgrounds are massively under-represented, we observe a higher presence of men (in absolute numbers) from these groups in both legislatures and the two terms under examination. Both in Portugal and Spain, there is no significant difference between left-wing and right-wing parties in their likelihood to include candidates of immigrant (or foreign birth) origin, as reported in Table 7.6, for Portugal, and Table 7.7, for Spain. The performance is equally poor across the board, and no party stands out as “immigrant-candidates friendly”, despite relevant differences in their positions on the issues of migration flow controls and immigrant integration (cf. Carvalho 2009). The same can be said for ethnicity. In both countries, party size emerges as the strongest predictor for including MPs from an immigrant background or ethnic minorities. As for the inclusion of LGBT people, country differences are remarkable, despite it being a recent phenomenon in both parliaments, as already mentioned. In Spain, for the 2016–2016 term, six openly LGBT MPs (three men and three women) were elected (three from the PSOE and three from the radical-left coalition integrated by IU and Ps), constituting nearly 2 per cent of MPs. In Portugal, just one openly gay MP (from the PS) competed and got elected at the 2015 election. Furthermore, while no differences were found between the two legislative periods in the categories of age, immigration background and (partially) ethnic minority, in the case of sexual orientation both countries went from zero representatives in the first legislative period to some occurrences in the second one. The small-N problem does not enable us to draw definite conclusions about political parties and the intersection of gender with sexuality. However, it should be noted that all openly LGBT MPs were elected with leftwing parties. Similarly, the fact that in both countries men were the first ones to come out – combined with the higher number of gay politicians – found in other institutions as compared to lesbian politicians suggests that it might be easier for men than for women to advance their political careers as openly homosexuals. Finally, in the case of ableness, Spain emerges again as a slightly more inclusive case than Portugal. In Spain, in the two legislative terms under examination, there were no visibly disabled MPs, but there have been at least three visibly disabled MPs in previous legislative terms since 2004 (all of men and from the PP). In Portugal, the only disabled MP, also a man (from the BE), serves in the current legislature. Although the PS included a blind female candidate in a winnable position in the 2015 elections, she remained one position away from entering the parliament. Overall, an intersection between gender and ableness seems to emerge, although the low number of cases calls for caution in drawing any firm conclusion.

Diversity gap in Portuguese and Spanish parliaments  123

Conclusion

Using an original dataset, this chapter has examined the diversity gap in the Iberian legislatures. The evaluation of the diversity outcomes achieved by the Portuguese and Spanish parliaments yields a mixed picture. ­Women’s descriptive representation has significantly increased over the last ­decade. In September 2018, Spain ranked fifth among European parliaments whereas Portugal ranked twelfth (IPU 2018) regarding the percentage of women representatives. Similar to other European parliaments, the other minority groups remain remarkably underrepresented, although Spain has been slightly faster at including the first MPs of traditionally excluded groups. Whereas Portugal outperforms Spain as regards the inclusion of MPs from ethnic minorities, Spain outperforms Portugal in the representation of LGBT and disabled MPs. Overall, none of the examined groups reaches a representational level that mirrors their numerical presence in society. The social group for which descriptive representation has grown the most between the early 2000s and the late 2010s is LGBT people. While some cross-party differences have been observed, all political parties contribute to the maintenance of a remarkable diversity gap in Iberian legislatures. The tendency for left-wing parties to elect more women representatives than right-wing parties in both countries has not thus far led to significant gains for other underrepresented groups, except for LGBT MPs and the inclusion of younger MPs – in the latter case only by the newest radical left parties. Regarding how gender may intersect with other markers of identity, although the small-N problem underpinning our analysis prevents us from drawing firm conclusions, our results suggest that gender produces specific effects on the representation of the various social groups. In the case of youth, we see that women MPs tend to be younger (only in Spain) than men MPs. In the case of other social groups, men are more present in the Portuguese and Spanish parliaments than women. Ethnic minority and immigrant background male MPs outnumber female MPs with similar characteristics. Likewise, gay men are more likely to obtain representation than lesbians. For their part, disabled women have not yet been present in the Iberian legislatures. This initial foray on the diversity gap in the Iberian legislatures opens several avenues for further research. On the one hand, since political parties are the gatekeepers to elected office in both countries, scholars should explore the diversity strategies they develop to increase the presence of underrepresented groups, looking at both participatory structures (sub-party sections, working groups, loose networks or collateral organisations) and equality guarantees (quotas or representation goals). On the other hand, the measures adopted by the Portuguese and Spanish parliaments to become ­gender-sensitive institutions, to promote the access of minority-group MPs, and to guarantee non-discrimination during their tenure should be examined.

Introduction

Parliamentary institutions are the main institutional arena of politics, and thus a core setting for MPs and interest groups to communicate their policy preferences. Simultaneously, interest groups or civic organisations aggregate citizens’ preferences and channel these into the parliamentary arena, mainly through contact with MPs. This interaction is of the utmost importance for political representation, not only because it fosters legitimacy, but also because it allows for policy responsiveness, accountability and democratic satisfaction. Following the literature (Berry 1997; Norton 1999; Jordan, Halpin, and Maloney 2004; Beyers, Eising, and Maloney 2008; Baroni et al. 2014; Gava et al. 2017), interest groups are understood as those organisations that have as primary aim to represent their members, who share a common interest or identity and that seek to influence policy decision-­ making. Moreover, they do not contest elections or assume responsibility for government. Interest groups include the so-called ‘usual suspects’ (unions, business organisations and other sectional groups), but also other types of civil society organisations (for instance, NGOs and religious groups), which attempt to influence policy to further their causes. Recent analysis on the interaction between organised interests and legislatures have highlighted the important role played by groups in terms of information for both parliamentary committees and political parties (Olson and Mezey 1991; Norton 1999; Hall and Deardorff 2006; Chalmers 2011). While parliaments may need interest groups for information, advice, cooperation and legitimacy, organised interests need institutional contacts to influence public policies and to defend their interests. In addition to information, there are also other exchanged goods such as votes or more symbolic benefits such as legitimation of policy decision-making (Bouwen 2002; Klüver 2012). Case studies have provided valuable evidence about the exchange between interest groups and other political actors (e.g. parties) or institutions, both formally and informally (e.g. through clientelist practices).1

Although legislative studies have grown in both Portugal and Spain (e.g. Liebert and Cotta 1990; Capo 2003; Leston-Bandeira 2005; Mújica and

8 Interest groups in the Iberian parliaments

Marco Lisi and Luz Muñoz Marquez

Interest groups in the Iberian parliaments  131

Sánchez Cuenca 2006; Palau and Muñoz Marquez 2018), there has not been a systematic comparison of the role interest groups play within legislatures. Interest groups and parliaments have been generally examined separately and little attention has been paid to this particular interaction, or it has been limited to a specific parliamentary activity such as hearings (Chaqués Bonafont and Muñoz 2016). The goal of this chapter is to examine the role of interest groups in the Iberian legislatures, paying attention particularly to how the institutional context has shaped this relationship, its evolution over the democratic period and the present debate on interest groups and transparency. The empirical analysis relies on primary and secondary sources, such as official documents, legislation and newspaper articles. We also collected and coded data on interest groups participation in parliamentary hearings. We created a unique database on interest groups hearings in both Iberian countries, following the same coding scheme and methodology to allow comparability.2 Both datasets contain information about the type of group participating in the hearing and the policy issue under discussion, as well as the parliamentary committee and the legislature. In addition, we conducted semi-structured elite interviews with MPs in Portugal and Spain, using a common questionnaire on the interactions between organised interests and parliament.3 The time span focuses on the most recent period, since 2000s onwards, although we also contextualise the historical development of organised interests during the whole democratic period. The following section will first address the institutional context framing the action of interest groups in their respective country. We concentrate in particular on parliamentary rules, the organisation of committees and the policy-making process in order to elucidate incentives and constraints for the action of interest groups. We then discuss the extent to which distinct types of interest groups mobilise and their interactions with MPs. The fourth section discusses recent trends in the participation of interest groups in the policy-making process. Finally, the conclusion summarises the main findings, highlighting the differences and similarities between the two countries and specific issues for future research.

Historical and contextual background

This section briefly outlines the main features of the Iberian legislatures and the rules that recognise the legitimacy of interest groups to interact with them. Broadly speaking, two main aspects distinguish the two parliaments in terms of the opportunities provided to groups to intervene in this institutional arena. The first is related to the semi-presidential nature of the ­Portuguese political system, establishing the direct election of the head of state, who performs an important role in appointing and dismissing the government. This is particularly important, because sometimes the president may act as a significant player voicing the preferences of specific organisations,

132  Marco Lisi and Luz Muñoz Marquez

such as the main economic actors (Confederation of ­Portuguese Employers, banks, trade unions), institutional or cause groups. The second is the decentralised nature of the Spanish state. A gradual decentralisation of political and administrative functions affecting the whole of the state has occurred in Spain since 1977, in a process considered by some authors as of ‘federalisation’ (Moreno 2001). The creation of the Autonomous Communities has led to a major transformation of the territorial power in Spain, devolving key policy areas to the regional level. Consequently, interest groups have to interact with both national and regional levels of governance. Indeed, some interest groups have adapted their structure to the new scenario (Medina 2016). Nonetheless there are also important similarities: political parties (and parliamentary groups) are a core element of the political system in both countries in terms of political recruitment, coordination within the legislature and delimitation of executive-legislative relations. Overall, Portugal and Spain fit the party government model, whereby office holders are influenced by values and policies derived from the party and are accountable to political parties. In Spain there has also been a trend towards the formation of stable, single-party governments and the overrepresentation of large parties, while in Portugal coalition or minority governments have been more frequent. This is relevant in terms of interaction with interest groups, as during majority governments these organisations are more likely to contact government parties, whereas during minority governments they have more incentives to interact with opposition parties.4

Pasquino (1995) claimed that these new democracies are characterised by the ascendancy of the executive branch over the legislature, thus fostering decisional efficacy to the detriment of representative goals. However, it should be noted that since then the powers attributed to parliamentary committees have increased, while mass media and public opinion have boosted their capacity to act as watchdogs of politicians, political parties and representative institutions. Both countries show significant changes in the role of parliamentary committees. In Portugal, during democratic consolidation, the Chamber was considered the major actor within the legislature, with key functions in terms of decision-making, executive control and agenda setting ­(Leston-Bandeira 2002). In addition, as several studies have shown (Opello 1988; Lobo 2005), policy outputs depended mainly on the executive rather than parliament, which offered few incentives to interest groups for accessing and influencing decision-making process. Things have changed since the mid-1980s, when committees emerged as key actors in policy-making and gained significant powers. The work of committees has also become increasingly open to the public and easily scrutinised by the media, thus favouring the mobilisation of interest groups. For instance, this change was further strengthened in Portugal by the new parliamentary rules approved in 2007, which established the general rule of

Interest groups in the Iberian parliaments  133

publicity for parliamentary hearings (Filipe 2009). The process of European integration has also enhanced the role of committees, especially for those dealing with EU issues or institutions (Fernandes 2015). In the case of Spain, previous analyses show that the parliament has a reactive role (Capo 2003), losing its hegemonic position following the period of democratic consolidation, and becoming controlled by the government (Chaqués Bonafont, Palau, and Baumgartner 2015). However, parliament remains an interesting target for interest groups because issues can get into the parliamentary and public opinion agenda through parliamentary ­activities – particularly policy formulation and monitorisation – and more importantly because the variations in the type of government open opportunities to influence policy decision-making (Chaqués Bonafont and Muñoz 2016). Overall, the Chamber has more prominence during minority governments, with parliamentary committees becoming more important during periods of majority government (Sánchez de Dios 1999). These institutional factors are significant in order to understand interest groups’ access to influence legislation. Since 1982 the Rules of Procedure set that committees and the Chamber should meet in public. Moreover, similar to Portugal, parliament has increasingly opened up to public scrutiny thanks to the role of the media and specifically social media, giving more incentives to interest groups to raise specific issues to parliament. In addition to these legislatures’ general features, it is also important to identify the participatory rights recognised by the Constitution to the organised civil society in both countries and the specific characteristics of parliamentary functioning. Constitutional rights of association and political participation are a key mechanism to legitimise (and institutionalise) interactions with interest groups in both countries. An example of the importance of this regulatory framework is the creation in both countries of the Economic and Social Council (CES), a governmental consultative body on labour and macroeconomic issues, created in the 1980s in Spain and in 1992 in Portugal. As the relationship between employers and workers is transversal to most economic sectors, this is an area in which cooperation between government and socio-economic interests (e.g. workers’ organisations, economic activities, families, autonomic regions and local institutions) is highly formalised. In both countries, rights of participation and audience have been institutionalised over time through secondary regulation that grants a more stable status to certain groups through their participation in advisory councils. Constitutional rights also recognise interest groups’ right of petition and, importantly the principle of participation in public affairs, that expressly refers to participation by certain groups.5 In addition, both Iberian Constitutions recognise the rights of participation and consultation of trade unions and employers, as well as consumer groups, youth associations and professional associations.6

134  Marco Lisi and Luz Muñoz Marquez

The Portuguese Constitution deserves special attention to the legacy of the 25 April Revolution, patent in Title III of the Constitution (‘Economic, social and cultural rights and obligations’), which acknowledges the positive role of a broad range of civil society associations. The most important role is attributed to trade unions and labour associations, whose participation in the decision-making process is mandatory for any matters concerning labour legislation and the elaboration of economic and social plans (art. 56). Likewise, agricultural associations are also mentioned as key players in this policy area. Overall, these constitutional provisions confirm the importance of the ‘25 April Revolution gains’ characterised by an intense mobilisation of civil society and the emergence of new social movements (Fernandes and Branco 2017).7 The Constitution’s leftist orientation is also confirmed by the fact that there are no references to employers’ organisations as collective actors. The fact that the Constitution did not recognise business associations any role in the Portuguese political system is also congruent to the overall principle of the subordination of the economic power to the political power. In both Iberian countries, the establishment of democracy meant an explosion of pluralism and the emergence of new dynamics of civil society through an increasing diversification of organised interests (e.g. Linz 1981; Branco et al. 2012). However, during the period of democratic consolidation, the interaction between interest groups and political parties in the two Iberian countries remained rather weak, concentrated in few groups and mostly based on ideological grounds and partisan alignments (Linz 1981; Morlino 1998). Institutionalised links have been established between leftist parties and trade unions, but also in this case the evidence suggests a weakening of these ties over the last decades (Barberà, Barrio, and Rodríguez-Teruel 2018; Razzuoli and Raimundo 2018). Yet, parties have targeted interest groups in order to gain political or electoral support and to strengthen their penetration into civil society (Jalali, Silva, and Silva 2012; Klüver 2012; Verge 2012). By the same token, further MP specialisation together with changing interest groups’ strategies and professionalisation in the last decades have led to a more pluralist lobbying relationship between them (Gava et al. 2017). In other words, interest groups’ lobby efforts no longer focus on government only, they also seek more direct interaction with MPs and parliamentary groups. In this respect, an important aspect seems to distinguish the two cases. Relying on the analysis of post-revolutionary politics in Portugal, Fishman (2011) argued that the legacy of the democratic transition made institutional power holders – particularly elected representatives – highly permeable to influence emanating from protest or other forms of collective action. He provided examples of informal meetings or contacts between MPs or government officials and representatives of interest groups and the broader system of civil society organisations, which have influenced the policy-making process. According to this argument, the social revolution that occurred in

Interest groups in the Iberian parliaments  135

Portugal was responsible for strengthening the impact the mobilisation of collective actors has on representative institutions, empowering ‘outsider’ actors and informal channels of interaction (see also Fernandes 2014).

The role of interest groups within Iberian parliaments

This section examines how the new democratic Iberian legislatures provide interest groups channels to access parliamentary institutions and opportunities to interact. In general, interest groups have three main access points to parliament: committees, parliamentary groups (as a whole) and individual MPs. The access points may be reached by using two distinct types of instruments:

1 Informal channels: the first type of group-parliament linkage is connection with MPs, which may develop through individual ties based on the deputies’ work representing their districts (or specific issues), or due to MPs’ belonging to a specific parliamentary group, thus mediating the relationship between parties and legislatures. Interest groups interact with MPs for different reasons. In particular, this interaction aims (a) to preserve contacts created during the electoral campaign and to remind MPs and parliamentary groups about their promises; (b) to monitor activities and to obtain better and clearer information about public policy; and also (c) to try to get issues into the political agenda and to have a voice in the final policy decisions through bill amendments. As parliaments are an open forum for agenda setting (Andeweg and Nijzink 1995), MPs may ally with specific groups with the aim to draw public opinion and media attention to their (common) political goals in order to shape the public agenda. 2 Formal channels. This interaction is regulated by parliamentary rules and is conducted mainly through committees; specifically, interest groups participate in hearings discussing bills or public policies. Through hearings, interest groups expose their preferences on specific matters. This participation is open in Portugal, where interest groups can ask to submit evidence in Parliament, whereas in Spain this is more closed, with access to hearings being controlled by parliamentary groups. In Portugal, MPs also devote one day (on Mondays) for contacts with their district (art. 57 of Rules of Procedures), with organised interests being particularly effective in mobilising to voice their grievances (especially territorially based issues, Interviews 2 and 3). In addition, in both countries organised interests can also present collective petitions.8

Both channels (formal and informal) can be used to provide information, for instance, through the submission of scientific reports, dossiers or other documents, as well as to convince parliamentary groups support their policy preferences. Table 8.1 summarises the means interest groups may use to

136  Marco Lisi and Luz Muñoz Marquez

access parliaments. In the empirical analysis that follows, we focus mainly on formal instruments, while providing some examples of how informal links work on the basis of interviews. Individual contact with MPs is mainly conducted through informal channels, such as direct contact (meetings or emails) or other interactions in the context of conferences and seminars. In the words of an MP interviewed this is a ‘spontaneous way to interact, less formalised and more based in everyday life parliamentary activity’ (Interview 6). The interviews show some agreement between MPs in considering informal interactions with interest groups as a general practice. For instance, an interviewee stated that MPs receive ‘everyday emails from interest groups (teachers, trade unions, disabled people, ecologists, etc.) asking for meetings to introduce themselves, their objectives, and their point of view about a particular policy especially at the beginning of the legislature’ (Interview 8). Also, there is a general agreement that interest groups target mainly MPs specialised in their issue or who belong to a specific parliamentary committee dealing with a particular policy of their concern (Interviews 1 and 8). For instance, by convincing a parliamentary group to submit a written question, interest groups may obtain information that otherwise would be difficult to get, because government is obliged to answer parliament (Interviews 5 and 6). Moreover, oral questions proposed by interest groups to parliamentary groups are a channel to try to get an issue into the media and the political agenda (Chaqués Bonafont, Palau, and Baumgartner 2015). Looking more systematically at this interaction, we ask the extent to which some groups are more likely to get access to the legislature than others. To answer this question, we use as indicator the number of hearings in which interest groups participate, looking at the percentage by type of groups and the differences between both countries. Interest groups are invited to appear in parliamentary hearings for policy implementation or bill discussions. To complete this picture, we also analyse the policy issues discussed in those hearings to illustrate if there are differences across distinct domains in relation to the type of groups that access parliament. We also examine the importance of petitions as a second indicator of formal interactions.

Table 8.1  Channels of interaction between interest groups and parliament

Formal Informal

Meetings with MPs (Portugal) Conferences, seminars, etc. Hearings in committees Ad hoc meetings (arranged by interest groups or MPs) Petitions Interest groups reports and information delivery to MPs Interest groups’ reports and information delivery request by MPs Letters, email, workshops and external debate forums

Source: Portuguese and Spanish Parliaments. Own elaboration.

Interest groups in the Iberian parliaments  137

In the Portuguese Parliament we need to distinguish between two types of hearings. The first are hearings by request, which take place when one actor asks the committee to be heard on a specific matter. The second type of hearing is when MPs or parliamentary groups call specific actors to attend committee meetings. In contrast, in Spain, interest groups hearings only happen when a parliamentary committee asks them to participate. Interest groups hearings are organised by non-permanent committees and permanent committees, which may also have legislative power (Comisiones Permanentes Legislativas). Interest group appearance is mandatory only when they are requested by the so-called investigation committees (Comisión de Investigación), otherwise they can accept or reject parliament’s request to attend a hearing. Our data show that most of the hearings relate to domestic public actors, for which consultation by committees is often required by law. In the case of Portugal, this kind of linkage has remained stable in the 21st century, ranging from 68.6 per cent in the 10th legislature (2005–2009) to 66.4 per cent in the 12th legislature (2011–2015). As far as Spain is concerned, during the three legislatures analysed (2004–2015) more than 75 per cent were government members and public official hearings, while experts and interest groups account for slightly more than 20 per cent. This is plausible insofar as this is one of the main scrutiny devices used by Parliament to obtain information and to make institutional and public actors accountable to the representative body. In the same manner, very often committees must conduct mandatory hearings with institutional actors on key policies, such as local administration, privatisations or other major reforms. In examining the different types of interest groups in Spain, NGOs are the main category (between 40 and 60 per cent of all groups), followed by business and professional associations. The high percentage for NGOs in Spain – substantially higher than in Portugal, see below – responds, to a great extent, to the politicisation of rights issues during the first and second legislatures of the socialist Zapatero (2004–2011), in which different bills on women’s rights and same sex marriage were discussed. NGOs were also invited to discuss the elderly protection and care bill. However, these figures are not due just to the socialist policy preferences, as during the Rajoy first legislature (2011–2015), NGOs are also the main category. This is not completely surprising as other European cases show the same trend (Binderkrantz, Christiansen, and Pedersen 2015). Overall, Spain is approaching a more pluralist model in the parliamentary arena, in line with other European countries such as Belgium or Switzerland (Binderkrantz, Christiansen, and Pedersen 2015; Gava et al. 2017). In particular, while there is a growing number of interest groups in the welfare domain – such as health, education and social policy – a neo-corporatist model seems to consolidate in economic and labour issues, in which the usual suspects (unions and business organisations) are the most common participants. According to similar data collected for the Portuguese case, during the 2005–2009 period trade unions displayed the highest proportion of

138  Marco Lisi and Luz Muñoz Marquez

appearances in hearings (39.1 per cent), but then the percentage declined to roughly 23 per cent. Business organisations have shown an opposite trend, ranging from 8.9 per cent (between 2005 and 2009) to 34.1 (between 2011 and 2015). NGOs are equally important and feature a relevant proportion of hearings (between 36.1 and 47.4 per cent). Finally, the visibility of professional organisations seems to have declined during the period analysed, falling from 14.9 to 6.3 per cent. If we analyse in more detail the type of issues addressed, in Portugal the committees with more hearings are Finance, Education, Constitutional matters, Economy and Labour, accounting for more than 50 per cent of the total number (Table 8.2). These results may be associated to the relative strong mobilisation power of the main trade unions (CGTP and UGT) and their links with political allies (i.e. leftist parties).9 In Spain the committees of social issues are also the ones that have more hearings with interest groups (Education and Culture, Health and Social Policy, see percentages in Table 8.3). In addition, the two countries also display similar figures in terms economic issues, which amount, on average, to approximately one fifth of the total number of hearings. However, important differences emerge with regard to European matters, both parliaments have a relevant EU affairs committee, but in the case of Spain their number of hearings is very low and no interest groups participated

during the period analysed. The same happens for justice in the Spanish Parliament. Finally, in both countries, interest groups are almost never heard in the Defence or the Foreign Affairs and Foreign Aid Committees. There are also important differences over time worthy of examination. In both countries, the Constitutional, Rights and Liberties Committee held an important number of hearings during the first legislature under analysis, but it shows a significant decline in the following period (see Tables 8.2 and 8.3). The same pattern can be seen in the agriculture and environment areas in Spain. In contrast, in both Iberian countries the number of hearings in the industry, trade and tourism shows a significant increase over time, as well as for budget and financial issues. The policy issues in which interest groups are involved depend greatly on the politicisation of the legislative agenda. MPs’ interviews highlight the important mobilisation of interest groups on issues such as energy and civil construction when they are discussed in parliament. There are also other issues that are highly politicised such as agriculture, fishing, patient rights and, particularly in Spain, other issues such as terrorism or accident victims in which the government is made responsible. Professional associations are also highly mobilised to defend the position of their profession, like the Engineer’s professional associations (Interview 4). On the other hand, it is interesting to note that in both cases there has been stability in the overall number of hearings over the last legislatures. This means that the crisis did not alter the intensity of interactions between interest groups and committees, which can also be considered as an indicator of the stabilisation of parliamentary activities. Yet, it should be noted that in Portugal the average number of hearings reached a peak

140  Marco Lisi and Luz Muñoz Marquez

during the crisis, but it is still too early to assess whether this was a conjunctural increase or the beginning of a more long-term trend. It is plausible also to expect that interest groups mobilise more when a new political cycle begins, that is, when the majority shifts from one party (or ideological block) to another. However, our results do not confirm this expectation, as there is relative stability over each legislative term with regard to the frequency of meetings.10 Another way to read these tables is in terms of the majority and minority governments dynamics. In the case of Spain, we can see that the number of hearings is stable during the Zapatero minority government legislatures (2004–2008 and 2008–2011), while there is an important decrease (almost 25 per cent) during the first Rajoy’ government (2011–2015), a majority one. As Chaqués Bonafont and Muñoz (2016) have argued, majority governments and their parliamentary groups do not provide incentives to discuss bills or promote policy evaluation through hearings. Unless the policy issue generates strong confrontation then interest groups will be invited to enhance decision-making legitimation. Finally, another common instrument that interest groups may use for interacting with parliaments in both countries is the use of petitions. According to recent data on the Portuguese case (Tibúrcio 2018), the overall number of petitions has significantly increased, especially since 2005 when a major reform allowed campaigners to submit e-petitions. However, this increase was due mainly to the explosion of individual petitions, which represent since 2000s, on average, more than 60 per cent of the petitions submitted to parliament (Tibúrcio 2018). Empirical studies have also shown that collective petitions present a narrower range of issues than individual petitions, focusing mostly on health and transportation (Tibúrcio 2015, 2018). On the other hand, trade unions seem to be the most active actor in terms of submissions, although their role has decreased over time, probably as a side-effect of their difficulties to mobilise civil society and the declining numbers of their membership. Citizen groups have also played a key role in using this tool to voice their preferences, submitting an increasing number of petitions over the last decades. Other important actors are professional associations, which also register a positive trend, especially during the crisis period (2009–2015). More importantly, Tibúrcio (2018) shows that collective actors are relatively successful in discussing their concerns in the Chamber, with their petitions being discussed in plenary sessions more often than those presented by individuals. In Spain, citizens and interest groups are also able to present petitions to parliament. Petitions (consultas) are channelled through the Petitions Committee (Comisión de Peticiones), which is in charge to coordinate replies to petitions between government and parliament. As in Portugal, most petitions are promoted by individuals, which means that this is not a privileged channel for interest groups that have many other instruments to interact and to trespass their worries to public policy actors.

Interest groups in the Iberian parliaments  141

Transparency and regulation of interest groups interaction with parliament

Interest groups make representations to policy-makers – what we usually call lobbying – in different ways, for example, by taking protest actions, disseminating studies, media releases or seeking more direct contacts with politicians. According to our interviews, informal contacts play an important role in shaping the relationship between MPs and organised interests, aiming at influencing the political agenda, party positions on specific issues, governments’ action or parliaments’ decisions. Portugal and Spain are also no exception to the general trend of increasing citizens’ mistrust in governments, growing disaffection and rising feelings of anger towards politicians. Moreover, political scandals and wrongdoings that emerged during the Great Recession have fuelled the crisis of political representation. In reaction to this turbulent scenario, attempts to recover the relationship between citizens and parliaments have involved two types of responses, namely through (1) the introduction of new mechanisms of citizen participation in policy-making and (2) the adoption of transparency measures and (the debate on) the regulation of MPs-group interactions. Complementing the above findings, this section explores recent trends through which interest groups intervene in the policy-making process. Portugal has seen an expansion of opportunities provided to groups to influence policy outcomes. For example, the program ‘Legislate Better’ (­Government resolution nº63/2006), approved in 2006, expanded consultation meetings and established a new (broader) system of negotiation between parliament and civil society. It also recognised the role of public consultations. On the other hand, the growing use of digital tools has stimulated the fragmentation of interest representation, especially for the policy-­making process (Interviews 1 and 3). The efficacy of these practices, however, seems to be rather weak. Indeed, empirical evidence regarding the policy-­making process shows that these exchanges are not integrated in the final reports elaborated by parliamentary committees and that the information is rarely transmitted to other institutions (Caupers, Tavares de Almeida, and Guibentif 2014). Most interest groups in Portugal do not have professional staff dedicated to supporting relationships with MPs or other office holders; instead, they rely on external services, especially by hiring public relation agencies to represent their interests (see TIAC 2017). These actors employ different means to influence policy-making. One of these means is based on parliamentary hearings, but informal contacts are also important. According to a recent study, the areas where interest groups mobilise more frequently to influence parliamentary work are the financial sector, energy, civil construction, Church and pharmaceutical industry (Coroado 2017). As seen above, informal channels of interaction are also constant in Spain and linked to everyday parliamentary activities. Nonetheless, the

142  Marco Lisi and Luz Muñoz Marquez

general impression of MPs is that interest groups interact directly with them and they have less contact with consultancies and professional lobbies, though professional lobbies are advisors of some interest groups. For instance, MPs mentioned that some of proposals of amendments from business groups are written by lawyer firms or other consultancies (Interview 5). NGOs are naturally those groups perceived by MPs as those with fewer resources to hire professional staff or professional lobby’s services. But this is not always the case, as the national organisation of blind people (Organización Nacional de Ciegos de España, ONCE) in Spain shows, being one of the actors with the highest record of mobilisation in the parliamentary arena. In both countries, there is also evidence that ‘dark lobbying’ is an important tool interest groups employ to influence the policy-making process. This phenomenon takes place through informal contacts between MPs and group representatives. Deputies are not obliged to make their agenda public; thus they can easily meet with interest groups. As there is no practice of ‘legislative footprint’, it is difficult to evaluate the extent to which interest groups are successful in shaping legislative outcomes. But parties’ weak roots within civil society, and the widespread legitimacy deficit of representative actors and public institutions, certainly make the establishment of informal contacts more appealing, increasing the lack of transparency of interest group actions. There are no laws or regulations sanctioning the intervention of interest groups in the policy-making process and the interaction with MPs in both countries. In Portugal, the problem of transparency and lobbying was first discussed during the right-wing government led by Passos Coelho (2011– 2015), when the secretary of state Pedro Lomba made the first attempt to regulate the institutional activities of interest groups representatives. The proposal aimed to create a mandatory register and a code of conduct for interest groups, but this was to be applied only to the government and public institutes, thus excluding parliament. However, resistance from some parliamentary groups and MPs, together with looming general elections, led to the failure of the government’s initiative. By the same token, transparency and interest groups regulation have been a central topic of political debate in Spain. However, the absence of consensus among parliamentary groups and the government on the type of activities that must be reported by MPs and interest groups, has hampered a final decision. Political scandals and the economic crisis boosted a transparency law approved in 2013 by Rajoy’s majority government, but its impact is still very modest in institutional and social terms. Autonomous Communities have gone a step forward in regulating interest groups, especially in Catalonia where the transparency law approved in 2014 established the creation of an interest group register following the European Union model. The register was created in 2015, including local

Interest groups in the Iberian parliaments  143

and regional administrations. Moreover, the Catalan Parliament regulated interest groups by establishing a register, but this has not yet been completely implemented. The aftermath of the economic and financial crisis and the emergence of corruption scandals in the political arena strengthened the importance of regulating lobbying. As mentioned before, interest groups interact on a regular basis with representatives and the participatory principle is recognised in both constitutions, paving the way for the establishment of (more or less) stable and institutionalised interactions through formal channels. Yet the debate is about the importance to regulate also lobbying activities and interactions that go beyond this principle. In Spain, several political scandals such as the Barcenas case, which involved the People’s Party (PP, Partido Popular) was one of the most publicised by the media. But this case and other scandals, which also involve the socialist party, are just the top of the iceberg. A similar trajectory emerged in Portugal. Following a scandal with the involvement of government members of the socialist government led by Costa (2015–2019), the executive decided to establish some ethical rules to prevent wrongdoings of public office holders. This code is an ‘auto-regulatory instrument’, which binds the members of the 13th constitutional government only and establishes some general principles (e.g. transparency, integrity, honesty), which should guide governing officials’ action. Moreover, it sets out limits for the reception of bids.11

The Portuguese Parliament has recently discussed the opportunity to adopt a register for interest groups, following international standards. One of the main problems discussed during the debate in the 13th legislature (2015–2019) is the establishment of a stricter regime of incompatibilities and a transparency register for public office holders. The proposals advocated by the CDS (Centro Democrático e Social, Social Democratic Centre) and PS (Partido Socialista, Socialist Party) were based on the creation of a lobby register, which should be public, free and facultative, with no sanctions for those not registered in the list. There seems to be a consensus with regard to the need to include in the register not only MPs, but also local and national office holders, candidates to the Presidency of the Republic, as well as consultants and state representatives in privatisation processes. However, both the Portuguese Communist Party (PCP, Partido Comunista Português) and the BE (Left Bloc, Bloco de Esquerda) have remained sceptical regarding the capacity to create a new register and sanction lobbying practices.12 One of the main weaknesses of the reform is that the oversight of incompatibilities and of conflict of interests seems to be ineffective. From this point of view, the BE defends the exclusivity for MPs, as well as the creation of a new body (Entidade de Transparência dos Titulares de Cargos Políticos e Altos Cargos Públicos) with the aim of strengthening transparency and the compliance to rules, while the other parties simply want to transfer these powers to the Constitutional Court.

144  Marco Lisi and Luz Muñoz Marquez

Conclusions

This chapter provides a first preliminary overview of the evolution and present characteristics of the relationship between parliaments and interest groups in Portugal and Spain, which have displayed significant similarities in terms of party system features and the functioning of political system. We now highlight the main findings. First, in both countries the institutionalised channels of contact are mainly based around parliamentary committees. However, informal contacts have also played a very important role in fostering contacts between interest groups and parliaments. This is clear especially when we observe the relationship between individual MPs and organised interests, as well as the role of intermediation that representatives (especially lawyers) play between groups, PR agencies and political powers. Finally, informal contacts also emerge when we consider information exchange and email communications, as well as interaction between parliamentary groups and organised interests to set the media and public opinion agenda – for instance, through conferences, workshop, etc. – or parliamentary activities (e.g. oral questions). Another important finding relates to the type of interest groups most likely to mobilise in the two parliamentary arenas, with interesting differences between the two legislatures. While in Spain NGOs are, in terms of hearings, those participating the most in the parliamentary arena, reinforcing the pluralist configuration of the system of interest intermediation, in Portugal more institutionalised groups and with more resources are the most effective in mobilising. This difference depends, to a large extent, on the distinct institutional framework of either country (e.g. decentralisation, party system) and on the legacy of democratisation on the mobilisation of organised civil society. Another relevant finding is that in both countries the connection between interest groups and MPs is poorly regulated, although there is an ongoing debate on how to ensure more transparency and accountability. From this viewpoint, it is also worth noting the importance of new digital devices to foster transparency and to facilitate channels of interest representation. For instance, the introduction of e-petitions was decisive in strengthening the role of civil society in the mobilisation of their demands. Last but not least, this chapter shows that the crisis has had a limited impact on the modalities of interaction between parliaments and interest groups. This can be interpreted as a result of the institutionalisation of the Iberian parliaments and the fact that most interest groups have tried to access more directly governments and party leaders rather than parliamentary actors. One limitation of our analysis is the focus on the national level of ­parliament- group relations, although we acknowledge that multi-level ­politics ­favour a diversification of the channels interest groups utilise to influence ­policy- making, namely through the European and local arenas. Certainly, more research is needed on the relationship between parliaments and interest groups in Portugal and Spain. It will require more empirical studies on MPs’ behaviour, the legislative process and the influence of interest groups on agenda setting. Implementing this research agenda from a comparative perspective

Interest groups in the Iberian parliaments  145

might improve our knowledge on the interplay of two key actors in the functioning of contemporary political systems.
