Appendix

Introduction

Parliaments are a pivotal building block of political systems in representative democracies. Indeed, they embody formal and popular political legitimacy. The constellation of functions of parliaments speaks to their importance. Members of parliament (MPs) are in charge of legislating, electing and toppling governments, and to carry out an array of activities on behalf of their constituents. Such perspective of representation confers a great deal of importance to the study of public opinion towards parliaments and their members. In representative democracies – both in the “mandate” and “accountability” declinations (Przeworski et al. 1999; Mansbridge 2003) – the trust of citizenry on their representatives is vital. To be sure, in the mandate view, voters select MPs based on their electoral pledges, even if they do not have the capacity to keep tabs on all their actions. In the “accountability” view, citizens make a retrospective appraisal of MPs’ performance wherein they reward or punish their representatives based on the extent to which they delivered benefits aligned with voters’ preferences. By and large, the higher the degree of citizenry information about the policy-making deliberations of their representatives, the more that will reflect on retrospective voting. However, in contexts in which most citizens are ill informed, uncertainty about the results of policies increases, and the reputation of politicians decreases, trust becomes all the more important (Bianco 1994). The level of trust will affect how they vote, and whether they have to offer ex post explanations (Bianco 1994). Similar considerations could be extended to other attitudes of citizens towards the Parliament. Building on Easton’s work, support attitudes towards institutions can be classified into diffuse and specific (1965, 1975). Diffuse attitudes are more generic, more stable and less responsive to short-term contextual factors. On the contrary, specific support is more dependent on concrete results and activities – that is, it will depend on the context and, also, on the extent to which the institution’s performance is assessed positively.

9 Iberian citizens’ attitudes towards Parliament and representation

José Santana-Pereira and Irene Martín

150  José Santana-Pereira and Irene Martín

In addition to Easton’s work, we adopt other frameworks in this analysis of public opinion, namely one that helps us to understand what makes representative democracy more legitimate in the eyes of citizens than other political decision-making methods. According to Eulau and colleagues, the fundamental question for the theories of representation is not just the fact that decision-makers are elected, but that “what representatives decide and the way they reach decisions is more nearly in accord with expectations and demands of the represented” than another system (1959: 743). One of the aspects that affects this perception of legitimacy is the idea of “role” (idem). Referring to Burke’s distinction between focus and style of representation, Eulau and his colleagues reminded us that there are different “foci” of representation. That is, different ways to delimit the interests that MPs should represent (i.e. local vs. national). According to Burke, the foci would be associated with a certain “style” of representation (i.e. MPs should trust their own criteria when deliberating and legislating, or those of certain groups). Even though this association between “foci” and “style” has been questioned (Eulau et al. 1959: 746), it is still relevant to find out what citizens think about the foci and the styles that the MPs should represent. Two other aspects contribute to this as well: the opinions about who should select the parliamentary candidates (Gallagher and Marsh 1988), and citizens views about the mechanism by which seats are assigned to the elected candidates, that is, the electoral system. In this chapter, we aim at providing a general overview of Iberian citizens’ attitudes towards their parliamentary institutions, taking both a comparative and longitudinal perspective. Our goal is to understand to what extent citizens support the representative institution par excellence, consider it to be a trustworthy institution and are satisfied with its performance, as well as how they understand and assess the parliamentary focus and style of representation, the candidate selection procedures and the electoral system used to elect MPs. By and large, these topics have been overlooked in the field of legislative studies. Moreover, they have been a secondary concern of public opinion scholars in Portugal and Spain, which means that both the literature and data are scarce. Some studies have paid attention to trust in parliament,1 either as a feature of broader political trust (e.g. Magalhães 2002, 2003; Teixeira and Freire 2010; Torcal 2014; Jaime-Castillo et al. 2016; Muro and Vidal 2016), or on its own (Montero 1989; Martínez and Crespo 1998; ­Méndez-Lago and Martínez 2002; Méndez-Lago 2006; Oñate 2003; Freire and Meirinho 2012; Mota 2016; López-Nieto 2017). We argue that there are several reasons to pay special attention to the public opinion regarding the parliament in Portugal and Spain. First, these are fairly recent third-wave democracies where there may be traces of antiparliamentarism in the population, due to the historical developments of the early and mid-20th century, exemplified by the bad reputation of MPs and parties (Montero 1989; Magalhães 2002; Torcal et al. 2002).

Iberian citizens’ attitudes towards Parliament and representation  151

Second, recent developments, namely the political consequences of the great recession (Bosco and Verney 2012, 2017), exponentiates the usefulness of understanding the longitudinal dynamics in terms of how citizens look at the institution that formally represents the different viewpoints in their societies, and whose sovereignty has been questioned in the context of the crisis by the imposition of measures by the EU. Finally, the frequency of minority governments with the parliamentary support of other parties in both countries also makes the study of these two countries especially interesting (Field 2016). The importance of the parliamentary arena has increased in the current context, where the number of seats of the ruling party in both countries is smaller than ever. In methodological terms, the focus on these two polities allows us to introduce a most similar systems framework to the analysis, in terms of political culture. This does not obviate the institutional arrangements put into practice after the transition into democracy, that mirror the diversity of Spain and homogeneity of Portugal, as explained in another chapter of this book. This chapter is organized in five sections. After this introduction, we discuss the available public opinion data and previous research on diffuse and specific attitudes towards the legislative institutions in the Iberian Peninsula: importance, interest and knowledge, trust and satisfaction with its performance. The second section deals with the perceptions and preferences in terms of focus and style of representation offered by the Portuguese and Spanish parliaments. Finally, we explore views and preferences regarding candidate selection and electoral system. The chapter concludes with a summary and discussion of the main findings.

Diffuse support: the importance of parliamentary institutions

The perception of parliaments as relevant political institutions seems to be particularly widespread in the two Iberian democracies. Between the early 1980s and the year 2000, four in every five Spanish citizens agreed that the parliament was important for the functioning of democracy and a necessary institution, while almost two-thirds believed that parliamentary activities had a great or considerable impact on their lives (Oñate 2003). Over the two decades, these attitudes have been slightly more modest but still very high: approximately 75 per cent2 of Spaniards considered the parliament important and 57 per cent believed that the Congress had a great or substantial impact in their lives (CIS 2003, 2008). However, the lower chamber is placed in the last position in terms of impact amongst the main political institutions, after the city council (73 per cent), the regional (66 per cent) and the national government (66 per cent) (CIS 2005). This pattern of modest decrease in the perceived relevance of the Spanish parliament can be linked to the relative loss of centrality it has suffered due to the European integration and the political decentralization of the State, both resulting in a loss of powers to supranational or subnational entities (Méndez-Lago and Martínez 2002).

152  José Santana-Pereira and Irene Martín

Figures for the Portuguese are similar, with 63 per cent of the citizens considering the parliament essential to the Portuguese political system in 2002 (Freire et al. 2003). The data collected by Bacalhau (1994), in 1984 and 1993, already showed the existence of majorities of citizens convinced of the parliament’s relevance (64 and 80 per cent, respectively). Also, in the mid-2000s, the Assembleia da República was seen by almost one-third of the population as the political institution that actually ruled the country, second to the prime minister (51 per cent) and before the Council of ­Ministers (25 per cent) and the President of the Republic (14 per cent) (Freire et al. 2006).

Interest in, and knowledge of, parliament

In 2005, only one-third claimed to be interested in parliamentary activities, while the interest in the activities of local and regional authorities and the national government was considerably higher, with percentages ranging from 45 to 54 per cent (CIS 2005). Lack of interest in parliamentary debates has been predominant in the Spanish society between 1982 and 2000 (Montero 1989; Oñate 2003). Spanish citizens seem to possess lower levels of information about the parliament than the Portuguese. In Spain, just around one-third of the citizens recalls the name of the president of the Congress of Deputies and less than 10 per cent that of the Senate’s president (Oñate2003). In Portugal, in 2008, only 11 per cent of the citizens claimed that they never heard of Jaime Gama, then president of the Assembleia da República (Freire and Viegas 2009).

Trust in parliament

Political trust has benefited from a flourishing body of scholarly work over the last decade. Such trend coincided with increasing concerns amongst political scientists, politicians, stakeholders and citizens at large about the erosion of trust of Iberian citizens on their political institutions. Figure 9.1 illustrates that, since the beginning of the sovereign debt crisis, trust in the national parliament has declined in both countries. In the early 2000s, about half of the Iberian citizens tended to trust their national parliament, in line with the EU average. These figures were slightly higher than those observed in Portugal and Spain in the 1980s and 1990s – all ranging from 35 to 40 per cent (Teixeira and Freire 2010). Some authors stress that the decrease in political trust predates the financial crisis (Muro and Vidal 2016). However, it seems undeniable that the political and economic woes of Iberian countries in the aftermath of the 2008 crisis functioned as a death blow to trust in parliament. To be sure, in 2013 only 12.5 per cent of the Portuguese and 6.8 per cent of the Spaniards expressed such positive attitude. In fact, parliaments have not been the only institutions affected by declining trust levels. Political parties, national

Iberian citizens’ attitudes towards Parliament and representation  153

governments and, particularly, the European Union have witnessed a decline in trust. The two Iberian countries were indeed among the countries with the largest decrease in institutional trust in Europe between 2008 and 2012 (Torcal 2014). Since 2015, there have been signs of recovery, although much clearer in Portugal, as a possible reaction to the political and economic events in the country (cf. De Giorgi and Santana Pereira 2016). By the end of 2017, trust in the Assembleia da República was again above the levels in the EU at large. In Spain, the recovery is still quite timid, and it is still clearly lagging behind the EU at large. These oscillations confirm that, besides being related to diffuse support, political trust is related to specific support depending on the evaluation of performance (Hetherington 1998). What factors account for variation in citizens’ trust in parliamentary institutions? Existing literature suggests different answers. For one, some emphasize cultural factors and stress the relevance of social capital and values as explaining factors (cf. Mishler and Rose 2001). Other authors point out the support for the incumbent as the single most important determinant of trust in parliament (Holmberg et al. 2017). Finally, some authors focus on political and economic performance (Mishler and Rose 2001).

Figure 9.1  Trust in the national parliament in Portugal, Spain and the EU, 2001–2017. Source: Eurobarometer (http://ec.europa.eu/commfrontoffice/publicopinion/index.cfm/Chart/ index) Note: Values are the percentage of interviewees who tend to trust in the national parliament.

154  José Santana-Pereira and Irene Martín

In the late 1990s, the main factors of trust in parliament in Portugal and Spain were related to support for the incumbent party. Furthermore, in the Spanish case, in addition to age and education, ideological radicalism had an impact in trust levels insofar as more extreme citizens expressed less trust on their legislative chamber (Magalhães 2002, 2003). Looking at data from the late 2000s, Teixeira and Freire (2010) show that trust in the parliament seemed to be more rooted in cultural and political factors in Spain than in Portugal, where both political and economic factors (employment status, assessment of the country’s economic situation) mattered. To what extent has the financial crisis and its political consequences stress the relevance of economic factors in Portugal and Spain? Torcal (2014) explores the role of citizens’ assessments of the economy and political responsiveness during the peak of the financial crisis (2008–2012). The author concludes that, together with perceptions of corruption, political responsiveness is the most important and robust predictor of trust in parliament. Interestingly, the study also reports a significant effect of economic perceptions on trust in parliament. Torcal’s findings could be used to argue that the financial crisis harmonized the factors underlying trust in parliament in the two countries and enhanced the role of political responsiveness/external political efficacy, especially in Spain. Studies on trust on parliament using aggregate data lend support to the relevance of economic factors. Muro and Vidal (2016) test the effects of political and economic indicators on aggregate levels of trust in political institutions in Italy, Portugal, Greece and Spain. Their multivariate analysis highlights the relevance of economic factors, such as public debt, even when controlling for political variables. The authors confirm the impact of perceptions of corruption and of assessments of the government’s record. ­Fernandes and colleagues examine trust in national political institutions in five Southern European democracies, from 2000 through 2015. The authors corroborate previous findings: higher levels of trust in Parliament are correlated with lower rates of unemployment and poverty risk, lower levels of corruption at the executive level and lower centralization of the candidate selection process (Fernandes et al. 2019). Overall, studies support the idea that both economic and political factors matter. Interestingly enough, both Portuguese and Spanish citizens themselves select corruption as the main factor of mistrust (CIS 2012; Belchior et al. 2014; Jaime-Castillo et al. 2016).

Satisfaction with the way parliaments work and other attitudes

One of the first systematic studies of attitudes towards the parliament in Portugal analysed aggregate data on the perceptions citizens had of the parliament’s performance in the 1980s and 1990s (Magalhães 2002). Most citizens expressed a moderate assessment of the parliament’s performance,

Iberian citizens’ attitudes towards Parliament and representation  155

as indicated by 52–74 per cent of the respondents choosing the response “so-so”. Typically, after legislative elections, the parliament has benefited from a popularity peak, resulting in a sort of honeymoon effect. Magalhães (2002) suggests that the Portuguese parliament is perceived as a distant political institution, too dependent on single-party majoritarian governments and strict party discipline. In Spain, available data on satisfaction with the way the parliament works cover the 1990s and 2006. Typically, less than half of the Spanish citizens declared themselves pleased with the performance of their national parliament (28–46 per cent). Such pattern is probably related to the fact that most citizens see MPs as devoting too much attention to irrelevant issues, a position held by 61 per cent of the Spaniards (CIS 2010). According to the data reported by Oñate (2003), these perceptions have worsened. Moreover, there has been a degradation in the way citizens assess the informational role of parliamentary debates. In Portugal, in 2006–2008, around six out of ten respondents agreed completely or partially that parliamentary debates contributed to inform citizens (Viegas 2009; Freire and Viegas 2009). In 2012 and 2014 this figure was about 20 per cent points lower (Freire et al. 2012; Belchior et al. 2014). In Spain, there has been a steady growth in the support to the idea that the parliamentary annual debate is not useful (28 per cent in 2010 and 41 per cent in 2014). The general reputation of MPs is also an issue in Iberian legislatures. In 2014, only 16 per cent of the Portuguese believed that MPs, once elected, tried to fulfil the pledges made during the election campaign (Belchior et al. 2014). Thus, it is not surprising that, in 2008, 58 per cent of the Portuguese believed that the number of mandates in the parliament should be limited by law (Freire and Viegas 2009). A few years later, 87 per cent were completely or partially in favour of reducing the current number of parliamentary seats to the minimum allowed by the Constitution (Freire et al. 2012). Accordingly, in 2005, 57 per cent of the Spaniards believed that the main reason for someone to run for a parliamentary seat was the power and influence stemming from the position. At the same time, only 26 per cent believed that the desire to participate in the resolution of the country’s problems or fight for her own and her party ideals were the main motivations (CIS 2005).

Focus and style of representation

According to Eulau et al. (1959), there are two important aspects in the analysis of political representation: the focus and the style of representation. The former defines who MPs represent (the population as a whole, their voters, their party, their electoral district?). The latter addresses the question of the criteria MPs use in their decision-making processes (do and should they act following the mandate of their voters, follow party guidelines or vote according to their own judgement?). In this section, we explore these two dimensions.

156  José Santana-Pereira and Irene Martín

Focus of representation

Table 9.1 shows that Portuguese citizens tend to have a rather party-­centric vision of representation. MPs represent first and foremost their parties and, in the second place, their constituents. Indeed, such vision is aligned with constitutional provisions whereby MPs have a country-level mandate at the expense of their district. However, for 64 per cent of the Portuguese, it is only natural that MPs try to benefit their electoral districts (Freire et al. 2012). Turning to the Spanish case, Table 9.1 shows that a plurality of citizens believe that MPs representation is more in line with the formal design of the Congress of Deputies, intended to be a chamber to represent the population, whereas territorial representation is guaranteed by the Senate. To be sure, in 2012, 31.6 per cent of the respondents acknowledged that MPs represent the population as a whole. Interestingly, a roughly similar percentage of respondents considers that MPs should represent their party. By contrast, only 6.9 per cent see MPs as representatives of their electoral districts. There is a considerable degree of congruence in how Spanish legislative elites and citizens regard the focus of parliamentary representation ­(Méndez-Lago and Martínez 2002). However, 87 per cent of Spanish citizens believe that MPs should establish a stronger dyadic representation with their district by paying more visits and keeping tabs on the problems and claims of their constituents. Furthermore, 66 per cent of the respondents think that MPs should live in the district where they have been elected (CIS 2005). For all the importance for citizens of an increased electoral connection, Spanish institutional setting provides weak incentives, as Field’s chapter in this volume shows. The idea of MPs as efficient representatives of the citizenry is not predominant at all. In 2002, 43 per cent of the Portuguese believed that members

Iberian citizens’ attitudes towards Parliament and representation  157

of the Assembleia da República had little or no idea of what citizens think, while only 30 per cent said that MPs had a fair or good grasp on public opinion concerns (Freire et al. 2003). In Spain, this latter opinion was expressed by a similar proportion of citizens (Freire and Meirinho 2012). These figures placed the two Iberian countries slightly above average in a sample of democracies (Freire and Meirinho 2012). Moreover, in the early 2000s, only about 30 per cent believed that the opinions of the MPs reflected perfectly or fairly well those of their voters – a figure that placed Portugal well below the average of 33 democratic regimes, and well below Spain, where two-thirds of the citizens felt their opinions fairly or well represented by MPs (Freire et al. 2003, 2006; Freire and Meirinho 2012). In the last ten years, the proportion of Portuguese citizens holding this positive attitude plummeted and reached a worrisome 9 per cent in 2014 (Freire and Viegas 2009; Belchior et al. 2014; Freire et al. 2017). Furthermore, in 2008, only one in every five Portuguese citizens believed that the laws reflected the interests of the majority of the population, and 60 per cent agreed to some extent that private interests had too much weight on the law-making process (Freire and Viegas 2009). The latter figure has risen to 75 per cent in 2012 and 81 per cent in 2016–2017 (Freire et al., 2012, 2017). Oñate (2003) reports data showing that, before 2000, 60 per cent of the Spaniards considered that MPs did not defend their interests at all, or hardly. In 1998, the majority of Spaniards considered that they were very or considerably concerned with their own interests and those of their party and hardly or not worried at all with the needs of their voters.

Style of representation

Regarding their style of representation, MPs can act as trustee, delegate, or politico (Eulau et al. 1959: 749–750). In the first case, representatives act freely by taking decisions according to their own assessment of the situation. On the contrary, by acting as delegates, MPs subordinate their independence to the will or mandate of a superior authority. Finally, representatives act as politicos when they follow a mix of both orientations. The more complex the decisions, the closer the role of representatives to that of the trustee (idem: 751). Over the past ten years, multiple surveys show that, if MPs face competing incentives from their party and their voters, a majority of the Portuguese voters consider that voters’ opinion should prevail. This figure increased to 88 per cent in 2016–2017 from 70 per cent in 2008. About three out of each four Spanish and Portuguese citizens contend that MPs should decide according to the opinions of voters in their electoral district, even if they hold a different opinion on the matter (Freire and Viegas 2009; CIS 2012; Freire et al. 2012, 2017). The Portuguese seem to be more divided when faced with a conflict between the opinion of an individual MP and the party she belongs. In 2008,

158  José Santana-Pereira and Irene Martín

almost half of the voters (49 per cent)3 considered that such situations the vote should be in line with the party position (Freire and Viegas 2009). In 2012 and 2016–2017, the most expressive opinion (44 and 65 per cent, respectively), was that the MP should vote according to his own opinion (Freire et al. 2012, 2017). Turning to Spain, in 2007 and 2009, the most relevant group (around one-third) supported the idea of MPs behaving in accordance with their own judgement (CIS 2007, 2009). Previous data on this, from the 1984–1997 period, show a completely divided Spanish society regarding this matter (Méndez-Lago and Martínez 2002). In sum, when the conflict affects the party and the MP, both Portuguese and Spaniards prefer that representatives play the role of trustee, and do not subordinate his/her actions to those of the party. However, this view coexists with a clear contradiction, as the predominant view amongst both Spanish (around 70 per cent; CIS 1994–2011; Méndez and Martínez 2002), and Portuguese citizens (57 per cent in 2008, according to Freire and Viegas 2009) think that if an MP abandons the party they must give up their seat and be replaced by another MP of the same party. In other words, the majority thinks that MPs must be subordinated to the party, in accordance with the electoral system rules (proportional, based on closed and blocked lists defined by the party organization).

Candidate selection and electoral system

Primaries and gender quotas

The non-preferential nature of the electoral system in Portugal and Spain has sparked, in recent years, a debate on candidate selection. Specifically, how parties should select their personnel. In this section, we focus on the Iberian citizen’s attitudes regarding less party-centric modalities of candidate selection and the implementation of quotas fostering the presence of women in parliament and therefore tackling known and overwhelming asymmetries in terms of descriptive representation. In the last decade, an overwhelming majority of Portuguese citizens – up to 80 per cent – believed it would be important that party members were able to select MP candidates by means of primaries (Freire and Viegas 2009; Viegas 2009). The Spanish public opinion seems to be divided between those in favour of opening up the election of the candidates of political parties to all of society (35–40 per cent) and those who would leave it in the hands of the party members, or of party members and sympathizers (around 35 per cent altogether). Few (around 12–13 per cent), however, would leave it in the hands of the internal party organs (CIS 2007, 2009). In both countries, there seems to be support for more inclusive, democratic and transparent modalities of candidate selection in a context of closed and blocked list electoral systems.

Iberian citizens’ attitudes towards Parliament and representation  159

What about gender quotas? In 2006, almost half of the Portuguese (48 per cent) was neither in favour nor against gender quotas in candidate lists (Viegas 2009). In spite of this, the measure would be implemented that same year (Baum and Espírito-Santo 2012). At that time, only 23 per cent clearly supported this measure. However, only two years later, the support for this measure almost doubled (Freire and Viegas 2009). In 2012, 66 per cent of the population were in favour of gender quotas (Freire et al. 2012). This trend is certainly not unrelated with an evolution in terms of how citizens perceive the gender misrepresentation in Parliament. In 2008, when asked if the fact that 4 out of 5 MPs are male posed a serious problem for the Portuguese democracy, the citizens were divided, and 45 per cent disagreed with that statement (Freire and Viegas 2009). Four years later, only 28 per cent discard this fact as a threat to the quality of democracy in Portugal (Freire et al. 2012). In Spain, citizens are divided in this respect, as two similar groups (43 per cent) back and reject, respectively, the idea that the parties should guarantee some positions for specific groups such as women or youngsters in their lists (CIS 2012).

The electoral system

Reforming the electoral system used in legislative elections is a recurring topic in the Portuguese public sphere. Parties, journalists and experts recurrently discuss the need to implement reforms and dissect the details of concrete proposals. The panorama, however, has been one of remarkable stability (Freire and Meirinho 2012; see also Sampaio 2009). To what extent is this stability linked to the fact that public opinion is satisfied with the system currently in practice? The average citizen’s notion of the electoral system is often quite generic and crude (Curtice 2004; Freire and Meirinho 2012), which makes the specificities of electoral systems a difficult issue to explore in terms of mass opinions and attitudes. Nevertheless, in the last decades, survey data on how citizens perceive the electoral system’s main features has been collected both in Portugal and Spain. In what follows, we analyse the most relevant data available. In 2008, 41 per cent of the Portuguese supported the ideal of proportional representation in the parliament, with a close resemblance between the vote shares and seat shares each party gets, but an expressive minority of 32 per cent would favour a certain degree of disproportionality leading to the most voted party getting the majority of the parliamentary seats (Freire and Viegas 2009). Interestingly enough, an overwhelming majority of MPs interviewed on the same occasion were supportive of the ideal of proportionality (around 80 per cent), a finding that the authors explain as being due to the fact that the Portuguese electoral system is disproportional enough to permit single-party majorities, and that proportionality is a constitutional requirement (Freire and Meirinho 2012). Four years later, the support for the proportional representation ideal had grown considerably, being favoured

160  José Santana-Pereira and Irene Martín

by 52 per cent of the citizens, and the preference for a disproportional result shrunk to 18 per cent (Freire et al. 2012). Citizens, however, are not pleased with all the aspects of the current electoral system. For instance, over the last ten years, an overwhelming majority of Portuguese citizens has been in favour of ending the monopoly political parties have in terms of presenting lists in legislative elections, being therefore favourable to having groups of citizens running as well. The support to this change of the electoral system grew from about two-thirds of the respondents in 2006–2008 to 72 per cent in 2012 and 81 per cent in 2014 (­Viegas 2009; Freire et al. 2012, Belchior et al. 2014). The goal of creating more favourable conditions for a closer relationship between MPs and their constituents lies at the heart of most proposals for electoral reform in Portugal presented in the last two decades (Freire and Meirinho 2012; Lobo and Santana-Pereira 2015). Citizens tend to see this goal as desirable, and there is a longitudinal trend of increase in terms of support to this idea. In 2008, 58 per cent of the citizens in Portugal agreed that it should be possible to vote more for candidates and less for parties; more recently, this opinion is held by roughly three in every four interviewees (Freire and Viegas 2009; Freire et al. 2012, 2017). In Spain, the pattern is similar, with two-thirds of the citizens in favour of such an electoral reform, and one-third even consider closed lists to be antidemocratic (CIS 2005, 2007). The biggest group of Spaniards (30–40 per cent), however, does not have an opinion about the electoral system, at least in 2007. As it stands, the current electoral system does not foster links between voters and candidates. Ballot structures include only party labels and, as a consequence, voters do not know candidate names. The percentage of Spanish citizens who remember the name of the candidate heading the list of the political party they voted for in the most recent legislative elections tends to be very low (Oñate 2003). Data collected in 2000 estimated it to be of around 35 per cent (CIS 2000). In Portugal, data from the Portuguese Election Study of 2002 shows that less than 30 per cent of the Portuguese citizens believed they were able to recall the name of any candidates running in their own electoral district, only a few months after the election had taken place, and only 19 per cent mentioned a correct name (Freire et al. 2003). This lack of knowledge about the specific candidates is unsurprising if one considers the fact that in both countries legislative elections use closed and blocked party lists. Citizens formally vote for parties, not for candidates nor for candidate lists.

Conclusion

This chapter sought to provide a panoramic understanding of the Portuguese and Spanish citizens’ attitudes towards their parliaments, by analysing the available survey data and previous studies on this matter.

Iberian citizens’ attitudes towards Parliament and representation  161

The analysis of this data allows us to conclude in favour of the existence of general diffuse support, but often negative attitudes towards the parliament in both countries. A majority of Spanish and Portuguese citizens consider the Parliament a relevant institution. Nevertheless, most citizens are uninterested in the parliament’s activities and unable to recall the name of its president. Distrust is considerable, but there is a great deal of volatility in this attitude, with the great recession taking a toll on the trustworthiness of parliaments in the eyes of Portuguese and Spanish citizens. The literature on these two cases identifies as main explaining factors political attitudes not directly related with the parliamentary itself (corruption, political responsiveness, support for the incumbent party and assessment of the government’s record), but the economy also matters. Specific support for the parliament is as feeble as trust. Citizens are not too enthusiastic when asked to assess this institution’s performance. Regarding attitudes in terms of the focus and style of representation, the Portuguese display a rather party-centric perception of representation, while the Spaniards display a more universalistic conception of the representation offered in parliament. Also, both the Spaniards and the Portuguese believe that MPs have no idea of what the average citizen thinks, and especially the latter do not feel that the MPs positions reflect the opinions of voters. In both countries the idea that personal motivations and interests outweigh the desire to represent the people’s interests is predominant. Regarding the style of representation, most citizens think that MPs should represent their voters’ opinions when these are in disagreement with the party’s position or the MPs themselves. When MPs disagree with their parties, citizens are split, but there is a trend of increasing consensus surrounding the idea that MPs should vote according to their own positions. In spite of this, and of very critical attitudes towards parties, citizens think that if an MP abandons their party, they should also abandon the seat, in consonance with the logic of party democracy (Méndez and Martínez 2002; Méndez 2006). Finally, citizens support a more participatory modality of candidate selection, and quotas that ensure a better balance in the presence of men and women in parliament. In both countries, the time is ripe for changes fostering a more personalized vote instead of closed lists. Against this negative backdrop, and despite the scarcity of recent data, it seems there are nowadays good news for Iberian MPs, as trustworthiness in the Parliament is on the rise. However, the positive trend observed in the last few years may only be partly due to the performance of parliamentary institutions strictu sensu and more directly connected with the general political and economic landscape.

Section IV Legislative processes

Introduction

Portugal and Spain share a common history. Both countries were under dictatorships for a similar period of time and they joined the European Community (EEC) together in 1986. In managing their transition to democracy in the 1970s/1980s, these countries had to change their legislative frameworks into democratic ones more or less at the same time, resulting in similarities in their procedural framework for the legislative process. However, Portugal and Spain have different types of political system, with different structures of parliament, legal and political backgrounds, forms of government and constitutional and legislative frameworks. Some common factors exist in their legislative process though, as this chapter shows. Common features of their legislative procedures include the entities entitled to initiate laws, the number and structure of readings and the role of the parliamentary groups [PGs] and of parliamentary committees. The similarities are partly the result of a common history, but also of ongoing communication between these two legislatures. We start our analysis by outlining the institutional framework and structures that frame the legislative process in these parliaments, to then proceed to analyse legislative performance over the past 40 years of democratic rule and finally consider some more recent developments towards strengthening transparency and enhancing citizen accessibility.

Institutional framework for the legislative process in Spain and Portugal

Despite similarities in their legislative procedures, the structure of the State is a key significant difference between Portugal and Spain and has a direct impact on the legislative process: the 17 autonomous communities in Spain act as federal states with legislative power, whilst in Portugal it is mainly the central State that legislates (government and parliament). Another key difference lies in the fact that the Spanish Legislature is bicameral, whereas the Portuguese Parliament, Assembleia da República (AR), is unicameral.

10 The legislative role of Iberian parliaments

Cláudia Ribeiro, Mercedes Araújo and Sónia Rodrigues

168  Cláudia Ribeiro et al.

Spanish bicameralism is constitutionally shaped as imperfect, being unbalanced in favour of the lower chamber, the Congreso de los Diputados (CdlD), which, for instance, has exclusivity of key competences such as government investiture and accountability, conferring the Senate to a clearly subordinated role in the legislative function. The AR is the principal source of legislative power in Portugal, sharing it mainly with government. Parliament has the power to enact legislation on all matters, except for those which are the exclusive responsibility of the government. Its legislative powers sub-divide into three types of competence, which relate to different areas of policy: (a) exclusive competence: matters that can only be legislated by parliament, such as elections, the state budget, the underlying principles of education and national defence; (b) partial exclusive competence: matters that parliament can delegate to government by means of delegated legislation, such as rights and freedoms, tax system, agricultural policy and information services and (c) shared competence: matters not covered by exclusive or partial competence, where both parliament and government may enact legislation. The power to initiate a bill lies with MPs, PGs, government, groups of 20,000 registered voters, and the legislative assemblies of the Autonomous Regions (Azores and Madeira). The latter approve regional regulations and may present draft bills to the AR. The Portuguese Constitution grants equal value to laws (deriving from parliament) and decrees-law (deriving from government). It is important to underline also that legislative power is more and more fragmented in both countries. In addition to parliament and government, we are also witnessing the multiplication of political players and pressure groups in society, often people with different interests. The so-called soft power has a growing influence in legislation. From trade unions to powers in the legal, business and communications worlds, there is a widespread of entities that have the means and tools to exercise substantial power. Similarly to Portugal, parliament holds the heart of legislative power in Spain. Government can also issue laws in the form of royal decrees and legislative decrees, but the former need ratification by the CdlD and the latter are delegated legislation. Thus, even if initiated outside the institution, legislative power needs to go through parliament, requiring its intervention one way or another. Overall, legislation can be initiated by government, the Congress and the Senate, the Legislative Assemblies of the Autonomous Regions and by popular legislative initiative through the collation of 500,000 signatures; as in Portugal, signatures can be collected electronically. In relation to the Senate, save for a few exceptions, bills must be initiated in the Congress, which also has the last word, delegating the Senate to a secondary revising role; still, within its vetoing power, it has on occasion forced the Congress to think again in relation to key legislation, such as the stage budgets for 2005 or 2011, for example. Resulting from a combination between the key role played by parties and the parliamentary governance system, the Spanish model entails a close

The legislative role of Iberian parliaments  169

cooperation between parliament and government, bodies which, in turn, legitimize decisions taken by the parties (García-Pelayo 1977: 59–61). This results in a clear predominance of the executive of the ordinary legislative process, particularly when the government is supported by a majority in parliament. Thus, government’s legislative initiative prevails, with its bills being considered on a priority basis over parliament’s bills. Government’s bills’ priority in the consideration process is but one of the many expressions of the rationalized parliamentary regime enshrined in the system (García-Escudero 2000). Another key difference between Spain and Portugal lies in the fact that the latter is a semi-presidential system, which, amongst other key powers, means that the directly elected President of the Republic has some vetoing legislative powers. The President is responsible for promulgating parliamentary laws and governmental decrees-law or regulatory decrees, being able to veto these also. The presidential veto on government legislation is final. However, in relation to parliamentary legislation, parliament can force the enactment of vetoed legislation, through the (re-)approval of the bill by absolute majority, or a two-thirds majority in the case of specifically significant legislation (for example, organic laws, electoral laws and laws concerning foreign relations). This indicates the important role of the AR in the legislative process. These vetoes, and their subsequent consideration in parliament, can also result in an amendment of the law in line with the president’s suggestions. The president has therefore important revising powers. During the current legislature (2015–2019), the President of the Republic has so far vetoed seven parliament bills. Besides this veto power, the President can also refer bills to the Constitutional Court to check their constitutionality. The Portuguese institutional framework of the legislative process reflects therefore a solid balance of powers between its key institutions, with parliament playing a crucial role at its core. This is particularly patent in the current socialist minority government, which is effectively kept in power simply through a negotiated support with two of the opposition left-wing PGs, in exchange for specific policy concessions and with the objective to block a right-wing minority government. This solution turned the attention to the Portuguese Parliament, which has become the central player in the political landscape. The main bills have to be negotiated in Parliament and the opposition parties – namely the ones supporting the government, but also the other ones in some specific issues – are able to approve more bills. For instance, between September 2017 and July 2018, despite being the 3rd political force in the Portuguese parliament, the Left Bloc was the PG with the highest number of bills approved. In Spain, popular sovereignty is embedded through its Constitution, which establishes that national sovereignty belongs to the Spanish people, from which all State powers emanate and which is expressed in the political form of Parliamentary Monarchy. It is important to bear in mind this dual representative role when considering parliament’s legislative role.

170  Cláudia Ribeiro et al.

Amendments to the constitution: expanding parliament’s role or adapting it to a new era?

The Portuguese Constitution has been amended seven times since its approval in 1976 (1982, 1989, 1992, 1997, 2001, 2004 and 2005). Most of these reforms enhanced the powers of the parliament, but also seized the opportunity to introduce substantive changes according to society trends. This is in clear contrast with Spain, which has undergone only two revisions. Since 1976, Parliament has always been referred to as an organ of sovereignty, defined as the “representative assembly of all Portuguese citizens”. Thus, theoretically, each Member represents all the citizens and not just the specific district through they are elected, or their party’s voters. However, in a contradictory way, significant powers are ascribed directly to the PGs, to the detriment of individual Members. In short, the revision of 1982 created the Constitutional Court which has a key role regarding the legislative process, checking the compliance of bills with the Constitution. The 1989 revision set out an important tool of direct democracy – the referendum – and established the distribution of petitions to the competent parliamentary committees. The 1992 and 1997 amendments reinforced parliament’s exclusive legislative powers and introduced citizens’ right to present legislative proposals to parliament. The revision of 2004 increased the political and administrative autonomy of the two Autonomous Regions, namely by strengthening the powers of their legislative assemblies. Finally, the 2005 revision added a new article to the Constitution, allowing the holding of a referendum on the approval of treaties concerning the European Union. In parallel to this, political control of governmental policy has developed over the years, expanding parliament’s role in monitoring public policies carried out by government. In Spain, the Constitution of 1978 has only been amended twice, in both cases to respond to EU pressure. The first amendment, adopted in 1992, and supported in parliament by all PGs, allowed European citizens to vote and run for office in local elections. The second took place in 2011 and was not as consensual, setting out the principle of budget stability. Like in Portugal, the Spanish Constitution can be amended through two different procedures, a simpler one and a more demanding:

• the ordinary one works for most of the articles and demands a threefifths majority of Members in each House. If it is impossible to reach an agreement between the Houses, a Joint Committee is set out and the amendment is passed by a two-thirds majority in Congress and an absolute majority in the Senate. Moreover, one-tenth of the deputies or senators can impose a binding referendum to ratify the amendment. • a specific one, aiming to secure specific issues of particular significance such the “indissoluble union” of Spain, fundamental rights and the Crown. To amend these, a majority of two-thirds in both houses needs

The legislative role of Iberian parliaments  171

to be achieved and parliament is dissolved. Once the amendment is approved by two-thirds on the newly elected Houses, a referendum is held to ratify it.

Most scholars criticize this burdensome procedure, which in part explains the lower number of constitutional revisions. The current political situation in Spain has brought to light some public pressure to change the provisions of the Constitution. Never a provision of the Constitution had been so widely spoken as the Article 155, used by the central government to suspend self-rule in Catalonia after the voting of the unilateral declaration of independence by the regional parliament. Public opinion discussions have focused mainly on the need to change territorial organization, but this task seems difficult given the lack of absolute majorities. Regional financing and reforming the Senate are also on the table, despite the difficulties involved.

Major bills on which parliaments have a key role

The recent economic crisis in Southern Europe has caused a paradigm shift in what were considered the most relevant parliamentary bills. It is in this context that the legislative process to amend the law establishing the framework for the budgetary procedure in Portugal became particularly important. However, other legislation also has particular significance, such as that relating to national planning, and respective reports on its implementation, the State budget, general accounts of the State and other public accounts. These follow a specific parliamentary legislative procedure, completely different from the common legislative process, enshrined in the rules of procedure. Furthermore, organic laws, a specific type of bills that form the structure of the political system, are also considered particularly significant, as they deal with key fundamental matters, such as election to sovereign bodies, referendum, organization of national defence, or the acquisition, loss and reacquisition of Portuguese citizenship. To be approved, these bills require an absolute majority vote. Contrary to the Portuguese case, the Spanish Constitution establishes that the budgetary initiative belongs to the government (art. 134 SC). This means not only that it is exclusively incumbent upon the government to present the draft annual State Budget, with no room for tabling non-­government bills, but also that:

• It is not possible to table amendments to the whole presenting an alternative text to the said bill, in the very unlikely case that any PG was technically able to do so, but only amendments calling to the return of the text to the government. • Only the government can introduce direct amendments to the annual budget bill, by means of the extraordinary appropriations and the appropriation supplements (Moreno Ara 2012).

172  Cláudia Ribeiro et al.

• Once the State General Budget has been adopted, the government may ­table bills entailing an increase of public expenditure or a decrease of the revenues corresponding to the same budget year, whilst those proposals or amendments involving an increase of appropriations or a decrease in budget revenue require previous approval by government before its approval.

The central nature of the budget debate within parliamentary life means that all other activities, legislative or not, must be adapted to the calendar envisaged for its consideration. The political significance of the draft annual budget often turns it into a question of confidence, so that, once it is tabled, the chamber ratifies, or not, its confidence in the government to conduct its political program. This is illustrated by the dissolution of parliament in 1996, following a rejection of the government’s state budget proposal. Also indicating the importance of the budget bill, it is worth noting that the Senate’s veto power has been exercised almost exclusively in relation to draft Budget bills. When a budget bill is not approved by January the 1st, as happened as recently as 2012, 2017 and 2018, the previous financial year’s budget is automatically extended until the new one is approved.

Legislative performance in both parliaments

Overview of legislative performance

As explained above, Portugal has two main legislative bodies: parliament and government, each with specific procedures to approve laws and decrees-law, respectively. However, the executive is responsible for most of the approved bills. In Portugal, individual Members can introduce draft bills, but, traditionally, few of these actually pass. However, recent political ­circumstances have been reversing this pattern, with the opposition getting their bills approved on a much larger scale, as shown below. ­Moreover, not only can government approve decrees-law, it can also present bills to ­parliament. ­Table 10.1 shows that government bills have a much higher likelihood to be

Table 10.1  Number of bills presented and approved in the 12th legislature (2015–2018), Portugal

Presented Approved

Proposed by MPs or PGs 974 283 Proposed by government 143   91 Total amount of approved bills 262

Source: Parliament database Note: The total number of approved bills is smaller than the total amount of draft laws passed because sometimes the final text of the bill incorporates two or more draft bills.

The legislative role of Iberian parliaments  173

approved: while only 13 per cent of the bills presented by MPs/PGs were approved, 61 per cent of government bills became law. In Spain, the predominance of government’s legislative initiative is particularly patent. This is clear when it comes to the Budget, which takes precedence over the other business of Congress, as seen above. The executive also has several tools to enforce its legislative priority role:

• As compared to opposition bills, government bills must not go through the initial consideration debate by the Plenary of the Congress. • Government can declare or request the urgent procedure, not only in relation to its initiatives, but also in relation to non-government bills and in any stage of the procedure. • Government can request that the Houses meet in extraordinary sittings, also to consider legislative initiatives and in any of the stages of the procedure. • Government can oppose the consideration of any non-governmental bill or amendment which is considered as contrary to a currently valid legislative delegation.

The government’s power to oppose the consideration of non-government bills was hardly used when it had the support of an absolute majority. This was because, on the one hand, the executive almost totally controlled legislative initiative and, on the other, there was no need to resort to vetoing in order to avoid the consideration of non-government bills as the majority of the Chamber voted directly against their consideration. During the current 12th legislative term (2016–current), where the government does not have absolute majority, it has made a much more intensive use of its vetoing power. Successive Governments have showed their disagreement with non-government bills as follows:

• 1st Legislature (1979–1982): 23 • 2nd Legislature (1982–1986): 22 • 9th Legislature (2008–2011): 23 • 12th Legislature (2016–2018): 65

This shows that during the current legislature the Government has exponentially increased its use of this constitutional power, thus turning it into an obstructionism technique to hinder legislative initiative stemming from bodies other than the Government. Despite the latter, on some occasions the Government’s disagreement has not been accepted by the majority of the Bureau of the Congress of the Deputies, which has led the Government to raise two constitutional conflicts on competences against the relevant agreements adopted by the Bureau of the Congress.

174  Cláudia Ribeiro et al.

However, it is still too early to see results.

In Portugal, the average time to approve a law is extremely reduced and insufficient to guaranty the quality of the law and, namely, to hear all the relevant stakeholders and to use tools such as ex ante impact assessment and broad consultations. In fact, as Figure 10.1 shows, the average time to approve bills proposed by MPs or PGs is only of around 200 days. If we consider the bills presented by government to parliament the situation is even more limited: an average of 100 days to approve a bill. There is no available data for Spain concerning this issue, but the fact that this is a bicameral parliament generally entails more time for legislative procedures.

Parliamentary appreciation of government’ laws in Portugal

In Portugal, it is also important to consider another legislative tool: parliamentary appreciations. Parliamentary appreciations are a mechanism of political scrutiny by parliament of decrees-law. The parliamentary appreciation initiative can be triggered by a minimum of ten deputies, within 30 days after the publication of the decree-law. Transparency, citizens’ accessibility and how ICT can enhance it

E-participation in law-making process has recently assumed growing importance in modern parliaments, attempting to make them more accountable. This is achieved by providing information about parliamentary activity on the website together with a range of mechanisms, which aim at creating opportunities for citizens to participate and increase levels of interactivity. In the Portuguese parliament, its President created the Digital Parliament Working Group in 2016 to consider how technology and web

communications could enhance parliamentary work, and its perception by citizens. The Digital Parliament Working Group reported in 2018, enacting thereafter a number of important changes. One of its outputs was the reformulation of the Parliamentary website and of the TV Channel, as key tools to open parliamentary procedures to citizens. The Group led a thoroughgoing reform, including a decision supporting the presence of the AR in several social media networks. In March 2018, the Portuguese Parliament launched a new website, more citizen focused, with a more user-friendly layout, more contents, a different structure and a strong emphasis on open data. One of the innovations is the availability of digital platforms that allow citizens to collect signatures and present petitions, citizens’ legislative initiatives and referendum initiatives. Citizens may also present suggestions to parliamentary activity and have the possibility of subscribing alerts if they wish to follow the development of specific content. In Spain, even if it seems as if the crisis suffered by the principle of representation has been overcome, there are still demands of greater transparency and citizen involvement, which, together with the tools made available by the new technologies have no doubt influenced parliamentary activity. On the one hand, the number of hearings and appearances of experts and affected parties during the consideration of legislative initiatives

Table 10.8  Parliamentary Appreciations in Portugal (1997–2017)

has increased. Such hearings are not mandatory according to the parliament’s Rules of Procedure, but they must be agreed by the committees entrusted with the relevant legislative initiative. Their goal is to inform parliamentarians and hear the views of those involved in the respective policy being considered, and for this reason they are held after the debate on the whole in the Plenary once the initiative has been conveyed to the relevant committee and before the deadline to table amendments to sections thereof has expired. These hearings add up to those held in the sub committees and study committees, thus generating agreements and making citizen involvement possible. On the other hand, thanks to new technologies these hearings have sometimes taken place in videoconference format. Publicity of parliamentary activity is channelled through different paths: as in Portugal, the Spanish parliament has a Parliament TV channel, which is produced by the Congress of Deputies. This is a TV channel broadcast by satellite, cable and internet. Its programs are based on the live and pre-­ recorded broadcasting of the Plenary and Committee sittings, as well as informational programs produced in-house. The Congress’s web page also enables the public to follow live all parliamentary sittings held each day in the Chamber. Likewise, the Congress is actively present in several social media networks and has its own profile in Twitter, YouTube, Instagram and Facebook. The Senate is also present on Twitter, YouTube and Instagram. On the other hand, both the Congress and the Senate have incorporated a “Transparency Portal” into their websites, providing economic, budgetary and contractual information, as well as on the institution and its organization. This includes information on the parliamentarians’ activities and assets, how to contact them and, in some cases, their diary activities. Citizens may, in turn, request information through the Portal regarding the ­Chambers’ administrative activity. Parliament’s openness is thus undeniable, although its impact on the exercise of its classic functions has not been so remarkable. The said functions are now conducted with greater publicity, but in the same way as before. In order to simplify and improve legislation and also to motivate the participation of citizens and encourage their interest in the legislative process, the Portuguese Parliament also created, in the previous legislature (2011–2015), a Parliamentary Working Group to Consolidate Legislation. This Group led to making available an online form on the home page of parliament’s website inviting citizens to send suggestions to the AR for the simplification and/or improvement of laws. The Working Group held public hearings with university research centres, legal practitioners, associations representing sectorial interests and the general public, in order to increase the dialogue with the community and to receive their contributions in respect of areas where the quality of the law could be improved.

The legislative role of Iberian parliaments  183

Controversies and problems for parliaments and their legislative activity

However, when we look at the Portuguese and the Spanish parliaments, we realize reality is quite different and there is still a long way to go. For instance, in the 11th legislative term (2011–2015), the Spanish Parliament coexisted with a caretaker government. If until then parliamentary activity had not started until after the investiture debate for the Government, on this occasion the Congress, decided to perform the functions that it understood were incumbent upon it as Parliament duly established, according to its powers, although with the limitations entailed by the coexistence with a caretaker government. Therefore, committees were set up, non-legislative proposals (resolution proposals with non – legislative content) were discussed in the Plenary, as well as non-government bills tabled by the Chamber’s PGs. Additionally, the 2015 report by the Ombudsman was examined, the debate on the whole of the proposal for the reform of the Statute of Devolution of an Autonomous Region was discussed and a Royal Decree was ratified. However, the Government’s refusal to be subjected to the Chamber’s oversight, since it considered that there was no relationship of confidence between an acting Government and the Congress of Deputies, together with its refusal to appear before the Chamber beyond what it considered could be required from a caretaker Government, resulted in all PGs, except the Government’s, tabling a proposal to raise a conflict of competences before the Constitutional Court, in the understanding that the Government’s refusal had prevented the Congress from performing the oversight function enshrined in the Constitution, thus defying, among other provisions, article 66 of the Spanish Constitution. This proposal was adopted in the plenary sitting held on April 6, 2016, and thus the conflict of competences was raised before the Constitutional Court, no sentence having being issued to date. Majorities supporting one or another initiative fluctuate, so that the Executive’s implementation of its legislative agenda is not guaranteed, nor that of the draft Budget bill or any other, to the point that a Royal Decree was not ratified and therefore repealed, something that had only happened during the Constituent Legislative Term with Royal Decree 1/1979, of January 8, whereby the essentially necessary time for the work of the Barracks Confinement Central Board (Journal of Sittings of the Plenary of the Congress of Deputies, February 6, 1979) was extended, and during the 8th legislative term with Decree-Law 1/2006, of January 20, modifying tax rates of tobacco products. On the other hand, as we pointed out before, the Government has made an intensive use of its vetoing power regarding non-governmental legislative initiatives. This situation calls for constant negotiation and to trying to reach agreements on the different fields, agreements to cement legislative policy.

184  Cláudia Ribeiro et al.

To this end, during the current legislative term several study ­committees and sub committees have been set up, with a view to their drafting reports after hearing experts and stakeholders and analysing the documentation conveyed by competent bodies and institutions, so that they can be used for new regulations. However, to date only the report by the Sub Committee on a State Pact regarding Gender Violence has obtained the support of a large majority of the Chamber, whilst the Sub Committee for the Study and Definition of a National Justice Strategy has concluded its works without drafting a report, and the Sub Committee for the State Social and Political Pact for Education has come to standstill, having most of the groups decided to stop attending its meetings due to disagreements on its evolution, which has resulted in the lack of the necessary quorum to adopt any agreement. While it is true that during this minority Government period the Parliament’s legislative activity has increased, the Government’s obstructionism through its vetoing power has likewise increased. And although this situation has led to the establishment of deliberation bodies to attain consensus, the old parliamentary obstructionism techniques have been revived in those cases when their work did not obtain the necessary agreement. This situation is different from that of prior periods, when, despite the fact that the Government was supported by a large parliamentary majority, thanks to which it may have enforced its own initiatives, it decided on some occasions to promote an initiative stemming from the Chamber itself with the greatest consensus possible, so that this initiative was endorsed by a forced legitimation. That was indeed the case with the two reforms of the Constitution of 1978 adopted to date: the proposal for a constitutional reform of article 13.2, tabled jointly by the Socialist, Popular, Catalan (CiU) Izquierda Unida-Iniciativa per Catalunya, CDS, Basque (PNV) and Mixed Groups; and the proposal for the constitutional reform of article 135, tabled jointly by the two majority groups in the Congress, Socialist and Popular, reform published in the BOE number 233, of September 27, 2011. In Portugal, an ad hoc committee, created in parliament in April of 2016 to enhance transparency in public life and, therefore, to fight the lack of trust crisis, has not been able, until now, to reach an agreement to regulate lobbying practices, to create a Code of Conduct for Members or to modify the register of conflict of interests of Members and public office holders. This demonstrates the difficulties in reaching consensus, or even simple ­majorities, in these areas.

Conclusion

Despite the growing importance of the scrutiny function in parliaments and of the emergence of new key players contributing to the legal framework (UE, regulatory authorities, local and regional authorities), the

The legislative role of Iberian parliaments  185

legislative function is still the main competence of the Spanish and of the Portuguese parliaments and must be considered as a priority. Their influence on the legislative process is shown both in their ability to initiate legislation and to propose amendments to bills introduced, particularly in the case of Portugal. The number of approved bills in both parliaments illustrates this, although better lawmaking remains a goal to be achieved. Both parliaments had similar political situations, with minority governments forced to have negotiations in parliament and increasing this institution’s power. The Spanish and the Portuguese Parliaments are decisive for negotiating legislative coalitions and policies. The other side of this coin is that fragmentation of legislative power is reflected in the quality of laws, because they often result from difficult compromises and the text is always compromised to reach to an agreement. It also contributes to the lack of unity and coherence of the legal system. Despite having a bicameral parliament, the Spanish situation is not so different from the Portuguese one, since the Cortes Generales are not a real federal parliament and the Senate has limited powers concerning legislative policy. Still, one significant difference between the two parliaments is the dominance in Spain of government bills in the parliamentary agenda and the power of government to veto parliamentary bills, which has no equivalent in Portugal. In fact, in the Portuguese Parliament, we may have exactly the opposite: parliament has the power to call government’s decrees-law back to parliament to change them or to suspend their enforcement. Disaffection towards institutionalized political class has resulted paradoxically in a greater political involvement and a revival of the interest in politics, which brought new forces into parliament thus integrating external players. Social media, in turn, are now used for direct follow-up of parliamentary players. ICT represents opportunity and problems for both parliaments: it allows citizens engagement but, at the same time, it gives rise to movements of rejection of parliaments and denial of their representative legitimacy. It can also lead to important laws being blocked and to the ­opposite situation: a passage of laws motivated by private interests. On the other hand, the pressure from social media may lead to some laws being passed outside the conventional participation procedures and the public parliamentary debate. Thus, new technologies have irreversibly altered the way politics is conducted, as well as the relationship between parliamentarians and citizens, but they have not replaced representative democracy. So far, new technologies have not increased citizens’ involvement in the Spanish legislative procedure, although they have contributed to its publicity and transparency. However, in the Portuguese parliamentary lawmaking, citizens have a stronger and more important role, with several tools to participate in the legislative process. Both parliaments have regained a central role in the political system, but there is a long path ahead in both parliaments to achieve better regulation.

186  Cláudia Ribeiro et al.

Introduction

Together with representation, accountability is a building block of democracy. Regular elections are instruments for citizens to hold their representatives accountable (Manin 1997). In parliamentary democracies, legislatures are an electoral chamber of the executive (Cox 1987). In return, legislatures have a constellation of mechanisms to keep tabs on the executive, such as parliamentary questions, interpellations, inquiry committees and so forth. These mechanisms help to curb informational asymmetries between the parliament and public opinion, on the one hand, and the executive, on the other hand. Parliamentary accountability implies that the executive explains and justifies its actions and policy decisions to the parliament. If the policy-­ making outputs of the executive are not aligned with the median preferences of the legislature, the latter can sanction the executive by using, for example, no-confidence voting, an institutional resort designed to dismiss the executive. Portuguese and Spanish parliaments share similitudes not only in their institutional design but also in their historical development. However, the two countries also have differences that are worth examining. Does ­Portugal have a different model of parliamentary accountability because of its semi-presidential model? What are the most relevant accountability institutions in each country? To what extent has the institutional structure changed since the inception of democracy in the 1970s? Does bicameralism make a difference in the Spanish case? Over the past decades, principal-agent approaches have been successfully used to analyse institutions in liberal democracies. A chain of delegation is created when voters elect institutional agents such as the president or the parliament. In parliamentary systems, a second step in the chain of delegation is set up when a prime minister and its cabinet is invested as the agent of the chamber. In reverse, a chain of responsibility in a parliamentary democracy goes from an agent (the executive) to its principal (parliament) as a logical consequence of the delegation of power. This chain of responsibility
