Manuel Sánchez de Dios

188  Manuel Sánchez de Dios

is complemented by another that goes from the parliament (the majority) to the voters (Strøm 2000). In delegation models, agency loss is a consequence of diverging preferences between the parliament and the executive. Furthermore, asymmetric information bolsters agency loss insofar as the executive enjoys access to privileged information sources. Typically, the cabinet tries to maximise its preferences at the expense of the parliament’s, where the median position also encompasses opposition parties. Consequently, in parliamentary government, there is a conflict of interest between the two branches – that is, between the cabinet and the opposition. Furthermore, there is a competitive relationship between the executive and its supporting party/parties (the majority). Opposition parties use parliamentary accountability procedures to fight asymmetries of information, i.e., hidden information or hidden action. Monitoring and reporting requirements are some of the primary institutional mechanisms that principals use to keep tabs on their agents. There are three ways to gain information about the agent’s action: direct monitoring, attending what the agent says about his activities and attending a third-party testimony about agent action (McCubbins and Schwartz 1984). In parliamentary democracies, select committees and ad hoc inquiry committees help principals in their direct monitoring actions. Parliamentary questions and hearings of government members and civil servants are examples of tools designed to obtain direct information from the agent. Citizens and interest groups act as fire-alarms if agents dissent from the principal’s preferences. Ombudsman is also useful fire-alarms in fighting delegation perils. In monitoring the executive, opposition parties must decide, first, which accountability mechanism fits better their preferences regarding the outcomes they want to reach. That is, there is a strategic decision to take by opposition parties that should consider if their goal is to compete directly with the executive and the majority, or to cooperate with the government in order to modify the content of policies. For example, an oral question on the floor opens a general debate that nowadays, thanks to technology, can be seen by voters on TV or in social networks. Consequently, an oral question is a good way to criticise the government actions and to show to the public opinion a competitive attitude on the part of opposition parties. An example is the Prime Minister’s Question Time in the UK that is considered an adversarial party battle (Moran 2005). In a similar vein, a debate of no-confidence vote gives the opposition the opportunity to present an alternative programme under a competitive strategy trying to reach the public opinion and, if possible, to topple the government. On the contrary, if the strategy of an opposition party is to give orientation or indirrizzo to the government, it can propose a temporary committee about policy with the aim of producing a report that evaluates policy and can help the government to make reforms. This is a typical

Parliamentary accountability  189

mechanism of the National Assembly of France (Chevillier-Hiver 2000). Alternatively, the parliament can propose a resolution in a standing committee to give orientation to the executive, as usually happens in the Nordic countries (Damgaard 1992; Raunio and Wiberg 2003). In this chapter, we take into consideration how opposition acts under different forms of party government: majority, minority and coalitions. The success of an oppositional strategy is measured according to the theory of retrospective vote (Fiorina 1981) regarding support to a government and its majority in elections. It is also relevant to evaluate the erosion of a minority or coalition government when they are forced to call for early elections, and, finally, the most important indicator is alternation in government.

Parliamentary accountability in Portugal

The Portuguese Assembleia da República (AR) works in a semi-presidential context, based on a triangular relationship between the President, the Cabinet and the Assembly (Leston-Bandeira 2002; Costa Lobo 2012 ­Leston-Bandeira and Fernandes 2015). As a consequence of its institutionalisation, since the early 2000s, the relevance of the Portuguese parliament changed considerably. Today, the AR is a strong institution, with full autonomy and a pivotal position in the political system insofar as discussion and deliberations have become its main activity (Leston-Bandeira and Freire 2005). The Assembly has the competence to scrutinise the actions of the ­Government and public administration. MPs have the rights to question the Government, to request and receive any information or document, to form committees of inquiry and to vote on motions of confidence in the Government. Government members shall attend the Assembly sittings specially convened for scrutiny. If summoned, they are also obliged to appear before parliamentary committees, although they may also ask to participate in committee proceedings at their own volition. Besides, Government members have the right to attend and speak during plenary sessions of the ­Assembly. In what follows, we describe accountability mechanisms in detail in Portugal. Parliamentary written questions are an essential oversight instrument in legislatures. In the Portuguese case, there are two types of written questions. First, questions to the government (perguntas ao Governo) are meant to keep tabs on the executive. Their relevance increased after the 2005–2009 parliament, during which MPs tabled 6.617 questions, of which 75 per cent were answered. Second, questions to public administration (requerimentos) are meant to exert oversight on the work of public servants. Starting in the 1990s, they address primarily technical and administrative matters to which the government is obliged to answer within 30 days. These are cheap and easy mechanisms to obtain information. However, the public administration tends to have low responsiveness – answers late and in only 50 per cent

190  Manuel Sánchez de Dios

of the cases. Around 70 per cent are directed to the central administration and the rest to the local government. Parliamentary questions have been increasingly used since 1987. To be sure, the fact that MPs can table as many questions as they wish makes this a costless and easy instrument to use. Furthermore, because they are published online on the parliament’s website, parliamentary questions help MPs to advertise their productivity. Recent work suggests that, towards the end of the legislative term, MPs increase the number of parliamentary questions to help them obtain information to use in the electoral campaign (­Fernandes et al. 2018; Fernandes et al. 2019). The 1985 reform of the AR implemented Question Time as an accountability tool. However, oral questions to the government were usually described by MPs as being monotonous and uninteresting due to their lack of frequency and flexibility (Vitorino 1988; Leston-Bandeira and Freire 2005). The 2007 reform heightened the importance of Question Time (Filipe 2009). The Prime-Minister must stand before the Plenary fortnightly. Importantly, opposition parties have agenda-setting powers during Question Time, as well as the prerogative to open the debate. Ministers must go to the floor to answer questions at least once per legislative term. Table 11.1 shows the number of questions answered by the Prime Minister to be relevant, while minister’s answers are low and insignificant.

Table 11.1  Parliamentary accountability in Portugal (2011–2015)

Source: www.parlamento.pt

Parliamentary accountability  191

Interpellations (interpelações ao Governo) have been considered as the principal scrutinising mechanism of the AR. They consist of a broad debate on the general programme of government or sectorial policy issues, drawing high media attention. Unlike Question Time, the content of this debate is not flexible, that is, it is not possible to ask questions on unexpected topics. Each parliamentary party group is entitled only to two interpellations per legislative session. Due to their great public repercussion, motions are the most public and solemn mechanism of accountability. Importantly, all motions involve a relevant debate on the overall political responsibility of the government. In general, there are two types of motions: the vote of no-confidence (moção de censura) and the vote of confidence (moção de confiança). To rationalise the use of vote of no-confidence, parliamentary party groups do not have the right to present a second vote of no-­confidence in the same legislative term, if their first vote fails. Although the vote of no-­confidence is relatively frequent in the AR, only in 1987 was the use of this instrument consequential by toppling Cavaco Silva’s minority government. Furthermore, there is a third type of motion, specific to the debate on the government’s programme. As Leston-Bandeira and Fernandes (2015) show, the opposition has the opportunity to put forth a motion to stop the government from taking office by rejecting its programme. Since democratisation, this instrument has only been successfully used twice: in 1978, to reject a presidential-initiative government, and in 2015 to reject Passos Coelho’s ­minority coalition. There are also some other general debates on the floor that are becoming more and more relevant with time. Opposition parties can use them to keep tabs and criticise government policies. First, the annual debate on the state of the nation (Estado da Nação) wherein the Prime Minister has to answer questions. This debate must take place in one of the last meetings of the legislative session. Second, urgency debates and current debates that permit to discuss unexpected issues promptly. There are also thematic debates to consider on government policy. To hold the government accountable on current affairs, MPs can use PAOD (Periodo Antes da Ordem do Dia) debates – that is, a period at the start of each plenary session wherein parliamentary party groups and the executive can make political statements. Typically, opposition parties use this instrument to criticise the government. MPs also use this period to make protest votes to dissent from their parties, with the aim to voice local concerns from their constituents. To help combat moral hazard, the Portuguese parliament uses resolutions as an indirizzo procedure. In doing so, the AR “recommends” the government to take some decisions or to formulate a policy. Such a mechanism is designed for the legislature to relay its median position on a policy-issue to the government.

192  Manuel Sánchez de Dios

Next, we turn to committees as an accountability mechanism. The AR has 12 permanent committees, whose powers include the creation of sub-­ committees to deal with specific policy-areas (see Chapter 5). Committees have high importance in monitoring the government insofar as ministers must appear before committees at least four times per legislative session. Furthermore, committees can summon ministers, junior ministers, and civil servants for additional hearings. In addition to permanent committees, ad hoc committees – set up to conduct an inquiry into a specific subject – enjoy vast investigative powers. The accountability function of ad hoc committees raises two issues. On the one hand, these institutions have the power to define their task and to operate independently from the plenary. On the other hand, ad hoc committees are strongly criticised if the legislative majority uses them for political purposes. Ultimately, the counter-majoritarian nature of ad hoc committees of inquiry stems from the public debate they generate and the attention of their issues from the media (Vitorino 1988). Furthermore, the final report of the committee needs to be approved by a majority on the floor. Since 1993, committee hearings (audições and audiencias) have developed as a natural consequence of the general heightening of committee importance in the internal organisation of the chamber (Leston-Bandeira 2004). These hearings are a resource to obtain direct information from the government, the administration, interest groups, and even the citizenry.

Opposition strategies of Portuguese parties

Over the last decades, there has been a constant increase in parliamentary accountability in Portugal. Opposition parties have grown more and more active in their use of institutional prerogatives to keep tabs on the executive, in particular, in their use of written questions, question time and making indirizzo. For example, in the 1987–1991 legislature, there have been only 91 resolutions, against 1,586 in the 2011–2015 term. Table 11.2 shows the use of accountability instruments in Portugal depending on the type of government. In majority governments, opposition parties rely more on question time with the Prime-Minister, special debates on the floor. Furthermore, we can see that opposition parties have focused on showing policy alternatives as a way to confront the government. In the case of minority or coalition governments, written questions, committee hearings and resolutions emerge as the most relevant accountability strategies. Our data suggest a distinct behaviour of the left and right blocs. Indeed, when the Socialist Party is in opposition, the use vote of no-confidence and proposals to put forth ad hoc inquiry committees tend to increase. Furthermore, in those periods, these are a higher number of committee hearings as well as resolutions to give indirizzo to the government. We can conclude that the Socialists’ opposition strategy has been to polarise the political debate.

Government declarations Party declarations Majority declarations Proposals of resolutions

Hearings committee Inquiry committees Written questions

194  Manuel Sánchez de Dios

Turning to the opposition strategy of the rightist parties, our data suggest that ministerial rate of answers to parliamentary written questions is higher. Debates with ministers are more frequent when the right is in the opposition. Consequently, the opposition strategy of the Social Democrats may be characterised as follows. On the one hand, parliamentary party groups and MPs look for precise information by written questions. On the other hand, they confront the government directly by debating its policies on the floor.

Parliamentary accountability in Spain

A distinctive feature of the Spanish Cortes Generales is that they are an asymmetrical bicameral parliament. Overall, the lower chamber, the Congreso, is much more critical than the Senado. Most of the legislative and policy-making powers are concentrated in the lower chamber. However, the upper-chamber has an active role in accountability processes. Similar to the lower chamber, in the Senate there is regular question time, there are written questions and demanding administrative reports. Furthermore, the Senate can make use of interpellations and approve motions on the floor. The committees of the Senate have similar resources to the Congreso. Ultimately, as Table 11.3 suggests, a fair assessment is that the amount of accountability procedures is similar in both chambers. Let us now turn to a detailed description of the functioning of accountability mechanisms in Spain. Parliamentary written questions are fundamental in the Spanish context if anything due to their large number. The number of questions has been growing steadily since the 1982–1986 parliament when 9,200 were tabled. To be sure, MPs do not face any constraints in the number or content of their questions. Political parties do not control how MPs use parliamentary question. Written questions offer two advantages. First, roughly 90 per cent of them obtains an answer. Second, answers are typically exact. In Spain, written questions must be answered in 20 days. If not, MPs have the right to ask them as oral questions in the committee arena. In recent years, administrative reports requesting information to central public administration have become an important accountability mechanism. By and large, such reports give more precise information than written questions insofar as they contain the technical details of the decision-­ making process, expunged of the political jargon and spin of written questions (Guerrero 2004). The high demand for administrative report has had a pernicious consequence: overwhelmed civil servants tend to devalue these requests because they do not have the resources to deliver comprehensive reports (Guerrero 2004). Question Time in Spain is a necessary procedure to fight information asymmetries, although it does not have a similar political impact than in Westminster parliaments. Every week, a total of 24 oral questions must be answered in

Parliamentary accountability  195

plenary. The Prime Minister must take, at least, three questions. Parties have the prerogative to make a number of questions that is commensurate to their size on the table. Small parties, though, are protected – ­regardless of their size, they have the right to ask one question.

196  Manuel Sánchez de Dios

Oral questions make up for a good accountability mechanism for opposition parties to influence public opinion (Capo Giol 2005). Parties view them as part of their vote-seeking strategies to build an electorally attractive profile, particularly given the role of TV and YouTube in relaying question time to average citizens. Oral questions in committees are an efficient mechanism to monitoring policies. Although they are numerically significant, their efficacy is limited since less than a half are answered. At least two hours a week are devoted to debate questions and interpellations and, on some occasions, the time spent is doubled. Questions can be answered by ministers, junior ministers, and public officials. Over the past few years, it is becoming more and more common to see high public officials answering questions. Interpellations are a traditional accountability mechanism in Spain. Each week, after question time, some interpellations are debated. A general debate ensues, which ends in a vote, thus making this an extremely efficient mechanism for opposition parties. Each party can table a limited number of interpellations per legislative term depending on its size. Non-law propositions on the floor (also called resolutions) are usually tabled after a government communication and are debated similarly as motions. There are also non-law propositions in committee. Non-law propositions play an essential role, and their use has been increasing steadily. Non-law propositions and motions are related to the Italian idea of indirizzo, which means influence in parliamentary terms. Indirizzo activity is very high when there is a minority government in Spain. These procedures are used to combat moral hazard because they frame and give structure to the executive action. Committee hearings can happen at the request of the House, the committee, or the executive. Usually, opposition parties request them for accountability purposes. Committee hearings are crucial due to the specialised nature of committees. Moreover, the fact that committees have both legislative and control capacities makes them a central mechanism for partisan coordination (Mattson and Strom 1995). In committees, opposition parties can ask questions to the executive, approve non-law propositions, and summon the presence of people. Ad hoc inquiry committees offer a powerful arena for accountability insofar as they can summon any citizen to provide input. Since 1994, they have been open to the media, which typically has consequences for way MPs engage in parliamentary business. That is, there is an inverse relationship between the openness of committees and the extent to which MPs and parties are willing to cooperate. In the Spanish case, inquiry committees are difficult to set up because of executive resistance. Indeed, observers note that rules of procedure are seemingly made to protect the executive (Flores Juberias 2003: 583). Consequently, they tend to exist in minority government situations, when there it is easier for the opposition to bargain with the

Parliamentary accountability  197

government in exchange for occasional support in policy-making. For all their difficulties, the political and media importance of inquiry committees is becoming more relevant. The use of vote of no-confidence and vote of confidence is infrequent in Spain. To be sure, the vote of no-confidence is rationalised according to the German model – that is, it involves a constructive notion insofar as the proposing party needs to adduce an alternative head of government. Together with the investiture vote, both institutions protect and promote the existence of minority government. A vote of no-confidence was approved in 2018. Another general debate in which Prime Minister participates is the one on the “state of the nation” introduced using communication of the government. It should be annual, but it is not always the case. The Congreso de los Diputados also debates the annual reports of the Ombudsman (Defensor del pueblo) and of the Audit Office (Tribunal de Cuentas) that, unlike Portugal, have a delegated power by the Cortes Generales to scrutinise administrative action.

Opposition strategies of Spanish parties

During the process of democratic consolidation, minority governments prevailed in Spain. Consequently, the parliament emerged as a debate arena, where parties bargained and exchanged benefits (Powell and Powell 2000). However, from 1982 through 1993, the PSOE majorities conduct to a loss in the centrality of parliament as well as a weakening in its ­policy-making powers and public recognition (Paniagua Soto 1997). Since 1993, minority governments prevailed again, heightening the political capacity and influence of the parliament. A distinctive feature of Spanish minority governments is the role played by small nationalist parties. The pivotal position of these parties determines the process of parliamentary accountability, in that their opposition strategy is more often cooperative than competitive. Similar to Portugal, accountability intensity has been increasing over time. Table 11.4 displays differences between majority and minority governments. In minority governments, there are higher levels of accountability. Specifically, there have been more motions and resolutions approved on the floor and in committees. As mentioned above, the creation of ad hoc inquiry committees is also more prevailing under minority governments. Table 11.4 further suggests the increasing relevance of indirizzo, giving orientation to these executives, in minority governments. The primary agents of the indirizzo are the small nationalist parties that, while supporting the government, develop a cooperative opposition strategy to obtain policy payoffs. In minority governments, written questions tend to have higher answer rates. Moreover, oral questions in committees tend to be more debated in committees than in majority governments, as do oral questions on the floor,

Table 11.4  Number of accountability procedures held per session, Spain (1982–2015)

Parliamentary accountability  199

designed to show the opposition’s strategy to the public opinion. These indicators suggest that, by and large, minority governments are more subject to accountability. On the contrary, under majority governments, there is a decrease in all sorts of accountability procedures. The number of motions, resolutions on the floor and in committees is less than half of that of minority governments. In a nutshell, under majority governments, opposition parties look for precise, detailed, and specialised information to help them formulate policy alternatives. In the 1982–1986 and 2011–2015 legislative terms, Spain lived under ­majority governments. Over these two periods, the main opposition parties underwent massive internal crises, which hampered their capacity to make effective use of accountability mechanisms. To be sure, if the opposition is highly fragmented and polarised that curbs its capacity to hold the government accountable. Next, we turn to an analysis of the opposition strategy of rightist parties in Spain. To fight information asymmetries, rightist parties use motions, oral questions in committees and hearings on the floor. Furthermore, in the case of the three minority Socialist governments, indirizzo procedures are also relevant because of the activity of the small nationalist parties. The oppositional strategies of leftist parties have been characterised by a higher number of writing questions and hearings in committee. In particular, hearings of the executive members have been very numerous as well as temporary committees were also relevant. Opposition parties aimed mainly to fight information asymmetries using specialised arenas. Under rightwing minority governments, leftist opposition relied on numerous resolutions. The government’s dependence on the parliamentary support of small nationalist parties suggests that the opposition can influence government policies via indirizzo procedures. In a different vein, under rightist majority governments, leftist opposition turned to interpellations as a mechanism to display its disagreements publicly with the government, forging a competitive opposition strategy.

Conclusion

Overall, since democratisation, parliamentary accountability has grown steadily in Iberian legislatures. The Portuguese model of accountability is characterised by great importance of general debates on the floor. A purely competitive criterion mainly orients opposition parties – that is, they want to forge an alternative electoral profile for vote-seeking purposes. In doing so, opposition parties want to take advantage of the regular alternation of parties in government and by the succession of the various forms of party government (majority, minority and coalition). The Spanish Cortes have been “highly active” in controlling the executive and, as a result, one can say that the Spanish democracy has a high vitality in making accountability work. Direct monitoring is highly expanded

200  Manuel Sánchez de Dios

and, moreover, there is well-developed specialised scrutiny at the committee level. Together with indirizzo procedures, these mechanisms help curb moral hazard problems. In both countries, the number of written questions and requests for administrative reports is high. The number of written question is higher in Spain than in Portugal, while the contrary happens to administrative reports. Portuguese MPs tend to rely more on information received from public administration. Unlike Spain, where majority governments block them, ad hoc inquiry committees are a very relevant mechanism of accountability in Portugal. The debate on the creation of a committee is not relevant in Spain, but it is in Portugal. In both countries temporary committees and subcommittees are common. Their function is to report on policies and to operate as a mechanism to fight information asymmetries and moral hazard problems. Both countries have developed the use of committee hearings to obtain information. The use of this mechanism is more prevailing in the Portuguese Assembleia da Republica than in the Spanish Congreso. Indirizzo procedures ­directed at giving orientation to the government are standard in both countries. The primary determinant of accountability in Spain is the alternation of two parties, sometimes with a majority government and others with a minority one supported in parliament by nationalist parties. In Portugal, alternation of the right-wing coalition and the Socialist Party (sometimes with a minority government) has been the basis of parliamentary government. Such alternation has introduced a dynamic of highly bipolar competition between the two hegemonic parties that determines their opposition strategies. In Spain, the format of party government leads to two different opposition strategies: small nationalist parties are cooperative while big national parties are competitive. To sum up, a comparison of Iberian legislatures suggests that both countries have a similar accountability structure. However, the Spanish case has more developed accountability structures, particularly at the committee level. In the Portugal case, the existence of a popularly elected president is only relevant during the government formation stage. As of the 1982 reform, the executive is no longer accountable to the President. In that regard, in the two countries, the executives are only accountable to the parliament. In the Spanish case, bicameralism is relevant insofar as the Senate is very active in keeping tabs on the executive.

Section V Relations with other bodies

Introduction

Much has been said concerning the “democratic deficit” of the European Union (EU) since the first elections of members of the European Parliament [EP] by direct and universal suffrage in 1979. Furthermore, the role of national parliaments (NPs) in the European political arena has evolved dramatically in these last 20 years, with the Lisbon Treaty, in particular, inaugurating a whole new era, with the attribution of subsidiarity control, although in a limited way, to NPs. Indeed, the coming into force of the ­Lisbon Treaty has greatly modified the role of the EP, as well as that of NPs, in the activity of the EU. Doctrine has highlighted the central role that the Treaty reserves both for the EP and for the NPs (Mangas Martín, 2008, p. 338; Remiro Brotóns, 2008, p. 42). Article 10.2 of the Treaty of the EU [TEU] states that

Citizens are directly represented at Union level in the EP. Member States are represented in the European Council by their Heads of State or Government and in the Council by their governments, themselves democratically accountable either to their NPs, or to their citizens.

It also explicitly states that “NPs contribute actively to the good functioning of the Union…”. Even with this explicit commitment in placing parliaments at the political centre, there are still voices that denounce the lack of democracy in the EU. The problem is, surely, that the law alone is not enough for institutions and citizens to feel they are jointly responsible for political action in the EU, some practice that attests to it is also necessary. To check if this is the case in Spain and Portugal, this chapter analyses the instruments and procedures used by their national parliaments, and the Autonomous Communities in the case of Spain, to participate in EU decisions. In 2001, Maurer and Wessels classed the Iberian legislatures in the weakest category within their typology, as slow adaptation parliaments, that is, those parliaments less intervening into the EU polity. This chapter explores whether this is still the case or whether these parliaments have become more active.

12 The participation of the Spanish and the Portuguese parliaments in the activity of the European Union

Elviro Aranda Álvarez and Paulo Almeida Sande

206  Elviro Aranda Álvarez and Paulo Almeida Sande

The role of the Spanish and Portuguese parliaments in the European Union

As Chapter 3 showed, Spain joined the European Union in 1986 together with Portugal. Since then both parliaments have had an important role in the consideration of EU matters, although this varies according to the size of government majority. There is a parliamentary political direction of European affairs that drives the legislative and executive actions of the government in relation to the European institutions. That is to say, the government’s political direction needs to be combined with the democratic principle that is especially expressed in parliament and its activity so that, without prejudice to the executive powers in European decisions, the parliamentary democratizing participation remains open. At the moment of the accession, both Iberian Parliaments were a recent democratic construct, with barely 10 years of existence, after over 40 years under a dictatorship regime. Except for the year previous to the accession, the European integration debate rarely took place in parliament. The years after accession were still marked by the decision-making duo EC/Council of Ministers, with a strong intergovernmental nature, from which parliaments, either EP or NPs, were virtually excluded. The transposition of directives was a part of parliament competence only as a post-facto ratification procedure. There was no a priori discussion and authorization, no real scrutiny of the European decision-making process, the democratic control of the executive by parliament was thus weakened – hence the “democratic deficit” of the EU. Modifications came upon very soon after, triggered by the growing consciousness of that “democratic deficit”, not just a theoretical concept but a real problem and a delegitimizing situation. Soon after the accession, ­Portuguese MPs from different parties specifically focused on the conditions of the integration process, conscious of the fact that Portugal, a recent member, had a long way to fully integrate and understand the way European institutions worked. Law 28/87 laid down the terms for the scrutiny of European affairs by the Assembleia da República [AR], specifying the information the government was forced to make available to Parliament, including the government’s position on European policies. The new law established that “The Government shall consult the AR on the positions to be taken on the different European institutions”, particularly in matters involving parliament’s powers, notwithstanding its right to give opinions on matters of legislation or policy orientation of its own initiative to accompany the whole process of insertion of Portugal in the European communities. From then on, government had to send parliament the necessary information, including all legislative initiatives and projects. Parliament should each year evaluate the participation of Portugal in the Communities; the AR even had a role on structural funds, within the framework established by the national budget. This law would be revised in later years, each time strengthening the AR position in this scrutiny process.

Participation of Spanish and Portuguese parliaments  207

The first decade of accession was a period of great change in the EU and its legislative procedure. Norton identifies three major challenges (1996): first, greater integration, with ever growing law making at the level of the Community, more powers bestowed upon the European institutions (and a new role to the elected EP); second, the explicit requirement of the involvement of parliaments in EU affairs laid down by the Maastricht Treaty and third, the two promising new pillars of the newly denominated EU, although both intergovernmental, extended the organization powers and competences of the European integration to new spheres. Norton considered that the response to those challenges concerned four different variables: the Constitution, the political culture, the party system, norms and practices of parliaments and parliamentary workloads. At that time, the Portuguese Parliament was “more concerned with the process of decision-making of European legislation than with the process of transposition”, which, nevertheless, largely escaped its control (Maurer and Wessels, 2001, p. 363), leading to a major role of the ex ante control. This was the period in which the “communitarian” doctrine framed the classic concept of the so called “democratic deficit”, in a sense that is not used any more:

By transferring competences from States to supranational institutions, like the Council or the Commission, the Treaties exclude them from the control of the respective parliaments. The fact that a substantial part of those powers are not submitted to the control of the EP makes them unaccountable to any democratic control. This is the “democratic deficit”. (Sande, 1994, p. 378)

Later on in the decade, the Amsterdam Treaty adopted Protocol 13, ­empowering NPs. It stated that all Commission documents “shall be promptly forwarded to NPs of the MS” and reaffirmed the importance of making proposals for legislation available to NPs; it nevertheless “carefully avoided imposing an obligation on member state governments” (Barrett, 2018, p. 9). The author calls the Amsterdam provisions “limited” in respect to the powers of parliaments. The most important aspect of those provisions was their binding nature. Also relevant was the confirmation of COSAC [Conférence des Organes Spécialisés dans les Affaires Communautaires – that is, conference of the EU NPs’ European Affairs Committees]: created outside the legal institutional framework of the Union at the Conference of Parliamentary Committees for Union Affairs of Parliaments of the EU in Madrid, in 1989, and gathered for the first time in Paris in November of that same year, COSAC was finally recognized as the legislative cooperation body by excellence for exchange of information and best practice between NPs. Nice followed. It ought to be the Treaty that concluded the institutional reform started in the IGC that led to the Maastricht Treaty, but things did not go well, and only Lisbon, some years later, would accomplish that deed, despite its insufficiencies. Notwithstanding, NPs received a new impulse towards a deeper and more intervening relationship with other institutions

208  Elviro Aranda Álvarez and Paulo Almeida Sande

(declaration 23, “Declaration on the future of Europe”). The Conference (the IGC) “calls for a deeper and wider debate about the future of the EU”. NPs are called upon the discussions as “interested parties”. The process should address, among others, “the role of NPs in the European architecture”. The issues to be addressed were intended to improve “the democratic legitimacy of the Union and its institutions, in order to bring them closer to the citizens”. Later on, on the wake of the discussions of the Convention, the so called “Barroso Initiative” promoted a political dialogue with NPs. The new approach, part of the so-called plan D, became the cornerstone of the EU/NPs’ relationship, with more than 100 meetings between Commissioners and NPs in 2006 alone; in May, the EC communication to the ­European Council “A Citizens’ Agenda – Delivering Results For Europe” (COM (2006)211), foresaw the Commission obligation to send all new proposals to NPs, with an invitation for reactions. The Portuguese Parliament was, from the start, one of the most active in issuing opinions to the attention of the EC, as explained later on in this chapter. The Lisbon Treaty went even further than IGC 2004, the basis for the failed Constitutional Treaty, in associating NPs to the European process. Article 10 of the Lisbon Treaty declared that “The functioning of the Union shall be founded on representative democracy” and article 12 acknowledged the role of NPs as part of the institutional framework of the EU, explicitly and formally recognized in the letter of the Treaties: “NPs contribute actively to the good functioning of the Union” through a series of mechanisms and rights (article 12). Two protocols annexed to the EU Treaty defined how NPs should intervene in the institutional framework of the Union, particularly in the ­decision-making process. They concerned the information to be supplied (“Information for NPs”) and inter-parliamentary cooperation (Protocol 1) and the important application of the principles of subsidiarity and proportionality (Protocol 2). The AR participation and its legal responsibilities in the accompanying of European affairs was changed accordingly to the mentioned evolution of the European legislation. Law 43/2006, which revoked and replaced law 20/94, established the new subsidiarity principle opinion (not yet binding) in article 3. It also took in consideration the new role of parliament proposed by the “Barroso initiative”, rendering it much more effective either in the relationship with the government and its European policies or in respect to the interaction with the European institutions, in particular the EP.

The role of parliamentary committees and the plenary in the scrutiny of EU affairs

The role of EU affairs specialized committees

The Joint Committee for the EU is the central parliamentary body in the Spanish institutional system for the knowledge, inspection and parliamentary

Participation of Spanish and Portuguese parliaments  209

direction of the activity of the EU and the participation in said policy of the Union by the Spanish Government (Delgado Iribarren, 2015, p. 255). The importance of this parliamentary body is so great that we could even say that, in European matters, it is more important than the Chambers’ plenary sessions, which in many cases only intervene at the request of the Committee. The Joint Committee for the EU is more than 30 years old, time during which it has transformed itself to contribute to the political will to improve the democratization of the Union. According to the law, Spanish governments should inform it of all legislative proposals received from the Commission or other European institutions as soon as possible. It should also present to the Committee a report on the content of the legislative proposals of the Union that would have an impact on Spain so that the Committee, if considered it appropriate, can open a debate in which the Government participates and gives explanations to the Chambers. The law also provides that the government regularly submits a report on the decisions taken at the European Council for the purpose of scrutiny by the Plenary. The Joint Committee has been gaining political prominence over the years and the reinforcement of NPs given in the Lisbon Treaty has been a new and important incentive for its “centrality” in European matters. However, it is still possible to make improvements for better scrutiny by the Joint Committee, by making it less dependent from the parliamentary majority, with minority parliamentary groups having a greater role and the agreements of the Committee being taken more into account. Likewise, the European Affairs Committee (EAC) in Portugal is the centre of Parliament’s intervention in European affairs. This is recognized by law as at the core of European matters scrutiny, with the government being obliged yearly to present a report about the relationship between Portugal and the European Communities. The Committee has the responsibility to present an annual report on European legislation and the orientation of European policies, as a basis for debate in the plenary, having also to meet regularly with Portuguese MEPs. The Portuguese parliament monitors and assesses the participation of Portugal in the EU mainly through EAC, giving opinions on relevant matters which fall into its reserved legislative mandate. Initiatives and proposals, as well as government information and requests for an opinion, are channelled through the Committee, being then sent to the competent parliamentary committees. After the approval of a report by the Committee, it pronounces itself through a written opinion, pronouncing the compliance of a certain initiative with the principle of subsidiarity or simply giving an opinion about the documents submitted. Opinions approved by the EAC are sent to the President of Parliament, the government and the EU. If there is a reasoned opinion determining the proposal in question infringes the subsidiarity principle, the EAC submits it to the Plenary, where it is debated and approved or rejected.

210  Elviro Aranda Álvarez and Paulo Almeida Sande

The role of the plenary

The role of the Spanish Joint Committee for the EU is so important that, although it is common practice in parliaments to give primacy to the Plenary to the detriment of committees, certain plenary interventions in the Spanish legislature are only agreed to at this Committee’s request (Salazar Quiñonez, 2015, p. 197). However, the Plenary still plays an important role, as follows:

a The government must appear before the Congress of Deputies after each ordinary or extraordinary European Council meeting in order to inform the groups and debate with them (Article 4 Law 8/1994). To do this, the government must previously send a written report of the most relevant issues discussed in the Council meeting. b The Joint Committee may request the President of either of the two Chambers to hold a debate on a legislative proposal in the respective Plenary in which the government will participate (Article 3.c Law 8/1994). c The Plenary may intervene in the subsidiarity procedure (Article 5.2 of Law 8/1994) if the debate and vote on the opinion is taken up by the ­Plenary of one of the Chambers. In which case, the Joint Committee must submit its opinion to both Chambers. d The Plenary can oppose legislative initiatives taken by the European Council to authorize the Council to pronounce by qualified majority instead of unanimously, or to adopt legislative acts by the ordinary legislative procedure instead of adopting them through a special procedure. If this decision is ratified by both Chambers, it is sent to government for their knowledge.

As explained above, the EAC is the Portuguese’s key body for scrutiny of EU matters. However, despite the EAC’s centrality, the plenary has a concrete role to play, as follows:

a It is its responsibility to adopt legislation to transpose EU directives, as proposed by government. They are discussed and voted first by the different committees according to their subject area, and then debated and voted in the Plenary. b The annual list of “enhanced scrutiny” initiatives is submitted to the Plenary, resulting into a resolution. c A plenary debate, with the prime minister, is held before each European Council. For example, four of these debates were held between September 2017 and September 2018, with the first one having taken place in June 2012. d A plenary debate with the government takes place at the beginning of each Presidency of the Council to discuss its priorities, as well as the executive annual report on EU relationships.

Participation of Spanish and Portuguese parliaments  211

e A State of the Union debate following the one held at the EP by the President of the EC takes place every year (usually in September). f A plenary debate concerning all aspects of the EU economic governance, the European Semester and the Stability and Growth Program, is held every year, also with the government.

The role of other parliamentary committees

The existence of a specific committee for European affairs generates an organizational problem within the scope of the Spanish parliament. Committees are implemented according to subject areas (e.g. Defence Committee, Health Committee, etc.), but when a Committee is created with more of an “institutional” and horizontal function, such as the Joint Committee of the EU, a problem arises of a conflict of roles. To solve this problem, Law 8/1994, says that “The Committee may request, through the Committee of Congress, that another Committee or other Committees of both Chambers inform in advance concerning a specific issue”. In practice, however, this is not always linear, particularly in the cases of foreign policy, security and ­defence, which are discussed in the Foreign Affairs Committee, with matters related to justice and internal affairs in the Justice and Home Affairs Committee and those related to economic and monetary union in the Economy and Finance Committee. In Portugal, each parliamentary committee has a role in European affairs, depending on the contents of the matter – and the draft legislation. The horizontal nature of the EAC may sometimes become a problem, especially because it is its responsibility to distribute the relevant initiatives and legislative proposals received to the respective parliamentary committees. Those committees then produce a report for EAC. Reaffirming its authority and prevalence in European matters, Law 21/2012 establishes that whenever this Committee’s opinion diverges from the subject areas committees’ reports concerning the break of the subsidiarity principle, its view prevails.

Parliaments’ participation in inter-parliamentary cooperation

Inter-parliamentary cooperation is recognized in Articles 9 and 10 of Protocol 1 of the TEU. Article 9 calls for both the EP and NPs to jointly organize and promote “effective” and “regular” inter-parliamentary cooperation within the Union. In addition, Article 10 recognizes that COSAC may direct to the EP, the Council, and the Commission any contribution it deems appropriate. In particular, it establishes among the tasks of this Conference: the exchange of information and good practices between NPs and the EP and its specialized committees, and the organization of inter-parliamentary conferences on specific topics, in particular to discuss matters of common foreign policy and security.

212  Elviro Aranda Álvarez and Paulo Almeida Sande

In Spain, the Joint Committee participates in the exchange of information and the convening of meetings of the cooperating bodies that are in operation (COSAC, Conference of Presidents, Committees, etc.) within the scope of the EP and NPs. However, the intervention of Spain in some of these organizations has not been of great importance. For example, Spain did not have a representative in COSAC until 2010; the Joint Committee has not participated in many pilot COSAC programs on the application of subsidiarity; nor has it participated in the pilot tests on the Europol and Eurojust agencies (XLI COSAC, biannual report, Prague 2009, p. 232). The Spanish Parliament even stated that it was against the holding of regular debates on these matters (XLII COSAC Madrid 2010, p. 41). That is to say, the Spanish parliament has limited itself, at least until 2013, to attending the meetings of COSAC to carry out exclusively an exchange of information. Spanish participation has however taken place in other fora such as the Conference of Speakers of the Parliaments of the EU. Unlike Spain, Portugal’s participation in different inter-institutional fora and bodies with the EU has been longstanding and extensive, as patent in the following:

1 The AR is an active member of all inter-parliamentary platforms, and an extensive user of IPEX, the parliamentarian platform for information exchange at the European level. 2 The Conference of Speakers of the Parliaments of the EU is one of the most important fora for inter-institutional parliamentary cooperation. Held annually since 1980, it became an autonomous forum in 1999 responsible for coordinating that cooperation. 3 Portugal has been an active member of COSAC since its setting-up in 1989, with several of its meetings taking place in Portugal ever since. The last time COSAC gathered in Portugal was in 2007. The reform that would become the Lisbon Treaty was at the centre of its discussion, with the AR President underlining the role of NPs under the new Treaty. For the first time, the President of the EC, Durão Barroso, Portuguese, addressed the plenary of COSAC. Portuguese deputies have contributed extensively to the debates and conclusions of COSAC’s over 50 plenary meetings. In 2015, Maria J. Rodrigues (MEP) was keynote speaker in the debate that decided to invite the European Council to improve the reasoned opinion procedure and introduce a “green card” in the political dialogue. From January 2007 to the end of June 2008, the AR participated in the presidential troika of the Secretariat of COSAC. Plus, many other inter-parliamentary meetings and activities take place in this context. 4 NPs have representatives in Brussels, the so called “Antennas”. Each Member-Sate can decide to place them in order to reinforce their presence with the European institutions. The AR created this position in 2008, filled by a parliamentary official. Portugal was the

Participation of Spanish and Portuguese parliaments  213

32nd Parliament of all 40 chambers to establish a permanent representative in Brussels (before Spain, in 2010), and has had three such representatives.

The intensification of parliamentary cooperation goes both ways – from ­Europe and its institutions to NPs, as well as in the other direction, ­depending on parliaments’ initiative in deepening and diversifying such cooperation. The Portuguese Parliament has actively sought to reinforce these, such as, for instance, in 2011, when the President of the AR invited three EP committees to participate in an ordinary meeting in Portugal to consider the decentralization of the EP’s workload. But, as Dias Pinheiro puts it,

(…) NPs nowadays engage not only with their traditional and most obvious counterparts (national governments and EP), but also among themselves and with the EC, in a constant learning process that should lead to a consistent search for the operational, innovative and ­solution-oriented ways to strengthen their role in EU affairs. In this context, the other trend that can be identified (…) is a shift in the motivation that drives this cooperation, gradually evolving from a combination of efforts to produce a negative output by blocking proposals on the basis of a breach of the subsidiarity principle in a subsidiarity-obsessed way to a new dynamic process that actively proposes new paths and solutions. (2017, p. 103)

Even though annual reports on the activity concerning European affairs are explicit about the effort and motivation of Portuguese MPs in parliamentarian cooperation and European drive, the different perspectives of NPs make the prospect of a more constructive and effective process elusive.

The consideration of EU legislative proposals by the Spanish Parliament

All draft legislative acts, wherever they come from, have to be sent for knowledge and opinion, where appropriate, to the NP. The secretariat and the spokespeople of the Spanish Joint Committee of the EU then carry out a preliminary examination of the draft legislative acts sent by the institutions of the Union. Law 24/2009 establishes that as soon as the Congress of Deputies and the Senate receive a legislative initiative from the EU, they send it to the Parliaments of the Autonomous Communities, without prejudging the existence of autonomic competences, for the purpose of their knowledge and that, if applicable, they can send a reasoned opinion to the General Courts. To give this opinion, the Parliaments of the Autonomous Communities have a period of four weeks from the referral of the European initiative (Articles 6.1 and 6.2). They can also ask the government for a report on the conformity of the legislative act with the principle of subsidiarity.

214  Elviro Aranda Álvarez and Paulo Almeida Sande

The intervention of national and regional parliaments

The relationship between national and regional parliaments, in relation to the Early Warning mechanism isn’t always very straightforward, for a number of reasons. First with the concept itself of a NP, something raised when talks began about the Early Warning Mechanism. Working Group I of the Constitutional Convention dealt with the matter in depth and raised two major problems: how to manage countries with unicameral and bicameral parliaments and how to organize the intervention of regional parliaments in decentralized states. The solution was to attribute two votes to each parliament, so that if they have two chambers, each would have a vote. However, a third option has also arisen, such as the case of Spain, whereby NPs manage this matter through a Joint Committee of both chambers, in which case this is the body that makes the reasoned opinion. Equally important is the issue of how regional parliaments intervene in decentralized states. The Protocol makes an express mention of the matter when it says in Article 6 “It will be for each national parliament or each chamber of a national parliament to consult, where appropriate, regional parliaments with legislative powers”. However, this raises problems:

a The first problem is of a formal nature. When and how are the regional parliaments consulted? The national parliament is the one that has to open such a consultation, but bearing in mind that the time period for issuing the reasoned opinion is short, this could generate significant problems. In the Spanish case it was decided that the legislative act of the Union is referred to the regional parliaments ex officio, so that the procedure is not stopped in the national parliament at its outset, and the regional parliaments can issue an opinion in a period of four weeks from Parliament’s referral of the European legislative initiative, which can then prepare their opinion knowing the position of the regional parliament. b The second is of a substantial nature. What are the effects of the opinions of the regional parliaments? It seems that the requirement is of mere consultation, from which it can be deduced that they do not condition the position of the national parliament. However, we could find ourselves with a legislative act of the EU that by reason of its matter is the exclusive competence of one or several Autonomous Communities, which would lead us to have to rethink the question. The thesis maintained here is that regional parliaments cannot condition the action of a national parliament, which is also the case here. However, the latter should take into account the position of the opinions of those parliaments which represent the political position of those that have competence in the matter. The paradox that arises here is that the national parliament issues a reasoned opinion according to political criteria, but in the event that the competence on the matter belongs to the Autonomous Communities, it could result that, although their parliaments

Participation of Spanish and Portuguese parliaments  215

have elaborated their policy position on the legislative act of the EU, in the end it would be the national parliament that would fix the position of the Spanish State according to the political criteria of the state chamber.

Evaluation of the process of considering EU legislation

The Spanish Parliament has produced 254 Processing Reports, with 10 ­Observation reports and 14 Reports of infringement. An analysis of these reveals the following conclusions: The brief time available to the different institutions carrying out the scrutiny – eight weeks in general – has introduced a level of work in the Joint Committee for the European Union that almost monopolizes the meetings of its secretariat and spokespeople, as well as Committee’s sessions of the Plenary. This intensity of work was already recognized in the conclusions of a report launched in the 9th Legislature, which said that it would be more appropriate to establish an office to organize the development of all work related to this matter. Although parliamentarians individually or through parliamentary groups can present evaluation initiatives, the practice has been that it is the meeting of the secretariat and the spokespeople that decides which issues should be studied and submitted for evaluation. Obviously, everybody knows that this entails a “prejudice” in accordance with the principle of subsidiarity of all those acts that are considered not necessary to be reviewed. A previous judgement, made by a body of “government and functioning of the ­chamber” – the Committee’s secretariat – that does not have sufficient political powers to decide on such matters.

On the intervention of the government in the issuance of reports

The first thing that must be pointed out is that Law 24/2009 establishes that in order to issue a reasoned opinion, the Spanish Parliament can “request the government to report on the conformity of the legislative act with the principle of subsidiarity”. That is to say, the request for the government report is optional, with the government not being obliged as regards its preparation and issuance. Parliamentary practice has been for the Committee’s secretariat to send the corresponding legislative initiative to the government for its information. The government’s position at this time is of interest, although it has not yet revealed clear criteria for action: in 2010, of 22 requests for a report, it issued 10; 14 in 2011, of 19 applications, and 16 in 2012, of 22 applications. Therefore, the most frequent is the issuance of the report and, in most cases, it does so in accordance with the European legislative claim. In many of them, it even stops to point out the opportunity of the new norm and the conformity with the principles of subsidiarity.

216  Elviro Aranda Álvarez and Paulo Almeida Sande

When the government issues a report to a legislative act, in which it states that it may affect the principle of subsidiarity, the common practice is that the opinion of the Committee follows this criterion. This is the case of the two opinions of 2011, and in three of the six opinions issued in 2012. In the other three, the Committee also did not act on the government’s criterion since it did not report. What is not common is that the Committee’s position differs markedly from the government’s criteria. The practice of so-called “rationalized parliamentarianism” appears with remarkable clarity here as well. So much so that in the case that gave rise to Report 6/2012 on the “Regulation on the exercise of the right to adopt measures of collective conflict” on which the government reported positively and the appointed Chair negatively, this resulted in the report being rejected by the Committee, with a new Chair being appointed, who issued a positive report, in line with the government and which was finally approved by the Committee. It is noteworthy that in some cases where the legislative initiatives relate to issues of particular importance, such as fiscal policy, capital control, or copyright, the government has responded with silence and has not issued a corresponding report. Of course, the legislation does not oblige the government to send a report to parliament, but it is striking that in cases of such political importance the executive does not want to make a formal statement. See, for instance, Opinion 1/2012 of the “Directive concerning the awarding of concession contracts” in which the government, for the first time, did not report a legislative act of the Union and to which parliament issued an opinion of total disagreement on the understanding that the new norm could affect subsidiarity.

On the position of the autonomous communities in the issuance of their opinions

The first thing to note is the low number of opinions coming from the ­Parliaments of the Autonomous Communities and that a very large majority are in accordance. There seems to be some fatigue towards the issuance of opinions, leading to a decrease in its use. The reasons explaining this may be very diverse: perhaps they understand that the new European regulation does not affect their competences; perhaps they consider that since the Joint Committee is not obliged to follow their criteria, their own reports are of “little use”; surely one reason is the short period of time – four weeks – and the remarkable increase in workload that entails for the respective parliaments to carry out this process. In any case, and leaving aside speculations, what the data shows is that in many cases where the competence of Autonomous Communities can clearly be affected, few of them speak up. For example, in Opinion 1/2012 of the “Directive concerning the awarding of concession contracts”, opinions were only given by the Basque Parliament, the Aragonese Courts and the Assembly of Extremadura, despite this being a matter affecting the competencies

Participation of Spanish and Portuguese parliaments  217

of many other Communities. On the other hand, as already indicated, in this case the position of the Committee was one of disagreement with the criterion of the autonomous parliaments. Another very significant case is that of Opinion 3/2012 of the “Directive on priority substances in water policy” that most certainly affects the public health competencies of many Communities. However, only the Basque Parliament, the Courts of Castile and León and the Assembly of Extremadura reported.

The consideration of EU legislative proposals by the Portuguese parliament

As explained above, all draft legislative European acts have to be sent for knowledge and opinion to the NPs. The AR can, at any time, give an opinion about decisions pending on subjects of its reserved competence within the European decision procedure (art. 161 of the Portuguese Constitution) and about the general orientation of policies and actions of the EU. It can also ask the government at all time for information about the national orientation concerning political issues, particularly those of its reserved competence, and give a (non-binding) opinion about them. Also, since the Barroso initiative in 2006, it started issuing opinions on different matters in the framework of the so called “political dialogue”. And finally, it scrutinizes the respect for the subsidiarity principle in all European legislative proposals. The establishment of the methodology for that scrutiny in Portugal is a competence of the EAC, approved in 2013, and improved upon in 2016. That change, at the beginning of a new legislature (2015–2019), was justified by the need to improve AR’s influence over the European decision process. The implementation of the systematic scrutiny of European initiatives involving EAC and other committees, as well as the hearings with members of government, MEPs, Commissioners and other assures a dynamic and comprehensive involvement of the AR in the European integration process.

The early warning methodology at AR

Like in Spain, all draft legislative acts within the European decisional process are sent for opinion to the AR. After Lisbon, the subsidiarity a priori control became, at least theoretically, the most important intervention of NPs in the EU decision-making process. It became known as “Early Warning Procedure” (EWP). Since 2008, coinciding with the reinforcement of the role of parliaments, the Lisbon Treaty and the AR participation in the COSAC troika (see above), the Portuguese Parliament has systematically led the ranking of NPs in terms of scrutiny of European initiatives and political dialogue. The new EWP enhanced the role of permanent representatives, as well as the importance of the information flux between Brussels and Lisbon, with a major contribution by Portuguese MEPs.

218  Elviro Aranda Álvarez and Paulo Almeida Sande

The steps for this assessment are as follows: the permanent Working Group for Scrutiny of European initiatives, which has five MPs, each from a different parliamentary group, and integrates EAC, receives the documents sent by the EU (as well as government’s requests and information). The Group identifies the initiatives to be scrutinized and submits these to the EAC for ratification, following which the initiatives, whether legislative or non-­legislative, are sent to the competent parliamentary committees. Their opinion, which may also include those of the Legislative Assemblies of ­Portugal’s two Autonomous Regions, is forwarded to the Committee when concluded. This is then followed by a written opinion drafted by a rapporteur from EAC, which is then debated at Committee level and, if a non-­ compliance with the principle of subsidiarity reasoned opinion is adopted, the Plenary meets for consideration and final vote. If not adopted, the opinion is sent to the European institutions and government and placed in IPEX. The whole process must be completed in eight weeks.

Early warning process and political dialogue

Since 2010, the AR, through EAC, annually identifies a number of European initiatives priorities, based upon the Annual Working Program of the EC. They are considered priority for scrutiny. There were only seven in 2010 (AR resolution n.98/2010), six in 2011 and 2012, and then 65 in 2013 (AR resolution n.79/2013), a number that dropped into more reasonable figures in the following years: 24 (2014), 32 (2015), 23 (2016), 21 (2017) and 26 in 2018 (AR resolution n.139/2018). The priority initiatives do not necessarily correspond to the number of initiatives scrutinized; they just identify policies and objectives. In 2018, for instance, some of the initiatives approved include the EU action plan for circular economy, multiannual financial framework, creation of the post of European Minister for Economy and Finances. In some cases (namely the last one), there is not even a concrete legislative initiative, in others several legislative draft acts may be proposed. Portugal ranked systematically first in the overall issuing of opinions, ­either within the context of EWP or political dialogue. In 2011, for instance, according to the EC annual reports, 184 opinions were transmitted, the second most prolific Parliament being the Italian (with 76). That year the EC acknowledged AR as “the most active national Parliament” in the legislative European process (Report, 12th Legislature, p. 7). And yet, in 2006 no opinions were transmitted; in 2007 there were already 19, the fifth more prolific parliament; 65 in 2008, 106 in 2010. In 2013, a new high was reached, 192 opinions, a number that decreased in 2014 (but still 118, representing 23 per cent of the total). Reflecting the new methodology approved in 2013, the number of opinions sent to the Commission in 2015 was 55, still the highest amidst Parliaments/Chambers, and 57 in 2016, third in the ranking, led by the Italian Senate. The number of opinions transmitted to the EC reached a peak of 192 in 2013 and has been decreasing since. The workload became

Participation of Spanish and Portuguese parliaments  219

more reasonable: 63 in 2016 and 64 in 2017 (out of the 1,427 included in IPEX since 2006, an average of 119 per year). On the other hand, in 2011 the Portuguese Parliament considered for the first time, in one case, that the subsidiarity principle had been breached. In 2012 another proposal was considered as in breach. In 2013, under the new methodology approved by the AR, one other proposal was also considered a breach of the principle. The issue of breach of the subsidiarity principle would only then be raised again in 2016. The establishment of a permanent representative of the AR to the EU institutions in 2008 consolidated a more effective participation of the ­Portuguese Parliament, particularly in the process of scrutiny. It is in part due to the network of parliamentarian antennas in Brussels that the exchange of information between parliaments – sharing arguments, ideas and opinions – became possible. The so-called Monti II is a good example of that interaction: following an intensive exchange of information between NPs, through their permanent representatives in Brussels, three AR Committees, including of course EAC, were encouraged to give their opinion: the one from EAC was in support of a breach of subsidiarity. Approved by the Plenary the 18th of May 2012, it became the first “yellow card” in the sense of Protocol 2. The AR had not envisaged at first such a position, but the vote assured the necessary threshold of 18 votes (Dias Pinheiro, 2012, p. 87); that change of position was in part due to the extensive exchange of communication between Lisbon and the network of permanent representatives in Brussels. In short, between 2011 and 2018, the AR approved four reasoned opinions declaring the non-compliance of proposals with the principle of subsidiarity:

• Proposal for regulation COM (2011) 560, approved the 4th of November 2011. • Proposal for regulation COM (2012) 130, approved the 18th of May 2012. • Proposal for regulation COM (2012) 788, approved the 26th of February 2013. • Proposal for regulation COM (2016) 53, adopted the 15th of April 2016.

AR and the regional parliaments

Some of the issues raised above in relation to the relationship between the national and the autonomous Parliaments in Spain, apply mutatis mutandis to its equivalent in Portugal, in relation to its Legislative Assemblies for Madeira and the Azores. Notwithstanding, in view of the differing nature of the two political systems, the Portuguese situation does not raise the same kind of issues as in Spain. The archipelago of the Azores and the Island of Madeira and Porto Santo constitute Autonomous Regions of Portugal, endowed with a legal character of public law, with political, legislative, administrative, financial and patrimonial autonomy. The Portuguese Constitution in its article 227 establishes the powers of the autonomous regions

220  Elviro Aranda Álvarez and Paulo Almeida Sande

and foresees their participation in the European integration process in all matters concerning them. Whenever it is the case, these regions’ legislative assemblies can submit their opinions to EAC, but those opinions are not binding: if the national opinion does not coincide, it prevails, the regional opinions being sent as an attachment to it. This can be exemplified with the recent reasoned opinion on the Proposal for a Directive on the Approximation of Laws, Regulations and Administrative Provisions of the MS concerning Tobacco (COM 788/2012) (2017). The Portuguese opinion considered that a breach of the subsidiarity principle existed, the same opinion of the Azores’ Legislative Assembly, but contrary to Madeira’s evaluation. The national view was therefore sent with all those opinions attached, the one of the AR prevailing.

AR and the government

Parliament’s scrutiny of the executive’s European policies varies considerably. In rare cases, parliaments are effective in determining the negotiation limits the government must follow when debating European public policies within the legislative process procedures, with the most notable case being the one of the Danish Parliament. According to Christensen, this is due to its strong up-stream procedures, which ensures a considerable level of consensus between parties and affected interests (2010). His thesis is simple: in Denmark, the handling of European affairs, including European legislation, is done “up-stream”, that is, issues are dealt “before and during the ­negotiations at EU level”. And “If up-stream control is effective it ensures that parliament is involved at a stage where EU legislation is still in the making” (Christensen, 2010, p. 3). The paradox is that in a country such as ­Denmark, in which European issues have been divisive since the beginning of its accession to EU, consensus exists and EU legislation is not very present in the whole juridical corpus of the country; due to the up-stream control installed since the beginning. In Portugal such up-stream is non-existent or, at least, rather weak. The capacity of parliament to determine the boundaries of government’s commitment in any given EU legislation is, to say the least, limited. Political scrutiny by the AR relies on hearings of members of government, the analysis (post-facto) of the government’s annual report about the Portuguese participation in EU and debates in the Plenary or casual requests for information in specific cases. But this is more or less all and it cannot be compared to the Danish example, in which control is done a priori – when it is more effective, even if it jeopardizes the government’s negotiation margin. Can we then conclude about the Portuguese Parliament – one of the most active in terms of European legislation scrutiny – that it still is, as advanced by Maurer and Wessels (2001), a “slow adaptation Parliament”? In fact, change is of the essence and the Portuguese legislature evolved

Participation of Spanish and Portuguese parliaments  221

tremendously in these last 30 years since the accession. MPs exercise a thorough and comprehensive scrutiny of European legislative initiatives and they are very active, particularly through the EAC, at examining, debating, proposing and giving opinions on European issues of all kind. Thus, the scarce control of the executive “up-stream” simply reflects a political culture that still envisages the executive as the main engine of participation in international bodies. Furthermore, governments are the expression of majorities in parliament, and thus their decisions taken in a rather un-popular context as the one in Brussels are not a matter of public interest. Even when the government, in terms of parliament’s majority, depends on political parties that are against the European integration – as the case in Portugal during the 2015–2019 legislature –, European issues seldom emerge as relevant and even less decisive. Government decides and parliament follows, based upon a common vision of the parties at the centre of the political scene, and that is simply a consequence of the political culture of Portugal. Although the law is seemingly adequate to fill the democratic gap, its enforcement is not always perfect. Indeed, as a political source in the Portuguese Parliament put it, the majority of those acting in Parliament, either MEPs (with the possible exception of those in EAC) or even officials, tend to avoid the European issues, either because they do not know them, or because they are not prone to attract media attention. This does not mean that the Portuguese Parliament has not become much more an intervening legislature in European affairs than it used to be. The number of opinions issued, the presence in Brussels, the interaction and cooperation with institutions and bodies in the EU, the constant activity of the EAC and its centrality, the number of auditions and meetings concerning Europe and the European integration process, confirm it.
