Introduction

Several countries have experienced processes of decentralization, federalization, and devolution in the last four decades. The regional (sub-national) political arenas gained political competences and jurisdiction over a large number of relevant fields, broadly influencing citizens’ everyday life. With those processes, the newly created or transformed regional arenas – and their institutions and elites – became particularly important political entities, interacting with the national statewide ones, and influencing and shaping them in many ways. This is why the study of sub-state or regional arenas and their institutions matters, as well as that of their linkages, cooperation, and coordination relationships with national-wide ones. This chapter analyses how national and regional parliaments interact in Spain and Portugal. These are very dissimilar countries regarding the level of decentralization. Spain is highly decentralized, as the country is organized as a whole in regions with significant autonomic administrative, political, and financial powers; whereas Portugal is not very decentralized, with only two autonomous regions, corresponding to the two Portuguese archipelagos. If the former can be considered as a paradigmatic case of a multi-level political system, the latter can hardly be designated as such. We assess whether the relationship between national and regional political bodies is substantially affected by the level of decentralization, by highlighting the similarities and differences arising across both cases. Notwithstanding its differences, comparing these two cases is of relevance, as they are also very alike regarding its recent political history, allowing to exert some control over the effects of other political variables. As a general consequence of such comparison, there is much more to say about the Spanish case than the Portuguese. In the last four decades Spain became a highly decentralized – de facto federal – multi-layered country. The 17 regions (comunidades autónomas) exert extensive and intensive powers in the most important political domains, designing their own institutions and developing their policies, with administrative, political, and financial autonomy (managing nowadays most of the public expenditure). Regional political elites have also

13 The relationship between regional and national parliaments in Spain and Portugal

Pablo Oñate and Ana Maria Belchior

224  Pablo Oñate and Ana Maria Belchior

developed in these regions, sharing some traits with the national one, but showing some distinguishing characteristics of their own (Oñate 2018a; Oñate and Viera 2018). The Constitution defines the Senate as the ‘territorial representation chamber’, but it does not fulfil this role. It follows party lines in its integration and functioning and does not work as a coordination and integration forum of the multi-level system. Cohesive and centralized political parties are the key leading actors, shaping the relationships between national and regional politics.1 Their parliamentary party groups are (ultra)-integrated-parliamentary party complexes (Heidar and Koole 2000), where a few hands control the activity in the chamber, and individual MPs always follow party leadership guidelines (Oñate 1999; Oñate and Delgado 2006; Oñate 2008). Unlike Spain, transition to democracy did not drive Portugal towards decentralization. Only the archipelagos of Azores and Madeira are autonomous regions endowed with political-administrative statutes and self-­ governing bodies. These autonomous regions are constitutionally granted a broad range of political and administrative powers (Article 227). As the Portuguese Constitution posits, the political-administrative autonomy of these archipelagos does not affect the integrity of State sovereignty (Article 6). The country remains as unitary. What is more, the Portuguese Constitution does not allow the existence of regional parties (Article 51º). Although differently from Spain, as there are no regional political parties, parties in Portugal also play a core role in shaping the relationship between the national and the regional levels. This is partially explained by the fact that the Portuguese parliamentary system is highly cohesive and party-centred, which is enlightened by the tendency for strong party discipline (Braga da Cruz 1988; Leston-Bandeira 2009; Lobo et al. 2012). Notwithstanding the growing relevance of regional political arenas, and the increasing salience of parliaments for political life, the relationships between national and regional parliaments is still an understudied field. In fact, there is no appropriate comprehensive framework to study those interactions. Given the overwhelming leading role political parties play, we will draw from the literature on vertical integration of political parties (Duverger 1954; Janda 1980; Smiley 1987; Dyck 1991; Deschouwer 2003; Hopkin 2003; Thorlakson 2003, 2009, 2011; Pruysers 2014), borrowing some concepts to study the interdependence, linkages and interaction between national and regional parliaments. We analyse the linkages and relationships between national and regional parliaments in Spain and, to a lesser extent, in Portugal (because it is not decentralized but also due to lack of data). To do so, the section “Institutional linkage-mechanisms for coordination and cooperation between national and regional parliaments” investigates the institutional formal linkage mechanisms and their effectiveness. Given the importance of external party organization for parliamentary life, the section “National and regional parliaments’ linkage through political parties’ vertical integration”

Relationship between regional and national parliaments  225

focuses onto political parties’ mechanisms of vertical integration, interdependences, and linkages among their national and regional branches. The section “Vertical integration at the MP level” analyses national and regional vertical integration from the individual or micro-level point of view: MPs’ profiles, multi-level trajectories, and contacts with members at the ‘other’ level in their everyday political activity. The section “Conclusion” presents the main conclusions and findings and suggests further avenues for research in this (under-studied) field.

Institutional linkage-mechanisms for coordination and cooperation between national and regional parliaments

Spain can be considered a federal system. The institutional setting at both levels, national and regional, has been intensively developed in the last three decades. Nevertheless, neither the Constitution, nor the regional Statutes for Autonomy, or the national and the regional parliaments’ Rules of Procedure provide for strong formal linkage mechanisms for cooperation and coordination between them. And as explained above, the Senate does not play that linkage-role. The intense cooperative relationships are developed mainly by the respective executive branches, with interaction between the legislatures of a much smaller scope and significance. Nevertheless, there are five formal institutional mechanisms of interaction between national and regional parliaments, with varying levels of efficacy in fostering linkage and cooperation:

a Senators appointed by regional parliaments. There are 207 senators, directly elected by citizens. Additionally, each regional parliament is entitled to appoint one senator plus another one per million of inhabitants in its respective region (Spanish Constitution (SC) section ­69-5), adding up to 58 senators appointed by the regional parliaments. These appointed senators may be members of their respective regional parliament, although this is not compulsory (except for Cantabria, La Rioja and Madrid). The behaviour of these senators is no different from that of the 207 directly elected: They act by partisan criteria and vote according to their party’s instructions, rather than in territorial terms. In their everyday life, they are considerably detached from the regional parliament appointing them. And when a member of both legislatures, they are usually more devoted to their Senate duties to the detriment of their regional ones. On the other hand, their colleagues senators elected by citizens in the same region can take to the Chamber whatever initiative of interest for the region and interact with regional parliament members in their constituency. Therefore, this mechanism doesn’t provide the linkage and interaction effects it could, given the strength of political parties, with MPs of both, national and regional parliaments, following party lines much more than territorial ones, and

226  Pablo Oñate and Ana Maria Belchior

the lack of connection of appointed senators with their regional parliament (Navarro 2006). b Introduction of bills in the national parliament by regional parliaments. Very few countries vest regional parliaments with this competence, but Spain and Portugal do. In Spain, the regional parliaments have that initiative in subject areas beyond their legal competence or jurisdiction. After approval of the text by a majority in the regional parliament, three of its members defend the initiative in front of the Plenum of Congreso de los Diputados (CdlD). If accepted for consideration, the rest of the procedure is the same as that for any other bill at the CdlD and Senate. The interaction consists formally in defending the initiative in the other chamber, although such initiatives imply more informal interaction ­between both legislatures’ members and parties. This mechanism is not often used and, when so, it is seldom efficient. In fact, it would be much more fruitful to ask the Government or one of the parliamentary groups at the CdlD to present the initiative as if it was theirs. In many occasions, the regional parliament’s initiative is presented so the national legislature has to take a position on specific issues, or controversial ones (specially, when the party majority in the regional parliament is different to the national one): the aim being to get public attention on specific issues at national level. For example, in recent times some of these related to government’s austerity policy, other, more specific, aimed to declare a given area as a national park. In the 10th Congress (2011–2015), 20 of these regional bill initiatives were presented and none of them was accepted for consideration (11 were ­rejected, 4 postponed, 2 lapsed, and 3 withdrawn). These figures are similar to previous Congresses, even if there were more of these (42, 34 and 28, respectively, in the 7th, 8th and 9th Congresses). These figures are self-evident: Rather than efficient multi-level cooperation and interaction, this mechanism aims mainly to get national attention for regional issues or to contest national government policies. c Cooperation agreements between regions: In some cases, they have to be previously approved by the national parliament before being signed by the regions. This kind of cooperation between regional and national parliaments implies considerable informal interaction between both territorial levels, at least between the majorities in both legislatures, in order to get the agreements passed. The increase of different areas for cooperation between regions has raised the number of inter-­regional agreements sent by regional parliaments to the national one for approval (Ridaura 2009). d Regional parliaments’ proposals of Constitutional Court judges. The Senate appoints 4 of the 12 judges of the Constitutional Court.2 In order to take into account the regions’ opinion in those appointments, each of the 17 regional parliaments sends to the President of the Senate a

Relationship between regional and national parliaments  227

proposal of two names. After receiving those proposals, the appointments committee of the Senate presents the Plenum a shortlist of four names (that don’t need to include any of the 34 names suggested regionally). Given the proposals by regional parliaments are mostly driven by party lines, different parliaments suggest the same names according to intra-party coordination. This mechanism is another example in which intra-party coordination overrides potential linkages and cooperation initiatives between regional and national parliaments. e Revisions of the regional statutes or constitutions. Given the regional statutes belong to the so called “constitutional corpus”, their revision needs to be approved both by the regional and the national parliaments. Once approved in the regional parliament, a delegation of their members defends the proposal in front of the CdlD’s Plenum. If the proposal receives amendments in the CdlD’s Constitutional Committee, a joint committee (regional parliament and CdlD) is summed to agree a compromise. The revisions then go to the Senate for its approval. Once again, this linkage mechanism implies mainly informal interaction initiatives, although they are usually conducted through party lines.

In the case of Portugal, the possibility to create autonomous administrative regions was open by the Constitution. Their institutionalization was dependent of being voted by the majority of the Portuguese citizens in a referendum (Portuguese Constitution [PC], section 255–262). That consultation took place in 1998, but the electorate rejected the proposal to regionalize the country. Therefore, regionalization has never come to effect. As we’ve seen above, Portugal only has two autonomous regions: the archipelagos of Azores and Madeira. The cooperation and coordination links between national and regional bodies in both regions are constitutionally granted: The Constitution postulates that the national and regional bodies should cooperate in order to ensure the economic and social development of the autonomous regions, particularly aiming to correct inequalities derived from insularity. The regional political bodies are constituted by a legislative assembly, a regional government, and the regional public administration. They may legislate at the regional level or, through consent, on matters falling within the competence of the national Assembly of the Republic (AR). The scope of the legislative competence is limited, though. They are not able to legislate on matters such as the election of regional MPs, their own political-­ administrative statute, or the framework of regional finances. These are the responsibility of the AR. Besides their own executive competences, they also have regional tax and administrative competences. Furthermore, the regional government is accountable towards the regional legislative assembly (PC, section 225–234). In general, there is not a strong institutionalization of formal mechanisms of interaction between national and regional parliaments acknowledging significant autonomic powers to the latter, as

228  Pablo Oñate and Ana Maria Belchior

in Spain. Instead, there are a set of formal procedures and requirements which grant institutionally some integration of regional parliaments in ­national politics:

a The appointment of a representative of the Republic. Each of the autonomous regions has a representative of the Republic, who represents the national state, embodying the main formal linkage between national and regional parliaments. The representative of the Republic is appointed and exonerated by Portugal’s President of the Republic after consultation with the national government (PC, article 230), with their mandate lasting the duration of the President’s mandate. The formal competences underlying this position allow the national level to have relative control over political power at regional level. Mirroring national general elections’ processes, the president of the regional government is appointed by the representative of the Republic, taking into account the electoral results for the legislative assembly. The representative of the Republic also appoints and exonerates the members of the regional government, on the proposal of the respective president. More importantly, it is the responsibility of the representative of the Republic to sign and publish regional legislative decrees and regulatory decrees. The representative might veto these, triggering a new consideration of the bill. If the regional legislative assembly confirms the vote by an absolute majority, the bill goes through. Through this representative, the national parliament scrutinizes the production of regional legislation, constraining the effective autonomy of the regional assembly. Formally, the representative of the Republic uses their veto power discretionarily, taking into account their interpretation of the bills’ constitutionality. In practice, this somewhat reflects the game of political forces between the continent and the archipelagos. For instance, since 2007 the representative of the Republic of Azores vetoed seven regional decrees, triggering new considerations of these by the legislative assembly. Most of these vetoes occurred when the regional and national governments were from different political factions. No veto has been presented since 2014, when both governments have belonged to the same party.3

This position has lost political relevance, being gradually deprived of its competences since it was established in 1976. For example, it used to have a seat in the national cabinet whenever regional issues were discussed. Nowadays, the veto power is the most relevant competence, but also the most controversial one, so there is some regional pressure to eliminate this position. b Regional representation in the Council of State. Presidents of the regional governments have a seat in the national Council of State (PC, section 141–146). The Council of State is the President of the Republic’s political body of consultation, including representation of key

Relationship between regional and national parliaments  229

political figures (such as the President of the Assembly of the Republic, the Prime-­Minister, former presidents of the republic, among others). The Council’s main competences are to advise on the dissolution of the Assembly of the Republic and of the legislative assemblies of the autonomous regions, as well as on the government’s dismissal. Although the issues the Council advises on are very important, the fact this is a consultative council reduces its impact. Furthermore, the frequency of such consultations is quite low. Therefore, besides having a narrow scope and limited importance to foster regional/national cooperation, the fact that this Council is seldom mobilized hinders its relevance for the cooperation between regional and national levels. c Accountability of the regional assemblies and the representatives of the Republic towards the President of the Republic. Portugal has a ­semi- ­presidential system where the president has veto powers over ­parliament and government. These powers extend to the autonomous regions. Similarly to its power to dissolve the national legislature, the ­president has the competence to dissolve the legislative assemblies of the autonomous regions (provided that the Council of State and the parties represented therein have been previously consulted) (PC, sections 133–140). The president also has the power to appoint and dismiss the representatives of the Republic for the autonomous regions, under the ­government’s ­advice. Although of relevance, these powers are seldom used. An exception was the dissolution of the legislative assembly of Madeira in 2015. This decision was preceded by the exoneration of the president of the regional government and consequently the resignation of the regional government. The president’s dissolution of the assembly was, therefore, more of a formal requirement to elect a new government, than a political decision. d Regional initiative of national bills and referenda. As in Spain, Portuguese regional assemblies can initiate legislation in the national parliament (PC, section 161–170). As said before, this is an unusual competence for regional parliaments. According to the archive of the Assembly of the Republic, the regional assembly of Madeira has presented a total of 197 initiatives since 1976, and the regional assembly of Azores 87. Regional assemblies can also propose regional referenda (PC, article 115), but no regional referendum has taken place so far. e Relationship of the autonomous regions with the judiciary branch of the Republic. This is particularly important in relation to two courts: the Court of Accounts (Tribunal de Contas) and the Constitutional Court (Tribunal Constitucional), two core bodies of the Portuguese judicial system. The Court of Accounts is the supreme body to oversight the legality of public expenditure and the public accounts. The public accounts of the autonomous regions are therefore subject to its reviewing process (PC, article 214). The Constitutional Court reviews the constitutionality of legislation. The representatives of the Republic may submit to the

230  Pablo Oñate and Ana Maria Belchior

Constitutional Court requests to consider the constitutionality of any norm contained in a regional legislative decree (PC, section 277–283). The legislative assemblies of the autonomous regions, their presidents, the regional governments, or one tenth of the deputies of the respective legislative assembly, may also require the assessment of the constitutionality of laws, when the request is based on a claim of violation of the rights of the autonomous regions. These are formal linkages between the regional and national levels, reporting to the regular functioning of the judicial system and, therefore, relatively unimportant to strengthen the cooperation and coordination between both levels. f Revisions of the regional statutes. Finally, and similarly to Spain, revisions of regional statutes should be approved by both the regional and the national parliaments. The statutes can only be revised at the initiative of the regional legislative assembly, through the elaboration and approval of a draft sent to the AR. The revision of the statute is decided by an absolute majority of the deputies in the regional assemblies (PC, article 161). As in Spain, in practice this process is led through party lines. A recent example of the parties’ initiative to revise the statutes was that of a small left party (the BE, Left Bloc) presenting to the Legislative Assembly of the Azores, in April 2018, a proposal to revise the statute of the region, within the framework of the Parliamentary Commission for the Reform of Autonomy. This proposal addressed the need to improve the geostrategic position of the region and its participation in international treaties and was generally justified by the party as a way to increase the autonomy of the Azores region.

National and regional parliaments’ linkage through political parties’ vertical integration

Spanish parliamentary party groups (PPGs), both at national and regional parliaments, follow the general convergence tendency towards the (ultra)-integrated and parliamentary party complexes models of PPG (Heidar and Koole 2000: 10–14 and 259–262). A few number of leaders control all the activity of the group’s MPs and are closely linked to the external party organization (EPO). All PPGs share the same structure at the regional and at the national levels, which is controlled by the EPO. National and regional Spanish MPs are deeply dependant on their parliamentary party groups (both regarding their everyday work and their nomination for the following election). Given the high level of integration and control EPOs exert over their PPGs, political linkages and interdependences between national and regional parliaments can be traced by analysing the degree of vertical integration between the national and the regional organizations of each party. We focus on the four main Spanish statewide parties (Partido Popular [PP], Partido Socialista Obrero Español [PSOE], Ciudanos [C’s], and Podemos

Relationship between regional and national parliaments  231

[Pds]), leaving aside truncated ones (non-statewide parties)4 and United Left (which joined the Podemos lists at the last election). All of these parties are quite subordinate to the national leadership in key domains, even if formally the regional organizations enjoy a wide share of functional autonomy. Linkages and interdependences can be identified through a number of variables, (Thorlakson 2009, 2011; Pruysers 2014), but as Thorlakson (2011) suggested, vertical integration can be measured in three different dimensions: (a) the organizational structure; (b) the nature and functioning of cooperation and collaboration patterns regarding strategies and programs; and (c) ideology, principles, and values. Given the linkage between PPG and EPO, we extend this vertical integration to parliaments.

a At the organization structure level, all four national parties acknowledge in their statutes their multi-level organization structure, and the functional autonomy of their regional organizations. But there is evidence of a large number of linkages and interdependences between both levels in each party: Even if there are differences among PP, PSOE, Ciudadanos and Podemos, their national and regional organizations’ relationships share many relevant elements: names of the national and regional parties, their governing organization structures, the principles, the values and the general political positions contained in the national statutes, as well as the loyalty to the party-as-a-whole; membership (with a common census of members, with identical rights, duties, and fee; statutes and internal regulations); autonomy of the regional branches for the candidate selection, the coalition agreements, the electoral manifestos and the electoral strategies, even if the national governing bodies have a final say (except in Podemos); linkage of the national MPs to their party national bodies, and not to the regional ones that nominated them. b There is also clear evidence of strong vertical integration regarding patterns of ‘soft practices’ (Thorlakson 2009) of coordination and cooperation between national and regional organizations.5 Both upward and downward interactions occur, reinforcing the vertical integration provided by the formal organization structure factors in all four analysed parties: Sharing the regional offices and regional organization, and holding regular national and regional coordination meetings; integrating joint national and regional bodies (or national bodies where regional leaders hold a relevant number of seats); joint national and regional task forces to write the electoral programs or decide on electoral strategy plans; shared electoral polling and survey research, rallies, and endorsing campaigns; and money transfers upwards or downwards between national and regional organizations. Additionally, in all four parties, national leaders exert informal influence over the regional organizations, playing a key role in solving conflicts or appointing leaders, in spite of the formal autonomy of the regional organizations.6

232  Pablo Oñate and Ana Maria Belchior

Table 13.1  Congruence of ideological position in each party according to its voters in 17 regions, Spain

Podemos PSOE Ciudadanos PP

Source: Position of each party by its voters at the regional level. Centro de Investigaciones Sociológicas (CIS) Poll number 3141, Pre-National Election December 2016, questions 14-a and 16 (www.cis.es).

c Shared ideology, principles, values, norms, and general political lines: party programs and manifestos are very similar in all 17 regions and are also similar to those approved at national level. This ideological congruence can be measured by how party voters in each region perceive the party in the ideological 1–10 scale, and the coherence with that same perception at the national level: The positions for each party are similar in all 17 regions, with small deviations from the national position. As Table 13.1 shows, the one with a more coherent ideological image among its voters is Ciudadanos, given it has two basic political messages since its national expansion in 2014: national unity and democratic renewal. But the other three parties also show considerable ideological coherence: the standard deviations of the regional positions are low, as is the variation range of those 17 measures. Each of these parties are one single political project, with basically the same principles, political platforms, policies orientation, and ideology.

Spanish political parties show therefore strong patterns of vertical interdependences, linkages, and interactions, which can be extended to their PPGs in the national and the regional parliaments. There is considerable evidence of that strong vertical integration at the three levels where it can be measured, according to previous findings (Thorlakson 2009, 2011): party structure, patterns of upward and downward cooperation, and identity. Nevertheless, that interdependence is not balanced, but settled as a mechanism of control from above (the national structures), in cohesive and centralized political parties, leaving little autonomy for the regional branches. And it is so to a larger extent after the reform of these four parties’ statutes carried out in 2017. Their PPGs follow the (vertical) integrated and parliamentary party complex models (Heidar and Koole 2000; Thorlakson 2009, 2011): Their leaderships are appointed by the party leadership, and their MPs follow leaderships’ political lines, strategies, and guidelines. As mentioned above, the national statutes of all these parties foresee and design a decentralized organization structure, although main decisions still depend on the central national governing bodies. We can measure the

Relationship between regional and national parliaments  233

degree of autonomy of the regional organizations have: “its capacity to act without constraint or interference from the federal [national] party” (Thorlakson 2009: 162, 2011: 717). Following Thorlakson (2011), in order to do so we can examine decision-making regarding coalitions, manifestos, and leaders and candidates’ selection. In all these four variables, the Spanish national parties allow for low levels of autonomy to their regional branches: The latter are entitled to make their own decisions concerning leaders’ selection (Ciudadanos is even more centralized: the national executive committee appoints the spokesperson and five members of the regional leadership; the PSOE executive committee ‘suggests’ the leader of the PPG to its members). But their proposals regarding candidates, electoral strategies and manifestos, and coalition agreements have to be later ratified (or changed) at the national level, and they always have to be in accordance with the party lines and principles approved by the national bodies. As we know since Schattschneider (1942: 64), “he who can make the nominations is the owner of the party”. Podemos is the only case in which the regional party decisions on these matters enjoy more autonomy, as long as the regional decisions don’t disagree with national party lines, principles, and strategies. In Portugal, parties control the candidate-selection process, selecting MP candidates for the party lists in a highly centralized process. Since regional parties are banned by the Constitution and MPs’ representative mandate is a national one, rather than constituency based, the institutional incentives for MPs to dissent regarding their party, or to converge with their voters in the constituency, are rather low. This is so because re-election as legislators or other party position is in essence dependent on party loyalty. The Portuguese case is not, however, different from that in Spain, and none of our cases are outliers in the Southern European context. Portugal along Greece, Italy, Spain, and Turkey are seen as having party dominated parliaments where MPs have little autonomy (Norton and Leston-Bandeira 2005). As at the national level, MPs elected by each party or coalition of ­parties in Azores or Madeira may form a parliamentary group and they have, alongside the regional government, the initiative of regional legislative decree in matters covered by political-administrative statute. According to the statutes of the two mainstream Portuguese political parties, the Socialist Party (PS) and the Social-Democratic Party (PSD), as well as of the smaller parties, Popular Party (CDS/PP) and the Left Bloc (BE), the organization of the parties in the autonomous regions of the Azores and Madeira is endowed with autonomy, statutes, and their own bodies. In some cases, the statutes formally foresee some kind of regional accountability towards the party national bodies. This is the case of the BE, whose statutes explicitly contemplate that the party’s regional organization is accountable towards the party national governing bodies. The PSD also makes the amendment of the statutes dependent on the ratification by national party bodies. Differently, regarding the Communist Party, the statutes only

234  Pablo Oñate and Ana Maria Belchior

provide the existence of a regional assembly, also called regional congress, in each autonomous region, which is responsible for defining the guidelines for the respective autonomous region. The national/regional articulation is generally done by the representation of regional party members in the parties’ national bodies, as well as by hearing the regional bodies when regional issues are at stake. The parties’ regional organization aims to respond to the geographical and political-administrative constraints arising from insularity. Nevertheless, they remain the arms of national parties, thus to a great extent contributing to the centralization of the political decision-making and constraining regional autonomy.

Vertical integration at the MP level

Vertical integration must also be measured at the MP level. This can be done in different ways, such as examining individuals’ multi-level trajectories (both as party officials and as elected representatives) and their interactions (contacts and meetings with ‘the other’ level) (Pruysers 2014). In this respect, there are data available only for the Spanish case. In Spain, regional and national parliamentarians share personal and political characteristics (Oñate 2018a; Oñate and Viera 2018). Some of these relate to their previous multi-level political experience and trajectories, as party and public officials. This previous experience at other levels provides them with a wider vision and experience which enhances the vertical integration of parliaments. As Table 13.2 shows, nearly two-thirds of the members of CdlD elected in 2016 had previous experience as party official at the regional level, whereas a bit less than one-third of them had that experience at the national level (Oñate 2018a). More than 75 per cent of PP and PSOE national MPs had that previous experience in the regional party. In that election, Ciudadanos and Podemos were elected for the first time to the regional and national parliaments, with their candidates not having previous political experience as public officials, given they claimed being the “new”

Relationship between regional and national parliaments  235

parties, vis a vis the “old” status quo professional ones. Interestingly, a significant number of their national MPs were party officials at the regional level when they got their seats at the CdlD: 66 per cent of Ciudadanos MPs and more than 60 per cent in the case of Podemos. This indicates these parties were not that new, nor that different. They quickly fitted into the general trend of being “professional insiders” or “political party-centred professionals” who have a previous trajectory in their party organization (Cotta and Best 2000: 523). As could be expected, fewer members of the regional parliaments elected in the 2015 and 2016 elections had that kind of party experience. Still, more than half of them had been party officials at the regional level before getting their seat in 2015–2016, whereas a bit more than one-fifth had had a position in the national party structure prior to their election in 2015–2016 (Table 13.2). Although the figures regarding previous experience as an appointed public official at the ‘other’ level are lower, a significant number of MPs had had that experience before getting their seat in 2015–2016: Nearly one in five national MPs had it (23 per cent among PP’s MPs and 35 per cent among PSOE’s). Only slightly more than 5 per cent of regional MPs had that previous experience at the national level before getting their seat in 2015–2016 in their regional parliament. The figures are a bit higher regarding previous experience as an MP at the parliament at the ‘other’ level ­(Table 13.2): nearly one in four members of CdlD had previously been regional MPs when elected in 2016 (35 per cent and 23 per cent among PP’s and PSOE’s MPs, respectively). And still almost 8 per cent of regional MPs did have previous experience in the national parliament. In spite of the party system change in 2015 and 2016 elections, these figures regarding MPs’ multi-level trajectories are not that different from those of previous Legislatures (Oñate 2018b). These “multi-level MPs” contribute with their previous experience, networks, knowledge, and attitudes to strengthening the vertical integration of parliaments in multi-layered political systems and to socializing their MPs colleagues in the multi-level institutional organization culture. Besides the multi-level MPs’ trajectories and background, MPs develop interactions with the ‘other’ level MPs in their everyday political activity. These contacts (meetings, telephone conversations or other kind of interactions) are indeed a relevant mechanism to increase the vertical integration between parliaments in multi-level political systems. As Table 13.3 shows, Spanish regional MPs often interact with national authorities regarding their parliamentary business: More than one-third of them talk more than once a week with members of their own national party leadership; nearly half of them do so with national MPs regarding their committee business; and nearly two-thirds of regional MPs talk more than once a week with their own party national MPs elected at their same district or region (more than one-third of them do so more than twice per week).

(*) Districts or electoral constituencies are the same for regional and national (except for Murcia region; in next regional election, the district will be the same as the one used in national elections).

Most Spanish regional MPs are aware of the relevance of the other levels of government and find the interactions kept with national authorities useful for their different activities in their everyday representative work. There are no significant differences in their usefulness in regards to each kind of activities, as Figure 13.1 shows. Still, MPs find these contacts more useful regarding their relations with society and their law-making activity (6.8 and 6.7, as a standardized mean, respectively, being 1 not useful and 10 very useful). Most Spanish regional MPs report that meetings kept with national MPs elected at their own districts/region were arranged by MPs (either themselves or the national ones), whereas one-third say the meetings were institutionally and periodically fixed. When the result of MPs’ initiative, usually it is mainly initiated by regional MPs: nearly three quarters report they usually ask for the meeting (whereas only 9 per cent say it is more frequent that the meeting is a consequence of the national MPs’ initiative). Anyway, more than half of the Spanish regional MPs consider the frequency of contacts they keep with their national counterparts good enough, seeing no need to increase it. Nevertheless, 46 per cent thinks it would be good to increase those contacts and meetings with their national colleagues. It seems, therefore, that there is quite a high intensity of contacts and linkages between individual MPs from both levels of governance, although for quite a number of regional MPs there is still room for improvement. Nevertheless, being aware of the relevance of the ‘other’ level of governance and the usefulness those contacts for the regional everyday activity doesn’t seem to make them more inclined to move to the national parliament. When asked where they would like to be in five years’ time, one-third of regional MPs see themselves away from active politics; one-third says

Relationship between regional and national parliaments  237

they would like to go on being a regional MP; and only 5 per cent reports they would like to be a national MP. These figures are similar to the number of national MPs with previous experience as regional MPs.

Conclusion

This chapter aimed to analyse the connections between national and regional parliaments in Spain and Portugal. Given the lack of an appropriate framework to study parliaments’ multi-level relationships (beyond Heidar and Koole 2000), we borrowed analytical categories from the literature on political parties and explored those linkages from the point of view of parliaments’ vertical integration, understood as multi-level interdependences, linkages, and interactions (Smiley 1987; Dyck 1991; Thorlakson 2009, 2011; Pruysers 2014). Given Spain and Portugal have a dissimilar multi-level structure, we analysed vertical interaction in three different dimensions: (a) institutional formal mechanisms for vertical integration (macro-level);

0

Figure 13.1  Usefulness of interaction between regional-national MPs, according to regional MPs’ different representation activities. Source: CONREP-2. Own conducted survey to MPs of Andalusia, Cantabria, Valencia, ­Murcia, Navarra, and Basque Country regional Legislatures (fieldwork: March-June, 2016; N = 413).

238  Pablo Oñate and Ana Maria Belchior

(b) political parties’ mechanisms for vertical integration (meso-level), differentiating three levels: organization structure, cooperation and coordination patterns, and ideology and principles (Thorlakson 2011); (c) individual MPs vertical integration interactions (micro-level). The formalized institutional mechanisms don’t seem very effective in either country, due to their early poor design (their regulation was not adapted to the development of decentralization) and political parties’ dominant role, as party lines interfere in those institutional instruments and prevent them from working in a real multi-level way. Contrary to this, both in Spain and Portugal, political parties are very efficient instruments for parliaments’ vertical integration: their almighty role embeds their high degree of strong vertical integration into parliaments. Finally, at the individual MP multi-level dimension, evidence also shows considerable vertical integration in Spain, both regarding their political profiles or trajectories, and their interactions with MPs from the other level. Although vertical integration in multi-layered political systems can be positive to enhance effective governance democratic patterns, it can also be used as “a means of control and intervention from the centre”, as Thorlakson put it (2009: 173). Judging from the way political parties drive their vertical relationships (and the direction of the 2017 reform of the Spanish main political parties’ statutes), the latter seems to be the case in the Iberian parliaments. Further research is necessary in this field, to set a comprehensive theoretical framework, suitable for a deeper understanding of national and regional parliaments (and MPs’) interaction in multi-layered systems. It would also enable explaining how these interactions and interdependencies affect the respective parliamentarian elites (national and regional MPs), and how these institutions interact with political parties in that vertical integration. That framework would benefit from drawing from several sources and building from the merge of literature on parliamentary party groups (updating the seminal book edited by Heidar and Koole 2000) and parliaments’ organization, federalism, and contributions on multi-level party organizations and vertical integration. At the same time, empirical cross-country analysis would improve the comparative approach in this field, certainly still understudied.
