Constitutional Court and Parliament in Spain: ways of interaction and current problems

Background and appointment mechanism

In 1978, Spain opted for a centralized model of judicial review of legislation echoing several European states that already had experience with this model.2 Several reasons justified this choice, inter alia, it was alleged at the time that a constitutional court that centralized judicial review of legislation would: ensure legal certainty; enhance democratic legitimacy vis-à-vis the existing ordinary bureaucratic judicial power; bring specialized expertise to complex constitutional challenges; and entrust to a novel institution, selected by the newly constituted powers, the protection of the Spanish Constitution (SC hereafter), instead of relying on the bygone judicial power bequeathed by the former Franco dictatorship (Ferreres Comella 2013: 215–216). The 1978 Constitution was the result of a complex bargaining process between all political forces after a long dictatorship (Ferreres Comella 2013: 12–24). In these complex negotiations, the idea of reaching a “consensus” on the foundation of the new constitutional regime was paramount. Therefore, the Constitution was left open in a few issues. Some relevant matters were agreed in principle, but not in detail. In the context of this open-ended constitutional text, the Spanish Constitutional Court (SCC) had a key role, particularly in the 1980s, in consolidating and building the core constitutional values of democracy, fundamental rights and political decentralisation enshrined in the Constitution. Hence, the SCC was called to define and develop the “consensual” spirit of the constitutional text. The 1978 Constitution adhered to the predominant postwar European continental constitutionalism and created a standard Constitutional Court.3 The SCC is composed of 12 members, all prestigious jurists from a variety of backgrounds – judges, prosecutors, professors, public officials, lawyers – with at least 15 years’ experience. The Parliament elects eight members – four by the Congress and four by the Senate – with a majority of three-fifths. Furthermore, the Government selects two and the General Council of the Judiciary elects the remaining two.

14 Constitutional Courts and national Parliaments in Spain and Portugal1

Nuno Garoupa, Joan Solanes Mullor and Teresa Violante

242  Nuno Garoupa et al.

The Parliament has a pivotal role in the selection of members. In Congress, rules demand a qualified majority parliamentary majority, which forces dominant parties to coalesce with, at least, the opposition minority party leaders. The Senate elects four SCC members. In 2007, the Organic Law on the Constitutional Court was amended to enable the autonomous communities (CCAA henceforth) assemblies to nominate candidates to fill these four positions. However, the Senate’s difficulty in fully accommodating the CCAA’s interests owing to the shortfalls in its constitutional design and the weakening of the CCAA assemblies’ nomination powers due to a later SCC decision have limited the influence of regional interests in the selection of constitutional judges. Ultimately, these regions’ capacity to shape the Court’s direction is restrained. Candidates are subject to a parliamentary hearing in which their credentials and competencies are scrutinised. In practice, however, the hearing filter differs from the strict control exercised in other legal systems (Ferreres Comella 2013: 217). In several recent SCC renewals, reaching an agreement on candidates has been an arduous process, delaying appointments and forcing judges whose terms had expired to fill the vacancies until the newly elected judges took office. Consequently, the Organic Law on the Constitutional Court was amended in 2010 and now provides a shorter term to be served by judges whose appointments were delayed. Despite the low importance of parliamentary hearings and the deadlocks in recent appointments, the Parliament’s influence on the election of SCC members looms large. Such influence arguably heightens its democratic legitimacy, particularly when the Constitutional Court has to review the constitutionality of statutes coming from the Parliament itself. The same applies to the position of the assemblies of the autonomous communities whose statutes are also subject to the judicial review. Apart from the appointment process, the Parliament’s influence is limited. The SCC members are independent and irremovable during their nine- year tenure in office. SCC judges have the same status of independence as the ordinary judicial power and can only be removed for a good reason. To be sure, the incompatibilities regime forbids SCC judges from exercising, for example, a representative function or a managerial role in a political party, which reinforces the strict separation between the Parliament and the SCC. The Parliament, in relation to the elected judges, partially renews the judges by thirds every three years and, therefore, there is a link between the composition of the SCC and parliamentary majorities. Ultimately, the SC establishes a dynamic doctrine of separation of powers – checks and balances - whereby the different powers interact and control each other – through the Parliament’s power over the appointment of SCC judges, for instance – even if each branch operates separately and independently. Against this institutional framework, which combines interdependence and autonomy in the relationship between the Parliament and the SCC, the latter undertakes its functions as a supreme interpreter of the Constitution. The SCC exercises constitutional review of legislation, that is, it controls the

Constitutional Courts and national parliaments  243

adequacy of parliamentary statutes – and other sources of law with the same rank, including some norms approved by the executive branch and international treaties – within the scope of the constitution, either regarding norms protecting fundamental rights or in reference to other substantive constitutional provisions. The CCAA statutes are also subject to constitutional review by the SCC. Through the review of these statutes, together with national law, the SCC exercises its second most important function: as a referee of conflicts of competence between different levels of government. The SCC has been a critical player in this field because of the open-ended constitutional model of territorial organisation and the complex system of distribution of powers between the central government and the CCAA – with shared competences as a critical mechanism. The Basque Country, Catalonia and Galicia have been the most active CCAA in bringing territorial conflicts to the SCC.4 On exercising these competencies, the SCC can declare unconstitutional the statutory provision under examination, a declaration with erga omnes (i.e. towards all) effects that may eliminate the provision from the legal system. The SCC, therefore, in reviewing legislation against the constitution, acts as a check to the Parliament and constitutes itself as a veto player. However, the role of the Parliament varies, depending on different constitutional procedures envisaged by the SC to promote constitutional review of legislation.

Constitutional litigation

The constitutional challenge in abstract review (recurso de inconstitucionalidad) can be filed by the President of the Government, the Ombudsperson, 50 Congress members, 50 Senators, the executives or the assemblies of the CCAA. Although this mechanism is also in the hands of the majorities and the executive, the constitutional challenge is specifically designed to bolster parliamentary minorities – 50 congress members or 50 senators – and allow their concerns to statutes passed by the majorities to be echoed, which may trigger unconstitutionality claims. Practice reveals that more constitutional challenges were lodged during periods of absolute majority, reinforcing the idea of a mechanism in the hands of parliamentary minorities (Ferreres ­Comella 2013: 228). The CCAA executives and assemblies may also file a constitutional challenge in abstract review when central statutory law breaches their competences. However, CCAA actions challenged by the President of the Government can be automatically suspended at the President’s request, but not vice versa. This is a procedural advantage for the central government that has been often employed over time. As the constitutional challenge is an ex post abstract control mechanism, the legitimate actors can only file the challenge once the statute has been enacted and has entered into force, within three months after its publication in the Official Gazette of the Spanish State (BOE).5 The only exception to the ex post nature of the constitutional challenge is the preventive review of the CCAA Statutes of Autonomy, the highest laws of these regions. It is still abstract control but in the form of a preventive constitutional challenge.6

244  Nuno Garoupa et al.

Ordinary judges in the exercise of their jurisdictional function can raise the constitutional question (cuestión de inconstitucionalidad). When a statute or equivalent source of law is decisive for the outcome of the case, if the judge has cast doubts on the constitutionality of the relevant provision and all possible interpretations to make it compatible with the Constitution have been exhausted, then a constitutional question can be filed in the form of concrete control. In a centralised model of constitutional review of legislation such as the Spanish one, ordinary judges cannot directly disapply statutory law – unlike infra-statutory law, which can be directly disapplied – contrary to the Constitution. Therefore, they must suspend the ongoing procedure and raise a constitutional question to the SCC. The design of the constitutional question seeks to preserve legislation and, therefore, the Parliament’s democratic legitimacy vis-à-vis the non-elected ordinary judiciary. Judicial review of legislation is reserved to the SCC, a specialised institution with indirect democratic legitimacy due to the election of their members by the political branches of government. The review of the constitutionality of international treaties receives special treatment. Ex post control is possible through the constitutional challenge mechanism, but there is also an ex ante mechanism to analyse the compatibility of a treaty with the Constitution before it is ratified. This preventive tool may be used – it is not mandatory – by the Government, the Congress and the Senate. The SCC must review the treaty in light of substantive constitutional norms, while also taking into account the formal rules for ratifying a treaty where the Parliament’s role is crucial in case its approval requires an absolute or simple majority. In this sense, this procedural channel is vital to ensure the appropriate reaction from the Parliament in safeguarding its role in the ratification process of international treaties. However, the decision to trigger the preventive review is in the hands of the plenary of each chamber: minorities are not in charge of the procedure, which may jeopardise their position. Finally, the individual complaint (recurso de amparo) is considered a mechanism of concrete review by which individuals can act when some fundamental rights protected by the SCC are violated. This subsidiary mechanism, which can only be initiated once all ordinary jurisdictional remedies have been completed, can be used against a chain of judgements that do not redress the violation of fundamental rights by public authorities. Most individual complaints target judicial decisions, administrative actions or, at most, general administrative rules, when the violation is not due to legislative action. If the violation is identified at the legislative level, the constitutional issue is dealt with by the SCC en banc – the chamber handling the individual complaint will have to suspend the process. The plenary of the SCC will issue a judgement that the chamber will subsequently use to resolve the individual complaint. This particular procedure is a guarantee to the Parliament, whose legislative action will only be analysed by the court en banc and not by a small section-chamber of the SCC. Within this institutional and procedural framework, the SCC has exercised its functions and developed its relationship with the legislature. In this

Constitutional Courts and national parliaments  245

regard, the SCC’s role has several shortfalls. Beyond improving the appointment process – to give teeth to the hearings and overcome deadlocks – the SCC’s action has been criticised on several grounds. First, the slowness of processes, which may last up to ten years, meaning, more often than not, that the unconstitutionality declaration is well overdue, frequently when there are different majorities in Parliament.7 Second, in high-profile political cases, as the Catalan independence crisis has made vividly clear, the SCC has been perceived as a politicised institution influenced by political parties, thus losing its standing as an independent court.8 Third, some autonomous communities do not feel comfortable with the power of influence and representation inside the SCC, which results in the devaluation of the legitimacy of the SCC’s role as a referee in conflicts of competences within the CCAA framework. Fourth, in the aftermath of the economic and financial crisis, the SCC has faced criticism because of its role before austerity measures. Its case law has shown two main trends. On the one hand, it has been very deferential to the legislator, deciding to push constitutional social rights into the background and, on the other hand, it has reinforced the powers of the central government vis-à-vis CCAA. Finally, having been focused on technical matters, the SCC should become more involved in substantive issues and, thus, play a more active role as a counter-majoritarian institution (Ferreres Comella 2013: 229–230).

Constitutional Court and Parliament in Portugal: from interaction to conflict9

Background and appointment mechanisms

In its original 1976 version, the Portuguese Constitution (PC henceforth) spread the competencies for constitutionality review between two bodies: the Comissão Constitucional (Constitutional Commission), tasked with concrete review, and the Conselho da Revolução (Council of Revolution), in charge of abstract control. Ex ante review and the review of unconstitutionality by legislative omission were also introduced. The exclusive competence for abstract review (ex ante, ex post and by legislative omission) was conferred upon the Council of Revolution, a military body that provided tutelage to the political transition to democracy. The Council of Revolution was also competent to declare the unconstitutionality of a legal norm with general binding force ensuing at least three unconstitutionality decisions delivered by the Constitutional Commission on concrete judicial disputes. A full-fledged system of constitutional review was only introduced in the context of the first constitutional revision in 1982, with the creation of the Constitutional Court10 (PCC) and the extinction of both the Council of ­Revolution and the Constitutional Commission. Despite the creation of the PCC, the mixed control system, combining diffuse concrete control with an abstract centralised model, remained in place. Portuguese judicial review hybrid system is one of the legacies of the

246  Nuno Garoupa et al.

transitional period that preceded the consolidation of democracy. Accordingly, this system entails concrete control by the PCC and all other courts, even courts of arbitration since they are all competent to disapply unconstitutional norms. In order to guarantee the uniformity of constitutional interpretation and the role of the PCC as the last (and specific) instance of constitutional jurisdiction, both the PC and the law have set forth a mandatory appeal for the Public Prosecutor in case an ordinary court disapplies a norm on the grounds of its unconstitutionality or fails to follow a previous unconstitutionality ruling delivered by the Constitutional Court.11

The PCC is composed of 13 judges, all of them with a law educational background. The Parliament elects ten judges whilst the remaining three are co-opted amongst themselves. There is a two-thirds majority requirement for a parliamentary approval of judges, which induces a consensus-based nomination process. If anything, because a single party alone is not able to select and vote a judge. Since September 2007, the parliamentary election of constitutional judges has been preceded by hearings. Although the hearings have not been introduced explicitly for the PCC members, their importance and public impact have grown with time. Like the Spanish case, public hearings of judicial candidates are not as important as other systems, for example, the United States. However, the Portuguese Parliament has increasingly taken seriously these opportunities to confront the future constitutional judges with their stances regarding matters of judicial philosophy and constitutional interpretation. The appointment process is grounded on gentlemen’s agreement between the two major political parties (Socialist Party – PS and Social Democratic Party – PSD) dating back to 1982 (Araújo 1997). The two major parties share the same number of appointees. Traditionally, PS deferred to the Communist Party (PCP) the choice of one judge whilst the PSD deferred to the smaller Centre Democrats (CDS) the choice of another. After the 2015 general election, the Left Bloc (BE) chose one of the constitutional judges, a fact that was received with public animosity by the PCP. Recent changes in government dynamics (see Fernandes et al. 2018) might mean that the Socialists will defer to extreme-left parties the choice of two (rather than just one) constitutional judges in the future. Constitutional judges are independent and can only be removed from office in case they accept a position or undertake an action which is incompatible with the fulfilment of their legal duties or dismissal or compulsory retirement following a disciplinary or criminal procedure. They cannot hold any duties in political parties or related institutions, nor be involved in any political or party activities of a public nature. Furthermore, during their term, any formal affiliation with political parties or associations is automatically suspended under the law. The judges are appointed to nineyear non-renewable mandates and enjoy the same status of standard career judges in regular courts of law. The PCC meets in en banc plenary sessions and sections or chambers, depending on the competencies it exercises.

Constitutional Courts and national parliaments  247

The core competence of the PCC is to verify the constitutionality (and some illegalities) of legal norms. The PC and legislation also assign a vast array of other competencies to the PCC.12 On what concerns explicitly the Assembly of the Republic and the Legislative Assemblies of the Autonomous Regions, the PCC is competent to adjudicate, on final appeal, electoral claims and appeals on internal parliamentarian elections, as well as to rule on appeals filed by MPs concerning their loss of mandate. The PCC is also competent to rule on national and regional referenda proposals as well as to control the financial accounts of parliamentary groups, single MPs or independent MPs. Decisions adopted by the PCC are binding to all public and private entities and prevail over all other courts and authorities. This is particularly relevant concerning the PCC competences on constitutional review of legislation. Such review may relate to concrete review, abstract review or omission review.

Constitutional litigation

Concrete review of legislation takes the form of an appeal of constitutionality (recurso de constitucionalidade) that arises in a concrete legal dispute. It is filed by one of the litigants or, in some cases, by the Public Prosecutor’s Office even if it is not part of the dispute. According to the PC, all the judges shall refuse to apply unconstitutional norms. This stands in contrast with the typical Kelsenian-system that rests upon a reference from the ordinary judge to the Constitutional Court. In Portugal, the abstract review system is centralised, that is, only the PCC has the competence to abstract constitutional review of legislation. However, concrete review is diffuse (i.e. decentralised) in that all judges have the power to deliver a ruling on the constitutionality of legislation. Such decision, unless appealed against, becomes final and binding. The PCC has been framed as a typical Kelsenian-like court with some specificities that last up to this day. The most striking distinctive feature of the Portuguese system is that rulings delivered by the PCC on concrete review only have inter partes effect, restricted to the dispute where the appeal emerges. Erga omnes (i.e. towards all) effects of unconstitutionality decisions are only ascribed to abstract review decisions. In the case of three or more concrete unconstitutionality decisions, the court may also deliver a declaration of unconstitutionality at the request of the Public Prosecutors or one of the justices. The role of concrete review in the formal sources of law is limited and indirect. Importantly, it is fair to say that there is hardly any interaction between the PCC and the Parliament whenever concrete review comes to play. In most cases, the object of review is not the norm on its face, but a concrete judicial interpretation related to the normative semantic force of the legal provision. Whatever the theories of interpretation and separation of powers one may adopt, these situations do not concern a direct relationship between the court and the legislator, be it in the form of a dialogue or a direct

248  Nuno Garoupa et al.

conflict. This feature stands for a relative uniqueness of the Portuguese system of constitutionality review bringing it closer, on what concerns concrete review, to the decentralised American model of judicial review. The focal task of the PCC is the review of constitutionality, which is restricted to legislation. Indeed, the PCC does not have the powers to review other acts of public power, such as judicial decisions or administrative acts. Like the Italian case, there is no mechanism such as the Spanish recurso de amparo or the German Verfassungsbeschwerde. Being exclusively normative in its judicial review competencies, the PCC has however extended its powers to review the constitutionality of legal norms on not only its face but also following the concrete interpretation given by the a quo court. This judicial development of the object of review has enhanced, in practice, the court’s competencies bringing it closer, in some situations, to scrutinize the constitutionality of judicial decisions. The system of abstract review is quite generous on comparative terms. For one, it grants several institutions the power to request the intervention of the PCC. These institutions comprise both legal and political litigants who can employ this power to constrain the course of politics. In this regard, it is particularly relevant the power of the President of the Republic who can initiate proceedings regarding all types of constitutional review and holds the main request powers regarding ex ante reviews on national legislation as we will see below. These powers are per the semi-presidential character of the Portuguese regime and may enhance the PCC’s role as a veto player (Magalhães 2001). Additionally, the Portuguese system does not set forth any time-frame limit on the possibility to request the constitutionality review. It is, therefore, a powerful weapon against not only contemporary majorities, but also any piece of legislation. The system of sources of law is therefore always subject to a possible veto by the PCC in case it is requested to intervene by any of the multiple institutions competent to draw such request. Abstract review may take place before the legislative process has been completed (ex ante review) or afterwards (ex post or successive review). The ex ante review can only be requested by the President of the Republic (or one of the Representatives of the Republic in the autonomous regions if a regional autonomy concern is at stake). This procedure may be triggered to review the constitutionality of international treaties or agreements as well as any statutory bill that has been referred for ratification. In the case of provisions entailed in bills that shall be enacted as organic laws, the Prime-­ Minister and one-fifth of MPs may also request the ex ante review. Several legal and political actors, including parliamentarians, may request ex post abstract review. As far as these are concerned, both the President of the Parliament and one-tenth of MPs may trigger the control procedure. Control of unconstitutionality by legislative omission cannot be triggered by any parliamentarian. Furthermore, findings of unconstitutionality by the Constitutional Court are not binding upon the legislator.

Constitutional Courts and national parliaments  249

Unconstitutionality rulings under ex post abstract review have general binding force whereas under ex ante review they have the mere force of a pronouncement for the unconstitutionality, something that the legislator may overcome if a strengthened majority re-enacts the bill (to today, this only happened once in a case concerning regional legislation). Formally, and apart from the parliamentary election of constitutional judges, the PCC and the Parliament have no direct interaction. The Parliament chooses the members of the court but, once elected, the justices are independent and do not take ownership from the political forces that appointed them. Moreover, apart from the possibility of overcoming a pronouncement of unconstitutionality in the case of an ex ante review, there are no other formal mechanisms that may allow for a dialogue through constitutional review between the PCC and the legislator. Considering that such mechanism has only been used once (in case of a regional bill), its theoretical relevance lacks empirical strength. Furthermore, article 282 PC regulating the effects of the unconstitutionality decisions under abstract review seems to rigidify further possibilities of dialogue. According to par. 1, the unconstitutionality decisions have a general and binding force meaning that the provision at stake is nullified from the moment it entered into force. However, par. 3 allows the court to determine more limited effects according to reasons of legal security, fairness or a vital public interest. Traditionally, this provision was interpreted as allowing the court to issue a policy judgement suspending the unconstitutionality effects until the date the decision was delivered or published. Recently, during the austerity constitutional case law (Canotilho et al. 2015), the PCC opted to suspend the effects of its decision pro futuro, something that was fiercely criticised by some legal scholars (Roque 2012; Blanco de Morais 2013). The case concerned provisions of the Budget Law that enshrined a partial or total suspension of the payment of the holiday and Christmas allowance to public workers and pensioners. Since it was a budgetary provision (i.e. yearly valid) the suspension of the effects of the decision transformed the ruling, in practice, in a mere declaration of unconstitutionality because the provisions were applied in 2012 and the cuts were put to force. However, the PCC never made use of such power again. Considering the criticisms and the emergency context in which it was used, it is unlikely the PCC will be willingly resorting to such power again. The relationship between the PCC and the national and regional Parliaments (particularly the legislative assembly of Madeira) has not always been easy. The criticism to constitutional case law on regional autonomy by Madeira officials is well-known and has even generated constitutional revision proposals drafted by MPs aimed at the extinction of the PCC. At the national level, the PCC has also proved its counter-majoritarian role, particularly in the mid-1990s and, more recently, with the austerity period and the critical unconstitutionality rulings that caused public outrage by the Government and its supporting parties. Such outrage was followed by

250  Nuno Garoupa et al.

international institutions such as the International Monetary Fund and the European Commission. There had never been such an apparent clash between the PCC and other branches of power. To be sure, despite the legal and political controversies, the court’s role as the guardian of the Constitution has been strengthened and legitimised since the country’s ability to straighten its budgetary position was not endangered. In such a critical period, the PCC proved its success by striking a delicate balance between upholding the constitutional supremacy and applying flexible constitutional interpretation during a financial crisis emergency.

Empirical evidence on judicial behaviour at the Spanish and Portuguese Constitutional Courts

Empirical studies about judicial behaviour and constitutional review have a long tradition in the United States (Epstein et al. 2013; Epstein 2016). The Spanish and Portuguese Constitutional Courts have been the object of empirical analysis quite early for European standards (Garoupa 2019). More recently, legal economists and political scientists have used econometric instruments and ideal-point estimations to appraise them. Overall, studies point out that both courts have a strong left-right or progressive-­ conservative cleavage (Hanretty 2012). However, the specific patterns of ­judicial behaviour and empirical determinants vary. In Spain, a pioneering study showed how judges who sided in favour and against specific legislation regarding the two most hotly contested issues at the time (abortion and expropriation of a controversial business tycoon) formed two distinct clusters, progressive and conservative. The study suggested that, over the 1980–1985 period, judges concurred significantly more often with judges of the same cluster, than with those of the other (del Castillo Vera 1987). A later empirical study questioned the actual prevalence of party interests and offered a more nuanced empirical characterisation of judicial behaviour in the SCC (Araújo and Magalhães 2000). The study discussed the importance of unanimous decisions in the period 1980–2001 as well as the institutional constraints that individual judges faced in the SCC. More recently, Garoupa and colleagues (2013) show that political variables, including the progressive (PSOE) and conservative (Popular Party, PP) characterisation, partially (but not entirely) explain the behaviour of constitutional judges in Spain. Looking at 297 decisions (recurso de inconstitucionalidad), for the period 1980–2006, the authors find that 192 were unanimous decisions, 64 per cent of which were decisions for constitutionality whilst 36 per cent invalidated the challenged statute. The patterns do not seem to vary across time and political cycle. Whilst party alignment is, by and large, the dominant determinant of decisions, other considerations seem to matter, for example, the involvement of Catalan and Basque parties. Additional work about the SCC seems to validate the idea that party loyalty and ideology play an important role, but in no way do they fully

Constitutional Courts and national parliaments  251

characterise judicial behaviour in Spanish courts (Ramos Romeu 2006; Garoupa et al. 2012; Mayoral Díaz-Asensio 2013). The issue of federalism and autonomous communities related litigation in the SCC has inevitably attracted significant attention. Given the somehow incomplete nature of the SC, and the political trend to shirk responsibility over contested policies, in particular concerning the balance between national and regional powers, the SCC has been confronted with a controversial role (Sala 2013). Consequently, the role of ideology and party loyalty is tempered by the weighting of critical federal challenges (Sala 2014). Inevitably, judicial behaviour might respond to specific institutional constraints when it comes to historical autonomous communities such as the Basque Country and ­Catalonia (Sala 2011). In fact, empirical evidence (Garoupa et al. 2013) supports the idea that constitutional judges face conflicting and diffused interests when addressing the balance between the two national parties (PSOE and PP) and strong regional parties (e.g. Basque Nationalist Party, PNV, in the Basque Country and the former Convergence and Union, CiU, in Catalonia). Other mechanisms – such as the civil law tradition and legal background – might also reinforce the observed trend to unanimous voting in earlier periods of the court. More recently, a new empirical study focused on cases addressing conflicts of power between national and regional governments for the period 1981–2014 (López-Laborda et al. 2018) shows that there is no general statistical predisposition to favour either side. In this study, 365 decisions on conflicts of competence between central and regional governments for the period 1981–2014 are considered. Whilst the 1990s seems to have low constitutional review, the 1980s and the post-2003 periods have witness strong litigation. The Basque country dominated the earlier periods (although Catalonia, Galicia and other regions also play a significant role) whilst the latter period experienced intense litigation on the matters of Catalan jurisdictional conflicts. Decisions tend to favour the central government, wholly or partially (about 77 percent overall, above 80 percent for Catalan cases and 78 percent for Basque cases). Unanimous rulings prevail (81 percent), with a lower incidence in Catalan cases (about 77 percent) and a significantly higher number for Basque cases (almost 90 percent). The authors suggest that a multifaceted arrangement of ideological, legal and strategic motivations describes judicial behavior in the SCC. In Portugal, the main message from the empirical studies is that knowing how a specific party voted in Parliament on a particular law can serve as a reasonably good, albeit imperfect, predictor of how a judge appointed by that party is likely to decide in the court, particularly in what concerns ex ante review. The first wave of studies focused on the period before the 1997 reforms and detected strong party loyalty as well as other relevant determinants such as career judiciary versus other backgrounds (Araújo 1997; Magalhães and Araújo 1998; Magalhães 2003). The second wave of studies on the PCC updated the empirical methodology and discussed later periods (Amaral-Garcia et al. 2009; Santos 2011; Hanretty 2012; Coroado et al. 2017). Broadly speaking, it confirmed previous

252  Nuno Garoupa et al.

results. Importantly, these studies find that judges appointed by left-wing parties are more likely to vote against the constitutionality of statutes and laws they scrutinise than right-wing justices, even controlling for whether those parties are part of the incumbent governmental majority (and other possible sociodemographic determinants such as background, age, gender). The original leftist ideological leaning of the PC, linked to the specific historical circumstances of its enactment, has been preserved in many respects despite several amendments. Hence, it is not surprising that empirical findings suggest that progressive judges, that is, those appointed by PS and PCP, appear more often as guardians of the constitutional conformity of legislative policy (concerning invalidating statutes). Finally, most empirical studies, after controlling for the ideological leaning of the parties that appointed them, suggest that being appointed by a party in government increases the probability of that judge voting for constitutionality. In sum, ideological considerations and adjustment to partisan priorities seem to operate simultaneously in explaining judicial behaviour within the PCC. An earlier work (Amaral-Garcia et al. 2009) studies 270 preventive abstract review cases for the period 1985–2007. Unanimous decisions are not as high as in Spain, only 84 rulings (around 31 per cent). Unanimous decisions are disproportionately higher in the period 1985–1995 (under a PSD majority in the Parliament). Looking at individual votes, the rate of invalidation of statutes seems to be about 40 per cent, a significantly high figure, both by direct comparison with Spain and by international standards. It also varies widely across ideological lines: judges appointed by PSD and CDS have a rate of 21 per cent while judges indicated by PS or PCP exhibit a percentage of 57 per cent. Austerity legislation (Canotilho et al. 2015) in the complex period of 2010–2016 does not seem to have changed patterns of judicial behaviour, particularly party loyalty, ideological considerations and some inclination to dissenting (Coroado et al. 2017).13 This later work considers 60 abstract review rulings (both preventive and successive) for the period 2002–2016. Only 14 decisions were unanimous (close to 23 per cent). The rate of invalidation of statutes (or votes against constitutionality) is again close to 40 per cent, confirming the pattern identified in previous work. It does not seem widely affected by the particular period of austerity legislation. The authors find no statistically significant difference regarding court outcomes and party alignments between the period 2002–2009 and 2010–2016 as well as for austerity and other legislation within 2010–2016.

Conclusion

The Portuguese and Spanish Constitutional Courts emerged as important institutions in a period of these countries’ history, namely in the transition from a long period of authoritarianism to a new democratic regime. Both have played a significant role in stabilising democracy and institutionally modernising the countries since the 1980s up to the 21st century. However,

Constitutional Courts and national parliaments  253

there are significant differences between Iberian Constitutional Courts, if anything, they offer institutional solutions to different local traditions and political circumstances. The Spanish Constitutional Court has emerged in a political transition from the Franco dictatorship to democracy. The Portuguese Constitutional Court is the final institutional evolution stemming from a revolutionary context in which leftist parties dominated political decisions. Iberian Constitutional Courts have been veto players in many essential circumstances. Still, external institutional variations should be considered when contextualising the role of both courts as veto players. Party systems are somehow different while appointment mechanisms are significantly diverse, thus resulting in different political dynamics. Unlike Portuguese unicameral and centralised system, Spanish bicameralism and decentralisation have consequences for the balance of power (although in both countries, the legislative influence of the executive branch is preponderant). The role of the Portuguese president finds no resemblance to the Spanish monarch. Finally, access to the courts (including standing) is sufficiently different to explain varying degrees of veto playing. For example, empirical studies suggest that the Spanish Constitutional Court is more cohesive (unanimous decisions are about 65 per cent in Spain against 30 per cent or less in Portugal) and more compliant (invalidation of statutes or decisions against constitutionality seem to be about 30 per cent or less in Spain against about 40 per cent in Portugal) than the Portuguese Constitutional Court. These statistical differences have to be contextualised and have little political importance absent institutional analysis. Governments and Parliaments in Spain and Portugal, for possibly different reasons, have transferred to the court controversial and unpopular decisions. Inevitably, this has resulted in making Constitutional Courts critical political actors in some delicate matters. Not surprisingly, empirical studies show that both institutions are subject to a degree of politicisation, although it varies in time and intensity. Recent events – the Catalan process in Spain, and austerity in Portugal and Spain – have posed significant challenges to both courts. These controversial events have been shaping the court and public opinion’s perception of the court in the last couple of years. However, the exact direction of these changes (reinforcement of the court’s influence or political marginalisation) is still a matter of academic and political discussion. The ongoing changes in the party system in Spain, discussed in ­Chapters 2 and 3, will inevitably reshape both courts in years to come at two levels: composition (where old parties may lose ground to new parties) and litigation (the new parliamentary realities are likely to impact petitions for constitutional review).

Portugal and Spain kicked off the third wave of democratisation in the 1970s. In the ensuing decades, the two countries attracted extensive scholarly attention aiming to understand the causes and consequences of the transition to democracy and consolidation. At the same time, political science as a discipline witnessed exponential growth. Scholars dedicated their efforts to produce comparative and case-study research in English on parties, party systems, electoral behaviour, judicial politics, and the impact of European Union integration. Legislative studies lagged in the development of political science in Iberian legislatures. True, in both countries, there have been groundbreaking works on the Assembleia da República and las Cortes ­Generales (e.g., Leston-Bandeira 2004; Coller et al. 2018). Unlike other contexts, for example, the United Kingdom, Germany, or Italy, the development of legislative studies on the Iberian legislatures has been patchy and has not led to a consistent production of research outputs directed at international audiences. However, the past few years have witnessed a watershed in this regard. Today, in both Portugal and Spain there is an ever-growing interest in producing and disseminating knowledge on legislatures to an international audience. This volume makes a state of the art of the study of legislatures in Portugal and Spain. Our collection is inspired in works such as Esaiasson and Heidar’s Beyond Westminster and Congress (2000) where the authors examine Nordic legislatures from a topic-oriented perspective. Herein, we selected several essential organisational and behavioural topics of legislatures and challenged authors to examine Iberian legislatures in comparative perspectives. This collection aims to stand as the reference book in the English language for comparative politics and legislative scholars alike looking for information on Portuguese and Spanish parliamentary politics. Portugal and Spain share more than their condition as Iberian countries. Indeed, their political development and organisation have extensive similarities that make them ideal for conducting comparative work. As Field and Gray and Goes and Leston-Bandeira show in their contextual chapters, both countries have an electoral system that offers few incentives to cultivate a personal vote. Unlike most European democracies,

15 Conclusion

Jorge M. Fernandes

258  Jorge M. Fernandes

whose electoral systems offer citizens some mechanism to reward or punish representatives individually, the Iberian legislatures are similar in the extent they make the political party the pivotal organisational unit of representation. Indeed, both Portugal and Spain have legislatures whose internal organisation is focused on political parties, which have strong powers and control agenda-setting, financial resources, and so forth. Portugal and Spain diverge regarding party system development. As Field and Gray show, the Spanish party system is undergoing essential changes whose full-fledge consequences remain to be seen. The emergence of new parties, such as Podemos, Ciudadanos, and Vox, put an end to several decades of bipartisanship, during which PSOE and PP dominated. On the contrary, Goes and Leston-Bandeira show that, despite the deep economic crisis, the Portuguese party system remains stable, without new challengers emerging. In that regard, Portugal is one of the few European countries whose party system has not suffered any change over the past few years. On the one hand, the change (or lack thereof) suggests that existing actors are fulfilling the representation needs of the population. On the other hand, an anaemic and weak civil society does not offer fertile ground for the emergence of new challengers. Jalali and Teruel examine parliamentary party groups in Iberian legislatures. The authors show that, in both countries, there is a strong dependence of parliamentary party groups on the extra-parliamentary organisation. Such finding is in line with recent developments in European democracies, where party facets have morphed onto increasingly undistinguishable entities. In the Iberian legislatures, the structural organisational weaknesses of parties create incentives for extra-parliamentary structures to exert active control over parliamentary groups, in that the state offers vital financial and staff resources for parties. Jalali and Teruel suggest that, together with the electoral system, the internal balance of power in political parties erodes the independence of individual MPs. The authors further examine the extent to which multilevel governance influences how parliamentary party groups operate. They suggest that increases in decision-making powers at the sub-national level reduced the power of the national parliament. Parliamentary party groups adapt their strategies at the national level to focus primarily on their policy-seeking goals, whilst focusing on office-seeking roles at the sub-national level. Fernandes and Riera turn to committee systems, which, together with parliamentary party groups, make for the organisational backbone of the legislature. The most important finding of the authors’ chapter is that Iberian legislatures are now more alike to their European counterparts where committees have an essential role in the functioning of the legislature. Both countries share similar committee allocation procedures wherein political parties determine committee composition. Furthermore, the authors underline that Portugal and Spain have had a similar trajectory concerning committee openness to the media. Over the past

Conclusion  259

decade, media have had increasing access to committee work, making it less effective and turning it more into a plenary-style work. There are two differences between Portugal and Spain regarding committee organisation. Fernandes and Riera show that, despite both countries having similar minority governments experiences, in Spain, there is a low overlap between committees and governmental portfolios. Moreover, Spanish MPs have fewer incentives to specialise because there are no multiple membership restrictions. In their contribution, Freire and Coller examine the ideological congruence between parliamentary elites and the citizenry before and after the 2008 Great Recession. Overall, the authors suggest that elites are increasingly mirroring society regarding gender, age, education, and profession. Indeed, Iberian legislatures are now more reflective of societal heterogeneity than ever before. Such empirical finding has important normative implications. Unlike conventional wisdom, which sees an ever-growing gulf between elites and citizens, Freire and Coller suggest that the gap is closing. In doing that, Iberian legislatures have more capacity to voice the demands of citizens and increase their responsiveness. As for the implications of the 2008 Great Recession, the authors suggest that in Spain the fledgling changes in the party system are a by-product of economic woes. Specifically, they see high electoral volatility and the entrance of two medium-size competitors as signs that citizens are open to new competitors in the electoral market. In the Portuguese case, the authors remark that the most critical effect of the crisis has been the changes in the coalescing patterns between the Socialists and the extreme-left parties. Freire and Coller’s contribution points out to the high uncertainty of such changes. Indeed, the authors say that upcoming elections will offer more evidence on the consolidation or erosion of representation changes. Espírito-Santo, Verge, and Morales make an innovative analysis of descriptive representation by examining the Iberian legislatures’ descriptive representation beyond gender. To be sure, over the past decade, research on gender in Portugal and Spain has benefited from several seminal contributions. In their contribution, Espírito-Santo and colleagues incorporate overlooked dimensions, such as age, ethnicity, sexuality, and ableness. Regarding female representation, Spain fares well in comparative terms (5th in Europe) whilst Portugal ranks worst (12th). In terms of other representation dimensions, the authors find that Portugal fares better than Spain in the inclusion of ethnic minorities. On the contrary, Spain outperforms Portugal in representing LGBT and disabled MPs. In line with most existing research, the authors find that minority representation in the Iberian legislatures is behind societal heterogeneity. Formal and informal barriers to representation subsist in both Portugal and Spain. In examining the role of interest groups in the Iberian legislatures, Lisi and Muñoz offer several insights well worth noting. Similar to other European countries, committees are the privileged arena for interest groups to exert

260  Jorge M. Fernandes

their influence. The fact that committees have significant decision-making powers, and conglomerate representatives with an above-average interest in the topic, makes them the most obvious channel of influence. However, the authors also underline the importance of informal contacts as a mechanism of influence. Lisi and Muñoz draw attention to differences between the Iberian legislatures. In Spanish pluralist model, NGOs play a more significant role in parliamentary hearings. On the contrary, Portugal has a more conservative, corporative, model wherein institutionalised groups have more opportunities to influence policy-making. The authors point out to ­differences in state centralisation and party system as the most critical determinants of cross-national differences between Portugal and Spain. Santana-Pereira and Martín examine political trust and support for parliaments in the Iberian legislatures. Such a topic is of particular relevance in an era of populism and citizen disaffection. Using survey data, the authors show that there is diffuse support for legislatures, which suggests that citizens see them as pivotal bodies for representation and decision-making in democratic polities. That is, citizens understand in abstract terms the importance of the legislative branch. However, the authors also find that citizens tend to have negative attitudes towards parliament in both countries, exhibiting high levels of distrust. By and large, perceptions of corruption and of lack of political responsiveness are the most important determinants for specific disaffection with parliament. Furthermore, Santana-Pereira and Martín use micro-level data to explore citizens’ opinions on parliamentary styles of representation. Their findings suggest that voters see MPs as being out of touch with ordinary citizens. Also, that MPs do not reflect the median opinion of voters. Such findings are puzzling insofar as they offer contradictory evidence to Freire and Coller’s chapter where the authors suggested an increased congruence between elites and voters. In their chapter, Ribeiro, Araújo and Rodrigues deal with the legislative process. The authors point out the pivotal role of legislatures in law-­making in both Portugal and Spain due to the prevalence of minority governments. Indeed, the existence of minority governments puts the legislative arena at the heart of political bargaining. However, Ribeiro and colleagues point out to problems in the quality of laws insofar as the need to accommodate differing positions results in legislative patchwork. There is an essential difference between Portugal and Spain that impacts the balance of power in executive-legislative relations: in Spain, the executive holds significant powers in legislative agenda-setting. Furthermore, the executive has extensive powers to veto parliamentary bills. On the contrary, in Portugal, executive-­ legislative relationships are tilted towards the legislative branch. Indeed, the Assembleia da República has the power to call back government’s ­decrees-law, change them and even suspend their enforcement. Sánchez de Dios analyses the accountability function of the Iberian legislatures. Overall, the author finds that both countries have a similar accountability structure. Legislative actors make extensive use of parliamentary written questions, oral questions, indirizzo, and committees. The chapter

Conclusion  261

provides rich empirical data on the use of accountability tools. Sánchez de Dios reminds us that the Spanish committee system has more powers in terms of its accountability function. Furthermore, in Spain, the Senate plays a vital role in monitoring the executive. The existence of a popularly elected president does not change accountability patterns in Portugal. Indeed, similar to Spain, the Portuguese government is solely accountable to the legislature. Aranda and Sande turn to how Iberian legislatures operate in the multilevel governance context of the European Union. In the early 2000s, ­Iberian legislatures were classified as slow adaptation parliaments due to their lack of involvement in European Union affairs. Today much has changed. Both parliaments have developed internal structures to deal with the increasing workload at the supranational level. However, there are differences between the two countries. In Spain, EU-related matters are concentrated in the ­European Affairs Committee. On the contrary, in Portugal, there is a horizontal distribution of labour in that workload is distributed depending on the committee’s focus, regardless of the national or supranational source. Furthermore, the Spanish Parliament has been slower than the Portuguese in taking up opportunities to have representatives in supranational cooperating bodies. Despite Spain’s decentralisation, Aranda and Sande suggest that regional parliaments do not use institutional channels to have a say in EU affairs. Oñate and Belchior turn to the relationship established between national and subnational legislatures in Iberia. The authors underline the role of political parties as cooperation and preference alignment mechanisms in vertical integration. That is, parties exert a strong centripetal influence curbing dissent and potential agency loss. Overall, the authors show that political ambition is a driving force, particularly in the Spanish case. Because MPs want to rise in the party-ranks and rise from regional to national politics, there are incentives to cultivate a party-centred approach to career vis-à-vis a regional-focus perspective. Finally, Garoupa, Solanes Mullor and Violante turn to the relationship between legislatures and constitutional courts in Iberia. In their contribution, the authors suggest that constitutional courts have been essential mechanisms for the consolidation of democracy. Recently, they have played an essential role as referees of critical political issues—specifically, the Catalan question in Spain and austerity politics in Portugal. Such interventions have increased the importance of constitutional courts in the eyes of the citizens as a potential veto player. Overall, Garoupa and colleagues suggest that there is a ­politicisation of these courts. The consequences of the 2008 Great Recession, particularly the changes in the Spanish party system, remain patent in the courts for years to come.

Future research

Legislative studies on the Iberian legislatures remain in their infancy. We hope this volume, first, contributes to disseminating existing work to a wider audience, and, second, establishes a roadmap of research for the years

262  Jorge M. Fernandes

to come. The increasing easiness in data access will facilitate research on topics that have been explored extensively in more mature democracies— for example, candidate selection, the impact of party discipline, committee assignments, the intervention of interest groups in decision-making. The study of the Iberian legislatures is a burgeoning academic field. We hope this volume paves the way for more contributions in the future.
